APPENDIX A-DESCRIPTION OF THE SERVICES
1     SCOPE OF WORKS
1.1   Environmental Scoping
The Consultant will describe and evaluate the current environmental situation
of the country, including:
a)    an overview  of national environmental policy, legislation and
directives relevant to the ROADS3 sector programme
b)   integaration within the overall road sector strategy for Mozambique;
c)   review of specific environmental guidelines for the roads sector
developed under the ROCS programmes
d)    overview of biophysical and socio-economic aspects including areas of
high priority for conservation of natural habitats, formally protected
areas such as national parks, fragile ecosystems, demographics,
ethnicity, gender aspects, HIV/AIDS etc. The Consultant will identify
distinct biophysical/socio-economic zones within Mozambique that
would be impacted on.
e)   review  of institutional capacity within ANE, other government
institutions and the private sector to monitor and enforce
environmental guidelines and to undertake EIAs on sub-projects.
1.2   EIA's for Phase 1: Investment programme
The Consultant will identify typical environmental impacts that will result
from road works in the distinct biophysical/socio-economic zones identified
during the environmental scoping exercise and especially with regard to the
roads program of Phase 1 of the Investment programme.
The Consultant will propose environmental guidelines to be used in the
individual road sub-projects to mitigate negative impacts. Existing EIA
material used by the Ministry of the Environment (MICOA) will be used
where available and the recommendation should be based on the existing ANE
Environmental Guidelines. Special recommendations with respect to
collaboration with MICOA on screening of sub-projects and undertaking EIAs
should be provided. The guidelines will cover:
a)    environmental screening of sub-projects, with reference to the
specific characteristics of the distinct ecological, demographic,
ethnic and socio-economic environments in Mozambique. The
screening guidelines should conform with the overall
FILE  COPY                    requirements of MICOA



b)    carry out the full comprehensive EIA on specific Phase 1
Investment programme, sub-projects as defined within ANE
known and approved (Road Sector Strategy study) works.
These studies should also include the requirements for EIA
standards adopted by MICOA
c)    comprehensive formulation of required mitigation measures,
cost impacts and assessments with related recommendations
c)    proposed modifications to existing Conditions of Contract and
works   specifications  to  incorporate  environmental
requirements.  These EIA's should reflect regional and
international  experience  and  environmental  guidelines
published by the Word Bank and other donor agencies
1.3   Monitoring and Enforcement
The Consultants will make recommendations for strengthening existing
institutions and building capacity to manage the environmental requirements
for sub-projects. This will include:
a)   recommendations  for  strengthening   ANE's  in-house
environmental capacity to manage, assess, monitor and control
the environmental impacts of the ROADS3 sub-projects
including: position of the environmental unit within ANE;
organisational structure; manpower resources and training
requirements; equipment needs and availability; budget
estimate for the unit; and monitoring and implementation
procedures  (ie  checklists,  environmental  performance
indicators etc)
b)    assessment of local private sector consulting capacity for
undertaking EIAs and routine environmental monitoring of site
works
c)   recommendations for allocating specific responsibility for
monitoring contractor performance at site level within the ANE
environmental unit, consultant supervision teams and the
provincial road agencies (DEPs).
1.4   Training Needs
The Consultant will identify needs for environmental training within ANE,
government institutions and private sector contractors and consultants.
Outline proposals for training programs for each group of stakeholders will be
prepared. An estimate of the cost must be made and a financing plan
proposed.



1.5   Public Consultation
Public consultation is an integral part of the EA process. Initial public
consultation will be undertaken with local communities, national and
international NGOs, scientific experts, relevant government agencies (at
national, provincial and district level) and the private sector.  The draft
recommendations of the study will be discussed with key stakeholders at
workshops to be held in Maputo, Beira and Nampula. Feed-back from the
workshops will be incorporated in the Final Report.
1.6   Environmental Management Plan
The overall mitigation plan will recommend feasible and cost effective
measures to prevent or reduce significant negative impacts to acceptable
levels. The Consultant will estimate the impacts and costs of these measures,
and of the institutional and training requirements to implement them.
Compensation to affected parties for impacts which cannot be mitigated
should be considered. The Consultant should prepare a short and concise
management plan including proposed work programs, budget estimates,
schedules, staffing and training requirements, and other necessary support
services to implement the mitigating measures.
1.7   Reporting
The draft EA report should focus on findings of the environmental scoping
exercise and preliminary conclusions and recommendations. The
comprehensive reporting of the EIA's for Phase 1: Investment programme
should be accommodated as a priority deliverable of the EA.  The
requirements within 3.2 should thus be allocated as a major component of the
EA but must furthermore be provided within the strict time scales defined
below.
The final reporting should also include alternative recommendations for the
environmental management system for presentation to the stakeholders.

IMPACTO
I INTRODUCTION AND ADMINISTRATION
1.1 INTRODUCTION
This document describes the socio-economic context of Mozambique for the purposes of the
environmental assessment of the ROADS3 Program. Information is provided to describe
population trends, the macro economy, and political context. More specific information is
provided in terms of overall access to services such as education and health and crop
production.
1.2 ADMINISTRATION
Mozambique is a large country with a land area of 784,090 square kilometres (kmi2) located in
south-east Africa sharing borders with Tanzania, Malawi, Zambia, Zimbabwe, and South
Africa. The Indian Ocean coastline of Mozambique is in excess of 2,700 kilometres. The
country is divided and administered into ten Provinces (See Map 1). Each Province is divided
into Districts of which 129 exist in Mozambique.  Each District is sub-divided for
administrative purposes into Administrative Posts and further sub-divided into Localities. The
country is also divided into three broad regions: North, Central and South. Northern Region
consists of Cabo Degado, Niassa, Nampula and Zambezia Provinces. Central Region consists
of Tete, Manica, and Sofala Provinces. Southern Region consists of Maputo, Gaza, and
Inhambane Provinces.  Regional categorisation is used more for the calculation and
presentation of broad indices such as agricultural production or to describe climatic conditions.
Each province is administered through a Governor selected by the Mozambican President. At
the provincial level there exists the extensions of the departments of government such as
Ministries of agriculture, education, and health. Policy is made at the central government level
to be implemented by the various provincial ministries. The Governor perforns the function
of co-ordinator of the various ministries. The Governor does not have the authority to select or
replace a provincial head of a ministry. This authority is vested in the respective Ministers of
government. These administrative and organisational structures are undergoing a period of
change as described in more detail in the political section below.
1.3 POLITICAL ENVIRONMENT
The second multi-party election was completed successfully in December 1999. There was a
high voter turnout of 64 percent. This was almost the same as the turnout for the first elections
in 1994. The ruling party, Frente de Libera,ao de Mo,ambique (FRELIMO), and its president
Joaquim Chissano were re-elected with 52.3 percent of the vote. The margin of majority over
the opposition, the Resistencia Nacional Mocambicana (RENAMO) leader Alfonso Dhlakama,
was reduced compared to 1994. FRELIMO, however, increased its parliamentary majority,
from 129 of 250 seats to 133 of 250 seats.
The first-ever local elections in Mozambique took place in the largest 33 cities and towns in
1998, and the new municipal assemblies began work in 1999. Although local elections were
boycotted by RENAMO and suffered from a low turnout, candidates from citizens' lists and

opposition parties were elected in six cities and have formed vocal oppositions within the local
assemblies. These elections marked the first step in an extensive process of decentralisation, in
which the new municipalities are progressively taking on powers over local services, basic
health and education, and local economic development.
Mozambique was a one-party State from independence in 1975 until 1990, when a new
constitution was adopted, creating a multiparty system with a separation of powers. The
system has a strong President who appoints the Government, a Parliament that must approve
the annual budget and plan as well as all legislation, and an independent court system. A
revised draft constitution, which would have shifted powers from the President to Parliament,
has been deferred to the new legislature, since consensus could not be reached on the final
version.
The changing political process runs much more deeply than simply elected bodies. A vibrant
civil society of pressure groups, associations of business people, women, peasants, trade
unions and churches is growing and becoming more active. An independent press is now well
established, ensuring that a broad spectrum of political views and interest groups is heard. This
political growth and vibrancy is particularly remarkable considering the national context: a
country only recently emerging from a long and bitter period of conflict, low levels of literacy
and widespread poverty, and all of this in a large country with poor communications.
The new land law marks the most direct interaction between the new democracy and economic
development issues. The law was approved in 1997 and regulations to enforce it came into
effect in 1999. This involved four years of consultation and debate, with frequent redrafting,
in one of the most open and democratic processes seen in Mozambique. Active campaigning
and lobbying by civil society and intervention by Parliament led to the approval of firm
occupancy rights for peasants and other landholders and the rejection of international pressure
to allow land sales and mortgages. Other provisions saw women's rights increased, traditional
leaders given a greater role, and the creation of a form of collective tenure. In addition, the
powers of the Council of Ministers to reallocate land were restricted.
The law confirmed the overriding rights of present occupants, and required that all outstanding
applications for land for development projects be resubmitted. Fewer than 10 percent of these
were, ending fears that widespread land grabbing would displace peasants. New and renewed
applications would only be considered if local people had been consulted. Pressure groups and
peasant associations launched massive publicity campaigns in 1999 to inform people of their
rights, to good effect.
1.4 TRADITIONAL ADMINISTRATION
Traditional administrative structures still exist in Mozambique based upon the concept of a
Chief or headperson of a community or ethnic group. This traditional structure exists, to lesser
or greater extent, in most localities side-by-side with the more modern administrative apparatus
of the State and political parties. In general terms the first government of an independent
Mozambique from 1975 attempted to replace the inherited colonial and traditional systems
with a centralised system based upon the political doctrines of socialism. With political and
economic reforrn since the mid-1980's the intention of the Government has been to replace a
highly centralised system by devolving increasing amounts of decision making authority to
provincial and district levels. This process is dynamic and subject to political influences.

2 POPULATION CHARACTERISTICS
2.1 POPULATION
The population of Mozambique according to the 1997 national census was 15.3 million. The
actual population recorded by the 1997 census was below expectations. Before the census the
population estimates were about 17 million and this is why documents often state the
population of Mozambique as over 17 million. The population growth rate is 2.2 percent per
annum. This will result in a Mozambican population in 2004 of about 18 million inhabitants.
Approximately 35 percent of Mozambican's are under the age of 30 years of age. The age
structure is as follows. 0-14 years constituting 46% of the population (male 4,141,915 and
female 4,115,191), 15-64 years constituting 51% of the population (male 4,324,102 and
4,868,518 female, and 65 years and over 3% of the population (male 184,606 and 243,595
female). Household sizes are large at 5.5 members, and the ratio between economically active
and passive members (less than 15 years or more than 60 years) of the population is high with
a dependency ratio of 1:1. These figures are presented in Table 1.
Table 1:     Household size, composition, and dependency ratios
Rural   Urban  Mozambique
Average household size       4.6      5.4       4.8
(persons)
Average dependency ration   55.5     53.7       55.2
Source: Understanding Poverty and Well-Being in Mozambique (December 1998)
Mozambique is a relatively sparsely populated country with an average population density of
19 persons per kM2. Some Provinces such as Niassa have population densities below 10
persons per km2. The most populous Provinces are Nampula with 2,977,233 inhabitants and
Zambezia with 2,891,809 inhabitants. The greatest population concentration is to be found in
Maputo Province in the contiguous urban areas of Maputo City and Matola City where
approximately 10 percent of Mozambique's population live. Most Mozambicans, over 80
percent, live in rural areas.

The urban population has been increasing in all areas of Mozambique. At the beginning of the
1980's, 13 percent of the population were located in urban centres. By the mid-1990's, 27
percent of the population were living in urban areas. Despite a generally rapid rise in the rate
of urbanisation, no single city in Mozambique has a population greater than one million
inhabitants. Maputo City combined with the City of Matola has a population of almost 1.5
million. The first eleven cities in Mozambique ranked by population size are as described in
Table 3.

2.2 ETHNICITY
The largest ethnic group in Mozambique is Macua-Lomwe who mainly live north of the
Zambezi River. This group accounts for around 40 percent of the total population. The other
main ethnic groups are the Tsonga in the southern lowlands, the Chopi and Tonga along the
coast and around Inhambane, the Shona in the central areas and the Makonde in the province
of Cabo Delgado. Mozambicans have been mixing with different cultures for centuries and
have absorbed many different characteristics and customs. Arabs had been trading and living
along the coast for hundreds of years before the arrival of the Portuguese, and when the new
colonisers arrived they quickly settled and intermarried, creating a rich cultural mix.
Mozambicans today reflect that melding of races.
The diversity of population is reflected in the variety of languages spoken. Approximately 60
languages and dialects are spoken. The official language is Portuguese although only about 10
percent of Mozambicans speak it as their first language. The main local languages are Macua
(38%), Tsonga (24%), and Sena (10%).
2.3 GENDER
Most Mozambican ethnic groups are patrilineal. Although most ethnic groups are patrilineal,
the prevalence of male migration necessitates that in many rural areas particularly in the
southern region of Mozambique, women run their families and livings. In Zambezia Province
the introduction of the prazos, the semi-feudal system for administration for several hundred
years of Portuguese dominance, was based on inheritance through the female line. FRELIMO
as part of the liberation struggle promoted concepts related to the equality of women. With
independence in 1975 these progressive attitudes towards the role of women continued
58602 Annexure B                   February 2001                             2-2



IMPACTO
including the introduction of new laws, which attempted to enshrine protection of rights for
women.
Despite the advancement of the rights of women under independent administration,
Mozambican girls and women do less well than boys and men. Maternal mortality rate is high
at 1,100 per 100,000 births. Access to health services directed specifically at women is
generally very poor. Literacy rates for women are much lower than for men. Family law in
Mozambique still discriminates against women. A married woman has limited rights to make
independent economic decisions or enjoy full ownership of assets. A widow's rights to
inheritance are secondary to males in the family. Girls in rural areas in general marry young,
often as soon as they reach puberty.

IMPACTO
3 ECONOMIC CHARACTERISTICS
3.1 ECONOMY
Mozambique made significant economic and social progress in the years immediately
following independence in 1975 particularly in terms of rapidly rising literacy rates.
Unfortunately the on-set of armed conflict rapidly eroded any gains made and ultimately
resulted to the collapse of the economy and civil society in general.  The eventual
establishment of the peace accords of October 1992 left Mozambique with devastating
physical, social, and economic damage. Economic damages were estimated at more than $20
billion (10 times the annual GDP in 1992).
Mozambique has undergone a period of significant economic recovery since the signing of the
peace accord in 1992. The annual growth rate, as defined by gross domestic product (GDP), in
the past three years has been calculated at 8.8, 9.7 and 7.3 percent, respectively. Annual
inflation has fallen from a level of almost 50 percent in 1996 to a current level of below 6
percent.
Despite the progress made, post-war reconstruction has still not been completed. Infrastructure
remains generally poor. Floods, such as those that occurred in southern Mozambique in
February 2000, set farmers, fishermen and other small businesses back many years. Many
believe Mozambique will continue to need high levels of international assistance to complete
reconstruction and to build a more robust base for the kind of development that is essential to
ending acute poverty.
A major privatisation programme was begun in 1990 and, by the end of the decade, 1,200
firms had been privatised. Most large companies, including those in the banking, brewing and
cement production sectors, were taken over by foreign firms. Many smaller companies were,
however, sold to local entrepreneurs. Private management of core state services is another
aspect of the ongoing structural reform programme as well as seeking foreign management
support. Examples include the assistance given to Mozambique customs, foreign management
of the urban water supply and the State Telephone Company. Private management of ports
and railways is now being organised but also in a way that maintains some Mozambican
interest.
Foreign investment has grown rapidly. In the seven years 1993-1999, $4.2 billion in foreign
investments were approved, $1.2 billion in foreign direct investment and $3 billion in loans.
Implementation has been slower with only $294 million actually spent in 1998 ($213 million
in investment and $81 million in private sector credit). In 1999, work started on the $1.3
billion Mozal aluminium smelter, by far Mozambique's largest investment project. The
project was commissioned in September 2000. In large part due to Mozal, 1999 saw $348
million in actual foreign direct investment and $495 million in foreign loans to the private
sector. Mozal alone is believed to account for more than 2 percent of the 7.3 percent per capita
GDP growth rate for 1999, leading to fears that growth is increasingly being generated by large
projects.
58602 Annexure B                  February 2001                            3-1



IMPACTO
Neighbouring South Africa accounts for nearly one half of the foreign investment, either
directly or via British-based companies. South African investment is split between large
projects like Mozal, privatisation of the breweries and small tourism and industry projects.
France and Portugal are the other two large investors. Concern is growing inside Mozambique
about the increasing dominance of foreign investors and contractors, which are stronger, better
capitalised and more experienced than domestic firms are. There is an acknowledged need for
support to the emerging indigenous private sector.
Traditional exports, notably prawns, cashew nuts and cotton, have remained steady over the
past three years, at $230 million per year. The Cahora Bassa dam located in Tete Province
began exporting power to South Africa and Zimbabwe in 1998 and electricity export earnings
jumped to $52 million in 1999. Service income from transport and tourism also stabilised at
$130 million a year. Exports of goods and services cover only one half of imports, excluding
capital imports for the big projects.
Aid remains absolutely essential to the Mozambican economy, and development assistance
levels are falling more slowly than had been predicted. Grants and loans to Mozambique
declined from $1,141 million in 1995 to $835 million in 1998 and are believed to have fallen
to $800 million in 1999. With the near total phasing-out of emergency assistance, food aid and
resettlement assistance, there has been a shift towards longer-term development goals. Of
assistance provided in 1998, $593 million (71 percent) was grants, of which $448 million was
bilateral, $93 million from the European Union and $52 million from the United Nations
system. The remaining $238 million comprised loans, of which $133 million came from the
World Bank. One third of donations and all loans are for investment projects; nearly one third
of grants is direct balance-of-payment support to the Government. Mozambique remains one
of the most aid-dependent countries in the world, but declining levels and the recalculation of
GDP means that aid in 1999 was only 19 percent of GDP, compared to 49 percent in 1995.
This is still well above the average for sub-Saharan Africa (7 percent in 1997) and for low
human development countries (10 percent in 1997).
Debt relief under the first phase of the Heavily Indebted Poor Countries (HIPC) initiative was
granted in mid-1999. More debt relief was granted than had been proposed in 1998 because
the international financial institutions had overestimated Mozambique's export growth;
roughly one half of Mozambique's public and publicly guaranteed debt was cancelled. In
1998, Mozambique had been scheduled to pay $344 million in debt service (interest and
principal repayments) but paid only $104 million. After HIPC debt relief, the debt service
payments in 2000 will be $76 million, a real gain of $28 million, although not enough to cover
the fall in aid. Mozambique is expected to have a further amount of debt cancelled in 2000
under the modified Cologne terms of HIPC, which could bring debt-service payments in 2001
down to $57 million.
The banking system, although now privatised, remains concentrated at Maputo. It has so far
proved unable to mobilise national savings or to lend effectively to entrepreneurs and small
businesses in rural areas, which will be key to development. Interest rates remain prohibitively
high (22 percent compared to inflation of under 6 percent). In general the business sector in
Mozambique remains weak, having not recovered from skill deficits and the decapitalization
caused by the war.
58602 Annexure B                  February 2001                            3-2



IMPACTO
Tax revenues remain at about 11.5 percent of GDP. Government current expenditure rose
from 9.5 percent of GDP in 1997 to 10.6 percent in 1999, and the levels are rising slowly.
There was substantial growth in 1999 because of an increase in the minimum wage and an
increase in civil service salaries. The Mozambican Human Rights League identified prevailing
low wages as the main cause of growing petty corruption. Health and education spending have
risen rapidly, while military spending has been falling since the end of the war. In 1999, the
Government expected to spend $96 million on recurrent education costs (such as teachers'
salaries), $89 million on defence and security, $86 million on debt service (after having been
granted HIPC debt relief) and $57 million on recurrent health costs.
3.2 POVERTY
Poverty in Mozambique has been defined as the inability of individuals to ensure for
themselves and their dependents a set of minimum basic conditions for their subsistence
(Ministry of Agriculture and Fisheries, (1998), Programmea Nacional de Desenvolvimento
Agrario (PROAGRI): Vol. I,II,e III). Although Mozambique's economy grew rapidly during
the last six years, poverty levels remain some of the highest in the world. Average monthly
consumption was estimated to be Mtl60,780 or about $170 per capita per year in 1998. In
accordance with the overall economic growth per capita income in Mozambique in 1999 had
risen to $230.
Mozambique was ranked 169 of 174 countries in the human development index for 1997.
Increasing life expectancy, higher enrolment rates and rising GDP are beginning to push up the
human development index at an increasing rate, with improvements of more than 3.5 percent
expected for both 1998 and 1999. Mozambique is well below the sub-Saharan average for
most development indicators. Approximately two-thirds (69%) of the population fall into the
category of poor (live on an income of less than US$1 a day), one-third of the population is
considered to be very poor, which translated into monetary terms means they live on less than
40 US cents a day.

IMPACTO
The First National Poverty and Well-being Assessment, published in 1999, showed that 69
percent of all Mozambicans live in poverty, with poverty higher in rural areas (71 percent) than
in urban areas (62 percent).  There are considerable variations in poverty rates across
Mozambique. The Central Region has the highest poverty rate (74 percent). The poorest
Provinces in Mozambique are Sofala (92 percent), Tete (82 percent), and Inhambane (82
percent). These figures are approximately the same as those published for 1997.
There is a strong relationship between education and poverty. There is a particularly strong
correlation between the education of the household head and the poverty of the household:
families whose head has a better education tend to be less poor. The Government of
Mozambique has set a target to reduce poverty by the year 2010 by about 30%. In order to
achieve this reduction Action Guidelines have been produced which include:
u Maintain rapid and sustainable economic growth
u Priority to human capital
u Increase agricultural production particularly in the family-sector
o Develop infrastructure in the rural areas
n Protect the most vulnerable groups of society through social security and assistance
programmes
o_ Promote employment
u  Improve technical and administrative capacity to monitor poverty

4 SERVICES
4.1 ACCESS TO SERVICES
Table 5 summarises access to a range of services.
Access to basic communications services, such as a post office or public telephone, is
extremely limited, with only three percent of rural households living in villages with such
services. The most commonly available services are markets and transportation services.
Distances to medical services other than traditional healers are long, averaging 20 to 30
kilometres, and reaching an average of 45 kilometres to a doctor. Below twenty percent of
rural households live in villages with a nurse, midwife, health post, or health centre and only
two percent of households live in a village with a doctor. There are differences across the
country with the Central Region having the poorest access to health services and the Southern
region the best.

Access to clean drinking water for most Mozambicans is a major problem. Currently only 12
percent of the rural population have access to a piped water supply and 31 percent have a
latrine in their home, compared to 66 and 68 percent, respectively, in urban areas.
4.2 EDUCATION
Mozambique's education system is divided into pre-primary school and educational schools.
Schools consist of general, technical or professional, and higher education. General education
consists of two levels, primary and secondary. Primary education consists of seven years of
schooling divided into two levels. Level 1 up to grade 5 (EPI) and level two from grade 6 to 7
(EP2).  Secondary education consists of five years divided into two cycles, first cycle
secondary from grade 8 to 10 (ESGI) and second cycle from grade 11 to 12 (ESG2).
Approximately two-thirds (68%) of households live in villages with a primary school. In
contrast, only two percent of households live in villages with a secondary school as shown in
Table 6.
Table 6:     Access to schools among rural household (percent)
All rural areas (%)
Primary school in village                   68
Primary school within 1 hour's travel time  30
Secondary school in village                 2
Source: Understanding Poverty and Well-Being in Mozambique (December 1998)
Table 7 shows that for adults aged between 18 to 65 the national literacy rate is 40 percent, but
much lower for females at 23.6 percent. The group with the lowest level literacy rates is
females in rural areas where only 15.7 percent are literate. Enrolment rates for children
between 7-11 years are shown in Table 8. 49.2 of this group are enrolled in schools with lower
rates (44.7%) for girls.

4.3 HEALTH
Mozambican life expectancy at birth is estimated to be 42 years. Infant mortality per 1,000
live births is 134. Malaria, measles, diarrhoea, and acute respiratory infections are the biggest
killers of children. Nutritional status is low with national estimates of stunted growth high at
10.8 percent of infants indicating excessive levels of malnutrition. One of every six children
born in Mozambique has a low birth weight, defined as less than 2.5 kilograms. Low birth
weight typically reflects poor health or nutritional status of the mother during pregnancy.
The war cut Mozambique off from the neighbouring States, and delayed the arrival of the
HIV/AIDS epidemic, although the return of refugees and the new opening to trade led to rapid
change. Malaria remains a bigger killer, and adult HIV infection rates are about 15 percent,
significantly less than in some neighbouring States (South Africa 25%, Zimbabwe 26% and
Malawi 23%). Mozambique, however, proved unable to make the maximum use of the extra
window of opportunity, and HIV/AIDS gained real prominence as an issue only in 1999. One
reason was the rising death rate, at present over 60,000 per year, and the recent
acknowledgement of AIDS as the cause of death in the obituaries of prominent people.
The national strategic plan to combat sexually transmitted diseases and HIV/AIDS was
approved in 1999, and aims to keep HIV/AIDS levels at 17 percent of the adult population.
Even so, the impact of the rising incidence of HIV/AIDS on the country will be dramatic. In
the absence of effective and timely measures to curb the spread of the virus, new projections
suggest that by 2005 there will be 150,000 adult deaths and 20,000 child deaths each year.
This will actually reduce life expectancy in Mozambique from the present 42 years to 37 years,
and will halve the rate of population growth. The number of orphans caused by AIDS will
exceed 700,000 by 2005. The economic impact is expected to be significant; peasant-farming
families (the majority of the population) will have fewer workers and more dependants, while
industry and the state apparatus will lose skilled people.
The focus of the new strategy is to target people who have casual sexual relations (an
estimated 1.6 million in 1999) and those living with HIV/AIDS (approximately 15% or 2.6
million of the population) and to ensure access to essential and high-quality prevention
methods. This involves more work with vulnerable groups, high-risk groups, such as the
young and highly mobile people, and more inter-ministerial co-operation to involve sectors
such as education, agriculture, youth and others as part of setting up channels to reach all target
groups. Promotion of condom use, sex education, treatment for sexually transmitted diseases,
counselling and voluntary testing and strengthening the negotiating power of women and girls
are seen as essential. The aims of the national strategy seem modest, for example, to have one
third of adults using condoms during casual sexual relationships.
58602 Annexure B                  February 2001                            4-3






IMPACTO
5 LIVELIHOOD STRATEGIES
5.1 AGRICULTURE
An estimated 80 percent of Mozambicans live in rural areas. Of this group, 98.5 percent have
at least some land and 90 percent gain most of their living through subsistence agriculture.
Most farmers have only a hoe as their main farming implement and fewer than 10 percent use
fertiliser or incorporate irrigation practices. Raising the productivity of peasant farmers is
considered critical to combating poverty, and securing their land occupancy rights is seen as
the essential first step. Table 9 summarises farm details.
Table 9:      Percentage of population with agricultural land and mean
area per household (Ha)

Do not have agricultural land (%)      1.5    45.6        10.5

Households with more than one plot (%)   72.5    30.7     26.9
Mean area per household (Ha)          2.4      1.9        2.4
Source: Understanding Poverty and Well-Being in Mozambique (December 1998)
Maize is the most commonly grown crop, grown by approximately 75 percent of households
who produce crops. Cassava is grown by 60 percent of households who have agricultural land.
Just over 50 percent of households who have land grow beans. About 25 to 30 percent of
households in rural areas produce horticultural crops. Table 10 summarises the average yield
of important crops across the country.

IMPACTO
The food security and nutritional status of inhabitants in the Northern Region of Mozambique
are the highest in the country due to the occurrence of relatively fertile soils and good rain
conditions. The Central and Southern Regions are characterised by lower food security and
nutritional status due to poor soils and lower rainfall amounts. Farm sizes in the Northern
region on average are greater (greater than 2 ha) than the Central and Southern regions (less
than I ha). Poor agricultural production over large areas of the country has resulted in the
dynamic that 98 percent of crop production is subsistence and small holder based with only 16
percent of households having a surplus stock of produce to sell commercially.
Table 11 summarises the national figures for areas cultivated by crop and total production for
the growing season 1999 to 2000. The most important crop in terms of total area cultivated is
maize followed by cassava but cassava is more important in terms of yield than maize.
Table 11:    National Total Cultivated Area (Ha) by Crop and Total

Source: System National de Avis Provo August 2000
Livestock production occurs to a much greater extent in the Southern region where over 60
percent of the cattle populations occur.
Limited livestock numbers and poor agricultural productivity has resulted in a population that
has developed livelihood strategies that rely on natural resources. Such dependency is high
with 85 percent of the energy and between 30 percent and 70 percent of the protein
requirements of rural communities being derived from the natural resource base.  The
utilisation and trade of wildmeat constitutes a significant input to many rural household
economies. During the early 1990's it was estimated that about five million people in
Mozambique were dependent upon wildlife for meat protein, with rural communities deriving
between 40 percent and 80 percent of their protein requirements from bushmeat.
5.2 OTHER LIVELIHOOD STRATEGIES
Historically migration of a male member of a family has been a major livelihood strategy for
families particularly living in the Southern Region of Mozambique. During the second half of
the 1900's Mozambicans began labour migration to the South Africa mines. At its peak,
between 1950 and 1970, Mozambican migrant labour in South Africa was around 800,000
workers while the total number of migrants was over one million workers. Since the end of the
1980s the number of mine workers has fallen dramatically as the South African mining
industry has restructured. Currently there are probably only about ten thousand Mozambican
miners in South Africa. Although the numbers of Mozambican miners working in South
Africa is small in comparison to historical peaks, the social and cultural impacts upon families
58602 Annexure B                   February 2001                             5-2



IMPACTO
is still considerable. These impacts are reflected in the desire of rural Mozambicans, in
particular young men, to migrate to South Africa as soon as possible. With the reduction of
opportunities in the mining sector, Mozambicans have moved into other sectors of the South
African economy.
As with the sectors of the South African economy Mozambicans have moved into, the patterns
and duration of migration have also changed. The major changes are the duration of migration
periods, which tend to be far shorter, and the increasing evidence of more female migration.
The economic and social impacts of labour migration are dramatic, but perhaps still
underestimated and under researched. For example, the current devastation caused by the
rapidly rising rate of HIV/AIDS infection it is suggested can be attributed in part to the
negative impacts of labour migration.
58602 Annexure B                   February 2001                              5-3






IMPACTO
6 ARCHAEOLOGICAL AND CULTURAL SITES
Mozambique has a rich archaeological and cultural heritage. Formalisation of archaeological
and cultural sites is however generally low.
Indigenous archaeological and cultural sites are numerous and correspond to the history of
Mozambique's many different ethnic groups. Most ethnic groups have sacred burial sites
normally used for Chief's and often located in well wooded or forest areas. In the higher areas
of Mozambique particularly in more mountainous Provinces such as Manica sacred cave sites
have been identified. Many ethnic groups identify sacred forests, usually the venue for a variety
of spiritual ceremonies by spirit mediums, chiefs and selected elders from the difference clans.
Any new road construction programme has to identify such sites with local populations to avoid
possible damage to local archaeological and cultural sites.
Sites such as forts exist up and down the coastline of Mozambique as testaments mainly to the
colonisation by the Portuguese from the 15th Century. Along the main rivers such as the
Zambezi there is also the remnants of forts and Portuguese colonial buildings. The only
internationally recognised cultural heritage site is Mozambique Island off the coast of Nampula
Province in the Northern Region of Mozambique.

1 VEGETATION AND FLORA
1.1 VEGETATION TYPE IDENTIFICATION
At a broad level of mapping 22 broad vegetation/habitat types can be distinguished in
Mozambique (Figure 1). The vegetation map and description of flora is based on mapping
units defined by Wild and Barbosa (1967). At a more detailed level of mapping many other
sub-habitats may be distinguished within these broad vegetation types.
In Mozambique the main vegetation type, in terms of structure, is savanna woodland. The
savanna experiences a distinct cooler and drier season (April to October) alternating with a
hotter and wetter season (November to March).
The most common woodland type (based on species composition) is miombo woodland.
Miombo is the term used to describe woodland types dominated by members of the genus
Brachystegia and/or closely related genera such as Julbernardia. Miombo covers most of
Zimbabwe and Zambia, is dominant in Malawi, Tanzania, Angola and Mozambique and occurs
in Botswana and Congo. In Mozambique miombo covers much of Niassa, Cabo Delgado,
Nampula, Zambezia, Sofala, Manica and Inhambane Provinces. There are several miombo
woodland type.
The second most extensive woodland type is mopane woodland occurring in the Limpopo-Save
area and in the mid-Zambezi Valley. Mopane woodland is dominated by the tree species
Colophospermum mopane. The typical mopane tree savanna is more or less open and occurs on
variable but generally calcareous soils.
Other woodland types include Acacia woodland (in drier southern and central areas) and sub-
littoral coastal woodland in southern coastal Mozambique.
Non-woodland vegetation types include:
o small "islands" of Afromontane habitats (forests and heaths) along the eastern border with
Zimbabwe and Malawi where the Mozambican plains rise up sharply to the western rim
of the great southern African plateau and other isolated montane areas;
o  inland halophytic communities in the valley of the Changane River, a tributary of the
Limpopo River;
o  freshwater swamps/sudd occur on the Mozambique-Malawi border the largest being
associated with Lake Chilwa;
o  vegetation formations on alluvial plains are predominant in the Zambezi, Limpopo and
Nkomati valleys;
o parabolic dune forests along the southern sector of the Mozambican coast; and
o  mangroves in sheltered bays, estuaries and deltas.
Because of their small extent mangroves are not shown in Figure 1. Mangrove distribution is
presented separately in Figures 3a, 3b and 3c.

1.2 DESCRIPTION OF VEGETATION TYPES
i   Libombos Woodland/Shrubland
This vegetation type occurs at moderate altitude between 350 and 800m on the Libombo and
Pequenos Libombos. Mountains on the border with Swaziland and South Africa. Annual
rainfall is 600-1200mm. The tree and shrubs of this type are generally secondary and include
Pterocarpus rotundifolius, Bauhinia galpinii, Peltophorum africanum, Acacia nigrescens, A.
swazica, A. caffra, Maerua rosmarinoides, etc. Olea africana is sometimes common on
slopes. There is a rich herbaceous flora.
Monospecific patches of Androstachysjohnsonii (the Libombo ironwood or "simbirre") occur
on rhyolitic soils near Goba within this vegetation type.
ii    Acacia Woodland
There are two extensive areas of Acacia woodland in Mozambique viz.:
A southern formation (in the area of Moamba, Magude and Guija) where Acacia nilotica
subsp. kraussiana, A. senegalense and A. nigrecens, are the main Acacia species.
A second large area occurs in central Mozambique running approximately in a north-east
direction through Manica and Sofala Provinces comprising communities of scattered trees and
shrubs where Acacia nigrescens is the dominant in the tree/shrub layer.
iii    Sublittoral Woodland and Forest
An Albizia-Afzelia-Sclerocarya mainly open woodland occurs between Maputo and Macia in
the sublittoral zone of Mozambique south of the R. Limpopo. Much of the natural woodland
has been replaced by small-scale farms especially adjacent to the ENI. Only wild species and
introduced tree species with edible fruits are left in the farming areas. Treeless secondary
regenerating vegetation is common on sandy soils and often Helichrysum kraussii is dominant.
In the south-eastern sublittoral region on Quatemary and Recent sand dunes (Bela Vista,
Salamanga and Zitundo) the Albizia-Afzelia-Sclerocarya formations are replaced by Syzygium-
Garcinia-Dialium formations. Patches of swamp forest occur scattered in the area south of the
Maputo Elephant Reserve. Swamp forests were never extensive and many have been drained and
cleared for agriculture. The remaining relicts rate top priority for conservation. True swamp forest
canopy tree are Ficus trichopoda, Syzygium cordatum, Voacanga thouarsii and Rauvolfla caffra.
iv    Mopane Woodland
Colophospermum mopane woodland, after miombo woodland, is the second most important
woodland type in Mozambique. Extensive mopane formations occur in two main areas, the
Zambezi valley and the area between the Limpopo and Save Rivers
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                                3
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPA CTO Lda                                                           40SMEC
In the Zambezi valley the Colophospermum mopane woodland is usually medium or tall (10-
1 5m), almost pure but with some Adansonia digitata (baobob). The soils are brownish, clayey,
compact, with some calcareous material, relatively deep and mainly derived from Karroo
formations. In pans and depressions with calcareous soils grassland with Setaria species is
dominant.
In shallow, stony and also calcareous soils Acacia nigrescens (subdominant), Dalbergia
melanoxylon, Acacia nilotica, Combretum ghasalense, Dalbergia boehmii, Adansonia digitata,
(basbob) Albizia harveyi, Terminalia prunioides, T stuhlmannii, Ziziphus mucronata
(ma,anica), Combretum apiculatum, Afzelia quanzensis (chanfuta), etc. accompany the still
dominant mopane.
On the banks of the Zambezi River the mopane is often replaced by Ziziphus mauritiana,
Diospyros mespiliformis and sometimes Berchemia discolor.
The mopane woodland between the Limpopo (including it's tributary the Elefantes River) and
Save River basins occur on mainly on Quaternary calcareous soils. Annual rainfall is irregular
and light, being less than 400mm and decreasing inland.
The typical mopane tree savanna is more or less open. Accompanying species are Ximenia
americana, Salvadora angustifolia var australis, Azima tetracantha, Adenium obesum var.
multiflorum, Boscia albitrunca, Pachypodium saundersii, Dombeya kirkii, Sansevieria spp.,
Euphorbia spp., Courbonia glauca, etc.
Within the main mopane savanna patches of other associations occur. These are too small to be
mapped and include:
o Tree savanna with Guibourtia conjugata frequent on in light sandy soils.
o Patches of dry Androstachysjohnsonii (ironwood) woodland on reddish brown sandy soils.
o Julbernardia globiflora woodland with Brachystegia glaucescens on shallow soils on
stony slopes and outcrops.
o Patches of tree-shrub Acacia woodland Acacia goetzei or A. nigrescens, or A. senegal
communities.
o In drier alluvial areas on the valley floors of the large rivers are formations containing
Acacia nigrescens. These are very tall in the alluvium of the Elefantes River.
v    Lowland Palm Savanna
This vegetation occurs in coastal areas of Nampula, Sofala and Inhambane Province. It
occupies badly drained lowland, sublittoral zones with an abundance of palm and grading
usually into savanna woodlands. Palm species include: Phoenix reclinata, Hyphaene crinita
and Borassus aethiopum. In pools and swamps occurs Phragmites communis, Hydrocotyle sp.,
Nymphaeae capensis, Cyperus, etc.
58602 Annexure C                  December2000                                4
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                               SMEC
vi   Deciduous Miombo Woodland
Several types of deciduous miombo woodland are found in Mozambique distinguished by
species composition.
Deciduous miombo Woodland occurs on Sul do Save Sands (Brachystegia spiciformis). This
formation is found on sandy sub-littoral soils between Xai-Xai and Nova Mambone and may
become very dense.
A mosaic Berlinia orientalis, Brachystegia spiciformis-Pteleopsis occupies the sub-littoral
zone of Northern Mozambique between Macomia-Mipande and Palma-Pundanhar at low
altitudes, generally below 200m.
A  mosaic Adansonia, Sterculia, Pteleopsis, Millettia-Brachystegia spp., Julbernardia
globiflora type occurs in the north-east of Mozambique in a belt between the sublittoral
lowlands and the beginning of the mesoplanaltic zone between Matibane (Nacala) and
Macomia. There is also a small patch in the extreme north-east between Pundanhar and
Quionga, near the Rovuma River, at altitude between 50 and 250m.
A Julbernardia, Brachystegia, Ostryoderris, Sclerocarya, Pterocarpus, Albizia, etc. deciduous
miombo is confined to the sublittoral zone from Nova Sofala to Mambone between the Buzi
River.
vii   Dry Deciduous Miombo
Dry deciduous miombo (Southern Sub-littoral Urrongas) comprising Brachystegia spiciformis
- Julbernardia globiflora in a mosaic with deciduous forest of Afzelia - Sideroxylon - Balanites
occurs on the mottled red or reddish-brown soils of the Urrongas region between Inhambane
and Mambone. This vegetation type occupies much of the centre of Inhambane and occurs
inland from the Deciduous Miombo described above. It consists of species such as Julbernardia
globiflora, Pterocarpus angolensis, Burkea africana, Albizia versicolor, Afzelia quanzensis,
Sclerocarya birrea, Strychnos spp., Strophanthus spp., and Securidaca longipedunculata
A Julbernardia type occurs in the Provinces of Manica and Sofala, Tete and Zambezia In
Manica and Sofala Provinces it occupies the northern slope of the valley of the R. Save.
A Brachystegia boehmii, B. allenii, Julbernardia-Adansonia mosaic formation covers the
lower valleys of the rivers of the northern portion of Mozambique northwards from the R.
Lurio and the subplanaltic areas between 150 and 700m.
viii   Tardily Deciduous Miombo - Lowland Type
There are various types of Lowland Tardily Deciduous Miombo.
Brachystegia spiciformis, B. boehmiii, Julbernardia globiflora type.
This type occurs in Zambezia Province, i.e., in Baixa Zambezia from Derre to Ligonha and the
Moma region between elevations of 100 and 200m.
Brachystegia boehmii, Julbernardia, Hirtella-Parinari, Protea, Philippia type.
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                                5
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                              4jSMEC
The miombo element consists of Brachystegia boehmii, Julbernardia globiflora, Hirtella
zanguebarica and a poorly drained tree/shrub savanna of Parinari, Protea, Philippia, etc. This
type is found in the sublittoral zones north of Maganja da Costa and Namacurra to Moma.
Brachystegia spiciformis, B. boehmii-Adansonia, Sterculia type
This type covers the sub-planaltic zone between 50 and 500m. especially in the sub-littoral
areas from Angoche to Namapa in soils which are generally red to grey and derived from
granite, gneiss. Annual rainfall is about 1000mm.
ix    Tardily Deciduous Miombo (North-Eastern Median Altitude)
(Brachystegia utilis, B. boehmii, B. spiciformis and Brachystegia spp.).
This type covers the large meso-planaltic areas above 500m in the north of Mozambique i.e in
the regions of Alto Lugenda, Maua, Marrupa, Nungo and along the slopes to Balama and Alto
Mesalo as well as along the mountain ridge to the Macondes plateau. Annual rainfall is
between 900-1400mm.
x    Semi-Moist Deciduous Miombo (Median Altitude)
Brachystegia spiciformis-Julbernardia globiflora savanna woodland is very important on the
plateaux of Manica and Sofala Provinces; it covers a great part of Zambezia Province and
stretches over extensive areas north of the Ligonha River.
xi   High Rainfall Moist Miombo Woodland
This type of Brachystegia spiciformis woodland merges with the mesoplanaltic semi-
deciduous forests of Pteleopsis, Erythrophleum and Newtonia. It occurs in the Chimoio plateau
and on the higher parts of the Zambezia Province.
xii   Deciduous (Basement Complex) Woodland
(Pteleopsis-Combretum-Pericopsis-Acacia)
There are two important areas of this type represented in Figure 1. One is found north of
Molumbo near the Malawi border in the Amaramba slopes facing the Lake Chilwa depression
and the other in a similar region near the Lugenda River extending from Malawi.
xiii   Mixed Baobab Woodland
This woodland type occurs along a zone of medium altitude (about 500 - 600 m.) between the
southern margin of the Zambezi Valley and the Barue plateau north of Mungari on formations
derived from granites and gneisses or from post-Karroo lava. It comprises Adansonia digitata,
Sterculia africana, Kirkia acuminata, Cordyla africana, Sterculia apendiculata, Milletia
stuhlmanni, Lonchocarpus capassa, Otryoderris stuhlmanni,Sclerocarya birrea etc.
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                                6
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                              wpSMEC
This formation grades into (eastwards) a dry deciduous tree or shrub savanna (sub-planaltic)
comprising Adansonia, Sterculia-Acacia nigrescens, Pterocarpus brenanii-Diplorhynchus.
xiv    Moist Forest/Woodland
(Pteleopsis myrtifolia-Erythrophleum suavelons- Brachystegia)
Mixed formations of dense semi-deciduous tall forest, at low altitudes, of Pteleopsis
myrtifolia-Erythrophleum suavelons with Hirtella zanguebarica; forming a mosaic with the
catena facies of Brachystegia spiciformis-Julbernardia globiflora occupy the sublittoral central
zone in two belts to the north and south of the Zambezi delta, e.g., in the south from Beira
(Cheringoma) to Inhaminga and Marromeu, and in the north in the areas of Nicuadala,
Namacurra and Maganja da Costa. The forests on the Cheringoma plateau represent the best
hardwood production forests in Moambique.
xv    Moist Semi-Deciduous Forests of the Mesoplanaltic Slopes and Lowlands of
Eastern Mozambique -
(Pteleopsis myrtifolia, Erythrophleum, Newtonia or Millettia).
In this group are included two subtypes as follows:
Pteleopsis myrtifolia, Erithrophleum suaveolens forests with an abundance of Newtonia
buchananii are typical of the subplanaltic slopes of Manica and Sofala.
Similar forests but with Newtonia buchananii rare or absent are typical of the slopes of the
Macondes plateau in Cabo Delagado Province.
xvi   Littoral Thicket and Forest of Recent Dunes
This vegetation type occurs on recent dunes formed from sands of aeolian origin. The most
extensive formation of this type occurs along the parabolic dune coast between Ponto do Ouro
and Bazaruto Island. Some of the highest vegetated dunes in the world are reported to occur
along this stretch of coast. Dominant tree species include Diospyros rotundifolia, Euclea
natalensis, Mimusops caffra, and Apodytes dimidiata. Figure 2 shows a typical dune
vegetation transect .

xvii   Dry Conifer Montane Forests
(Widdringtonia, Podocarpus or Juniperus).
In Mozambique this type is restricted. It is found the Chimanimani and Tsetsera Mountains
(Manica Province) between 1500 and 2000m where Podocarpus milanjianus is frequent.
Widdringtonia cupressoides is found in the same areas as well as at Rotanda and Mavita. Both
species also inhabit the southeast slope of the Gorongosa Mountain. Podocarpus milanjianus
also occurs on the slopes of Gurue Mt. in Zambezia Province and on Domue Mt. at 1850m.
near Metengo-Balama, Angonia, Tete Province.
xviii   Moist Evergreen Forest
(Maranthes polyandra, Khaya, Aphloia, Macaranga).
Relict areas of this type exist mainly below the dry conifer montane forests on the slopes of the
Chimanimani, Inyanga and Gorongosa Mountains. Some of the principal tree species include
Erythrophleum suaveolens, Blighia ungugata, Zanha golungensis, Melanodiscus oblongus.
Similar forest patches are found on the slopes and gullies of the mountains at medium altitudes
between 1200 and 1600m where annual rainfall is about 1700 to 2000mm. Examples occur in
Zambezia, especially in Gurue, Milange, Tacuane, Metolola-Chiperone and in smaller patches
at Namarr6i, Morrumbala, etc.
xix    Vegetation on Saline Soils
A large expanse this inland halophytic type is found in the valley of the Changane River, a
tributary of the Limpopo. Annual rainfall is about 500mm. The soils are known locally as
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                                 8
Mozambique Road Sector Programme       Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                             *SMEC
"tonga". These areas are arid and uninhabited. In areas of high salinity trees are virtually
absent. The flora is strongly halophytic with succulent, creeping plants of Arthrocnemum sp.
Chenolea and Salicornia and scattered bushes of Atriplex, Suaeda, etc.
xx    Vegetation on Alluviums
The largest extent of this vegetation type occurs in the lower Zambezia River/Delta system. In
the higher and better drained zones the alluviums may support a tree/shrub savanna (mainly
Acacia spp.). Grasses mainly belong to the Paniceae. In badly drained areas trees such as Ficus
spp., Kigelia and Pandanus may be present. In swampy depressions or around lagoons are
palm communities with Borassus aethiopum, Hyphaene crinita, H. coriacea or Phoenix
reclinata.
Extensive moist grasslands occur in low-lying badly drained areas with Setaria spp. dominant
and Ischaemum afrum very common. In the Zambezi delta, in very wet localities, elephant
grass (Pennisetum purpureum) is common.
Aquatic flora of swamps and lagoons comprise floating species such as Eichhornia crassipes,
Pistia stratiotes and Trapa natalensis (the least common). The zone with plants having
submerged or growing at lagoon edges are Phragmites spp, Cyperus papyrus, Nymphaea spp,
Typha latipholia subsp. capensis, Cyperus esculentus, C. rotundus, Marsilea, Scirpus spp,
Ludwigia spp, and Paspalidium platyrachis.
The lower Limpopo and Nkomati riverine systems are characterized by a fringing riverine
forest comprising Ficus spp. (usually dominant), Trichilia emetica, Kigelia africana, Lannea
stuhlmanii and Xanthocerra zambeziaca. Behind this fringe extensive grasslands with scattered
trees occur periodically flooded, badly drained alluviums. Tree species include: Acacia albida,
Diospyros mespiliformis, Ficus sycomorus, Ficus glumosa, Lonchocarpus capassa and
Cordyla africana.
A seasonally-inundated lowland formation (the Gorongosa "tandos") links the Zambezi
Valley with the Pungue system in the south via the Urema trough (Rift Valley). Tree species
include Acacia spp. and Hyphaene sp.
xxi   Swamp/Sudd
Sudd is well developed on the shores of Lake Chilwa (bordering with Malawi) and consists of
Cyperus papyrus, other Cyperus spp., and Phragmites reed beds.
xxii   Mangroves
Mangrove forests are floristically well developed in the northem and central sectors of the
coast and less so along the southem sector. Estimates made in 1992 based on satellite imagery
indicated that the mangroves cover 396,080 ha in Mozambique. This represents a reduction of
3.9% since 1972 (Saket and Matusse, 1994). The distribution of mangroves along the
Mozambican coast is shown on Figures 3a, 3b and 3c.
58602 Annexure C                  December2000                                9
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                               E$SMEW C
Three main coastal types can be distinguished in Mozambique viz. the northern coral coastline,
the central swamp and delta coastline and the southern parabolic dune coastline. The mangroves
associated with each of these coastlines are described below:
(a) Northern Sector: c. 770km between Rovuma River in the north to the Primeiro/Segundo
Archipelago in the south (17209: covering entire coast of Cabo Delgado Province and the
coastline of Nampula Province to Mossuril.
The northern coastal sector which runs almost N-S between the Tanzanian border and the town
of Mossuril is highly indented with several small bays, including Pemba Bay. Other small bays
and river mouths are sheltered by sandy spits on their southern sides.
Ten major streams enter in this sector and tidal forests are confined to the vicinities of their
mouths as well as to the shores of bays and along salt-water creeks that may penetrate several
kilometres inland. In creek mangroves Rhizophora mucronata and Sonneratia alba line the
canals behind which Bruguiera gymnorrhiza and Xylocarpus granatum may dominate In drier
sites thickets of Ceriops tagal (often stunted) may form broad belts. These may give way to a
dwarf Avicennia marina zone.
Further inland the mangrove is replaced by large expanses of highly saline tidal flats. Salt pans
for the commercial production of salt are located in these areas. Well developed creek
mangrove formations occur in Nacala Bay, on the mainland in the Lumbo/Mossuril Districts
opposite Ilha de Mocambique and Mecufi south of Pemba.
(b) Central Sector: c. 970 km between Angoche (16 14'S) and Bazaruto Island (21 10'S):
Covering southern coast of Nampula Province and entire coastlines of Zambezia and Sofala
Provinces.
Between Mossuril (Nampula Province) and Quelimane (Zambezia Province) the coastline
comprises many small deltas interspersed by sandy strips. A dozen major streams reach the coast
here, including the Meluli, Ligonha, Molocue, Licungo and Luala.
Mangroves are especially well developed near Angoche (1610'S/3958'E) on the Meluli Delta,
near Moma (1646'S/3910'E) at the mouth of Ligonha, near Nova Naburi (1647'S/38052'E) at
the mouth of the Molucue River, near Moebase (1704'S/3841'E) at the mouth of the M'lela
(1708'S/38034'E) and at the mouth of the Raraga River (1753'S/3657'E) on the Luala Estuary.
These rivers are tidal for many kilometres upstream. Mangroves are continuous along the open
coast in places; elsewhere they occur immediately behind low coastal dunes.
Mangroves spread laterally along tributaries, which enter the main streams parallel to the coast, a
situation best seen at the Raraga Mouth north of Quelimane.
The Zambezi River discharges via distributaries through a wide swampy delta. Mangrove swamps
are extensive in the lower delta, but they do not extend up the main channels for more than 15 km,
although the tide penetrates much farther than this.
South of the Zambezi, the alluvium from no less than 18 rivers has produced a continuously
deltaic coast as far as Beira 1949'S/34052'E), and mangroves here occur on the open sea coast.
They are also present in depressions behind frontal sand dunes, and along the numerous channels
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                                10
Mozambique Road Sector Programme       Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                                SMEC
which gives the coast a scalloped appearance. Mangroves are also prolific south of Beira, for a
distance of some 160km, on a stretch, highly indented, and island dotted coast. This is fed with
alluvium and freshwater by six major rivers including the Pungue, Buzi, Gorongosa and Save,
which drain the central western highlands. At the mouth of the Pungue River, at Beira, a vast
swamp some 50km deep and 27 km long reaches inland from the south bank of the river.
Coastal swamps are extensive at the mouth of the Buzi River, and there is a particularly fine
stand at Novo Sofala Bay (201O'S/3440'E). They continue down the coast, in a belt traversed
by a dozen small streams, to the delta of the Save River.
The delta of the Save is covered by mangroves, and tides penetrate far upstream. Swamps and
lagoons fringe the lowest 30 - 40 km of the river. This extremely swampy section of the coast
ends at Macavane (21028'S/35'04'E) where the Govuro River enters the head of a bay behind the
spectacular spit of Bartolomeu Dias. This river is entirely coastal and flows parallel to the coast
from S-N. The landward side of Bartolomeu Dias Bay is deeply dissected, deltaic, and covered
with tidal forests where nine small streams enter the bayside, but the great spit itself is sandy with
only a few mangroves.
The tidal forests between Beira and Macavane are the richest in Mozambique, and are probably
among the richest on the entire East African seaboard.
(c) Southern Sector: c. 850 km from Bazaruto Island southward to Ponta de Ouro: covering
most of the coastline of Inhambane Province and the entire coastlines of Gaza and Maputo
Provinces.
The most well developed mangrove systems occur in Maputo Bay, Inhambane Bay, the
Morrumbene estuary and Bazaruto Bay. In Maputo Bay the largest mangrove formations occur
along the sheltered western shore of the Machangulo Peninsula, the southern shore of the Bay and
the Nkomati estuary.
Mangroves near to Maputo and Matola have been devastated. The Costa do Sol mangrove has all
but disappeared.

1.2 FOREST COVER
A total of 19,054,000 hectares has been identified as high and medium potential for forest
production based on the forestry maps and a preliminary forest inventory. The areas of forest
production potential occur mainly in the northern and central provinces (Saket and Matusse,
1994). Areas of production forest per province are given in Table 1.

The most commercially valuable hardwood tree species occurring in these forests include

(chamfuta), Dalbergia melanoxylon, Khaya nyasica and Berchemnia discolor. All these species

1.3 PLANT CONSERVATION STATUS
Due to the long period of civil unrest, together with the vast size of the country and lack of
trained personnel and operational support, large areas of Mozambique are poorly documented
from a biological perspective. The current conservation status of Mozambique's flora is
therefore, imprecisely known. A preliminary estimate indicates that, of the 5500 plant species
recorded for Mozambique, some 247 plant species in 67 families may be of conservation
concern.
Areas with large numbers of endemic plant species include Afromontane areas (e.g. the
vegetation of the Chimanimani and Gorongosa Mountains) and southern Mozambique. The
latter is known as the Maputaland Centre (MC) of Endemism and contains an estimated 2000
to 3000 species of vascular plants which at least 168 species/infraspecific taxa and 4 genera are
endemic/near-endemic (van Wyk 1994). The biological importance of these areas is discussed
further in Chapter 4 "Areas of Outstanding Value".

Mozambique Road Sector Programme       Draft

2 FAUNA
2.1 BACKGROUND
The fauna of Mozambique, besides mammals and birds, is poorly documented. The known
number of species per major taxonomic group are given in Table 3.
Table 3. Summary of animal species of main taxa in Mozambique
Number   Rare/
Group       of Species  Endangered
Mammals   222           10
Birds       580         24
Reptiles    167*        1
Amphibia   39*          0
Total       1008        35
*Groups poorly documented; probably an under-estimate
2.2 TERRESTRIAL MAMMALS
Mozambique is characterised by a rich diversity of mammal fauna. According to Smithers and
Tello (1970) there are 211 terrestrial mammal species and 11 marine mammal species in
Mozambique. These are summarised in Table 4.
Table 4. Summary of the terrestrial mammal biodiversity in Mozambique
Order                                       Families  Genera  Species  Endangered
Insectivora (Shrews)                           3        9       20         0
Chiroptera (Bats)                              7        21      62         0
Primata (Monkeys and baboons)                  3        5        8         0
Tubulidentata (the aardvark)                    I        I       I         0
Hyracoidea (hyraxes)                            1       3        3         0
Artiodactyla (Antelopes, warthog and bushpigs)  3       19      26         4
Lagomorpha (rabbits and hares)                  1       2        4         0
Rodentia (rodents)                             9        32      51         0
Pholidota (pangolins)                           I        1       2         0
Carnivora (carnivores)                         6        25      30         2
Proboscidea (the African elephant)              I        I       I         I
Perissodactyla (rhinoceros)                     1       2        2         2
Total                                          42      132      211        10
There is only one mammal species considered to be endemic to Mozambique viz. a white
bellied red squirrel confined to Namuli Mountain.
58602 Annexure C                    December2000                                  18
Mozambique Road Sector Programme        Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                               ;SMEC
Although there are a large a large number of mammal species the populations of these species,
especially the larger mammals, have been significantly reduced both inside and outside of
protected areas due to lack of enforcement during the armed conflict (1981 - 1992).
The Gorongosa National Park, suffered massive declines in large mammal populations. The
number of elephant, prior to the conflict was in the order of 3000 whilst in 1994 only 108
elephants were recorded during an aerial survey. Similar massive declines were recorded for
buffalo (from 14,000 in 1979 to zero in 1994), hippo (form 4800 in 1979 to zero in 1994),
wildebeest (from 5500 in 1968 to zero in 1994) and waterbuck (3500 in 1988 to 129 in 1994).
Similar trends were observed for Marromeu Buffalo Reserve originally famous for it's large
herds of buffalo and elephants.
These data are based on aerial transect surveys and uncounted populations may still occur in
wooded areas. Recent information indicates the large mammal populations are recovering in
the Gorongosa National Park and Marromeu Reserve.
Niassa Reserve in northern Mozambique (frontier with Tanzania) was less affected by the
armed conflict. This is the only protected area that still supports significant populations of
large mammals. A survey carried out in 1997 revealed the presence of large populations of
elephants, buffalo, sable antelope and greater kudu and leopards. Three endemic large mammal
subspecies occur in the area:
o  the blue Niassa Wildebeest (Connochaetes taurinusjohnstoni) which is characterized by a
white band across the muzzle (believed to be endangered);
Li a sub-species of Burchell's Zebra (Equus burchelli subsp. bohmi); and
O Johnson's Impala (Aepyceros melampus - subsp. Johnstoni), considered to be rare.
Population data of selected large mammal species in the Niassa Reserve area are presented
below:
o  the elephant (Loxodonta africana). The population in the Niassa Reserve and Buffer Zone
may be one of the largest in Africa. Preliminary estimates for the Niassa Reserve, the
proposed extension and buffer zone indicate that there are between 8000 and 10000
elephants in the area
o  African or Cape Buffalo (Syncerus caffer) are abundant within the Reserve and buffer zone
and occur in herds of up to 500 in the Niassa Reserve. The estimated total population is
6500-8000 animals.
o  Sable Antelope (Hippotragus niger) are widely distributed throughout the Reserve
especially in woodlands surrounding wetlands and open grasslands. The estimated total
population 3750-5000.
o  Greater Kudu (Tragelaphus strepsiceros) are scattered in small groups throughout the area,
although not in large numbers. The estimated total population is 3000-3750.
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                                19
Mozambique Road Sector Programme       Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                             wNSMEC
o Eland (Taurotragus oryx) occur in small herds from 10-50 individuals throughout the
Reserve and Buffer Zone. The estimated total population is 1600-2000 animals.
L the Blue Niassa Wildebeest (Connochaetes taurinus johnstoni) occur in woodlands
dominated by the Knob-thorn acacia. The estimated total population is 400-550. This
species should be considered endangered.
o  Burchell's Zebra (Equus burchelli subsp. bohmi) occur in wooded grassland.  The
estimated total population is 950-1250.
o Lichtenstein's Hartebeest (Alcelaphus lichtensteinii) with an estimated total population of
900-1250.
o Waterbuck (Kobus ellipsiprymnus subsp. kondensis) are confined mainly to the larger river
systems: the Rovuma, Lugenda, Chuilezi, Misangeze and Miuro Rivers. Their nmbers are
limited (estimated total of 600-900) and their status should be considered vulnerable.
At a national level several large mammal species are believed to be extinct or on the verge of
extinction e.g., the black and white rhino, giraffe, roan antelope, tsessebe, the mountain
reedbuck and the African wild dog.
Two rodent species of conservation concern occur. The first is the Selinda veld rat, Aethomys
silindensis, which is only known from the Mozambique-Zimbabwe border area in rock areas at
700-800 m in miombo woodland. The second is a species of woodland mouse, Grammomys
comestes, only known from the coastal forest belt (also in Tanzania and South Africa). The
chequered elephant shrew subspecies Rhynchocyon cireni cireni is known only from
Quelimane, Zambezia Province. Its occurrence and conservation requirements in the coastal
forest belt require investigation.
2.3 BIRDS
Five hundred and eighty-one bird species have been recorded for Mozambique, out of
approximately 900 species recorded southern Africa (Roberts, Birds of Southern Africa 1993).
There are a number of near endemic species, mostly associated with isolated montane
habitats such as the Gorongosa Massif, the Chiperone and Namuli Mountains and the
Njesi plateau. Some rare and restricted range bird species include the following:
o The Namuli apalis (Apalis lynesi) is known to occur only in the woodlands and forests of
Mount Namuli. It is one of the world's most restricted bird species.
o The Dappled-mountain Robin (Modulatrix orostruthus) is a rare and restricted range
species only recorded for Mount Namrnli (Zambezia Province) in Mozambique.
u The Chirinda Apalis (Apalis chirindensis) a restricted range species believed to be endemic
to the Chimanimani/Nyanga Mountains on the Mozambique-Zimbabwe border and
Gorongosa Mountain.
58602 Annexure C                  December2000                               20
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                               W$SMEC
o Swynnerton's Forest Robin (Swynnertonia swynnertoni) has only been recorded for
Chimanimani Mountains (Mozambique-Zimbabwe) and Mount Namuli.
o A separate subspecies of the Whitebreasted Alethe (Alethe fuelleborni) has been recorded
for Gorongosa Mountain and lowland forests near Dondo (Sofala Province). The species is
partly migratory breeding in montane forest and moving to the foothills and coastal forest
in the cold season. The birds recorded at Dondo are probably breeding on Gorongosa
Mountain. The species must be considered rare and very susceptible to habitat destruction.
o In Mozambique the Greenheaded Oriole (Oriolus chorocephalus) is recorded only for
Gorongosa Mountain. This is considered an isolated sub-species characterized by a white
wing patch (0. c. speculifer).
o The Oliveheaded Weaver (Ploceus oliviaceps) is a miombo species found only in a small
patch of coastal miombo in the Inhambane Province. The Inhambane population has been
described as a separate subspecies.
The freshwater and marine wetlands of Mozambique are important sites for migratory and
resident aquatic bird species. One of the most important wetland sites in Mozambique is the
Zambezi Delta. Over fifty species of waterbirds have been recorded for the Delta. The Delta
supports numerous vulnerable and threatened species of global concern, including the Wattled
Crane (Bugeranus carunculatus), Grey Crowned Crane (Balearica regulorum), African
Skimmer (Rynchops flavirostris), White Pelican, Pinkbacked Pelican, Woolynecked Stork
(Ciconia episcopus), Openbilled Stork (Anastomus lamelligerus), Sanddlebilled Stork,
Yellowbellied Stork (Mycteria ibis), Black Stork (Ciconia nigra), Redwinged Pratincole
(Glareola pratincola) and Caspian Tern (Sterna caspia).
The biological importance of the Zambezi Delta is discussed further in Chapter 4 "Areas of
Outstanding Value".
The conservation status of the birds of Mozambique is currently being determined through the
preparation of a Bird Atlas for the entire country (due to be completed by 2005). A
preliminary list of birds on conservation concern is given below:
a    Of Special Concern:
