2.3 REPTILES
One hundred and sixty reptile species have been recorded for Mozambique (including marine
turtles). The conservation status of reptiles, with the exception of sea turtles, is largely
unknown. According to Dutton and Dutton (1974), 60 reptile species (including 34 species of
snake) have been recorded for the Chimanimani Massif of which one is endemic (Platysaurus
ocellatus - the flat rock lizard).
One snake species, the African Rock Python (Python sebae natalensis A. Smith) is believed to
endangered and is protected by law. Threats include collecting for food, skin, medicinal
purposes, the pet trade, and destruction due to its perceived danger to man and livestock.
Habitat destruction may cause local extinction. Crocodiles are becoming threatened due to
hunting for their skins.
All five species of Indian Ocean sea turtles nest on beaches along the Mozambique coast: the
Loggerhead turtle (Caretta caretta), the Leatherback (Dermochelys coriacea), the Green Turtle
(Chelonia mydas), the Hawksbill turtle (Eretmochelys imbricata) and the Olive Ridley turtle
(Lepidochelys olivacea). All species are protected in Mozambique.
The Loggerhead (Caretta caretta) and Leatherback turtles (Dermochelys coriacea) nest along
the coast from Ponta do Ouro to Bazaruto Archipelago. The most important areas are Ponta do
Ouro - Machangulo Peninsula coastline (including the Maputo Elephant Reserve), Inhaca
Island, Qewene Peninsula and Bazaruto Archipelago (Hughes, 1971a). The peak nesting
season is between November and January (Hughes, 1971b; Costa, 1989; Gove and Magane,
1995).
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                                22
Mozambique Road Sector Programme       Draft



AASMEC
IMPA CTO Lda                                                            4SM
The Green Turtle (Chelonia mydas) nests from Qewene Peninsula to Quirimbas Archipelago.
The biggest concentration appears to be in the Primeiras e Segundas Islands (Hughes, 1971).
Nests of this species were found recently on the Bazaruto Archipelago (Gove and Magane,
1996).
The Hawksbill turtle (Eretmochelys imbricata) and Olive Ridley turtle (Lepidochelys olivacea)
occur on the northern coast (Hughes, 1971 b) but their nesting areas are unknown.
2.4 AMPHIBIA
Amphibians are important "biological indicators" of environmental stress in wetlands.
The amphibia of Mozambique are not well documented. The exact number of species of
amphibia in Mozambique is unclear, due to taxonomic uncertainty. The Natural History
Museum has records of only 39 species.
Thirty-five amphibian species were recorded for the Chimanimani Massif of which two are
endemic: Bufo vertebralis grindleyi (Grindleys toad) and Anthroleptis troglodytes (the cave
squeaker).
The conservation status of the amphibia in Mozambique is unknown.
2.5 RIVERINE FISH FAUNA
Only the fish species in the major river systems such as the Zambezi, Limpopo, Pungue and
Nkomati rivers have been documented (Werger, 1978). The lower Zambezi has by far the
greatest fish biodiversity. The conservation status of fish species in Mozambique is not known.
Riverine fish species include in the lower Zambezi River include: Tilapia rendalli (Cichlidae);
Clarias gariepinus, Heterobranchus longifilis (Clariidae); Limnothrissa miodion (Clupeidae);
Barbua fasciolatus, B. lineomaculatus, B. marequensis, B. unitaeniatus, Labeo altivelis, L.
congero, L. cylindricus, L. molybdinus (Cyprinidae); Aplocheilichthys johnstoni, A. kattangae
(Cyprinodontidae); Distichodus mossambicus, D. schenga (Distichodontidae); Malapteruru
electricus (Malapteruridae); Eutropius depressirostris (Mochokiadae); and Synodontis
nebulosus, S. zambezensis (Schilbeidae). Species of Glossogobius, Marcusenius and
Mormyrops are also important in all the rivers.
In 1968 the Lake Tanganyika sardine (or kapenta) Limnothrissia miodon was introduced into
Lake Kariba in Zimbabwe. Kapenta is now thriving in Lake Cahora Bassa apparently by
invasive spread from Lake Kariba.
A list of fish species in the Lower Zambezi, Limpopo and Pungue Rivers is given in
Appendix 1.

3 PROTECTED AREAS
3.1 GAZETTED PROTECTED AREAS
Existing National Parks and Reserves in Mozambique were established prior to Independence
under Decree N 40040, 20 January 1955. There are six categories of protected area. Parques
Nacionais (national parks), Reservas Especiais (game reserves), Reservas Parciais (partial
reserves), Regimes de Vigilancia (vigilance areas), Coutadas (areas set aside for hunting and
photographic safari) and Reservas Florestais (forest reserves). The Direccao Nacional de
Florestas e Fauna Bravia (DNFFB) - Ministry of Agriculture and Rural Development is
responsible for their administration currently.  The DNFFB is also responsible for the
management and conservation wildlife and forestry resources outside of the protected areas
network. The Government of Mozambique has decided to transfer responsibility of the
National Parks and Reserves to the newly created Ministry of Tourism. It is envisaged that the
transfer will be concluded in the first quarter of 2001.
National Parks and Game Reserves are managed for ecological, cultural and/or aesthetic
objectives. Hunting activities under Licence are permitted in the Coutadas (Controlled Hunting
Areas). The exploitation of forest resources in the Forest Reserves is strictly controlled.
The numbers (in parentheses) of protected areas per category are: National Parks (4), Game
Reserves (5), Controlled Hunting Areas or Coutadas (13), Vigilance Areas (2) and Forest
Reserves (16). The list of protected areas and their size is presented in Table 4. Their
distribution shown in shown in Figure 5.
A new Forestry and Wildlife Law has recently been approved by the Council of Ministers and
the Parliament (1999). The new Law recognizes three categories of protected zones for the
conservation of biodiversity and fragile ecosystems: National Parks; National Reserves; and
Historical - Cultural Use Zones.

National Parks are total protection zones for the protection, conservation and management of
vegetation and wildlife as well as the protection of sites of special scientific, cultural or
aesthetic interest and for recreation that are representative of the national heritage. Hunting,
forestry, agriculture, mining, prospecting, digging and land-filling are prohibited.
National Reserves are designated for the protection of rare species of fauna and flora,
endemics, species threatened with extinction or in decline, sensitive ecosystems such as
wetlands, dunes, mangroves and corals as well as the conservation of flora and fauna within
these ecosystems. Activities that are prohibited in national parks are also prohibited in national
reserves although certain resources may be exploited upon issuance of a license.
The recognition of Historical-Cultural Use Zones is a completely new category for
Mozambique reflecting the new policies to ensure that local communities are involved in
natural resource management. These Use Zones are areas for protection of forests of religious
importance or cultural use in accordance with the customary norms and practices of the
respective communities. The forestry and wildlife resources within the zones may be used in
accordance with customary norms and practices.
It is planned to reclassify the existing protected areas gazetted under colonial times in
accordance with the new Law. The existing national parks and reserves will probably maintain
the same category of classification. No Historical-Cultural Use Zones have been declared
although the first protected area of this category has been proposed for the Chimanimani area
(Sussundenga District, Manica Province) under the Transfrontier Conservation Areas Project
(see below).
3.2 TRANSFRONTIER CONSERVATION AREAS (TFCAs)
In 1996 the Government of Mozambique approved the implementation of a Transfrontier
Conservation Areas (TFCA) Project funded by the Global Environmental Facility. Importantly,
the new concept of Conservation Areas (rather than National Parks) places greater emphasis on
multiple resource use and management by local communities living in/adjacent to these areas.
A TFCA is considered to be a relatively large region of mostly communal land incorporating
one or more recognised biodiversity conservation or utilisation centres (e.g. National Park,
Reserve, Coutada etc.), the whole to be developed in an integrated fashion for the economic
benefit of indigenous communities without loss of biodiversity. The TFCA's are contiguous
with protected areas in a South Africa, Swaziland and Zimbabwe (Figure 6).

Three TFCAs, based an biodiversity, social and economic criteria, are currently being
developed:
The Lubombos TFCA, southern Mozambique, Maputo Province - contiguous with the
Tembe and Ndumo Reserves in South Africa (KwaZulu-Natal)
This area lies in the south-eastern part of Maputo Province, in the extreme south-east of
Mozambique. It includes the Maputo Game Reserve (700 kM2) and the proposed Futi corridor
(an area of undisturbed habitat and relatively low population density), which would link the
Maputo Reserve with the Tembe Elephant Park and the Ndumo Game Reserve in Kwazulu,
South Africa. In the past elephants and other wildlife ranged across these areas. The project
area forms part of the Maputaland Centre for Plant Diversity which ranks as a first order site of
global botanical significance. Habitats in the TFCA are pristine and the high area has a
potential for conservation and ecotourism-based development.

Gazaland TFCA south-west Mozambique, Gaza and Inhambane Provinces - contiguous
with Kruger National Park in South Africa and Ghono-Re-Zhou National Park in
Zimbabwe
The Gaza TFCA covers 20,700 km2. of protected area in the districts of Mabalane, Chigubo,
Chicualacuala, and Massegene in the Province of Gaza, and the district of Mabote in Province
of Inhambane. Gaza Province borders on Zimbabwe in the north-west and South Africa in the
south-west. This is an important TFCA as it includes three main conservation areas: Zinave
(3,700 kM2) and Banhine National Parks (7,000 kM2) and Coutada 16 (a 10,000 km 2 wildlife
utilisation area); the latter is contiguous with the Kruger National Park in South Africa. The
Gaza TFCA also borders on the Gono-Rhe-Zhou National Park in Zimbabwe.
The Gaza TFCA forms a corridor between the rapidly developing wildlife and tourism
industries in south-east Zimbabwe and the more slowly developing, but potentially lucrative
tourism industry along the Inhambane coast and Bazaruto Archipelago.
In November 2000, the Government of Mozambique signed an agreement with the
Governments of South Africa and Zimbabwe to incorporate Coutada 16 into an expanded
transfrontier national park to be named the Gaza-Kruger-Gono-Rhe-Zou (GKG) Transfrontier
National Park. Due to its proximity to conservation areas in South Africa and Zimbabwe, the
potential for artificial and natural restocking is high.
Chimanimani TFCA, west, central Mozambique, Manica Province contiguous with the
Chimanimani National Park in Zimbabwe.
The Chimanimami TFCA is contiguous with the Chimanimani National Park in Zimbabwe,
this TFCA in Manica Province is a relatively small area of 1,740 kM2, and includes the eastern
escarpment and foothills of the Chimanimani massif. The area in Mozambique is better
preserved, and represents a relatively intact Afro-montane ecosystem, with high species
endemism (notably plants), and rich wildlife and bird populations (see Chapter 4 below)

4 AREAS OF OUTSTANDING VALUE
Eight areas of outstanding ecosystem, biological and/or scenic value which warrant special
attention in Mozambique have been identified. These are shown on Figure 7 and described in
the following.
4.1 GORONGOSA MOUNTAIN- RIFT VALLEY COMPLEX.
This area encompasses the isolated massif of Gorongosa Mountain which rises to 1 863m and
the southern-most, Mozambican sector, of the African Rift Valley (known as the Urema
Trough). The trough lies 15m above sea level at a distance of 120km from the coast
Gorongosa Mountain is an isolated montane block 160km inland from the sea. Orographic
rainfall (where moist air rises, condenses and falls as rain) provides the mountain with an
annual rainfall of over 2000mm per year. The mountain supports tropical to montane rainforest
on its summits with heath grasslands.
Endemic and near-endemic plants and animals occur within the mountain's habitats. Examples
include the Greenheaded Oriole (Oriolus chlorocephalus) sub-species O.c. speculifer,
characterised by a white wing patch is found on Gorongosa Mountain, the Dappled-mountain
Robin (Modulatrix orostruthus), the Chirinda Apalis (Apalis chirindensis) a restricted range
species, Swynnerton's Forest Robin (Swynnertonia swynnertoni) and separate subspecies of the
Whitebreasted Alethe (Alethefuelleborni).
The Rift Valley is a floodplain ecosystem reliant on both the amount and distribution of rain in the
Urema catchment. The Gorongosa Mountain is the most important perennial source of water for
the floodplains in the Valley. The central position of the Urema Lake in the floor of the Rift
Valley makes it a recipient of primary importance to which the flooding processes are eventually
related. The changes in inputs into the Lake are clearly displayed by the extension and
contraction of the lake waters across the surrounding plains. During maximal flooding the Urema
Lake expands from a dry season minimum of 10 km2 to about 200 km2 area.
A variety of wetlands habitats occur in the Rift Valley including rivers, lakes, temporary pans,
reed swamps, floodplain grassland and palm savanna. The diversity of habitats in the Rift Valley
makes it one of the finest wildlife grazing ecosystems in Africa as reflected by the spectacular
wildlife that inhabited the Valley prior to the armed conlfict. The southern portion of the Rift
Valley is protected within the Gorongosa National Park
Tinley (1977) lists 46 large mammal species (of size greater than5 kg mass) for the Gorongosa
area. Several of these species are believed to be near extinction or extinct e.g., tsessebe, the roan
antelope and the black and white rhino.
The Gorongosa-Rift Valley Complex is diverse in terms of ecosystems, plant formations,
habitat types, unique assemblages of big game population and rare or confined (endemic)
plants and animals. The outstanding value of this area thus encompasses:
o high scenic values;
o large geological diversity;
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                               30
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                                                  @SMEC
o endemic plants and animals;
o high ecosystem and biological; and
o potential big game spectacle.

4.2 THE CHERINGOMA PLATEAU
The Cheringoma Plateau (300m above sea level) on Cretaceous sediments comprises tropical
forests containing a mixture of local endemics with equatorial, north-east coast and southern
African flora.
The forest on the Plateau contain several commercially important hardwood species such as
Milletia stuhlmani (panga-panga), Afzelia quanzensis (chanfuta) and Pterocarpus angolensis
(umbila). Current access to these forests is via the Dondo-Caia road which is in poor condition.
Access to these forests will be increased when the Gorongosa-Caia road is completed.
4.3 ZAMBEZI DELTA GRASSLANDS AND SWAMPS.
The Zambezi Delta covers an area of about 18,000 ki2, from its apex at Chupanga to its 120
km front with the Indian Ocean coast extending from Quelimane south-west to Machesse.
Marromeu Buffalo Reserve is located on the southern portion of the Delta (Figure 8). In
addition to buffalo, elephants, water buck and reedbuck occur in the Delta although their
numbers have been much reduced by hunting.
|-  TETE
S. la.    *MCn  ,olnM
Delta
Figure 8. The Zambezi Delta
The Zambeze Delta represents an important wetland for resident and migratory bird species.
The Wattled Crane (Bugeranus carunculatus) and the saddle-billed stork, (Ephippiorhynchus
senegalensis), both of special concern, occur in the Zambeze Delta.
The Zambezi Delta also supports numerous vulnerable and threatened species of global
concern, including the Wattled Crane, Grey Crowned Crane, African Skimmer (Rynchops
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                                32
Mozambique Road Sector Programme       Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                                  SMEC
flavirostris), White Pelican, Pinkbacked Pelican, Woolynecked Stork (Ciconia episcopus),
Openbil led Stork (Anastomus lamelligerus), Sanddlebil led Stork, Yellowbellied Stork
(Mycteria ibis), Black Stork (Ciconia nigra), Redwinged Pratincole (Glareola pratincola) and
Caspian Tern (Sterna caspia). The delta supports colonies of thousands of pairs of White
Pelicans among the coastal mangroves in southeastern Marromeu Complex, one of the largest
breeding colonies recorded in Southern Africa.
Three immense breeding colonies of storks and herons have also been recorded in the
Marromeu floodplains (Beilfuss and Bento, 1997). The largest colony supports hundreds of
pairs of Openbilled Stork, Yellowbellied Stork, Sacred Ibis (Threskiornis aethiopicus), African
Spoonbill (Platalea alba) and three species of egrets (Egretta spp). Large breeding colonies of
Reed Cormorant (Phalacrocorax africanus), Squacco Heron (Ardeola ralloides), Grey Heron
(Ardea cinerea) and Blackheaded Heron (Ardea melanocephala) were also noted. Large
groups of migrant Abdim's Storks (Ciconia abdimii) utilize the delta grasslands in the summer.
Wetland scientists believe that the Zambezi Delta qualifies as a Wetland of International
Importance under the Ramsar Convention, and is of great socio-economic and cultural value to
Mozambique.

4.4 THE GREAT INSELBERG ARCHIPELAGO
The lnselberg archipelago presents a truly remarkable landscape of tall granite core remnants
in a savanna plain.
58602 Annexure C                    December2000                                 33
Mozambique Road Sector Programme        Drat_



IMPACTO Lda                                                               VSMC
This series of habitats occurs south of the Lurio River occupying a rectangular area of
approximately 500km  by 160km  wide lying in a south-west to north-east direction
(approximately between 140 to 17S; 3540'E to 380 E thence NE-wards to 3940'E).
The Inselberg Region has high scenic value as well as high biological value. The latter is
characterised by unique flora and fauna (endemics and biogeographical outliers) contained in
the isolated rainforest, patches of which form aprons around the flanks and base of the
inselbergs. Many of the areas are unknown biologically and it is highly likely undescribed
species remain to be discovered. Like Gorongosa Mountain these inselbergs are high enough to -
result in a large continuous belt of high rainfall, an orographic perennial aquifer.
Mount Namuli is an isolated mountain peak rising out of a plateau at 800m to a height of just
over 2400m. It is the highest mountain in northern Mozambique. The slopes of the peaks
support a mix of moist montane forests and grassy meadows. The forests are characterised by
high levels of endemism. The Namuli apalis (Apalis lynesi) is one of the world's most
restricted bird species and is known to occur only in the woodlands and forests of Mount
Namuli. The Namrnli forests are also home to a further five globally threatened bird species and
14 biome restricted species. Other globally threatened species include the Thyolo Alethe
(Alethe choloensis), the Dapplethroat (Modutlatrix orostruthus) and the Oliveheaded weaver
(Ploceus olivaceiceps). The forests of Mount Namuli include an endemic small mammal (the
white bellied red squirrel).
Figure 10. Mount Nami-i
The rare Dappled Mountain-Robin (Arcanator arostruthus) and the White-winged Apalis
(Apalis chariessa) occur in the forests of Mount Chiperone

o spectacular and unique lnselberg scenery;

4.5 CHIMANIMANI MASSIF MOZAMBIQUE-ZIMBABWE
FRONTIER ESCARPMENT REGION
The Chimanimani Massif forms part of the great eastern escarpment of the Interior Continental
Plateau of South Central Africa, along the Mozambique-Zimbabwe frontier.
The escarpment mountain range is separated into a series of massifs by the water gaps which
have cut saddles in the escarpment. The Chimanimani Massif rises to 2436m at Binga Peak,
the highest point in Mozambique. As a physiographic unit it is demarcated to the north, south
and east by the natural boundaries formed by two major perennial tributaries of the Buzi River
which flows east to enter the Indian Ocean in the Bight of Sofala only 150km distant.
The whole massif area is some 40km from north to south and 20km wide, contained within
coordinates 19024' to 2005', and between 32050' and 33025', standing mostly above the 800m
contour. The Chimanimani Massif although relatively small in area is characterised by an
exceptionally high diversity of habitats and species.
The massif is composed of strongly contorted and folded quartzites and schists. Rugged
outcrops and crags are formed by the quartzites and smooth rounded or planar surfaces by the
schists.
The scenery is stepped with a series of spectacular steep rocky scarps and ravines separated by
flattened undulating terrain, with high waterfalls plunging off each step.
Leached, acid, sandy soils develop on the quartzites which support ericaceous heaths and
restios, upland miombo woodland with dense montane evergreen forest in ravines. The
quartzites support a rich endemic flora of some 50 known species. The red clayey soils
derived from schists are seasonally waterlogged and these support open (treeless) montane
grasslands. The endemic animals are two frogs and one reptile.
There are well preserved Rock Paintings throughout the area depicting all the local and
lowland big game of the region.
Dry montane forests are found in valleys and on gentle slopes at higher altitude (1000-1600m).
Commonly occurring tree species include Iex mitis, Macaranga capensis and Myrica
pilulifera. The coniferous species Podocarpus milanjianus and Widdringtonia nodiflora
represent the more xeric elements of these forests. Along river courses the tree fern, Cyathea,
is common.
On well-drained and shallow quartzite soils sclerophyllous thickets occur. They vary greatly in
floristic composition but some members of the Ericaceae (eg. Erica and Phillipia) and
Proteaceae (eg. Protea and Faurea) are nearly always present. Several species belonging to
these families are endemic to the Massif.
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                               35
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                              a; SMEC
On shallow rocky soils Aloe and Euphorbia species commonly occur. The endemic Vellozia
argentea grows on almost bare rock and is able to withstand long periods desiccation. These
communities may occur up to 2000m.
Nearly 1000 vascular plant species have been recorded for the area of which 45 are endemic
(Dutton and Dutton, 1975). No less than five Aloe species are endemic in the Chimanimani
mountains (A. munchii, A. hazeliana, A. howmanii, A. plawsii and A. wildii) hilst three species
of Erica (E. lanceolifera, E. pleiotricha and E. wildii) and two species of Protea (P. crinita and
P. enervis) are considered endemic.
Large mammals are well represented on the Mozambican side of the Chimanimani Massif
although poaching by dogs and snares is common. According to Dutton & Dutton (1975)
commonly occurring species include buffalo (Syncerus caffer), bushbuck (Tragelaphus
scriptus), eland (Taurotragus oryx), sable (Hippotragus niger), Klipspringer (Oreotragus
oreotragus), common duiker (Sylvicapra grimmia) and blue duiker (Philantomba monticola).
Reed-buck (Redunca arundinum) occur on the alluvial floodplains and along river courses east
of the Massif. Reports of a small population mountain reed buck (Redunca fulvorufula)
inhabiting the Chimanimani Mountains have not been confirmed.
Over 160 bird species have been recorded for the Chimanimani (Dutton & Dutton, 1975) some
of which are considered endemic to the Afro-montane regions of eastem Africa. Swynnerton's
Robin (Swynnertonia swynnertonia) inhabits the moister elements of the montane forests
(Collar & Stuart, 1985) highlighting of the faunal connections that exist with other dysjunct
montane areas (eg. the Eastem Arc Mountains of Tanzania). Other rare avifaunal species
occurring in the moist forests of the Chimanimani Massif include Circaetis fasciolatus
(Southern Banded Snake Eagle), Cercococcyx montanus (Barred Cuckoo), Andropadus
importunis (Sombre Bulbul), Prinonops scopifrens (Chestnutfronted Helmetshrike) and Batis
fratum (Woodwards' Batis).
Thirty-five amphibian species, of which two are endemic (Bufo vertebralis grindleyi and
Anthroleptis troglodytes) and sixty reptile species (including 34 species of snake) have been
recorded for the Massif. Crocodiles are reported to occur in all major river systems at lower
altitudes.
The outstanding universal values of the Chimanimani Massif are:
u Spectacular mountain and waterfall wilderness scenery, with big game;
o  High plant endemism;
o  High plant community diversity from montane heaths and grasslands down to lowland
tropical rainforest in the gorges and valley bottoms;
o  Well preserved San Rock Art.
4.6 THE MAPUTALAND CENTRE OF ENDEMISM (MC)
The MC (26,734 krn2) is defined as that part of southem Mo,ambique and north-eastern Natal
bounded in the north by the Inkomati-Limpopo River, in the west by the western foothills of
the Libombos, in the south by the St. Lucia estuary and in the east by the Indian Ocean.
58602 Annexure C                   December2000                               36
Mozambique Road Sector Programme      Draft



IMPACTOLda                                                               WSMEC
The area is characterised by a flat low-level coastal plain with a maximum elevation of 150 m.
The eastern shoreline is characterised by a parabolic dune system rising to almost 200 m.a.s.l.
in certain places, considered to be the tallest vegetated dunes in the world.
The MC contains extensive wetland notably Lake St. Lucia (c. 350 kin2), Lake Sibaya (65
kM2) and the Kosi Lake System in South Africa and Lakes Piti, Xingute and Satine in Southern
Mozambique.
The MC flora is very distinct comprising to 2000 to 3000 species (van Wyk, 1994). Of these,
at least 168 species/infraspecific taxa (this is probably an underestimate) and 4 genera
(Brachychloa, Ephippiocarpa, Helichrysopsis, Inhambanella) are endemic/near-endemic to the
centre. Several of the endemics are rare and known only from a few collections. The MC
clearly has floristic links with the more tropical parts of Africa to the north. The centre is at the
southern end of the tropics in Africa and many tropical species reach the southern most limits
of their ranges in this area.
The associated fauna of the MPR, and particularly of the MC, is interesting and rich. Of the
more than 472 species of birds in the MC (57% of South Africa's total), 47 subspecies are
endemic/near-endemic to the centre. The MC corresponds with the southern part of the South-
east African coast Endemic Bird Area (EBA). This has three endemic restricted-range birds
species, which occur in a wide-range of habitat types (van Wyk, 1994).
As with its flora, the MC is the southernmost part of the range of many components of the East
African fauna. The centre is of exceptional biogeographical interest because of the sharp
biogeographic transformation in the region. Other endemic/near-endemic species and
infraspecific taxa (total indigenous to the centre in brackets) include 14 mammals (102 species,
about 4 locally extinct) 23 reptiles (about 112 species/subspecies), 3 frogs (45
species/subspecies) and 7 freshwater fishes (67 species); (van Wyk, 1994).
4.7 COASTAL BARRIER LAKES
A characteristic feature of the Ponta do Ouro - Bazaruto stretch of coast are the extensive
coastal lakes, swamps and temporary rain-filled pans which occur behind the parabolic dune
systems. The most important coastal lakes from north to south are: Lagos Dongane, Poelela
(9250 ha), Maiene, Quissico (3250 ha), Nhamabvale, Nhamzume, Uembje (Bilene) (3200 ha),
Muandje (2250 ha), Pati (1850 ha), Piti (2.700 ha), Xingute (1150 ha), Satine (500 ha) - see
Figure 11. These lakes are located on a plain of low elevation and most are separated from the
sea by a well developed longshore dune system.
The coastal lakes provide habitat for many bird species including Ciconia episcopus (White
Stork), Egretta garzeta (Little Egret) and Phoenicopterus ruber (pink flamingos) which occur
in the lakes of the Maputo Reserve.
Besides their importance biologically these coastal systems have high scenic value. A proposal
has been developed to declare the Maputaland wetlands (between Ponta do Ouro and Inhaca
island) a Natural World Heritage Site. This will be submitted to the Mozambican Government
for consideration. The South African component (St. Lucia Estuary/Kosi Bay) of this unique
wetland system has been declared a RAMSAR Wetland.

4.8 PEBANE EVERGREEN COASTAL FORESTS
Evergreen Coastal Forests in northern coastal Zambezia Province have been encroached upon
by slash and burn agriculture. Recent surveys (Branch, 1998) have high-lighted the biological
importance of these forests. Remarkably a new (and possibly endemic) tree species was
discovered in these forests during an EIA carried out in 1998.
A rich and diverse reptile fauna occurs in the coastal forests of Pebane. Sixty (60) species of
reptiles have been recorded: 21 lizards, I amphisbaenid, 31 snakes, 6 chelonians and a
58602 Annexure C                     December2000                                  38
Mozambique Road Sector Programme         Draft



IMPACTO Lda                                                                #SMEC
crocodile. It is estimated that an additional 20 terrestrial species, mainly snakes, may still be
discovered in this region. In a recent survey two new reptile species were discovered; a new
snake (Dromophis sp.) and a new dwarf day gecko (Lygodactylus sp.). The snake was collected
in Coastal Forest. The new gecko was also collected from forested habitats. Both reptile
species at present are considered endemic to the area.
The Zambeze soft-shelled terrapin (Cyciodermafrenatum) listed as "Near Threatened" in the
South African Red List was recorded for Moebase and represents an important range extension
for this species into the coastal drainage systems of northern Mozambique.  -
Coastal forest bird specialists occurring in these habitats include the Crested Guineafowl,
Green Coucal, Yellowspotted Nicator, Chestnutfronted Helmet Shrike, Yellow-Bellied
Sunbird, Silvery-cheeked Hombill, Whitefronted Plover, Redwinged Pratincole, Black Coucal,
Cinnamon-breasted Tit, Woodward's batis and Livingstone's Flycatcher.

I TOPOGRAPHY
The major topographic features of Mozambique are shown in Figure 1. The country has three
broad topographic regions and these are descried below.
The northern part of Mozambique is a great mountainous block with maximum elevations
along the eastem edge of the East African Rift Valley system (border with Malawi). Here the
summits of Mt. Jeci (1250'S/3509'E) and Mt. Chiperone (16'30'S/35044'E) reach 1836 and
2054 m asl respectively, and more centrally Mt. Namrnli (15025'S/37000'E) reaches 2419 m.
From these westem heights the land slopes gently eastwards and south-eastwards towards the
Indian Ocean with many extensive plateaux. Drainage is by 18 perennial rivers and many small
streams (Hughes and Hughes, 1992).
The central region is dominated by the lower Zambezi Valley and its delta plains. In the
interior, in Tete Province, the rims of the Valley are mountainous, reaching elevations of
2095m at Mt. Domue (1428'S/3415'E) on the border with Malawi. In this part of the basin
drainage is into either the Lake Cahora Bassa, or the Zambezi below it, by numerous fast
flowing seasonal streams. These streams rise in Zambia, Malawi and Zimbabwe. The Shire
River, draining Lake Malawi, enters the Zambezi in the lowlands.
The southern part of the country, south of Beira (1949'S/3452'E), comprises a broad coastal
plain backed by mountains along the western national border. The land is generally below
00m asl in the south, except along border with Zimbabwe and South Africa, and more than
85% of the land surface is below the 200m contour. Along the western border with Zimbabwe,
the land rises up sharply to reach the southern Africa plateau, the largest plateau in the world.
This north-south mountain range in this area known as the Chimanimani Mountains (Manica
Province), forms part of the Great Escarpment. The highest elevation is attained at Mt. Binga
(2436m), a montane area reaching out from Zimbabwe (19'47'S/33'03'E). A number of
important watercourses drain the highlands along the western border, including, from N-S, the
Pangue, Revue, Buzi, Gorongosa, Save, Limpopo, Incomati, Umbeluzi and Maputo Rivers.
Not all streams are perennial. Most are reduced to a series of isolated pools by the end of the
dry season, especially since impoundments have been made in their upper reaches. Most of the
rivers rise in other countries, and many are dammed before they reach Mozambique, e.g., the
Save, Limpopo, Incomati, and Maputo Rivers.

2 GEOLOGY
2.1  BACKGROUND
Central-southern Africa experienced successive uplifts between the late Mesozoic (100 million
years BP) and Tertiary times (65 million years BP) forming the Great African Plateau which is
the largest plateau in the world. The highest parts of the southern African plateau occur on the
Eastem Escarpment which coincides, in places, with the western border of Mozambique with
Zimbabwe e.g., the Chimanimani and Inyanga mountains.
The original edge of the plateau is thought to have formed the coastal margin of the continent
at one time. As the sea floor became exposed during uplifts the edge of the plateau receded
from the coast. Of outstanding importance, with regards central and southern Mozambique, is
the low-lying landscape that slopes gently towards the Indian Ocean. In contrast to other
coastal countries in the sub-continent, Mozambique has extensive coastal lowlands and most of
the area south of Beira is characterized by a broad coastal plain.
A striking feature of East Africa is the Rift Valley System. The western Rift passes through
Lakes Tanganyka and Niassa (the latter is also known as Lake Malawi) and penetrates
Mozambique in a north-south direction via the Shire River valley which drains Lake Malawi.
The Rift Valley crosses the present course of the Zambeze River slightly upstream from Caia
where it branches and runs in a SW direction to form the Buzi Trough and SE to disappear in
the area between Beira and the old Arab port of Sofala. The southern-most Mozambican sector
of the Rift is known as the Urema Trough. This is enclosed on either side by higher plateau
country. On the western margin the Urema Trough is surmnounted by the isolated block of
Gorongosa Mountain and the eastern margin by the Cheringoma Plateau. Gorongosa Mountain
is 160 km inland from the sea, and the centre of the Rift Valley within the same transect is 120
km. The nearest large highland massifs to Gorongosa are the Mozambique-Zimbabwean
Escarpment, 100 km inland to the west (the Chimanimani Mountains) and the isolated
Morrumbala Mountain 150 km to the north-east near the confluence of the Shire with Zambeze
River. A remnant of the former coastal plain was left as an isolated elevated block which forms
the eastern side of the Urema Trough, known as the Cheringoma Plateau.
Quaternary climatic fluctuations and associated sea level rises and falls further influenced the
soils and drainage systems and their associated vegetation. Large areas of south-eastern
Mozamb;que are characterised by interior sandy plains and interior dunes of marine and
aeolion origin.
Geomorphological features of Mozambique are shown in Figure 2.

2.2 GEOLOGICAL FORMATIONS
The main geological formations are shown in Figure 3. They ares
i   Pre-Cambrian (> 550 millon to 2000 million BP)
Geological formations of the Pre-Cambian comprise rocks rnore than 600 million years old and
cover almost the entire northern and central, western areas of the Mozambique (Niassa, Cabo
Delgado, Zambezi, Tete Provinces and parts of Nampula and Manica Provinces).
The oldest rocks are granitic more than 2000 million years old of the Zimbabwe Craton within
the "Manica System" along the Zimbabwe. Also associated with the Manica System are
quartzites and schists rocks older than 1600 million years BP.
Slightly younger is the metamorphic crystalline Pre-Cambrian basement occurring in north-
central western Mozambique comprising granitic and migmatic gneisses. These metamorphic
rocks belong to the Barue Formation. In the Provinces of Niassa, Nampula and Tete and, to a
lesser extent, Cabo Delagdo towering outcrops of granite rock (known as inselbergs), some
reaching elevations of more than 1000 m, rise sharply above the plains. Around these granitic
intrusions occur pegmatites, mainly comprising quartz and feldspar but some with rare
industrial minerals.
In central Tete Province ultrabasic rocks form the so-called "Tete Complex" comprising
amphibolites, gabbro and pyroxinite.
ii   Karroo System (350 - 130 million BP, overlaps with the Jurassic and Cretaceous
periods)
In Mozambique the Karroo is represented by sedimentary and volcanic rocks about 350 - 130
million years old.
The Stromberg series comprises sandstone, basalt and rhyolites. This formation is found at the
junction of the crystalline basement with the Cretaceous sediments along the major tectonic
line of the Zambeze River Valley and Lower Zambeze trough running north-east between
Espungabera and the Zambeze River (mainly basalts) and again along the Libombo Mountain
range forming border with South Africa and Swaziland ( rhyolites and basalts).
Coal deposits occur in the Ecca series in the area of Moatize, Tete Province.
iii   Jurassic to Cretaceous (about 208 million to 65 million BP)
The uper Lupata sandstone are post-Karroo red sandstones and mudstones cemented by
argilaceous and tufaceous material running approximately northwards from Gorongosa
Mountain to Lupata in association with the Maringue karroo sedimants Red sandy clays and
clays are produced from the weathered products with a calcium rich sub-soil.
58602 Annexure D                  December 2000                            2-3
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                              41 SMEC
The oldest Cretaceous sediments are continental in origin. Marine cretaceous sediments,
known as the Grudja (running north-east over the Buzi River immediately to the west of the
sandy plains), overly the continental Cretaceous sediments and are characterised by the
presence of fossil shells. The marine cretaceous formations are the source of hydrocarbon
minerals.
iv    Tertiary (65 million - 1.8 million BP)
Early Tertiary formations occur north east of Magude, the Cheringoma Plateau (the
Cheringoma. Formation, age 54 million years) and in smaller areas of Cabo Delgado Province.
Late Tertiary formations (7-26 million years) may overly the Early Tertiary formations e.g. the
Mazamba Formation in Sofala Province.
v    Quaternary to Recent (age 1.8 million years BP to Recent)
Quatemary to Recent Formations, mainly non-sedimentary sands and alluviums occurs
throughout much of southem Mozambique south of Beira. It includes the entire area south of
the Save River except for the Libombo rhyolites and basalts and consists almost entirely of
marine quatemary deposits.

2.3 SEISMIC ACTIVITY
The Rift Valley is a geological fault still undergoing tectonic movement causing seismic events
that mainly affect central western Mozambique. Over 52 years, 24 seismic events (<4 on the
Richter Scale) and 4 seismic events (>4 on the Richter scale) occurred in this sub-region
(Figure 4). A combination of deforestation, high rainfall and seismic events probably caused
the Mt Tumbine landslide disaster in Milange District, Zambezia Province in 1997 with the
loss of more than 100 people.

3 SOILS
3.1 DESCRIPTION
The major soil types of Mozambique are shown in Figure 5.
The soils in the northern and some parts of the central and western areas are light clays and
clay loams of medium fertility.
Heavy-textured black soils occur along the northern sector of the coast (Cabo Delgado
Province) and to the east of Espungabera (Manica and Sofala Provinces).
Red sandy soils of low fertility occur in Sofala Province north of Beira (Cheringoma Plateau)
and between Inhambane and Vilankulos.
Alluvial soils occur in the Umbeluzi, Maputo, Incomati, Limpopo and Zambeze river basins.
Though prone to salinization, particularly in delta areas, these soils have a high potential for
agriculture.
Sandy soils of marine and aeolion origin, very low fertility, cover a large portions of southern
Mozambique. Smaller areas of light sandy soils occur in middle Zambezia, Nampula and
Cabo Delgado Provinces.

3.2 POTENTIAL FOR SOIL EROSION
The potential for soil erosion is low to moderate for most of low and mid altitude
Mozambique. There is a high potential for soil erosion in high altitude areas (e.g., The
Lichingo plateau, Niassa Province, and the foothills of the Chimanimani Mountains, Manica
Province) and the mid-Zambezi Valley. Along the Libombos mountains (westem frontier with
South Africa) the potential for soil erosion is considered to be extreme. Soil erosion potential
for Mozambique is shown in Figure 6.

4 CLIMATE
For most of the year the weather is dominated by the high pressure system which prevails over
the southern African Plateau. North-east and south-east air streams from the Indian Ocean
bring rain during the months of October-March. Despite its proximity to the sea, and the warm
southward flowing Mozambique Current, much of the interior of the country is relatively dry.
Rainfall is erratic and unreliable, with high inter-annual variation, especially in southern and
central districts.
Rainfall is highest on the coast. It decreases rapidly on passing inland, but increases again on
the slopes of the mountains along the western borders.
Mean annual rainfall is 750 mm at Maputo (25058'S/32035'E), but only 550 mm at Massingir
(2305 I'S/3158'E), while over much of the southern interior between the Save and Limpopo
Rivers it is below 400 mm.
The entire coastline receives 800-900 mm of rain a year, with four more humid pockets on
SW-NE stretches of coast, where the SE Trade Winds blow perpendicular to the coast in
summer. Elsewhere the prevailing winds are oblique to the coast. The humid pockets are
situated to the east of Xai-Xai (2503'S/33048'E), between the Tropic of Capricorn and
Vilanculos (2201'S/3519'E), from Beira (1949'S/3452'E) to the southern part of the
Zambezi Delta, and from the vicinity of Quelimane (1753'S/3651'E) to Pebane
(1714'S/3810'E). In the first two areas mean annual rainfall is 1000-1200 mm, while in the
second two it is 1200-1400 mm.
The north of the country is generally more humid than the south except for part of the lower
Zambezi Valley in Tete Province, which receives less than 600 mmlyr. There are a series of
very humid pockets along the western border on the upper slopes of the mountains between
Mt. Binga and Mt. Inyangani (18 18'S/3254'E in Zimbabwe). Between 1500-2000 mm of rain
falls in these places in the wet season (mid-November to March) and the Pungue, Revue,
Lucite and Buzi Rivers rise there, or close by in Zimbabwe. The upper courses of these streams
are rocky, and full of cascades and cataracts. Rainfall also exceeds 1500 mm/yr on the isolated
Gorongosa Massif (1830'S/3403'E), standing midway between the western highlands and
Beira on the coast. All the higher mountainous areas north of the Zambezi are wet. Mean
annual precipitation exceeds 2200 mm over a substantial area around the summit of Mt.
Namuli, and a surrounding area of 36,000 km2 receives between 1200-1800 mm/yr. In this
region the Lurio, Meluli, Ligonha, Mol6cue, M'lela, Licungo (Lugela) and Luala Rivers have
their sources.
Mean annual rainfall isohyets for Mozambique are shown in Figure 7. There is a close
relationship between broad "ecozones" and precipitation. Areas receiving more than
2000mm/yr are characterised by rain forest - with decreasing rainfall there is a gradation moist
savanna to arid savanna (Figure 6).
January is the hottest month and July the coldest month. Mean annual temperatures along the
coast increase gradually from south to north: Maputo (22.2C), Xai Xai (22.8C), Inhambane
(23. 1C), Nova Sofala (24.9C), Beira (25C), Quelimane (25.1C) and Pemba (25.5C). Very hot
temperatures exceeding 38C can occur in summer

Mean annual temperatures decrease with increasing altitude e.g. mean annual temperatures for
Lichinga (Niassa Province) at 1350 m asi is 180 C and absolute minimum temperatures reaching
freezing point may occur during the winter months.
The striking feature of the climate of Mozambique is the similarity in rainfall patterns across
Mozambique. There is a distinct rainy season between November to March and a distinct dry
season between the months of March and October. This climate pattern is characteristic of
savanna biomes in general (see Figure 8).
Winds are light to moderate. Most stations in the southem lowlands record mean annual speeds
of 6-8 km/hr, but high winds of up to 100 kin/ accompany summer storms. Most of the
country experiences at least 3000 hours of sunshine a year (8 hr/day).
Cyclones are generated in the south-west Indian Ocean from November to April. On average,
15 to 20 cyclones are generated annually during this season, some of which may reach the
Mozambican coast especially in February and March. Cyclone paths generated over the Indian
Ocean between 1980 and 2000 are shown in Figure 9. Over the last forty-five years, there were
24 cyclones that strongly affected the north-central sector of the Mozambican coast (Table 4).

5 DRAINAGE
5.1 RIVERINE SYSTEMS
The main river system and lakes of Mozambique are shown on Figure 10. A description of the
main systems follows.
i   Rivers of Maputaland
The major rivers of Maputaland are the Maputo, with a catchment area of 29,800 kmi2, flowing
from Natal in South Africa; the Umbeluzi with a catchment of 5,622 km2, flowing in from
Swaziland; and the Nkomati which drains some 45,875 km2 and enters from South Africa. All
these Rivers discharge into the Estuario do Espirito Santo, Maputo Bay. The Maputo River has
a floodplain with peripheral pans and lakes, and it flows through much swampy land,
particularly along its east bank, for the full 100 km of its transit between the border and the
sea.
The Umbeluzi is impounded near Boane (the Pequenos Libombos Dam - filled in 1989) and
now floods irregularly in its lower reaches.
The Nkomati is formned by the confluence of the Nkomati and Sabie River at the town of Sabie
(25019'S/3216'E), above which, in Mozambique, both tributary streams have narrow
floodplains. Below Sabie the river flows north and east until it swings south and enters a great
marshy area, which develops below the confluence of many streams, some 13 km north of
Manhiqa (25023'S/32051'E). This region comprises lakes, notably Lake Chuali, and permanent
swamps as well as seasonal floodplain areas.
ii   The Limpopo System
The Limpopo is the second largest river in Mozambique with a catchment of more than
390,000 km2. It drains parts of Botswana, South Africa and Zimbabwe before reaching
Mozambique. Flows in the Limpopo River are extremely variable and it is often dry over long
sections during the winter.
Prior to the confluence with the Elefantes (Elephants) River at Quenelle (2407'S/3238'E)
there are swamps along the Limpopo, in a belt 1-2 km wide. Below Quenuelle the Limpopo
Floodplain, which is still comparatively narrow, continues until the river flow is checked by a
barrage, the Barragem de Macarretane, just above Chokwe (2431'S/33O00'E). Below this point
flood waters are controlled to some extent, but the floodplain widens as the river approaches its
confluence with the Changane near Chibuto (2440'S/3333'E). Thereafter it meanders over a
broad swampy floodplain to the coast where it flows into a wide lagoon after Xai-Xai. There is
an estimated 40,000-60,000 ha of swamp and floodplain below Chokwe.
The Elefantes River is a major tributary of the Limpopo River arising in South Africa where it
flows through the Kruger National Park. The River is impounded at Massingir.

iii   The Inharrime River & Drainage of Interior Inhambane
The interior of Inhambane Province, south of latitude 22S, has a diffuse drainage system. The
area north of Funhalouro (2305'S/3423'E) is characterised by innumerable endorheic
swamplets and seasonal pans, but in the south, there are swampy seasonal drainage lines
leading eventually to the Inharrime River System. From here drainage is to Poelela Lagoon.
Lake Nhangulaze (2400'S/3427'E) has a water surface of 1250 ha and is set in an area of
unbroken swamps covering about 60 000 ha. These swamps drain into the Lake which in turn
discharges via a small stream to the Inhassune. Sixty-five km downstream it joins the
Inharrime River which in total drains an estimated 11,850 km2 of Inhambane Province.
iv    The Save, Gorongosa, Buzi and Pungue Rivers
All these rivers are very seasonal in their flow regimes. The Save River has a catchment of
92,000 km2 and is impounded in Zimbabwe and the River is now seasonal. Lake Zinave
(3355'E) is the largest lake, some 2.5 km long and I km wide, associated with the Save River.
The Gorongosa River (not to be confused with Gorongosa National Park) drains 13,150 km 2 of
lowland flowing through swampy lands for 100 km to the muddy coast south of Beira.
The Buzi River rises on Mt. Selinda (2024'S/3243'E) just inside Zimbabwe. It flows NE,
picking up its major tributaries, the Lucite, which rises on Mt. Binga, and the Revue which
rises on Mt. Nusa (1845'S/3200'E), before being impounded in Mozambique (Lago
Chicamba) below the town of Manica. These upper catchments are all in high (1500-2000
mm) rainfall zones. In its lower reaches the Buzi drains swampy land. Tall grasses cover the
floodplains, with clumps of trees, including Borassus palms.
The Pungue River rises on the western side of Mt. Nyangani (2000 m asl) in Zimbabwe, and
then flows over waterfalls and cataracts and through gorges in a rapid descent to an altitude of
500 m. Thereafter, it flows east, supporting riverine and hygrophilous forest on its banks, and
then south-east towards the coast at Beira, collecting numerous tributaries en route. The most
important of these is the Urema which flows through Gorongosa National Park. This develops
from headwater streams and the permanent aquifer of the Gorongosa Massif. The swamps
along the lower reaches of the Pungue reach 15 km in width up to Nhamatanda
(19018'S/34 l lE).
v    The Lower Zambezi River
The Zambezi enters Mozambique at Zumbo (1536'S/3030'E) where it immediately swells
into the impoundment of Lake Cahora Bassa.
It leaves the lake in a narrow gorge some 35 km long, then descends the Quebrabasa Rapids
and flows south-eastwards in a narrow valley along the floor of the Tete Basin. It receives
tributaries flowing down from the rocky highlands of Zimbabwe in the south and from Zambia
and Malawi in the north. At Tete (1609'S/3335'E), the valley floor begins to broaden and the
58602 Annexure D                   December 2000                               5-3
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                             4i-SMEC
river develops a narrow floodplain. There is a floodplain up to 5 km wide and about 40 km
long on the south bank between Tambara (1645'S/3416'E) and Chiramba (1655'S/3439'E),
and swamps around Lake Lifamba on the north bank opposite Tambara. For much of its course
between Tete and its delta, the bed of the Zambezi is 1-5 km wide.
The most important tributary of the Zambezi in its lower course is the Shire River, which
drains Lake Malawi via Elephant Marsh. Below this the whole Shire Valley is swampy. There
is an extensive lake/swamp system just above the confluence of the Shire and Zambezi Rivers
(17 42'S/3520'E) near Mutarara of which Lake Mimbingue (5x3 km) is the largest.
Development has been quite intensive down the Zambezi Valley below Senna, and sections of
the floodplain, e.g. between Caia and Quelimane have been recovered for coconut plantations
and part of the delta has been converted to sugar plantations. The huge dual impoundments of
Kariba and Cahora Bassa have also affected the downstream flooding regime. The delta is
characterized by a large swampy floodplain especially on the southem bank covering an area
of 18,000 km2.
vi    Rivers of Zambezia Province
The major perennial rivers of Zambezia Province, the Licungo (Lugela), Raraga, M'Iela,
Molocue, Ligonha and Meluli. The largest, the Licungo has a catchment of 22 726 km2 and is
336 km long, but the others are significantly smaller. The Licungo has sources high on the
slopes of Mt. Mulanje (3000 m) in Malawi (1600'S/3537'E), and on Mt. Namuli in the
wettest part of Mozambique. Mean annual rainfall exceeds 2200 mm in both source regions.
Upstream these rivers support riverine forest and thicket forests, or patchy reed swamps, but on
the plain they are flanked by extensive belts of swamp forest and herbaceous swamp, grading
into tidal swamps towards the coast. In proceeding north-eastwards up the coast the succeeding
rivers carry less water. Although the Ligonha is the second largest stream it drains a drier
catchment and usually floods less than the other stretches.
vii   Rivers of The North-East Coast
The most northerly and largest river on this coast, the Rovuma, has a catchment of 155,400
km2 and is the third largest river in Mozambique. It forms the border between Mozambique
and Tanzania for some 700 km, almost the entire length of the border. The river has sources in
both countries on the mountains at the eastem edge of the Rift Valley and receives numerous
important tributaries on both banks. Two thirds of the catchment (101,160 kmi2) lies in
Mozambique and one third (54,240 kM2) in Tanzania. Below Gomba (1123'S/3830'E) the
gradient is gentle and the Rovuma traverses a widening, coastal valley for 130 km in an almost
straight line. Here the river floods seasonally, but almost exclusively on the Mozambican side.
Swamps occur along the river in its lowest 40 km, becoming more frequent towards the mouth.
The Nhica Swamp (1037'S 4016'E), occupying some 7500 ha, and over is km long, on the
south bank, is among the largest. Others are the Quitemba Swamp (1057'S/14013'E) and,
farther from the river, the Miula Swamp (1010'S/4016'E). The former covers 2500 ha and the
latter about 7000 ha. A number of lakes occur on the floodplains along the south bank. Of
these, Lake Nangade (I 1103'S/13942'E) is the most important. It is 12 km long and covers
5700 ha.
58602 Annexure D                  December 2000                              5-4
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                             4SMEC
All the rivers on the NE coast, the Rovuma, Messalo, Montepuez, Megaruma, Lurio, Mecuburi
and Monapo, have very seasonal flows, and carry less alluvium and water to the coast (Cabo
Delgado and Nampula Provinces) than those which reach the sea in Zambezia Province.
They have narrow sections of riverine swamp along their middle courses and along their
tributaries. The lower course of the Montepuez spreads into a long narrow lake (about 30 km
long) immediately prior to the coast (Lake Biribizi).
5.2 LAKE SYSTEMS
Major lakes of Mozambique were shown on Figure 9 and are discussed below. Some lakes
were discussed with their related rivers in the previous section.
i   Lake Cahora Bassa
Lake Cahora Bassa is a man-made reservoir created in 1975 and located on the Zambezi River
at 15029'-16000'S/30028'-32043'E. The lake covers an area of 2,665 km2 and is the fourth
largest man made lake in Africa.
ii   Lake Niassa
The largest natural lake in Mozambique is Lake Niassa (also known as Lake Malawi). Lake
Niassa lies 474 m above mean sea level in the Rift Valley. With a surface area of between
24,504 km2 and 30,800 km2 it is the third largest lake in Africa and the world's third deepest.
Lake Niassa is shared by Mozambique, Malawi and Tanzania. Mozambican territory occupies
6400 km2 (20.8%) of this area with a shoreline of about 250 km.
In the south Lake Niassa is drained by the Shire River through a series of marshy alluvial
plains (Elephant Swamp) which flows south along the Rift Valley floor to meet the Zambezi.
Lake Niassa supports one of the greatest diversity of fish species in the world with high levels
of endemism. The Lake is especially well known for its spectacular diversity of its endemic
cichlid fish fauna (Ribbink, 1987).
iii   Lake Amaramba
Lake Amaramba (1422'-1440'S/3552-3558'E) covers an area of about 8350 ha (4350 ha
open water + 4000 ha swamp) and lies 635 m asl. The Lake lies in a shallow depression with
much swamp land to the south and east. It drains part of Malawi, with a small river entering its
southern extremity, but in wet periods it becomes continuous with Lake Chiuta some 9 km to
the south, through a region of swamp land. The Lugenda River, the largest tributary of the
Rovuma, drains the entire system, leaving Lake Amaramba at its northern extremity.
iv    Lake Chiuta
Lake Chiuta (14'42'-14'47'S/35'50'-3554'E) covers an area of 32,000 ha (3500 ha open water
+ 28,500 ha swamp) at altitude about 639 m asi.
58602 Annexure 0                   December 2000                             5-5
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                               SMEC
The Mozambique-Malawi border crosses this small lake in such a way that about 17 km of
shoreline and 2000 ha of water surface falls within Mozambique. The lake is 10 km long and
up to 3.5 km wide. Large swamps extend southwards from the lake into both Malawi and
Mozambique. In total these cover about 28,500 ha, of which about 6250 ha of these are in
Mozambique. A sand bar along the southem border of the swamp separates the Lake Chiuta
system from the Lake Chilwa system farther south. The lake drains into the Lugenda River via
Lake Amaramba.
v    Lake Chilwa
Lake Chilwa (1509'-1531'S/3533'-3550'E) covers an area of 185,000 ha (including
lacustrine swamps) and lies at an altitude of 622 m asl. The shore of the lake forms the border
between Mozambique and Malawi for approximately 26 km, but very little of the water surface
is Mozambican territory. The swamps, however, extend into Mozambique.

Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                              ;SMEC
6 LAND USE
6.1 BACKGROUND
In comparison with neighbouring countries, the population density of Mozambique is
relatively low (an average 19 individuals per kM2). Most of the country still comprises
natural/semi-natural vegetation. Amajor land use is agriculture.
Agricultural production comprises two main socio-economic sectors. Firstly, the small-scale
family sector (the majority of the rural population)..This ranges from pure subsistence
agriculture to integration in to the market economy. The level of integration depends on agro-
ecological conditions and market opportunities. Secondly, the commercial sector (mainly large
companies) that is heavily reliant on capital inputs and land resources.
It is estimated that approximately 5% of Mozambique is under active cultivation by the small-
scale sector and only 0.2% by the private sector. Although the percentage under active
cultivation at any one time is low, the area of natural habitat affected by small-scale farming is
significantly higher. Small-scale farmers slash and bum mainly woodland to open up cropping
areas. After several years of cultivation a new cropping area is derived from natural or
secondary vegetation. The small-scale farming system comprises an arable (cropping) area as
well as fallow fields of varying ages. The overall area affected by small-scale farmers is,
therefore, much higher than the actual cultivated area.
6.2 FARMING
6.2.1    Small-sector Farming Sector
Most small-scale farms are derived from slash and bum of secondary or primary woodland. The
woody vegetation is felled in June/July and burnt in September/October prior to the rainy season.
The land is cultivated over a 3 to 5 year period after which it left to fallow for a further 3 to 5
years. Organic and inorganic fertilizers are rarely, if ever, used by the small-scale sector
Maize (Zea mays), intercropped with groundnut (Arachis hypogaea), cassava (Manihot
esculentus) and beans (Phaseolus spp.) are the principal crops. Irrigation is rain-fed. Produce is
mainly for home consumption (subsistence) although excess may be sold to supplement the
household income.
In the medium altitude (200 to 1000m above sea level) regions of Zambezia, Nampula, Tete,
Niassa and Cabo Delagado Provinces cassava is widely cultivated.
Maize is replaced by drought resistant crops, such as sorghum and millet, in semi-arid regions
(e.g., the interior of Gaza and Inhambane Provinces and Central Tete Province).
Cotton is an important cash crop for the small-scale sector in medium altitude regions of Sofala,
Zambezia, Nampula and Cabo Delgado Provinces. Cashews are also an important cash crop in the
58602 Annexure D                   December2000                               6-1
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                               jSMEC
aforementioned areas as well as coastal areas of Maputo, Gaza, Inhambane, Nampula, Zambezia
and Cabo Delgado Provinces. In coastal areas of Zambezia and Inhambane Provinces coconut is
an important component of the small-scale sector.
In low-lying wetland areas rice is grown. This is especially important in the floodplains of the
Limpopo River, the Buzi-Pungue floodplains and the Zambezi Delta.
Farmers rear small livestock such as goats, chickens and ducks. Cattle distribution is restricted by
the Tsetse fly. Only a few peasant farmers presently own cattle as most farmers lost their cattle
during the armed conflict. Traditionally cattle are important as draft power.
6.2.2    Commercial Sector
This sector is traditionally associated with large companies dedicated mainly to specialised and
intensive agricultural production such as coconut plantations, fruit trees, tea, cotton, sisal,
sugar cane, coffee and livestock production. Several of these companies have a long historical
association with Mozambique the most notable being MADAL, Sena Sugar Estates,
Companhia da Zambezia (Zambezia Province), Marromeu Sugar Estate, Buzi Sugar Estate
(Sofala Province) and Maragra Sugar Estate (Maputo Province).
The total land area occupied by the private sector is relatively low (1,446 square kilometres or
0.2% of the territory of Mozambique. However, private sector farms occupy some of the most
fertile soils most notably the alluvial soils of the Limpopo and Buzi/Pungue Valley and
Zambezia Delta.
6.3 MAIN LAND USE PATTERNS PER PROVINCE
6.3.1    Maputo Province
The soils of Maputo Province are mainly sandy or sandy loam texture. The family sector
farmers cultivate maize, cowpea, peanuts and cassava.
Alluvium soils occur the Nkomati, Umbeluzi and Maputo River valleys. These are under more
extensive agriculture by the private and small-scale sector. About 6,000ha is under sugar
plantation in the Nkomati valley floodplains near Manhica (Maragra Sugar Estate).
6.3.2    Gaza Province
The coastal area of Gaza Province has one the highest population densities in the country.
Soils are sandy and land use is similar to Maputo Province The most important annual crops
are maize, cowpea, groundnut, sweet potato and cassava. Due to the limited availability of
land, there is a tendency to intercrop all four crops. The production of cashew in this region is
one of the most important sources of income for the rural population.
Alluvial and peaty soils occur in the Limpopo valley. Near Chokwe, 20,000 ha is under surface
irrigation comprising medium-sized farms (4-50 ha, total 2,000 ha) and small scale-farms (5-4
ha, 10,000 ha). The remainder is large-scale private sector agriculture (e.g. cotton).
58602 Annexure D                   December 2000                              6-2
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                             QSMAEC
Further downstream, towards Xai-Xai small-scale farmers grow rice, bananas and sugarcane
on small plots.
The interior of Gaza Province has a low population density. It is one of the most arid regions
of the Mozambique with an annual rainfall of 400-600 mm. Due to the lack of soil moisture
sorghum and millet are grown in the region. The area has a high potential for natural (wildlife)
grazing and browsing.
6.3.3   Inhambane Province
The land use of Inhambane Province is similar to Gaza Province. There is a coastal strip south
of the Save River with high population density and a larger, drier interior with low population
density. Cropping regimes for these two areas are similar for Gaza Province.
The interior of Inhambane Province is characterized by large areas of intact woodland and
forest.
6.3.4    Sofala Province
The coastal region of Sofala extends from the Save River in the south to Zambezia River in the
north. The soils are generally sandy except in the Buzi/Pungue estuary and Zambezi Delta
where heavy textured alluviums occur.
In the regions with better drained soils crops of maize, sorghum, millet, cassava and cowpea
are found in association. Cashew and cotton are important cash crops in the farming systems.
In the heavy, seasonally inundated soils of the Buzi/Pungue floodplain and Zambezi Delta
areas the cultivation of rainfed rice predominates.
The are large private sector sugar cane plantations in the Buzi/Pungue floodplain (Buzi Sugar
Estate which is currently being rehabilitated) and the Zambezi Delta (Marromeu Sugar Estate.
This was recently privatized and is currently being rehabilitated).
The mid-altitude region of Sofala (200 and lOOOm above sea level) comprises mainly light
textured soils with some occurrence of heavy soils. Maize, sorghum, cassava and cowpea
predominate. In seasonal wetlands farmers cultivate sweet potato and rice. In this region there
is good potential to produce cotton. It is a region with a moderate to high population density.
6.3.5    Manica Province
Land use for the mid-altitude region of Manica Province is similar to that in Sofala Province,
namely the cultivation of maize, sorghum, millet and cassava. Cotton is an important cash
crop.
In the more elevated areas of the Province heavier textured soils occur and rainfall is higher.
Maize is the main crop. There are large areas of pine plantation on the slopes of the western
mountains.
58602 Annexure D                  December 2000                             6-3
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                             4SMEC
6.3.6    Tete Province
The central portion of Tete Province (Zambeze valley) is semi-arid. Sorghum and millet
predominate.
The medium altitude region comprises land between 200 and 1,000 meters above sea level.
The main crops are cassava, maize, cowpea and pigeon pea inter-cropped in furrows. Farms
vary between 1.5 and 3.0 ha.
In the high altitude region of Tete Province (Angonia District) the soils are principally
ferrasols of heavy texture. In this region maize is the dominant crop, with a high production
potential. Beans and potato are also important.
6.3.7    Zambezia Province
The soils of coastal Zambezia are mainly sandy, except in the Zambezi Delta where heavy
textured alluviums occur.
In the coastal sandy soils the agricultural system is characterized by the production of cassava
and millet. Cashew is an important source of income for family farmers.
Agricultural production in the Zambezi Delta is dominated by rice and coconuts. This area is
characterized by the occurrence of narrow, elongated slightly elevated dunes, locally called
"morrundas", separated by depressions, locally called "baixas", which are inundated and lie
parallel to the coast. In the morrundas the dominant crops are cassava, maize and nhemba
beans. In the baixas rice is the dominant crop during the rainy season and sweet potato and
second season maize during the dry season. Cultivation of coconuts on the "morrundas" is
important.
The private sector has large areas of land in the Zambezi Delta regions e.g., Madal, Sena
Sugar Estates, and Companhia da Zambezia. Of the three companies, Madal is the only one
fully operational. It has operations in the Districts of Quelimane (coconut and livestock),
Chinde, Inhassunge, Maganja da Costa and Namacurra and Nicoadala. The Madal palm
plantation areas for each district are, 2,460 ha, 2,601 ha 12,053 ha, 2,204 ha and 4,057 ha,
respectively.
Sena Sugar Estates (SSE) operates in Chinde District at the village of Luabo. Here SSE has
10,000 ha of sugar cane and 3,000 ha of coconut palm plantation. At present, these areas are
being rehabilitated.
Mid altitude Zambezia Province is characterized by red sandy soils. The main crops are maize,
sorghum and cassava.
In the high altitude region of Zambezia (land above 1,000 meters) soils types are principally
heavy textured ferrasols. Maize is the dominant crop, with a high production potential.
Private sector tea plantations have long been established in the highlands of Zambezia
Province.
58602 Annexure D                  December 2000                             6-4
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                              *SMEC
6.3.8    Nampula Province
The soils of the coastal areas of Nampula Province are mainly sandy. The main cropping
system is characterized by the production of cassava and millet. Cashew is an important cash
crop for the small-scale family sector.
The interior of the Province is medium altitude (200 and 1,000 meters in altitude). The texture
of the soils varies from sandy to clayish, consistent with the topography. There are two types
of cropping systems that differ by being dominated by either maize or sorghum. Cassava,
cowpea and groundnut are widely cultivated.
In the eastern portion of the medium altitude region cashew is very important. Cotton is an
important cash crop.
6.3.9    Cabo Delgado Province
Land use in coastal Cabo Delgado Province is similar to the coastal area of Nampula Province.
The soils are sandy and cassava and millet are the main crops. In seasonal wetlands rainfed
rice is cultivated. Cashew is an important source of income for family sector farmers
The interior region of Cabo Delgado includes the plateaux of Mueda and Macomia. The soils
are loamy to sandy. The dominant crop in the production system is maize. Sorghum, cowpea,
cassava and sesame are also cultivated. Cashew is also an important cash crop for small-scale
farmers.
6.3.10    Niassa Province
Most of Niassa Province is located at high altitude above 1,000 meters (the Lichinga plateau).
The soils and cropping systems are similar to the high altitude areas of Zambezia and Tete
Province, heavy textured ferrasols with maize being the dominant crop. Beans and potato are
also important components of the cropping system.
6.3.11    Main Land Uses in Each Province
The areas under cultivation by the small-scale sector and private sector and forest area per
province is given in Table 6.1.

IMPALJ'U
1 BIOLOGICAL & PHYSICAL ENVIRONMENTS
1.1 TOPOGRAPHY AND LANDFORMS
The road begins at the town of Chokwe, located on the south bank of the Limpopo River. The
road alignment traverses a mostly flat lowland area and heads approximately south from
Ch6kwe for a distance of 31km. The major environmental feature is the Limpopo River valley
with treeless floodplains approximately 5km wide (mainly on the south bank). The south bank
floodplains are under intensive agriculture and there is much permanent infrastructure.
Scattered pans occur for some distance from the floodplains. The Limpopo floodplain is
subjected to extensive seasonal inundation.
On leaving the floodplain, the vegetation is moderately open, interspersed with scattered
homesteads and small-scale farms. This is essentially an open grassland environment with
scattered trees. Numerous small pans and wetlands occur in association with drainage lines in
this region. Between the Limpopo and Mazimechopes Rivers the route traverses flat terrain
covered by a mosaic of open to closed woodland.
At its southern limit the road crosses the Mazimechopes River (2431'15"S, 3244'30"E),
which forms the boundary between the Maputo and the Gaza Provinces. It consists of a narrow
meandering channel (i 20m wide) with banks lined by a narrow fringe of riparian vegetation.
Agriculture (grazing and cropping) is practiced on the margins of the river. A few households
comprising brick houses are located near the river.
1.2 CLIMATE
This section describes climate data, which was obtained over the last five years (Instituto
Nacional de Meteorologia, 2000) and is temperature, rainfall and relative humidity.
The southern coastal region has tropical humid to sub-humid climate, with a distinct winter dry
season. But within the coastal zone rain typically falls in every month of the year with the
maximum during the summer months. Rainfall patterns are erratic and unreliable, with
substantial annual variations, especially in southern and central districts.
Figure 1 shows the mean monthly ambient temperatures for the Macia weather station, and
Figure 2 shows the mean monthly rainfall and humidity. The distribution of prevailing winds is
dominated by southerlies (SW and SE) as shown in Figure 3 and no cyclones have affected
Gaza Province over the 1999-2000 period.
Throughout the year, mean monthly temperatures vary between 19 and 27C, but rarely drop
below 20C. There is slight seasonality, with a cool dry season from June to September and a
warm wet season from October to May.
The dry season extends from April to September and the wet season for October to March. The
distinct seasonality in rainfall correlates with relative humidity, with the dry winter months
being less humid than the wet summer months.

1.3 GEOLOGY
The underlying rock is recent Quaternary and sedimentary in origin, comprising of Post-
Karroo sediments of the Karroo supergroup (Figure 4). This base rock is overlain with sandy
alluviums characteristic of mature river valleys. This sedimentary platform covers much of
southern and coastal Mozambique.

1.4 SOILS
The Limpopo Valley is located just north of the project road and is characterised by alluvial soils
of which scattered patches are sodic or saline. Intensive agricultural development occurs on the
alluvial floodplain of the Limpopo River. The alluviums are the prime agricultural soils in the
region; poor drainage and a high water table may be expected in these alluvial soils.
Away from the river channel, toward the project road, coarse material accumulates on the levees
and the soils have a low salinity. The soil types intersected by the project road are predominantly
clayey and sandy 'Mananga', characterised by a deep layer and relatively good to very good
drainage.
The soil types traversed by the project road are described in Table 1 and shown in Figure 5.
