1.5 LAND USE
Ch6kwe is one of the strongest economic Districts; a consequence of the extensive
transportation network, good natural resources and a comparatively more urbanised population
than is characteristic of most of the other Districts. The surplus in agricultural production
resultant from the interplay of these factors has been a major factor in increasing trade and
economic growth.  Ch6kwe is an area of great agricultural activity. Rural production
predominates; prevalent food crops include maize, cassava, groundnuts, sorghum, beans, sweet
potato and vegetables.
The reliance of the majority of the population on subsistence agriculture has resulted in a
dispersed settlement pattern and very low-density land usage. Most of the region is
undeveloped. The project road traverses through an area that has a very low population
density. Except for the first kilometre, there were no settlements situated adjacent to the road.
The road predominantly passes through inundated areas supporting wetland species and intact
woodland. The road access is also limited to 4WD vehicles after 26km and therefore traffic
volumes are low.
1.6 RIVERS AND WATER QUALITY
1.6.1    Rivers and Hydrology
The project road is located in the Limpopo River valley, south of Chokwe. The river flow and
floodwaters are controlled by a barrage (The Barragem de Maccaretane) situated upstream of
Ch6kwe, but the floodplain widens again as the river approaches its confluence with the
Changane River near Chibuto.
Old levees and river channels cover the entire lower floodplain; the abandoned channels
frequently giving rise to stagnant lakes and ponds. These are especially common to the west of
the river, reflecting the fact that the main stream has been shifting eastwards for the last several
thousand years.
Drainage throughout this area is diffuse in the main season, but in the winter it is concentrated
on the main river channel. The river is tidal for many kilometres and saline water penetrates
well into the lower swamps when winter tides are high. In 1957 the swamps spread for at least
8km on either side of the main channel, and the river was navigable for 57km. Today there
may be 40,000-60,000ha of swamp and floodplain below Ch6kwe.
1.6.2    Water Quality
River flows of the Limpopo River system are highest during the rainy season from mid-
November to March, with flows generally reaching a peak in January and February. River
flows are lowest during the dry season months of June to September.
Rivers are most turbid during the rainy season, when overland runoff and erosion are at their
greatest.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                          1-6



IMPAL'IU
Watercourses and wetland areas in the vicinity of the project road are relatively free of
chemical pollutants due to the absence of industry and settlements.  Its proximity to
commercial agriculture may have resulted in pollution from agrochemicals.
1.7 VEGETATION COVER
The road traverses two main vegetation types, including Acacia woodland and vegetation on
Limpopo and Nkomati Alluviums (Wild and Barbosa, 1967). Figure 6 shows the distribution
of this vegetation adjacent to the road.
1.7.1    Acacia Woodland
The main vegetation type in the area is Acacia Woodland (MU 48) and occurs south and north
of the Limpopo River. Acacia tree or shrub woodland is represented by an important area in
the region of Moamba, Magude and Guija to the Changane River. It occurs within semi-arid
zones with rainfall about 60mm per annum and on soils locally known as "clayey mananga"
and, in more restricted areas, on "sandy mananga". This vegetation type grades into deeper
alluvial zones, near the Limpopo River.
1.7.2    Vegetation on Alluviums
The vegetation on Limpopo and Nkomati Alluviums (MU 54) is characterised by fringing
forest occurring on the immediate margins of the riverbanks. The dominant species comprise
Ficus spp., Trichilia emetica, Kigelia africana, Lannea stuhlmanii and Xanthocerra
zambeziaca. Behind this fringe, extensive grasslands with Setaria spp.on periodically flooded
and badly drained alluviums. On slightly higher and better-drained ground a sub-arid open
woodland type may occur.
1.8 WILDLIFE AND CONSERVATION AREAS
1.8.1    Wildlife
There is an absence of large mammals in the vicinity of the Ch6kwe road due to a lack of
conservation areas within the vicinity and unsuitable habitat adjacent to the road. The
conservation value of the woodland for small mammals and reptiles is not known.
1.8.2    Birds
The Limpopo River valley and its associated wetland habitats are home to a diversity of bird
species; these species are listed in Table 2 together with their conservation status and the main
threats to their persistence. A complete list of bird species recorded in the Limpopo valley is
provided in Appendix A (Table Al).

1.8.3    Amphibians
The wetlands traversed by the Ch6kwe road are suitable habitat for a diversity of anuran
amphibians (10-15 species). It is possible that populations of the rare Afrixalus aureus occur
here. However, road rehabilitation is unlikely to impact negatively on this species.
1.8.4    Fish
There is a high diversity of fish species within the Limpopo River and its tributaries, however
no species of rare or threatened conservation status have been recorded.
1.8.5    Conservation Areas
There are no National Parks, Wildlife Reserves or proposed conservation areas located in the
vicinity of the Ch6kwe Road.
1.9 NOISE AND AIR QUALITY
Noise levels along the Ch6kwe Project Road, are very low due to the absence of traffic noise
and sparse settlement along the route. The noise levels are predominantly generated by natural
conditions (i.e. from insects, frogs and birds).
Ambient air quality in all of the proposed road areas is very good due to a lack of air polluting
industries and urban activities. Haze may be observed due to the frequently practiced slash
and burn agriculture and production of charcoal. Levels of airborne dust generated by winds
can also be high passing over agricultural fields, and smoke from woodland and grassland
fires.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  Februaty 2001                          1-9






IMPA' LW
2 SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
2.1 SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
2.1.1    Context and Administration
The purpose of this section is to describe the socio-economic environment for the EN405 road
in Ch6kwe District in Gaza Province. Information has been derived from existing reports and
publications where information is disaggregated at Provincial and District levels. Information
does not exist specifically related to the EN405 road. To collect specific information to the
EN405 road a field-visit was made to Ch6kwe District during November 2000 to supplement
information available at the Provincial and District level.
To describe the living conditions for the populations living along or in proximity to the EN 405
a variety of Provincial and District socio-economic indices have been used. Where possible
statistical information has been provided to enable the establishment of base line data from
which the impacts of road improvement may be made.
i   Provincial setting and administration
Gaza Province is located in the Southern Region of Mozambique. Gaza Province borders in
the north and east Inhambane Province, in the south Maputo Province, south-east the Indian
Ocean and west the Republic of South Africa.
Administratively Gaza is divided into 11 Districts, 44 Administrative Posts, and 122 Localities.
The Provincial Capital Ch6kwe and the towns of Xai Xai have Municipal Status. In terms of
area, Gaza is the fifth largest Province in Mozambique with a land surface of 75,709km2,
which represents approximately 9.5% of the land surface of country.
ii   Political environment
In the 1999 elections the ruling political party FRELIMO received the majority of the votes
cast in Gaza Province. Gaza Province is the birthplace of Joaquim Chissano the current
president of Mozambique. The first president of Mozambique Samora Machel was also born
in Gaza Province. This historical linage adds to Gaza Province strong political allegiance to
FRELIMO.
iii   Traditional administration
Traditional administration authority has no legal status in Gaza Province, although in some
districts there are close links between traditional authorities and formal administrative
authorities. The importance attached to the regulos varies from area to area. In those localities
where the regulo is strong and respected he will intervene in a variety of aspects of every-day
life. Such issues would include resolving social conflicts; allocating land for building housing,
managing water sources, supporting official health programmes such HIV/AIDS.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                          2-1



IMPACI'U
Like most parts of Mozambique, tensions exist between the two types of administrative
systems. These tensions increased during the armed conflict. Gaza Province was a strong
FRELIMO area during the armed struggle although there were significant incursions by
RENAMO into some towns in Gaza Province such as Xai Xai.
2.1.2    Population Characteristics
i   Population
The 1997 national survey estimated the population of Gaza Province at 1,062,380 inhabitants.
This constitutes the fifth largest Provincial population in Mozambique. The population density
for Gaza Province (14 inhabitants per kM2) is lower than for the average population density for
the country (19 inhabitants per kM2). The urban population (14%) is calculated on the basis of
the number of individuals registered in officially classified urban areas.
ii Ethnicity
The Changane are the main ethnic group present in Gaza Province. The languages spoken
reflect the dominance of the Changane ethnic group. 88.4% of the population speak Xitsonga,
the classification under which the Changane language falls. The other languages spoken are
Cichopi (6.2%) and Bitonga (0.5%). 2.7% of the population speaks Portuguese as their first
language.
iii   Gender
The Changane are patrilineal where inheritance follows the male line (father to son).
FRELIMO as part of the liberation struggle promoted concepts related to the equality of
women. With independence in 1975 these progressive attitudes towards the role of women
continued including the introduction of new laws, which attempted to enshrine protection of
rights for women.
Despite the advancement of the rights of women under independent administration,
Mozambican girls and women do less well than boys and men. Access to health services
directed specifically at women is generally very poor. Literacy rates for women are much
lower than for men. Family law in Mozambique still discriminates against women. A married
woman has limited rights to make independent economic decisions or enjoy full ownership of
assets, and a widow's rights to inheritance are secondary to males in the family. Girls in rural
areas in general marry young often as soon as they reach puberty.
2.1.3    Economic Characteristics
i   Economy
The economy of Gaza Province is largely based on agricultural. The most fertile areas relate to
the flood plain of the Limpopo valley, which traverses Gaza Province from west to east.
During the 1960's investment took place in and around the Provincial headquarters of Ch6kwe
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                        2-2



IMPAC'LW
for the commercial production of rice with the installation of a major irrigation system. This
scheme fell in to disrepair during the period of armed conflict and as yet no plans have been
established for rehabilitation. Recent figures for the period between 1996 and 1998 reveal that
the output of the economy of Gaza Province was the equivalent to $150 million a year
representing approximately 4% of the overall Mozambican economy.
The floods of early 2000 had dramatic effects upon Gaza Province. Tens of thousands of
people were displaced and lost all their possessions. The towns of Ch6kwe and Xai Xai were
completely submerged and major areas of Gaza Province particularly the most productive
agricultural areas were under water for several months. The floods caused major infrastructure
damage particularly to sections of the main ENI and bridges. Emergency repairs have been
made and the ENI is now open again. The floods caused significant reductions in agricultural
production. Approximately one-third of all crops with the exception of cassava were lost as
shown in Table 3. This loss of agricultural production has necessitated the importation of
basic food items to Gaza Province to feed the displaced population.
Table 3: Agricultural areas in Gaza Province affected by the 2000 floods
Crop          Area harvested (ha)     Area lost (ha)
Maize                     94,288               29,268
Sorghum                   10,141                3,273
Millet                    2,352                 786
Rice                      1,566                 7,964
Beans                     27,422                6,999
Cassava                   29,253                3,771
Source: System National de Avis Provo August 2000
Gaza Province has good potential for tourism development. Two principal tourist attractions
can be identified. The first relates to the potential for the development of coastal resorts with
the main attractions of pristine beaches and the warm Indian Ocean. The beach resort of Xai
Xai during the 1960's and 1970's attracted significant numbers of tourist particularly from
South Africa. The resorts that did exist were largely destroyed during the armed conflict of the
1980's. Redevelopment of these resorts is slowly taking place.
The second main tourist potential relates to the development of the Trans Frontier
Conservation Area (TFCA) in the eastern part of Gaza Province. The recent signing of an
international agreement between South Africa, Zimbabwe, and Mozambique could potentially
create one of the largest conservation areas in the world. The imminent product of the TFCA
agreement is to be the joining of the Kruger National Park in South Africa and Coutarda 16 in
Gaza Province. This will create a significant tourist attraction enabling tourists to pass freely
across the border of Mozambique and South Africa.
ii   Poverty
According to the National Poverty Assessment (1996-97) 64.7 % of the population of Gaza
Province are categorised as poor corresponding to 690,547 people. This is below the national
average of 69%. Life expectancy in Gaza Province (45.6 years) is above the average level
registered for the country. Life expectancy for women at 49.2 years is higher than for men at
41.1 years. More than 90% of the population depend on agriculture, forestry and fisheries for
their livelihood, mostly comprising the family sector. There are few formal sector employment
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                            2-3



IMPAL'fO
opportunities. The number of people who have had any formal education is low and the
situation is particularly acute with regards to the numbers of persons who have attained any
form of higher-level training.
iii    Education
In 1998 Gaza Province had a total of 557 schools 90% of which were primary (EPI) schools
and 10% upper primary (EP2) schools, six lower secondary (ESGI) schools, and one upper
secondary (ESG2) school.
iv    Health
Gaza Province has a total of 85 health units. 86% of these units are categorised as health posts
and 13% as health centres. In addition there are 4 rural hospitals and one Provincial Hospital.
In 1997 there were 23 doctors and 558 other medical workers registered for Gaza Province.
The illnesses occurring most frequently in Gaza Province are malaria, tuberculosis, bilharzia,
intestinal parasites, acute respiratory infection, AIDS, sexually transmitted diseases and non-
specific diarrhoea. Current rates of HIV infection are 23% and are projected to rise to 27% by
the year 2010. It is calculated that 50% children under five-years suffer from chronic
malnutrition and 10% from acute malnutrition.
v    Access to services
Access to services for the rural population is generally poor. Most villages do have a primary
school (EPI) (92%), but these schools could be categorized as rudimentary and often
availability of teachers is very low. Less the 1% of villages have a secondary school. Most
villages have access to a basic health centre, but only 7.9% have a doctor, which nevertheless
is significantly above the national average. Dependence on traditional healers is the norm for
the vast majority of the population and almost every village would have at least one person
categorised as a traditional healer. Table 4 shows the availability of services on a village basis
for Gaza Province.

2.1.4    Livelihood strategies
The principle economic activity in Gaza Province is agriculture. Most food production is
within the family sector upon which the majority of the population is dependent. The principle
crop grown is cassava (total production 143,339 tons/metric for 1999/200 followed by maize
(total production 53,728 tons/metric 1999/2000), and beans (8,888 tons/metric 1999/2000).

2.2 DISTRICT CHARACTERISTICS
This section describes socio-economic factors more specific to the ER405 road. The main
sources of information relate to existing written reports concerning Ch6kwe District through
which the ER405 passes.  In addition, a field visit to the ER405 was made during
November 2000.
2.2.1    Context and Administration
i   District setting and administration
The Chokwe ER405 is contained within Ch6kwe District located in the south of Gaza
Province. Ch6kwe District to the south borders with Maputo Province. Ch6kwe District is
divided into seven localities.
ii   Political environment
No information is available for voting practices based on the Districts of Ch6kwe. Gaza
Province voted strongly for the ruling party FRELIMO during the general elections of late
1999.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                            2-5



IMPACI'U
iii   Traditional administration
The traditional structure exists, too lesser or greater extent, in most localities side-by-side with
the more modem administrative apparatus of the State and political parties. In general terms
the first government of an independent Mozambique from 1975 attempted to replace the
inherited colonial and traditional systems with a centralised system based upon the political
doctrines of socialism.
2.2.2    Population Characteristics
i   Population
Ch6kwe District has 173,277 inhabitants (Census 1997) with 36,243 families with an average
household size of 4.78. The forecast population for Ch6kwe District as of 31 December 1999
was 200,656. Parts of Ch6kwe District were severely flooded during February and March
2000. These floods led to the displacement of a large proportion of the population mostly to
camps in other parts of Gaza Province. Most of the displaced population have now returned to
their home areas in Ch6kwe District.
ii   Ethnicity
The main ethnic group of the Ch6kwe area are the Changane. Succession and heritage for the
Changane are patrilineal, and the children are considered to belong to the father. Family
succession is granted to the eldest son. Changane men are polygamous and will take more than
one wife depending upon their economic status.
iii Gender
The Changane are patrilineal where inheritance is from father to son. Although the ethnic
groups are patrilineal, the prevalence of male migration necessitates that in many rural areas
particularly in Gaza Province, women run their families and livings. FRELIMO as part of the
liberation struggle promoted concepts related to the equality of women. With independence in
1975 these progressive attitudes towards the role of women continued including the
introduction of new laws, which attempted to enshrine protection of rights for women.
2.2.3    Economic Characteristics
i   Economy
The main economic activity for Ch6kwe District is subsistence agriculture. Ch6kwe town as a
District headquarters employs a number of civil servants as workers for the different
Government departments such as health and education. In addition, Ch6kwe is a major town
with a population of over 173,000 inhabitants. The District benefits from the economic
activity associated with a relatively large town. Better access to Chokwe District will
encourage more economic activity. Further agricultural production will be dependent upon the
rehabilitation of farming areas damaged by the floods. Irrigated rice is a major activity of the
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                          2-6



IMPAC'II
District. A return of production will require significant rehabilitation of equipment and
schemes.
ii    Poverty
The main period of poor nutrition is the period between October and March when crop stores
are at there lowest and before the new seasons harvest. The principal deficiencies in dietary
terms for Ch6kwd District are lack of vitamin A and iron leading to a high incidence of
anaemia. There is a problem related to intoxication due to the poor preparation of cassava
mainly causing problems of vomiting.
Categorizing families in terms of poor, medium, and rich a survey has indicated that within
Ch6kwe District over 75% of families are poor, 15% to 20% medium, and 0% to 10% rich.
Table 6 provides examples of the characteristics of these categories of households.

2.2.4    Services
i    Education
Ch6kwe District has 18 primary (EPI) schools and 3 upper primary (EP2) schools. Most
schools are built of locally available materials (mud and thatch) and only a small proportion
from bricks. Boy's account for the majority of the schools population. The situation becomes
more acute as pupil's progress from primary to secondary. On average in Gaza Province 75%
of pupils are boys and 25% girls. The main reasons identified for the imbalance between boys
and girls at schools related to the early age of marriage (often as young as twelve), initiation
rites, and the lack of jobs for women after schooling. On average 63.2% of the population of
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwv-Maputo Border    Februaty 2001                                   2-7



IMPA L7'O
Sofala Province are illiterate with a higher incidence of illiteracy amongst women (68.8%)
than for men (32.9%)
ii Health
Ch6kwe District has 1 rural hospital, 3 health centres and 9 health posts. The average distance
for the population to a health facility is approximately 8 kilometres. The illnesses occurring
most frequently are malaria, tuberculosis, bilharzia, intestinal parasites, acute respiratory
infections, HIV/AIDS, sexually transmitted diseases and non-specific diarrhoea. Sanitary
conditions are poor with most families not having access to a latrine.
iii   Access to Social Services and Facilitates
In Ch6kwe District there are currently approximately 74 shops, 10 trading posts, one formal
market, and 9 informal markets. Most agricultural surplus is sold in the towns of Chbkwe,
Xai-Xai City or Maputo City.
2.2.5    Livelihood
i   Livelihood Strategies
The families living along the Ch6kwe road are dependent upon subsistence agriculture. The
main food crops grown in 1999 to 2000 were maize (15,406 tons in 1999 to 2000), cassava
(576 tons in 1999 to 2000) and rice (535 tons in 1999 to 2000). Table 7 shows total cultivated
area by crop (hectares) and total production (tons/metric) for 1999/2000 for Ch6kwe District.
Livestock farming is very limited in the District. The extent of the agricultural activity is
dependent upon several factors, notably:
o fertility of the soil;
D availability of water;
o availability of good pasture;
ci availability of supportive transportation linkages to facilitate trade;
o availability of communication linkages to facilitate trade;
o population densities;
o availability of social services and amenities to support the population; and
o availability of supportive formal financial and credit institutions.
Production for the growing season 1999 to 2000 was severely effected by the floods. For
example, production of all crops was down almost 90 percent. It is likely production for 2000
to 2001 will also be significantly lower than normal years. The land is still saturated and the
concern is that above average or even normal rainfall will lead to more flooding.

ii   General characteristics of a family living along or in close proximity to the Chokwe
ER405 road
A typical day for a family living along the Ch6kwe ER405 road would involve women
working in the machambas. Women prepare the land, sow the seeds, weed, and construct
stores for the harvest. The responsibility of men is to cut wood and construct and maintain the
house. In the house women will look after the children, cook, clean the house, and would be
involved in most daily household activities including drawing water from a source often more
than one kilometre from the family house. The period of most work is between November and
February, the main period of cultivation and harvest. Women will control the household
stocks of food while men will be responsible for expenditure related to the children. The land
cultivated will belong to the married couple. In the case of the death of a spouse or divorce the
belongings will be divided between the wife and the family of the husband. Ch6kwe District
has a high rate of migration of adult males particularly seeking employment in South Africa.
As a result females in Chdkwe District take on a disproportionate level of household tasks
including those normally associated with men.
2.2.6    Archaeological and Cultural Sites
The road does not pass through or affect any specific archaeological or cultural sites. Any
work outside the road corridor would necessitate consultation with the Chef do Posto to
identify the existence of any cultural sites such as sacred forests.

3 POTENTIAL ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
3.1 IDENTIFICATION
This section provides an assessment of the potential environmental impacts associated with the
road works extending from Ch6kwe to the border with Maputo Province. Environmental
factors considered were:
o  land use change;
o  erosion and sedimentation;
o  water quality;
o  hydrology;
o  noise and vibration;
o  air quality;
o  biological effects; and
o  socio-economic effects.
Each of these issues are addressed in sections covering the physical, biological and socio-
economic environments.
The majority of direct bio-physical environmental impacts will be minor due to the limited
extent of works at any one site and the incorporation of impact mitigation measures into the
improvement works.
Indirect effects, may result from increased natural resource use due to increased local
enterprise activity to service increased traffic. These impacts are anticipated to be relatively
minor as the road is already well established along most of its length.
Many of the potential environmental impacts associated with the construction of a new road do
not occur to the same degree when a road is improved within the existing road corridor. The
most likely impacts include:
o altered land use patterns due to improved accessibility, such as a change from forestry to
agriculture, or from agriculture to industry (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997);
o  public health problems, particularly through the spread of communicable disease (Birley,
1997); and
o  increased illegal logging and poaching activities due to improved access into forest areas
(Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997).
58602 Annex E - Chokwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                          3-1



IMPAC7'O
3.2 PHYSICAL EFFECTS
3.2.1    Land Use
i   Construction
The existing road narrows for the last 6km approaching the district border. Along this stretch
there is a mix of inundated land and relatively intact woodland. The proposed road works will
require clearing of vegetation.
The proposed road works will have no direct affect on land use outside the road corridor. Tthe
construction works will be maintained within the corridor and no realignment of the road is
required.
Temporary areas of land use change will occur as a result of establishment of ancillary sites
such as batching plant sites, borrow pits and workforce camp sites. These sites will be selected
in consultation with the landowner and will be rehabilitated upon completion of activities.
Ancillary sites will be located in degraded areas, where possible, to ensure that large areas of
land will not need to be cleared and the change to land use is not significant. Total land
acquisition will be minimal relative to the project corridor length.
Where possible existing borrow areas will be utilised to avoid opening up new borrow pits and
causing disturbance. The rehabilitation of these sites after completion of the works will be
undertaken through revegetation and levelling the landform to its original condition. Where
new borrow pits are established these will be located at least 100m from the road in accordance
with current ANE guidelines.
ii   Operation
The road works may have an indirect effect on adjacent land use activities, due to an increase
in development in the region resulting from improved road access.
iii   Mitigation Measures
Due to the short length of the project road, ancillary sites such as batching plants and
workforce camps could be located nearby the township of Ch6kwe and will minimise the
disturbance of woodland or wetland habitat. All ancillary sites will be rehabilitated upon
completion of activities to their previous land use.
Mitigation measures for other temporary facilities include:
Li measures identified under socio-economics for selection and negotiation of use; and
o rehabilitation of all sites to their original condition, or better, to allow resumption of prior
land use.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kw6-Maputo Border  February 2001                               3-2



IMPAC"' 
3.2.2    Erosion and Sedimentation
i   Erosion Hazard
The dynamic interplay of several key factors dictates the degree of susceptibility to water-
driven soil erosion processes. These are:
oi seasonality and intensity of rainfall in relation to water run-off;
o landscape topography in relation slope gradients;
o soil profile parameters, notably depth and binding properties;
Li degree of vegetation cover; and
o magnitude and frequency of seismic events.
For road works in general, where the capacity of existing drainage works to manage water run-
off is exceeded then the interaction of the above factors will determine the extent and
magnitude of erosion processes. These considerations provide the basis to determine the
overall erosion hazard.
The erosion hazard along the project road is low despite the fact that it traverses alluvial soils.
These are deep soils formed on generally flat topography and therefore have a low erosion
hazard. The rainfall pattern within the region is typical of Mozambique with 80% of the
rainfall recorded between mid-November and March.
ii   Potential for Erosion and Sedimentation
Sedimentation is a secondary process of erosion and can be exacerbated by road works due to:
o deliberate and unanticipated modifications to surface and ground water hydrology;
o incomplete rehabilitation of roadside verge areas; and
o road rehabilitation through naturally hilly terrain.
Ground disturbance and the temporary stockpiling of materials during road construction works,
particularly in areas adjacent to watercourses and wetland areas may result in sedimentation.
The Sedimentation rates will be highest during the rainy season and can occur from the time of
ground disturbance up until sites have been stabilised along the length of construction works.
Overall, the low erosion hazard will be a function of the limited area of ground disturbance at
any one site, and the generally flat topography. The extent of works will also be marginal with
a general maximum width of 1 6m disturbed.
Sedimentation can occur from the time of ground disturbance until sites have been stabilised
along the length of construction works.
The fill for the road works will predominantly be obtained from existing borrows areas located
outside the area of the project road. These locations are subject to erosion if not graded and
revegetated after use is complete.
The installation of side drains along the road will minimise any sedimentation and runoff
effects.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kw6-Maputo Border  February 2001                            3-3



IMPA 'LW
iii    Mitigation Measures
The impact from sedimentation will be minimised by avoiding stockpiling of materials near
drainage lines and wetland areas and by not undertaking construction during times of heavy
rain, particularly the months of December to March.
Ground disturbance and the temporary stockpiling of materials during road construction works
has the potential for sedimentation to occur adjacent to the areas of wetland. This hazard will
occur from the time of ground disturbance up until sites have been stabilised along the length
of construction works. The fill for the road works will be obtained from existing borrow areas
located away from the project road so therefore erosion potential at these sites will be minimal.
The provision of culverts along the road will improve cross-road drainage, reducing
sedimentation effects and scouring within drainage lines. This in turn will improve the
stability of road embankments and minimise sedimentation to the waterways.
Minor reconditioning of the existing culverts will be undertaken where their discharge is
causing downstream erosion. Specific measures for erosion and sediment control include:
o  design of drainage works to ensure these collect from and discharge to natural drainage
lines. This may not always be possible for mitre drains and in those situations frequent,
low volume drains are preferred;
o  provision of energy dissipation devices where discharge velocities and flows will erode
downstream drainage lines;
o  minimisation of disturbed areas;
o  design and construction of erosion and sedimentation controls such as diversion banks,
filter structures (filter fences or windrowed vegetation) and sediment traps;
o  diversion of significant upstream flows around working areas; and
o  undertaking progressive rehabilitation and revegetation of all slopes and disturbed areas.
In addition, temporary erosion control drains will be installed prior to the wet season and no
new road excavation will be commenced during the wet season.
There are no significant slopes that the project road intersects and no significant erosion
stabilisation measures, such as retaining walls will be required on road fill or cut areas.
Establishment of vegetation will be undertaken to prevent localised rill erosion on slopes such
as batters and roadside embankments.
The provision of culverts along the road will improve cross-road drainage within the wetland
areas. If the road requires raising along the wetland areas, embankments will be vegetated to
minimise the effects of sedimentation during times of heavy rain. However, sedimentation
effects are likely to be negligible due to the flat terrain and low velocity of the runoff.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kw6-Maputo Border  February 2001                                 3-4



IMPAC' iO
3.2.3    Water Quality
i   Construction
Sedimentation and resultant water quality effects were discussed in the previous section. This
section looks at other water quality issues.
Improper sanitation and waste disposal procedures in temporary construction camps can result
in pollution to water resources unless proper management measures are implemented.
Surface waters can be contaminated by fuel and lubricants as a result of improper disposal
facilities and poor operation practices for construction vehicles and machinery. Improper
disposal of oil filters and other materials from vehicle and machinery maintenance can also
cause pollution. This can occur during both construction and road operation, but mostly during
the former.
Spilt petroleum fuel, as well as bitumen and explosives residue, also have a potential to
contribute to the pollution of water bodies, but to a very minor extent.
ii   Operation
Vehicles movements and the roadside maintenance of vehicles can result in the contamination
of water resources along the project corridor by fuel and lubricants. Oil and fuel from leaking
vehicles or from vehicle maintenance may enter rivers or streams.
However, this minor impact is occurring, and will continue, despite the proposed improvement
works. Improved road conditions will improve vehicle operating conditions and thereby
reduce such incidents. Road accidents involving accidental spills of fuel, lubricants, chemicals
or other toxic materials, which could reach water bodies, are likely to be reduced.
A direct accidental spill into a closed water body, such as pond (rather than in a flowing
watercourse), may cause a long-term and potentially irreversible impact, if this spill were to
occur. However, these are rare and random in occurrence, especially along the predominantly
agricultural road.
Borrow areas have the potential to result in adverse environmental impacts if they are not
rehabilitated correctly. If left in poor condition water can collect in the depressions created
and become weed infested and is a good breeding area for disease carrying insects such as
mosquitoes.
iii   Mitigation Measures
All fuels, lubricants and bitumen will be stored in bunded areas. Vehicle maintenance and
refuelling will be confined to areas in construction camps designed to confine lubricants and
fuel spillages.
58602 Annex E - Chokwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                               3-5



IMPAC7'V
Adequate waste disposal and sanitation facilities will be provided at construction camps. Any
wastewater or stormwater discharged from the site shall comply with the following objectives:
o  pH range                       6.0-9.0;
o  Total Suspended Solids (TSS)    <50mg/l;
o  Total Dissolved Solids (TDS)    <1 500mg/l; and
o  Oils and surfactants          No visible film of oils or petrochemicals.
The Contractor shall ensure that all bitumen and fuel storage areas shall be bunded to contain
at least 120% of the maximum capacity of the largest storage tank. The floor of the bunded
area shall be impervious, and storage facilities and bunds shall be inspected weekly for spills.
3.2.4    Hydrology
i   Construction
The road works will have a negligible impact to hydrology as the rehabilitation works will be
undertaken on the existing alignment. The main construction activity that has the potential to
impede flows will be the stockpiling of materials near drainage lines and wetland areas.
ii   Operation
The road does not cross any major watercourses. Although the road is an existing structure,
roadworks can cause an impedance to natural flows. The road will be raised along the wetland
areas and should be a nominal height of 0.5m above the surrounding terrain to ensure adequate
roadway drainage capacity. The provision of culverts along the road will improve cross-road
drainage, particularly along the wetland areas.
Adequate and stable cross-road and road surface drainage is essential to ensure that storm
runoff across the road causes minimal damage to the road and minimises the effect of
inundation caused by impeded flows. Cross-road drainage shall be provided by causeways and
culverts to pass catchment flows past the road, either over or beneath it. Road surface drainage
shall be provided by side drains to direct runoff off the road surface.
iii   Mitigation Measures
Particular attention shall be given to stockpiling materials away from existing drainage lines
and avoid filling in wetland areas.
All designed drainage works shall be installed at the time of initial road construction to ensure
that flows along drainage lines are not impeded.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                            3-6



IMPACT'0
3.2.5    Noise and Vibration
i   Construction
There are no sensitive sites, such as hospitals or schools, located along the project road.
Additional noise will be generated from the increased number of heavy vehicles during the
construction period, however due to the absence of settlements adjacent to the road, the impact
will be negligible. The increase in noise levels may provide a disturbance to the local bird life,
however this will be a short-term impact experienced during the construction phase. Peak
noise levels generated by construction activities are expected to be greater than the peak noise
levels from road operation
Vibration effects will be negligible due to the nature of the soil substrate and absence of
settlements. .
ii Operation
Accessibility will be improved following the road works but improvements are not expected to
generate additional traffic. Traffic growth is expected to low, and traffic noise levels will not
increase as a result of the project.
The project road is sparsely populated, and no sensitive sites are located nearby. As the
majority of the project roads traverse open cultivation land and wetland areas, the impact from
an increase in noise levels from increased traffic speed will be negligible.
iii Mitigation Measures
No national noise level standards exist for highway construction equipment, however the
World Bank recommends the use of construction equipment, which meets international
standards.
The following measures shall be implemented to minimise the impact of noise during the
construction phase:
o work compounds shall be located at least 0.5km from settlements;
o construction shall not occur between 19:00 and 06:00 hours at sites within 150m of
residential areas; and
o maintenance of machinery and vehicles shall be undertaken and repaired regularly to keep
noise at a minimum.
3.2.6    Air Quality
Construction
The deterioration in air quality from dust generation and vehicle emissions during road
construction will be a relatively minor environmental impact. Significant adverse effects are
58602 Annex E - Ch6kw6-Maputo Border  Februaty 2001                               3-7



IMPAU'IO
not expected because of the limited duration of construction at any one site and the few
receptors. Most of the work will occur in open, cultivated areas.
Deterioration in air quality during road construction will primarily occur from dust generated
by road works, spoil transportation and disposal. Most of the construction activities will
progressively move along the alignment, so prolonged deterioration in air quality will not
occur at any one site.
ii   Operation
Existing air quality is good and no significant air quality effects are expected during operation.
Traffic volumes are low and the road generally traverses rural road areas.
iii   Mitigation Measures
Minimising air emissions is particularly important during the construction stage, for activities
such as the operation of borrow pits and in-situ mixing of cement and sand, which have a
potential to generate high dust levels.
During the construction period, the following mitigation measures to suppress dust will be
implemented:
o  Water will be sprayed during the construction period at earth mixing sites, asphalt-mixing
sites, and on cleared surfaces. Water spraying will be undertaken during periods of high
wind, or when necessary, to suppress dust.
  Operations shall be suspended during periods of dry windy weather, in areas where wind
blown dust is causing a nuisance in settlements or towns.
L  All equipment and plant machinery shall not emit visible smoke for a continuous period of
greater than 10 seconds.
L  All exhaust systems and engines on plant and equipment shall be in accordance with the
manufacturers specifications and kept well maintained to ensure that they have a high level
operating efficiency.
3.3 BIOLOGICAL EFFECTS
3.3.1    Direct impacts
The proposed construction works will not affect ecological resources for the first 20km of the
road as the existing road corridor and side drains are currently of sufficient width. Along this
stretch of road the works required will be periodic maintenance.
There will be a direct loss of vegetation along the area where the road and traverses inundated
areas and dense woodland. The dense shrubland, approximately 90% vegetation cover, is most
likely due to the absence of large grazing fauna in the area. At this location the road corridor
will need to be cleared of the vegetation as part of the construction works to enable road
formation widening and construction of side drainage. The width of additional clearance
required for these works will generally be 6-lOm.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kw6-Maputo Border  February 2001                            3-8



IMPAC1VO
Although the absence of settlements has resulted in the absence of slash and burn agriculture
adjacent to road, is a lack of large mammals. The conservation value of the woodland for
small mammals, birds and reptiles is not known. However the road currently exists and the
level of disturbance required will be minimal in comparison to the road length. There are no
neighbouring conservation areas within the vicinity of the woodland and no known locations
for wildlife crossings.
The wetlands adjacent to the road appear to have expanded due to long periods of inundation
and along the last 6km, wetland species have established in low lying sections of the road
where access has been restricted. The wetlands in this area have not been identified as having
conservation significance and may recede again overtime when the groundwater levels
decrease, however they support a wide variety of birds and amphibians. Although the
construction works will impact on a marginal area of this habitat, the placement of culverts
will be required to ensure the road does not create a barrier and impede drainage.
3.3.2   Indirect Impacts
There is a potential impact to the ecology associated the indirect loss and degradation of
vegetation, habitat and species in areas adjoining the road.
These potential impacts are associated with:
oi improved access;
oL in-migration; and
o construction workforce behaviour.
The improvement of the existing road will provide improved access to the neighbouring areas,
thereby enabling easier natural resource access and removal. In addition, the improved road
may promote service industries along their corridors, resulting in a population increase in these
areas and an increased local demand for natural resources to service the new businesses and for
personal use. The main sites where increased natural resource harvesting is likely to occur are
from the woodland areas.
The population increase along the road corridor would be derived either from people migrating
to the roadside from the immediate area (i.e. the 5km region of influence and adjacent to this),
or from other areas. This in-migration has a potential to create an increased local demand for
fuelwood and construction timber, as well as other woodland products to support secondary
enterprise activities, such as forage collection for grazing animals. Hunting may also increase.
The proposed road improvements will follow an existing alignment and almost all woodland
areas neighbouring this road are currently accessible. The road will only increase potential
access to these areas, not open them up.
The extent of a road's region of influence on increased slash and burn harvesting and wildlife
poaching cannot be definitively bounded. These issues are of concern within the area, but in-
migration from the improved access is not likely to cause a significant increase in these
activities. The area of increased woodland resource use will be largely determined by access
and product demand, the ease of harvest and transport of resources to the roadside and to the
market, and the creation of additional resource demands along the roadside. Ecological
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                          3-9



IMPA  'lO
degradation in areas adjoining the road is likely to be greatest near the road and is likely to
continue to occur over an extended period of time due to the extended use of woodland
resources by local communities.
3.3.3    Mitigation Measures
i   Direct Impacts
Vegetation clearance along the road edge and at ancillary sites (such as borrow pits) will be
minimised. All construction areas will be clearly marked and construction activity prohibited
outside the defined areas. Tress within ancillary sites shall be retained wherever possible.
Retention of trees within areas such as worker camps will improve the camp amenity and
provide shade.
All temporarily disturbed areas will be rehabilitated following construction. The establishment
of ground cover is the initial priority of revegetation. Ground cover reduces raindrop impact,
binds the soil surface and creates a microclimate favourable for the establishment of other
vegetation. Species should be selected for cover crops and permanent revegetation. A cover
crop consisting of a rapid growing species and several vigorous grass species should be used to
rapidly establish ground cover on disturbed areas. Permanent revegetation species should be
selected to be consistent with the local native species in the area.
Revegetation will aim to stabilise the road verges and assist the many desirable secondary uses
of the road including:
L production of forage, wood, flowers, fruit, medicinal items;
i shade and shelter;
Li fencing and screens; and
a building supports.
Revegetation along different sections of road will be designed to achieve site-specific aims,
such as maximising shade within villages and providing a species composition that is
compatible with surrounding vegetation cover.
Adequate and stable cross-road and road surface drainage is essential to ensure that the effect
of inundation caused by impeded flows is minimised. There are areas of inundation and
wetland areas along the road. These areas have experienced a change in habitat regime due to
prolonged inundation from last year's floods. In these areas, the road may form a barrier and
divides the wetlands into two separate hydrologic bodies.
It is recommended that a wetland ecologist inspect the route to determine the need for
additional culverts or waterway structures to ensure connection between wetland areas on
either side of the road.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kw6-Maputo Border  February 2001                              3-10



IMPAC'IO
ii  Indirect Impacts
The most potential for indirect effects was identified to be associated with workforce camps.
This is a temporary effect but may last several years depending on the duration of the works
project. To reduce the potential for increased exploitation of natural resources the following
migration measures will be implemented. The Contractor will:
o provide and maintain adequate drinking and bathing water supplies;
i provide kerosene or other fuel source and stoves for worker use;
o educate workers about the responsible use of natural resources; and
o ensure workers act in a responsible manner and apply penalties for the harvesting of local
resources (wood, wildlife or wood products).
3.4 SOCIO-ECONOMIC EFFECTS
3.4.1    Direct impacts
i   Loss of agricultural land, buildings, sites of cultural value, population displacement,
and natural resources degradation
Improvements to the road will not involve the significant loss of land although minor rerouting
is likely to be needed due to recent flooding. No buildings will be lost to road improvements.
There will not be any displacement of population due to the road improvements.
Where an area of cultivated land has to be resumed for road construction, the farmer will be
compensated for loss of production. The following process will be applied:
L where possible the farmer should be notified of the loss of land one season in advance.
This is to avoid unnecessary loss of effort and seed; and
o  the value of the lost production should be determined. Compensation will be paid to the
farmer for the value of the lost production.
Borrow pits, storage and equipment areas, and construction camps will be located on marginal
or disturbed land. The acquiring of land for such purposes must be subject to ANE and
Provincial approval. In selecting land consultation must also be undertaken with local
communities and their traditional leaders as well as local authorities (at District and Ward
level).
The Ch6kwe road passes through an area of degraded miombo woodland. Improved access
may expose this degraded woodland to more depletion particularly in terms of production of
charcoal. This is discussed under in-migration in indirect impacts.
58602 Annex E - Chdkwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                         3-11



IMPA L'U
3.4.2   Indirect impacts
i   Access to services
Improvement of the E405 road is likely to have some socio-economic impacts in terms of
access to services. Availability of services to the populations of Ch6kwe District is relatively
good by the general standards of rural areas in Mozambique. An improved road would enable
for example sick patients to gain access to better health services.
ii   Improved access to markets
Poor communications has already been cited as a major problem, which perhaps most
importantly constricts the ability of the local populations to transport surplus agricultural
production to market. Similarly improvements to the road would enable goods and services to
flow more easily into the area particularly those associated with improving agricultural
production techniques and yields.
iii   In-migration of people
Improvement of roads in other rural areas within Mozambique leads often to increases in in-
migration as advantage is taken of the improved communication. It is probable that some in-
migration will take place particularly in areas in close proximity to Ch6kwe town.
Under existing Mozambican legislation all land belongs to the State. However, private sector
farmers may acquire long-term rights over land by obtaining a Title of Use and Exploitation.
Individuals may also seek concession rights over tracts of land to harvest timber.
Requests for "concessions" are lodged with an area's Provincial Agricultural Services. Once
access to hitherto isolated areas is improved following rehabilitation of a road it could be
expected that concession seekers will attempt to access these lands.
Although, at present, all land in Mozambique belongs to the State the defacto situation is that
land is being managed and allocated according to traditional customary land rights. The
potential for conflict over land between local communities and "concession seekers" is high.
Very few traditional farmers have formalized their rights to the land under existing legislation.
Demand for concessions may increase as a result of improved access. To assist traditional
landholders in future ownership debates, the existing traditional land "ownership" should be
mapped within the roads region of influence (taken to be five kilometres either side of the
road). This should be undertaken at the road design phase to reduce the later potential for
conflict between traditional landowners and concession seekers.
iv    Health issues
It is likely that the improvement of the road will necessitate the inflow of a number of
construction workers from other parts of Mozambique. The inflow of road gangs, particularly
into remote areas, has a potential for adverse effects. The most often cited problem is the one
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                           3-12



IMPACLU
of HIV-AIDs and other sexually transmitted diseases. Mitigation measures against these likely
impacts require working closely with respective government departments; local NGOs and/or
church based organisations, local communities, and road gangs. Educational programmes for
the local populations are considered to be extremely important in trying to reduce the
likelihood of HIV-AIDs infection.
v    Construction worker camps
The health issues related to construction worker camps particularly in terms of the spread of
HIV-AIDs and other sexually transmitted diseases have been mentioned above.  A
concentration of workers also attracts other inward migrants who attempt to exploit new
market opportunities. Such informal sites are often poorly managed in terms of adherence to
health regulations and waste disposal. Mitigation measures should include the identification of
suitable sites for the establishment of small shops and other facilities servicing road-gangs.
In conclusion road improvements to the ER405 will have positive impacts. Improved
communication would improve the ability of local populations to transport surplus agricultural
production to market. It would also likely to stimulate more production if farmers knew they
had a means of transporting their goods. Ch6kwe town would be a significant beneficiary of
road improvements to the ENR405 road. The main negative socio-economic impacts relate to
the problem of the spread of HIV-AIDs infection. Campaigns in other parts of Mozambique
have demonstrated that these problems can be mitigated against particularly if resources and
education are directed at the local population and not just the influx of construction workers.
Further studies are needed to better predict probable socio-economic impacts, which would
occur as a result of improvements to the ER405 road.
58602 Annex E - Ch6kwe-Maputo Border  February 2001                         3-13






IMPACL 0
4 UPGRADING AND MAINTENANCE MEASURES
The road works will include environmental impact mitigation measures within the Contract
Specifications. These measures will be incorporated as specific instructions and
recommendations to the Contractor and will be provided in a separate Environmental
Management Plan (EMP). The EMP will identify specific instructors to the Contractor and
will include responsibilities, outputs, costs and timing of mitigation measures.
Design, construction and maintenance mitigation recommendations for the proposed roads
have been formulated to avoid or mitigate potential bio-physical impacts.   These
recommendations were primarily formulated from observations made during the inspection of
constructed roads.
The EMP describes the practical means to ensure effective implementation of the mitigation
measures recommended for the potential adverse environmental impacts identified in the EIA.
The EMP will include but will not be limited to the following elements:
u practical means to implement the recommended mitigation measures;
u implementation of a schedule and supervision programme;
u monitoring and evaluation;
Li institutional strengthening, management and training; and
u further studies of environmental issues.
In addition, the program also specifies what action will be taken and by whom in the event that
the proposed mitigation measures fail, either partially or totally, to achieve the level of
environmental protection expected.
An outline of the EMP for the construction and operation stage is provided in Part E of this
report.

Appendix A Vegetation and Wildlife



IMPAC TO
1 BIOLOGICAL & PHYSICAL ENVIRONMENTS
1.1 INTRODUCTION
The long stretch of the ENI follows the coastal plain of southern Mozambique for a distance of
950km. Baseline descriptions of biophysical parameters are provided for its entire length,
mainly in the form of distribution maps (geology, soils, vegetation) and histograms (rainfall,
temperature, humidity). For the purpose of describing the salient biological and physical
features of the road was divided into four sections from south to north:
o Maputo to Xai Xai;
o Xai Xai to Inhambane;
o Inhambane to the Save River; and
o Muxungue to Inchope.
1.2 TOPOGRAPHY AND LANDFORMS
1.2.1    Maputo to Xai Xai
The ENI passes through many peripheral residential and semi-industrial neighborhoods of
Maputo City before it enters a gently undulating plain on the outskirts of Marracuene on the
banks of the Inkomati River.
The coastal plain north and south of the Maputo Estuary is relatively flat. However, ridges
rising between 1 20m and 200m are located inland. The Matola River, a fairly old, shallow and
wide river (average 15m deep and 1.5km wide) drains into the Maputo Bay (Enpro, 1998).
The estuary is bordered by mangrove of varying height followed by a mosaic of reed swamps
and halophytic vegetation and in more elevated sites, dune vegetation with reed beds (Impacto,
1998).
The major landscape feature is the Inkomati River valley. Along this section the road traverses
the mature river valley close to the river mouth. In this area there are extensive wetlands
associated with the river. Between the Inkomati and Limpopo River valleys, the road traverses
a flat to slightly undulating landscape of secondary woodland and small-scale farms.
The road limit is adjacent to the wetland system of the Espirito Santo Estuary. Two rivers
drain into the Espirito Santo Estuary, namely the Umbeluzi and Infulene Rivers. It is an area
characterised by the following habitat types: mangrove, mosaic of swamps and grassland
interspersed with farmed areas.
1.2.2    Xai Xai to Inhambane
The Xai-Xai - Inhambane section of the EN1 traverses the low-lying coastal plains of southern
Mozambique and rarely does the road pass over elevations greater than 100m above sea level
(asl). The Limpopo Valley dominates the landscape on the initial southern stretch. The coastline is
58602 Annex E- EN Road Projects   February 2001                              1-1



IMPAC LW
characterised by a series of high (parabolic) dunes generally parallel to the coast, which normally
attain their highest elevations just inland of the high water mark. Behind these dunes occur
depressed areas with coastal barrier lakes and related dunes.
1.2.3   Inhambane to the Save River
Along this section of road, the topography is uniformly flat and low-lying, never exceeding
1OOm asl. The coast is characterised by northward pointing sand spits, which shelter a calm
mangrove-fringed bay.
The road runs adjacent to the Govuro River for approximately 90km. The river is entirely
coastal and the road is located about 5km to the east resulting in a flat topography and low
lying areas subject to inundation with seasonal and perrnanent wetland habitats.
1.2.4    Muxungue to Inchope
The Muxungue - Inchope section of the ENI traverses undulating to hilly terrain. The road gently
inclines to a landscape of small Mountains and rocky whale back outcrops along the last 1 Okm of
the road section as it approaching Inchope.
This landscape is characterised by spectacular granitic outcrops formed by the Rift Valley as a
result of tectonic movement and uplift.
1.3 CLIMATE
The southern coastal region has a tropical humid to sub-humid climate, with a distinct winter
dry season. Within the coastal zone rain typically falls in every month of the year with the
maximum during the summer months. Rainfall patterns are erratic and unreliable, with
substantial annual variations, especially in southern and central districts. Climatic data were
obtained from four weather stations to ensure that latitudinal variation in climate along this
long stretch of road is adequately characterised. The stations are:
o Maputo (Maputo to Xai Xai);
o Macia (Xai Xai to Inhambane);
o Inharrime (Inhambane to the Save River); and
o Mambone (Muxungue to Inchope).
For the climate variables temperature, rainfall and relative humidity, five years of the most
recent data are summarised. Figure 1 displays mean monthly ambient temperatures for the
four weather stations.

Throughout the year mean monthly temperatures vary between 19 and 27C and do not vary
significantly with latitude. Mean temperatures rarely drop below 20C. There is a slight
seasonality with a cool, dry season from May to September and a warm, wet season from
October to April.

Despite the distinct seasonality in rainfall, relative humidity remains more or less constant
throughout the year. The dry season extends from April to September and the wet season for
October to March. The rainfall pattern is broadly comparable for the four stations and appears
to be independent of latitude.
The distribution of prevailing winds is presented in Figure 4. Unlike rainfall and temperature,
prevailing winds change with changing latitude. In the south and central regions, prevailing
winds are southerlies, while in the north they are predominantly from the east (E and NE).

Over the period 1999-2000, two cyclones affected this coastal area of southern Mozambique
(Instituto Nacional de Meteorologia) causing flooding, damage to infrastructures, and
disruption of services.
1.4 GEOLOGY
The coastal plain of Mozambique is composed of unconsolidated, but compacted, Quaternary
to recent sediments, mostly of sand, interspersed with silt and clay at the larger river mouths.
The regional geology of the southern part of the Mozambique coast comprises a succession of
non-marine, shallow marine, marine and estuarine sediments ranging in age from Cretaceous
(145 million years) to Holocene (recent age).
Between Maputo and Inchope the ENI passes over several geological formations, as described
in Table 1.
A map depicting the major geological features of the area traversed by this section of road is
given in Figures 5 and 6.

Table 1:      Geological formations found along the ENI from Maputo to Inchope
Code   Dominant Characteristics                            Rock Group        Period
QAI    Alluvium, Colluvium,                                Sedimentary       Quaternary
QD      Dune formation

schists, quartzites, crystalline calcariums).                        Primary
The road passes through the Great Coastal Plains, an area with heights less than 200m,
resulting from the Congo Cycle, which probably began in the Plio-Pleistocene (Afonso et al.
1998). The area contains sediments of the Tertiary and Quatemary, located South of the Save
River and along the coast.
Formations of the Quaternary are constituted by sediments deposited as a consequence of the
rise and fall of the sea level, an example being the accumulation of heavy sands along the coast
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects       February 2001                                   1-7



IMPAC7 O
on beaches and dunes. This accumulation occurs at depths of between 2 - 8m and gives rise to
mineral fields of economic importance such as the Marracuene, Limpopo, Xai-Xai, Quissico,
Zavora, Jangamo and Inhassoro heavy sand fields (Afonso et al, 1998).
Marine sediments deposited in the clear waters of the infra-littoral environment mark the
Tertiary period. This sequence, on average 200m deep is characterised by calcareous Gres
grouped in the Jofane Formation. They have high economic importance being an important
source of raw materials including plaster and calcium for white wash and cements (Afonso et
all, 1998).
The ENI also traverses volcanic basalt formations of the secondary period, that fall within the
Karoo Supergroup - Stormberg Series (Superior Karoo). The Karoo geological formations are
important as they house coal and bentonite amongst others (Afonso et all, 1998).
In the region of influence (ROI) of the EN 1 project road, geological formations of the Pre-
Cambrian are found in Manica and parts of Sofala Province. Younger rock formations occur
in western Mozambique comprising granitic and migmatic gneisses, metamorphic rocks, which
belong to the Barue Formation (Hatton & Munguambe, 1998).
The region north of Inhambane is located in the sedimentary basin of Mozambique. The age of
underlying geological formations decreases from west (Late Tertiary) to east (Late
Quaternary). The entire region is bisected by a series of major gedlogical faults.
Four main geological formations along the ENI project road are:
u  Alluvium deposits (QAI) of recent origin occur as a wide expanse along the Limpopo
Valley;
  Coastal dune formations (QDC) of recent origin run parallel to the coast with varying
width;
n  The formation of interior dunes (QD1) of Pleistocene origin covers a large area inland
from the dunes, north and south of the Limpopo River; and
u  Marine sandstones outcropping around Lake Inharrime (TTm) - the Inharrime formation.
1.5 SOILS
Underlying geology and geomorphology tends to be a major determinant of the evolution of
soil types and their fertility.
A description of the soil types traversed by the ENI between Maputo and Inchope are shown
in Figures 7 and 8 and described in Table 2.
The main soil types include:
o  Alluvial deposits (unit F);
o  Sedimentary basin soils - soils of coastal dunes, sandy soils, Mananga sediments,
sedimentary rocks (units A, G, M, S, W);
o  Volcanic rock zone (unit B); and
o  Pre-Cambrian socle (unit K).
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                             1-8



IMPAC LO
In the south, the basin of the Inkomati River is the major landscape feature, and can be
characterised as an extensive erosion plain gently dipping towards the coast and intercepted by
the valleys of the Inkomati, Sabie and Elefantes and by small rivers. The Inkomati has built an
extensive alluvial plain, inland from the coastal dune belt. Alluvial soils cover large areas in
the Inkomati, Sabie and their tributary valleys. The largest continuous alluvial deposits occur
in the Middle and Lower Inkomati, particularly south of Magude and in the Upper Inkomati
along the Sabie River. South of Manhica, the Inkomati drains an extensive floodplain,
periodically flooded, extending to the coast near Marracuene, north of Maputo.
North of Xai Xai the soils are closely related to geomorphological history, current landform, and
rainfall. In addition, there is a marked interrelationship between soils and vegetation types.
Most of the soils of the region are derived from recent aeolian, marine and fluviatile materials -
the larger proportion comprising sandy materials. Heavier textured soils occur as alluvial
deposits on the depositional plains of the Limpopo River.
Coastal dune soils (haplic arenosols) yellow to white in hue and low in organic matter occur along
the coast (DC). The soils in the eastern part of the interior dunes are mainly ferralic arenosols (dA)
yellow in hue with low to medium levels of organic matter. In the vicinity of Maxixe, orange to
reddish soil with a higher nutrient content occur (dAj). These soils are also classified as ferralic
arenosols. In depressions in the dunes where the water table may approach the surface,
hydromorphic sandy soils (Ah) occur. These latter areas occur scattered throughout this region.
The Limpopo Valley comprises alluvial soils (F) of which scattered patches are sodic or saline
(Fs/Fz). Alluvial peaty soils occur on the outer margins of the Limpopo floodplain at the foot of
the dune cordon.
North of Inhambane most of the soil types are of medium to low fertility. The only exceptions
are the soils of the Govuro River valley, which are fertile clayey and sandy soils. The western
region is dominated by low fertility sandy soils on underlying sandstones. Up to the Govuro
River, medium fertility deep red clayey soils predominate on underlying sandstone
conglomerates. Along the coastal zone to the east of the Govuro River, low fertility sandy soils
on underlying dunes, interior dunes and alluviums predominate.

1.5.1    Erosion Risk
The existing information relating to the levels of erosion in Mozambique is fragmented and
lacking in detail. Generally, erosion caused by water occurs throughout the project area
although the problem is confined to specific areas such as towns and villages and the costal
areas. Erosion along roads is a problem requiring urgent attention (van Dijk, 1997).
Criteria for determining erosion risk with regard to road construction, rehabilitation and
maintenance (M. R. Marques, INIA pers. comm., November 2000) are presented in Table 3.
Table 3: Criteria for determining soil erosion risk
Property                          Risk                    Feature
