Based on the soil types identified and described above, and in relation to slope and depth, the
soils along the road show low and medium erosion risk. A further description of erosion
hazard along the EN I is provided later.
1.6 LAND USE
Land use adjacent to the road was described from field inspections and the following maps:
o  1:250,000 forest cover, based on landsat satellite imagery and 1:40,000 aerial
photographs from 1990-1992;
o  1:250,000 land-use, based on landsat satellite imagery captured in April 1998;
o  1:250,000 Topographic, prepared and published in 1973; and
o  1: 2,500,000 vegetation map of the Flora Zambesiaca area (Wild and Barbosa 1968).
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects   February 2001                              1-14



IMPAL' (
1.6.1    Maputo to Xai Xai
Much of the initial stretch of this section (Maputo to Maccaretane) passes through a built-up
environment.
The Maputo area is characterised by high population density characterised by rapid
expansion of both residential and semi-industrial peri-urban areas.
As the road leaves the outskirts of Maputo, land occupation indicates that the demarcated
plots along the road are recent concessions. These are being developed for habitation
purposes and in some cases also for agricultural purposes. No large-scale farming occurs
between Maputo and Marracuene.
From Marracuene to Manhica, a significant drop in population density is noticeable, a change
that is reflected in the land use and land occupation patterns.  These areas are rural
environments with cultivated land and fallow areas with signs of bush encroachment. Prior
to Mozambique's independence in 1975, large plantations such as cashew nut plantations
occupied some of these areas. At present, the areas are mainly cultivated by the family sector
who practise subsistence agriculture.
The road limit is adjacent to a wetland system which is characterised by mangrove and
grassland interspersed with farm areas.
1.6.2    Xai Xai to Inhambane
Much of the woodland habitat, especially adjacent to the road, has been to converted to small-
scale farms and associated cashew and coconut plantations. Secondary woodland becomes more
prevalent moving away from the road.
The rural communities are wholly dependent on firewood for cooking and heating. Many
families residing adjacent to the road harvest firewood and make charcoal for sale to passing
motorists/lorry drivers to supplement their income. The most important being Brachystegia sp.
('tambeya') but other commonly used species include:
Acacia sp.                 'mikaye'
Albizia versicolor         Ivanganze'
Afzelia quanzensis          mstena'
Combretum sp.              Ichicucudze'
Dialium schleteri         'inziva'
Strychnos spinosa          massala'
Syzygium cordatum          'muhlu'
Terminalia sericea        'conola'
Similarly the interior dunes are an anthropogenic landscape of small-scale farms and patches of
coastal woodland.
Two basic agricultural systems, corresponding to each of the two broad geomorphological units
the road traverses, can be distinguished:
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              1-15



IMPAL'i()
i)    Small-scale (peasant) slash and burn farming practised on the coastal sandy soils.
ii)    More intensive farming (large-scale private sector, joint venture schemes and family
sector) practised on the fertile alluvial plains of the Limpopo Valley.
i   Small-Scale Farming
Following the signing of the Peace Accord (October 1992) and the successful holding of
elections (October 1994), many peasant farmers have returned to their original homesteads.
Newly cleared farms have been established along most of the country's road systems with fields,
or machambas, often within 3-1 Om of the road corridor.
Small-scale farms are concentrated in the peri-urban area around Xai-Xai, nearby villages, and
between Lindela and Maxixe. Farms are derived from secondary fallow (as little primary
woodland remains) of varying ages. The vegetation is slashed/felled in June/July and burnt in
September/October prior to the rainy season. Maize (Zea mays) intercropped with groundnut
(Arachis hypogaea), are the principal crops although cassava (Manihot esculentus), beans
(Phaseolus spp.) and a variety of horticultural crops are also planted. Irrigation is rain-fed.
Produce is mainly for home consumption (subsistence) although excess may be sold to
supplement the household income.
Coconut (Cocos nucifera) plantations occur along most of the road between Xai-Xai and
Inhambane. These are especially extensive between Lindela and Maxixe. Tangerine and orange
orchards, mainly belonging to the family sector, are also common in the Lindela-Maxixe area.
Most small-scale farms have cashew (Anacardium occidentalis) and mango (Mangifera indica)
trees. Cashew orchards are particularly extensive in the Xai-Xai area but these have not been
well maintained and productivity is reported to be low.
Besides the introduced exotic species such as cashew and mango, peasant farmers maintain
several indigenous fruit trees on their plots of land as well as harvest fruits from the surrounding
woodlands. These fruits may contribute significantly to the diet of the local population, and
many species are used to prepare alcoholic beverages ('tontonto').
Farmers rear small livestock such as goats, chickens and ducks. Only a few peasant farmers
presently own cattle as most farmers lost their cattle during the civil unrest. Traditionally cattle
are important as draft power.
Families living on the dune cordon adjacent to the Limpopo valley floodplain grow a variety of
crops on the dunes.
ii   Intensive Farming, Limpopo Valley
Large tracts of the fertile alluvial plains are under intensive agriculture especially around
Ch6kwe and Mohambe upstream of Xai-Xai. Farming is less intensive in the lower Limpopo
Valley (in the vicinity Xai-Xai) due to the poor state of the irrigation system (Sistema de
Regadio de Baixa Limpopo) and partial localised salinisation of soils.
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects    February 2001                              1-16



IMPACT'O
Where the plains are not under crop production, the extensive grasslands provide suitable
forage for herds of cattle.
The three main farming sectors practicing agriculture on the alluvial plains include Joint
Venture Schemes, Large-scale private sector, and Family sector.
Most of the co-operative schemes have been disbanded or transformed into family sector
farms. A wide variety of cash and horticultural crops are grown including cotton, rice, maize,
beans, cabbages, tomatoes, onions and garlic. Some farmers pump water from the Limpopo
River directly to their farms, and in some cases this has resulted in localised salinisation of
soils.
The local communities, especially those farming on the sandy soils, depend upon a variety of
natural resources derived from the woodlands and other habitats for their livelihood e.g.
firewood, fruit, medicinal plants, honey and wildlife.
1.6.3    Inhambane to the Save River
The main agricultural system is small-scale (subsistence) slash and burn farming practised on
the coastal sandy soils. More intensive farming occurs on scattered wetlands characterised by
hydromorphic soils.
While the general land use profile is predominantly undeveloped and agricultural, there are
variations within the region brought about by the influence of a range of other factors, which
include the availability of transportation linkages, the availability of potable water and the
suitability of the various areas for agricultural purposes (soil aptitude, climate, water supply
etc).
The agricultural activities of coastal Districts are characterised by a greater degree of trade
while based on much the same crops, but also include cotton, cashews, and rice as cash crops.
Fruit is an important dietary supplement throughout the region. Wildlife is still present and
game meat is an important food source to the populations of these Districts. Wood is used
for firewood, building materials and charcoal production and this has led to deforestation and
erosion. Livestock farming is very limited.
1.6.4    Muxungue to Inchope
The area is characterized by the Miombo forest (i.e. a low altitude forest), and small
cultivated fields. The forest comprises of species such as Brachystegia spiciformis and
Julbernardia globiflora.
Maize and cassava constitute the dominant crops being cultivated, but fruit trees, such as
paw-paw and cashew trees (cashew plantations reach an average of six trees per hectare) are
also present bordering most of the homesteads.
Settlement adjacent to the road is scattered and the population density in the area has
increased, resulting in more areas being cleared for agricultural purposes. The clearing of
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects      February 2001                                1-17



IMPAL 1U
vegetation has reached sizes between 0.5 to 4ha, and has resulted in large areas of degraded
woodland.
People from the area make use of the road to sell coal, sticks/wood and firewood and game
meat (antelope and cane rats).
1.7 RIVERS AND WATER QUALITY
1.7.1    Coastal Barrier Lakes
A characteristic feature of the southern Mozambique coastal region is the extensive coastal
lakes, swamps and temporary rain-filled pans, which occur behind the parabolic dune
systems. Hill (1975) postulated that the Mozambican coastal plain is Pliocene in age and was
cut by a marine transgression. In the succeeding regression the sea left a series of longshore
dunes as sandy ridges running parallel to the present shore.
The present coastal longshore dune is backed over long distances by narrow coastal lakes
behind which are a second series of longshore dunes.
The most important coastal lakes from north (Inhambane) to south (Ponta do Ouro) are:
Dongane, Poelela, Maiene, Massava, Quissico, Nhambavale (2100ha), Uembje/Bilene
(3200ha), Muandje (2700ha), Pati (1850ha), Maundo, Xingute (1 150ha), Piti (2700ha) and
Satine/Zitundo (SOOha). These lakes are located on a plain of low elevation and are separated
from the sea by a well-developed longshore dune system except for Lake Bilene, which has
occasional links to the sea.
1.7.2    Rivers
From south to north, the ENI project road runs parallel to the Govuro River and crosses over
four major rivers:
Li Inkomati;
o Limpopo;
o Inharrime; and
Li Save.
The salient features of these rivers and their catchment areas are briefly described below.
i   The Inkomati System
The Inkomati has a catchment area of 45,875km2 and formed by the confluence of the
Inkomati and Sabie River at the town of Sabie (2519'S/3216'E), above which, in
Mozambique, both tributary streams have narrow floodplains. Below Sabie the river flows
north and east, again across a narrow floodplain, until it swings south and enters a great
marshy area, which develops below the confluence of many streams, some 13km north of
Manhica (2523'S/325 VE). This region comprises lakes, notably Lake Chuali, and
permanent swamps as well as seasonal floodplain areas. To the north of this swamp, between
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                            1-18



IMPACI'TO
the courses of the River Mazimechopes (a tributary) and the Limpopo, there is a further block
of swampland which covers almost 50,000ha.
ii   The Limpopo System
The Limpopo is the second largest river in Mozambique with a catchment of more than
390,000km. It drains parts of Botswana, South Africa and Zimbabwe before reaching
Mozambique. Even so, flows are extremely variable and it is often dry over long sections
during the winter. It loses 185m in altitude along the 561km of its course through
Mozambique, thus descending to the sea with a mean gradient of only 1:3030. Having
entered Mozambique its upper course lies in a narrow and steep-sided valley, scarcely 7km
wide in places, until at Mepuze (2308'S/3215'E) the valley widens and the river flows over
plateau country to its confluence with the Elefantes (Elephants) River at Quenelle
(2407'S/3238'E). For some 150km above the confluence a narrow tongue of higher land,
carrying a plateau at an altitude close to 200m, intrudes between the two rivers. The end of
this is covered by hundreds of seasonal pans, extending over an area 75km long and up to
15km wide.
Prior to the confluence, are swamps which are fossil courses of the Limpopo River. These
two strip-like depressions, which are 1-2km wide, still collect water during the rains. Both
depressions and the riverine floodplain extend for unbroken distances of 20-40km, and, in
this region, there are approximately 17,000ha of semi-permanent swamps.
Below Queguele the Limpopo Floodplain, which is still comparatively narrow, continues
until the river flow is checked by a barrage, the Barragem de Maccaretane, just above
Ch6kwe (24-31lS/33O00'E). Below this point floodwaters are controlled to some extent, but
the floodplain widens as the river approaches its confluence with the Changane near Chibuto
(2440'S/3333'E). Thereafter it meanders over a broad swampy floodplain to the coast where
it flows into a lagoon, 8km long and 1.6km wide, before breaching the scrub covered dunes
in a channel some 200m wide.
Old levees and river channels cover the entire lower floodplain; the abandoned channels
frequently giving rise to stagnant lakes and ponds. These are especially common to the west
of the river, reflecting the fact that the main stream has been shifting eastwards for the last
several thousand years. To the east, surrounded by swampland and isolated from the river, is
Lake Inhamisse, with an open water surface of 750ha. Fossilised oyster beds, at
comparatively shallow depths in parts of the lower floodplain, indicate that marine conditions
prevailed in the region in Recent times. Drainage throughout this area is diffuse in the rainy
season, but in the winter it is concentrated on the main river channel. The river is tidal for
many kilometres and saline water penetrates well into the lower swamps when winter tides
are high. In 1957 the swamps spread for at least 8km on either side of the main channel, and
the river was navigable for 57km. Today there may be 40,000-60,000ha of swamp and
floodplain below Ch6kwe.
iii   The Inharrime System
The interior of Inhambane Province, south of latitude 22S, has a diffuse drainage system.
The area north of Funhalouro (2305'S/3423'E) is characterised by innumerable endorheic
swamplets and seasonal pans, but in the south, in addition to many little endorheic systems,
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                            1-9



IMPAL' 0
there are swampy seasonal drainage lines leading eventually to the Inharrime River System.
From here drainage is to Poelela Lagoon and thereafter, at least theoretically, to the Indian
Ocean.
A conspicuous seasonal water-course/swamp/lake system, about 1 km wide, runs
approximately due south along longitude 3424'E, from latitude 2345'-2359'S where it is
lost in the great expanse of swampland surrounding Lake Nhangulaze (2400'S/3427'E).
These swamps drain into the lake, and discharges to the Inhassunge River before jointing the
Inharrime River 65km downstream. This catchment drains a total area of 11,850km2 within
the Inhambane Province.
iv    The Govuro River
The only river of note along the road section between Inhambane to Save River is the entirely
coastal Govuro River flowing parallel to the coast from north to south, and located 5km to
the east of the road alignment. It has a catchment area of 11,200km2, with a mean annual
rainfall is between 800-1,000mm. A south-north orientated chain of small coastal barrier
lakes occur parallel to the Govuro River. The surrounding area to the east of the river
comprises mostly seasonal and permanent wetland habitats.
The prevalence of coastal lakes and lagoon systems is partly a consequence of the sandy
lowland nature of the coastal plain and the pattern of sand deposition. Changes in sea level
have stranded old coastline dune systems and lagoons inland and parallel to the present
coastline. Where coastal lagoons have become completely isolated, barrier lakes form. These
vary greatly in size, the largest have an open water surface area greater than 5,000ha. In this
region, these lakes are uniformly small.
Small affluent streams can feed these lakes while others are seasonal, drying up completely
during winter. Water quality varies widely. Temperature fluctuations increase with
decreasing lake size. Generally, the lakes are not saline, lying on sandy beds with substantial
organic deposits, pH values range from 4-7.5. The smaller lakes are often completely
occluded by emergent vegetation with distinct zonation according to water depth and degree
of disturbance.
v    The Save System
The Save River is very seasonal in its flow regime. It has a catchment of 92,100km2 and
along with its principal tributary, the Lundi, are impounded in Zimbabwe. Significantly less
water flows down to the sea than did formerly. The river crosses the country from west to
east, roughly separating the dry southern part of the country from the more humid north. For
much of its 330km course through Mozambique the river flows in a well incised bed some 1-
2km wide, but while in summer this may be brim full from bank to bank, its flow in winter is
frequently confined to a central channel barely 45cm deep and scarcely 50m across.
In winter the riverbed is a shining expanse of sand banks, with only occasional rocky or
sandy pools and pebble banks. However, water is never very far below the sandy bed, and a
little digging with bare hands generally reaches the water table. In some parts, a series of
little muddy pools lies close under its banks, which may be quite steep. Narrow swamps
occur on most of its tributaries, particularly those of its lower reaches, and between these
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects   February 2001                              1-20



IMPA L'T
watercourses the country is gently undulating with hundreds of small lakes and reed swamps.
Sometimes these lakelets mark the meandering and indistinct line of what becomes a
temporary watercourse after local rain.
1.7.3    Water Quality
River flows are greatest during the rainy season from mid-November to March, with flows
generally reaching a peak in January and February. River flows are lowest during the dry
season months of June to September.
Rivers are most turbid during the rainy season, when overland run-off and erosion are at their
greatest.
Rivers, ponds and wetlands in the vicinity of the proposed roads are free of chemical
pollutants due to the absence of industry.  Despite this, these water sources may be
microbiologically contaminated near settlement areas due to poor sanitation practices.
1.8 VEGETATION COVER AND USE
1.8.1   introduction
The distribution and location of the main natural vegetation types occurring in the area
traversed by the ENI are shown in Figures 9 and 10. Wild and Barbosa (1967) classify
vegetation based on mapping unit (MU) nomenclature as follows:
li woodland and savanna woodland (south-eastern sublittoral) with extra-tropical species
(MU 34);
z  dry semi-deciduous (lowland-sublittoral) forest (MU 5);
Li dry tree savanna-moist grassland-fringing forest-aquatic flora mosaic of big river
alluviums and deltas (lowland, sublittoral) (MU 54);
o deciduous miombo (north-eastern lowland and escarpment) savanna woodland (MU 29);
o  deciduous tree savanna with palms (badly drained, lowland) (MU 44);
o  littoral thicket and forest of recent dunes (MU 14b);
o deciduous miombo-deciduous forest (and savanna) mosaic southern sublittoral urron-gas)
(MU 25);
o  deciduous miombo savanna woodland (MU 23); and
o  deciduous dry miombo (lowland) savanna woodland (MU 30).
Each of the four sections of the EN 1 are examined in more detail below.
1.8.2    Maputo to Xai
The EN 1 project road from Maputo to Xai Xai (excluding the Inkomati River valley)
traverses two vegetation mapping units namely, woodland and savanna woodland (south-
eastern sublittoral) with extra-tropical species (MU 34) and littoral thicket and forest of
recent dunes (MU 14b) (Wild and Barbosa, 1967).

Figure 10:   Main Vegetation types along the ENI between Muxungue and Inchope
Woodland and savanna woodland (south-eastern sublittoral) with extra-tropical species
(MU 34, Wild and Barbosa, 1967) - an Albizia-Afzelia-Sclerocarya mainly open woodland
occurs between Maputo and Macia in the sublittoral zone of Mozambique south of the
Limpopo River. The soils are reddish, orange or grey, more or less rippled, sandy, with badly
drained or waterlogged depressions containing soils with black organic matter (machongos).
It occupies a large sublittoral belt in the extratropical zone from the southern boundary of
Mozambique to the River Limpopo where Brachystegia communities begin to be sporadic.
The annual rainfall varies between 900-1200mm. As the region is heavily populated the
vegetation consists mainly of more or less damaged woodland, patches of secondary
woodland and vegetation in which only the indigenous or sub-spontaneous fruit-bearing trees
have been spared.
The most common formation consists of woodlands (damaged or destroyed) with Albizia
adianthifolia, A. versicolor,. Afzelia quanzensis, Ficus burtt-davyi, Sclerocarya birrea,
Ziziphus mucronata, Strychnos spinosa, Trema orientalis, Ficus capensis, Syzygium
cordatum, etc. Understorey species have tended to survive destruction and give secondary
woodlands. These include variable combinations of the following species: Antidesma
venosum, Strychnos innocua, Tecomaria capensis, Turraea nilotica, T wakefieldii,
Vangueria infausta, Xylotheca kraussiana, Euclea natalensis, E. divinorum, Bridelia
cathartica, Artabotrys monteiroe, and Rhus macowanii.
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects   February 2001                            1-24



IMPA C'U
The large areas in which only wild species (and some introduced species) with edible fruits
are left may be considered as "indigenous orchards". They adjoin the localities with dense
rural populations. Typical "indigenous orchard" species are Sclerocarya birrea, Trichilia
emetica, Garcinia livingstonei, Strychnos spinosa, S. innocua, Dialium schlechteri, Syzygium
cordatum, Vangueria infausta, etc. The most common introduced orchard species is the
cashew (Anacardium occidentale), which is now sub-spontaneous. Mangifera indica
('mango') is also sub-spontaneous in more recently occupied areas.
Treeless secondary woodlands are also common on sandy soils and often Helichrysum
kraussii is dominant with the following Leguminosae: Crotalaria mucronata, C. monteiroi,
Indigofera podophylla, I. laxeracemosa, I. preladoi, Tephrosia forbesii, Aeshynomene spp.,
Stylosanthes mucronata, Zorna spp., Desmodium spp., Alysicarpus vaginalis, A. rugosus,
Rhynchosia spp., etc. Non-leguminous species include Epinetrum delagoense, Corchorus
unodii, Oxygonum delagoense, etc.
The areas of scattered tree and shrub savanna contain Syzygium cordatum, Garcinia
livingstonei, Trichilia emetica, Afzelia quanzensis, Sclerocarya birrea, Acacia robusta subsp.
clavigera, Dialum schechteri, Strychnos spinosa, Terminalia sericea, Tabernaemontana
elegans, Vangueria infausta, Acacia kraussiana, Tricalysia sonderana, Xylotheca kraussiana
var globifolia, etc. In some areas the tops of the trees are deformed by strong winds.
There are also patches of savanna-woodland or thicket of Terminalia sericea, Albizia
adianthifolia, Acacia karroo, Sclerocarya birrea and frequent Aloe marlothii. Patches of
swamp forest occur scattered throughout the coastal grassland. Swamp forests were never
extensive and many have been drained and cleared for agriculture. The remaining relics rate top
priority for conservation. True swamp forest canopy trees are Ficus trichopoda, Syzygium
cordatum, Voacanga thouarsii and Rauvolfla caffia.
Littoral thicket and forest of recent dunes (MU 14b). This vegetation type occurs on
recent dunes or juvenile soils formed from sands of aeolian origin. The most extensive
formation of this type occurs along the parabolic dune coast between Ponto do Ouro and
Bazaruto Island. Some of the highest vegetated dunes in the world are reported to occur
along this stretch of coast.
The more common pioneers include Sesuvium portulacastrum, Scaevola thunbergii, Ipomoea
pes-caprae, I. ficifolia, Canavalia maritima, Sophora tomentosa, Sporobolus virginicus,
Dipcadi longifolium, , and Carpobotus juritzii while dominant tree species include Grewia
occidentalis var. litoralis, Diospyros rotundifolia, Euclea natalensis and Mimusops caffra.
At the bottom of depressions there is often Suregada zanzibarensis with Encephalartosferox
in the understorey. Lianes and climbers include Rhoicissus revoilii, Maclura africana, Uvaria
gracilipes, Popowia caffra, Landolphia petersiana, Sarcostemma viminale, C. rotundifolia,,
Cynanchum ellipticum, A. setaceus, and Cryptolepis obtusa. Epiphytic orchids such Vanilla
roscheri, Cyrtorchis arcuata and Angraecum sp. were recorded. The ground flora is generally
sparse comprising species such as Phymatodes scolopendria, Zamioculcas sp. and Gonatopus
species.

IMPAI TO
i   Threatened Plant Species
There is no up-to-date information regarding threatened plant species in Mozambique. The
following is a list of some of the most important species classified as endangered, rare or
vulnerable occurring in the Inkomati River basin:
o  Ansellia giganta (Pakama, in Changane) Orchidaceae. An epiphyte occurring in sand
forests. Endangered.
L Encephalartos ferox (Chiahanga) Zamiaceae. From the original population only a small
number of individuals occur scattered along the coast. Rare.
u  Bonateapulchela- Orchidaceae. Scattered in very low numbers in coastal scrub. Rare.
o  Raphia australis - Aracaceae. Natural distribution limited along valleys of small rivers in
southern Mozambique and Kosi Bay (in Natal). It is only recorded for one area in the
Lower Inkomati (Bobole). It is protected and a special conservation area around the
Bobvole River was created in 1961. Endangered.
1.8.3    Xai Xai to Inhambane
For the first 1 Okm the road passes through the wide expanse of the Limpopo alluvial floodplain,
especially well developed along the southern bank (MU 54: moist grassland - fringing forest of
big river alluviums). The floodplain dominates the landscape between Ch6kwe and Xai-Xai.
Most of the floodplain is, or has been, under some form of agriculture although extensive areas
of agricultural land on the floodplains of the Xai-Xai region are not, at present, under cultivation
presumably due to high soil salinity. Cyperaceous species are especially common on these
uncultivated lands indicative of the waterlogged and saline edaphic conditions. Other commonly
occurring invasive species include Ammania auriculta, Cynodon dactylon, Hibiscus canabinus,
Nesaea spp., Phyla nodiflora and Solanum spp.
A single remaining tract of rare swamp forest (about 0.5ha) remains on the turf ('machongo')
soils adjacent to the surrounding dunes about lkm north of Xai-Xai. Once widespread on these
soils, these forests have been decimated due to clearing for agriculture and harvesting of
firewood. Only a few individuals of representative tree species such as Barringtonia racemosa,
Voacanga thouarsii, Ficus trichopoda and Syzygium cordatum remain in this relic forest. The
drainage and irrigation canals of the Xai-Xai floodplains are poorly maintained and free-
floating, submerged and rooted aquatic macrophytes such as Azolla africana, Eichornia
crassipes, Lagarosiphon sp. and Phragmites australis are widespread in these canals.
For most of its length after leaving Limpopo valley the road ascends elevated ground and passes
over undulating dunes formed from "Save Sands" (MU 20: miombo woodland on sul do save
sands (Brachystegia spiciformis)). Here typical miombo elements enter the transition e.g.
Brachystegia spp., Pterocarpus angolensis ('umbila') and Tamarindus indica (MU 20). Along
much of this stretch human occupation is heavy and much of the natural woodlands have been
replaced by small-scale farms with coconut, cashew and mango trees. Much of the woodland
has been converted to small-scale farmns through slash and burn and only the more important
indigenous fruit-bearing trees are left untouched for example Garcinia livingstonei ('bimbe'),
Strychnos spp. and Sclerocaria birrea. Large areas have been planted with coconut (Cocos
nucifera), cashew (Anacardium occidentalis) and mango trees (Mangifera indica).
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              1-26



IMPACIV'
A belt of sublittoral forest runs parallel to the coast c. 20-30km inland (MU 5: dry semi-
deciduous (lowland-sublittoral) forest (Sideroxylon. Afzelia, Balanites) after Wild & Barbosa,
1967). This forest formation differs from the littoral vegetation or recent dunes insofar that it
corresponds to a belt of vegetation occurring on ancient dunes comprising tree species such as
Albizia spp., Afzelia quanzensis, Garcinia livingstonei and Sideroxylon inerme. The road only
occasionally passes close to this vegetation type.
These eastern dunes comprise part of the extensive parabolic dune system that runs in an almost
uninterrupted fashion along the coastline from Ponta de Ouro in the south to Bazaruto in the
north. The dunes are characterized by a vegetation cover comprising succulent herbaceous
species on the fore dunes (pioneers) grading into well developed thicket or forest on the taller
dunes on the landward side - (MU 14 B: littoral thicket and forest of recent dunes). The road
does not pass through this vegetation type.
1.8.4   Inhambane To the Save River
Beginning at the Save River mouth, three discrete vegetation types occur from east to west:
L  A narrow strip of littoral coastal dune forest (Mimusops caffra) MU 14B;
o  A narrow strip of Miombo woodland on Sul do Save sands (Brachystegia spiciformis)
MU 20; and
o  A broad band of deciduous Miombo-deciduous forest mosaic (Brachystegia
o  spiciformis, Afzelia, Sideroxylon) MU 25.
South of the Save River, the vegetation is slightly different. Vegetation Types MU 14B and
MU 25 remain but another two vegetation types are also present:
o An extensive area of tree savannah (Julbernardia globiflora, Androstachys johnsonii)
MU 36; and
o A narrow band of deciduous tree savannah with palms extending inland from the Coast
(Phoenix, Hyphaene or Borassus) MU 44.
None of the vegetation types described above are of conservation concern or warrant special
protection measures. All are widely and extensively distributed throughout the southern
African sub-region.
Climate and soil are the major determinants of variation in vegetation cover. Moving from
the coast inland, there is a decline in average rainfall and an increased prevalence of
xerophytic vegetation comprising mostly deciduous species. Deciduous Miombo tree
savannah and forest/savannah mosaics dominates on the older clayey and sandy soils of the
interior, Semi deciduous miombo dominates on the darker alluvial soils, while littoral thicket
and forest on recent dunes dominates on the more recent dunes and youngest sandy soils of
the coastal zone.
1.8.5    Muxungue to Inchope
The section of ENI between the Save River and Muxungue is excluded from this assessment.
Nevertheless this section of road warrants a brief description prior to describing the ENI
between Muxungue and Inchope.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              1-27



IMPAU'fO
The Save is a broad shallow river that is mainly dry during winter. It is lined by intact
riverine forest dominated by Syzygium guineense, Ficus sycamorus, Mimusops obtusifolia,
Trichelia emetica, Rauvolfia caffra, Diospyros mespiliformis and Kigelia africana. The lower
banks are protected by a dense growth of the shrub Grewia sulcata. Heading north the road
first traverses Coutada Five, which is characterised by extensive undisturbed tracts of
miombo and mopane woodland.
Between Muxungue and Inchope the vegetation is more disturbed and subsistence farming is
prevalent.
The ENI in this area traverses two vegetation types, deciduous miombo savanna woodland
(Brachystegia spiciformis - Julbernardia globiflora) (MU 23) and Deciduous dry miombo
(lowland) savanna woodland (MU 30).
Deciduous miombo savanna woodland (Brachystegia spiciformis - Julbemardia globiflora)
(MU 23) is very important on the plateaux of Manica and Sofala Provinces. In Manica and
Sofala Provinces it is found on altitudes of between 600 and 900m. It occurs on a great
variety of soils derived from, for example, granites, gneisses, Umkondo sandstones,
quartzites and schists and where rainfall varies between 700 and 1200mm p.a.
With Brachystegia spiciformis the commonest associated trees and shrub are Julbernardia
globiflora, Pterocarpus angolensis, Burkea africana, Swartzia madagascariensis, Parinari
curatellifolia, Diplorhynchus condylocarpon, Pericopsis angolensis, Annona senegalensis,
Amblygonocarpus andongensis, Terminalia sericea, Millettia stuhlmanii, Protea spp., etc.
In the areas bordering vleis or dambos there is savanna woodland of Brachystegia boehmii,
Julbernardia globiflora, Burkea fricana, Protea, Uapaca nitida, Maprounea africana, etc.
whilst in secondary areas Julbernardia globiflora, Entada abyssinica, Annona senegalensis,
Antidesma venosum, Dichrostachys cinerea subsp. nyassana, dominate.
Deciduous dry miombo (lowland) savanna woodland (MU 30) occurs in Manica and
Sofala Provinces where it occupies the northern slope of the valley of the Save River. Below
100m the Brachystegia boehmii-B. allenii-Julbernardia globiflora escarpment, savanna
woodlands give way to almost pure Julbernardia globiflora. This type occupies the lower
parts of the escarpment or the ridges in low altitude areas alternating with Colophospermum
mopane zones, Acacia zones or Combretum-Terminalia zones along drainage lines.
The grass cover is sparse, similar to that under Brachystegia spiciformis- Julbernardia
globiflora savanna woodland but Heteropogon contortus and species of Eragrostis and
Aristida become more common.  This is a distinctively drier type than any of the
Brachystegia woodlands or savanna woodlands. Where ridges are steeper or more broken
than usual, a low altitude ecotype of B. glaucescens with relatively few pairs of leaflets
becomes prevalent. This has been observed in the lowest parts of the escarpment of Manica
and Sofala.
1.8.6    Ecologically Sensitive Plant Communities
Within the southern coastal zone a number of habitats have biological and ecological
importance, namely:
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                            1-28



IMPAC'iO
u swamp forests;
Li riverine forests;
o wetlands, swamps and floodplains; and
o coastal dunes.
Of these plant communities, the ENI only runs adjacent to the wetlands, swamps and
floodplain community and is described below.
Seasonal flooding is an essential component of the natural function of these ecosystems. In
turn these habitats act as giant sponge that help to regulate the river. During the dry season
the decomposing vegetation results in very low oxygen concentration away from the
remaining channels and lagoons. With the flooding of the swamps (main rains fall between
November and March) the fishes disperse into the surrounding shallow swamps and
floodplains where breeding occurs. Not only the breeding system but also the feeding regime
is adapted to these seasonal events.
1.9 WILDLIFE HABITAT AND THREATENED FAUNA
1.9.1    Wildlife Habitat
i   Maputo to Xai Xai
There are very limited data about the conservation status of the fauna in this region.
Research has been fragmentary and more related to reserves and parks in other geographic
areas of the country. Historically the Inkomati River basin was an important wildlife habitat.
The current status of most species remains uncertain and many species are now probably
locally extinct.
ii   Xai Xai to Inhambane
Much of the woodland habitat has been converted to small-scale farms and associated cashew,
mango and coconut plantations. Consequently there is little or no suitable habitat for medium to
large size mammals. Suitable habitat (mainly secondary woodland) for small mammal species
such as suni (Neotragus moschatus), common duiker (Silvicapra grimmia), red duiker
(Cephalophus natalensis) and steenbok (Raphicerus campestris) is still found in some areas.
The intact littoral dune forest is characterized by high bird species diversity. In contrast, the
anthropogenic landscape of the interior dunes does not offer suitable habitat for fruit and seed
eating bird species accounting for the relatively low bird species diversity. Interestingly, low
aquatic bird species diversity was noted for the lakes of the region in contrast to the large
number of aquatic species recorded for the Limpopo Valley (south) and Lake Quissico
further north (Vincent Parker, pers. comm.).
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              1-29



IMPAL'IO
iii   Inhambane to the Save River
In the past, this region was rich in wildlife, and populations of large herbivorous mammals
such as elephant, buffalo, kudu, eland, and carnivores such as lion, leopard and cheetah
occurred throughout the region. The protracted armed conflict and lack of formal
conservation initiatives led to a decline, and in some cases, the local extinction of most
species of large mammals. Today the area's fauna consists mainly of small to medium-sized
herbivores, bats, rodents, and small carnivores. Fauna are more prevalent to the west of the
national road ENI (Arco 1998). On the basis of fieldwork done for the EIS of the exploration
drilling, Arco (1998) estimate that fourteen species of mammals of conservation concern
occur in the region.
The area is rich in avifauna; which is to be expected considering the diversity of habitat types
within the region. Over two days of preliminary fieldwork a total of 50 species of birds were
identified. A previous brief survey identified 102 species (MICOA, 1997). However, the
species richness is likely to be much higher. In contrast to the distribution of mammals,
species richness is probably higher in the eastern section, which includes the Govuro River, a
diversity of wetland habitats, and the coastal zone. Again, a significant number of bird
species are likely to be of conservation concern (nine species, according to Arco, 1998).
Relatively limited data exist concerning the lower vertebrates (reptiles, amphibians, and fish).
Nevertheless species diversity for all three taxa is expected to be high, and it is probable that
several species of conservation concern occur in the Concession Areas. Previous studies of
herpetofauna (Arco, 1998) reported the presence of 23 reptile and five amphibian species,
though both are probably substantial underestimates of true species richness. Many of these
species (especially amphibians and fish) will be dependent on the diversity of wetland
habitats that characterise the region.
iv    Muxungue to Inchope
Despite the close proximity of Coutada Five and the extensive tracts of intact savanna habitat
this region is no longer rich in wildlife resources. The same is true for Zinave National Park
which is situated on the south bank of the Save River approximately 50km to the west of the
road. Flagship species such as elephant, buffalo, hippo, numerous species of antelope, and the
big cats were once common in this region. Now their status is uncertain and many species
may in fact be locally extinct. Early faunal surveys (Smithers and Tello 1976; Smithers 1983)
recorded 104 mammal species in this region. Avian biodiversity is remarkably high and
probably unchanged, with a total of 424 species recorded for the region (Maclean, 1985).
1.9.2    Threatened Species
Along this section of the ENI road, most important wildlife areas are in close proximity to
the major river valleys (Inkomati, Limpopo, and Save).
i   Fish
There are ten fish species of conservation concern known to occur within the ENI project
roads ROI. A description of these species is provided in Appendix A (Table A2).
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects     February 2001                               1-30



IMPAC'LU
ii   Amphibians
There is very little knowledge about the occurrence and conservation status of amphibians.
Frogs with a probable distribution along the major river basins and associated wetlands are
included in the Appendix A (Table A3).
iii   Reptiles
Fourteen species of reptiles of special conservation concern have been recorded for the
region, many of these species are inhabitants of the major river basins (Appendix A,
Table A4). There are only three species of known reptiles of vulnerable status, which occur
within the ENI project roads ROI, namely the Gabbon Adder, Nile Crocodile and South
African Rock Python.
iv    Birds
There is no Red Data Book for birds in Mozambique and only a few surveys on the
conservation status of birds have been produced during the last decade. Scarcity of game and
livestock limits foraging opportunities and, because of that, populations of species like the
Cape Vulture, and Lappetfaced Vulture are in decline in Mozambique.
Rare species such as the Saddlebilled Stork are sighted infrequently in their preferred wetland
habitats The decline is a result of hunting and human disturbance around wetlands. Other
declining populations include the Rufousbellied heron (Butorides rufiventris).
Rare non-breeding summer migrants like the Steppe Eagle (Aquila nipalensis) has been
recorded near Moamba.
Breeding resident populations of the uncommon Longcrested Eagle (Lophaetus occipitalis)
are in decline along river estuaries north of Maputo, due to pressure on the natural vegetation
and the reduction of wetlands.
The palmnut vulture (Gypohierax angolensis) has been recently observed breeding near
Manhica. Its preferred habitat is wetlands where the palms Raphia australis are present.
These are now restricted mainly to Bobole Reserve.
Vulnerable species only occurring in marshlands include the Pinkthroated longclaw
(Macronyx amelidae). However there are no actual records of the presence of previous
visitors and residents like the Chestnutbanded Plover (Charadrius pallidus), Whitecrowned
Plover (Vanellus albiceps), Dickinson's Kestrel (Falco dickinsoni), Thickbilled Cuckoo
(Pachycoccyx audeberti), Pel's Fishing Owl (Scotopelia peli), and Neergaard's Sunbird
(Nectarina neergardi).
The Lemonbreasted canary a near endemic species to Mozambique - is restricted to the areas
between Uanetze and Mazimechopes river, related to ilala palm (Hyphaene natalensis)
populations. The floodplains of the Inkomati River are considered by Phillip Clancey as the
natural frontier for two races of the vulnerable Rudd's Apalis, A.r. fumosa (south) and
A.r.ruddi (north)
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                            1-31



IMPALIY)
A summarized list of birds of special conservation concern known to occur within the ENI
project roads ROI is presented in Appendix A (Table A5). Bird species with a relatively
restricted distribution are listed in Appendix A (Table A6).
v    Mammals
There are no recent surveys of terrestrial mammals in the area. Anecdotal data suggests the
presence of some mammals with special biological and ecological value. Truly riverine
mammals not included in the Red Data Book were also reported such as Water mongoose
(Atilax paludinosus), Cape clawless otter (Aonix capensis) and the reed dependent Cane rat
(Thryonomys swinderanus).
Hippopotamus were recorded until recently in the major rivers. However, their current status
remains uncertain.
A list of the mammals of special conservation concern whose presence has been informally
confirmed within the ROI is provided in Appendix A (Table A7).
1.10 CONSERVATION AREAS
1.10.1    National Parks and Reserves
No proclaimed conservation areas (National Parks, Games Reserves or Forest Reserves) are
located in the region through which the road passes.
The 110km section of the ENI from the Save River to Muxungue (which has not been
prioritised for rehabilitation during this phase of the project) bisects one of the country's
thirteen designated controlled hunting areas (Coutada number 5).
The stretch of road between Vilanculos and Inhassoro (Inhambane Province) is adjacent to
the Bazaruto National Park. This is renowned for the pristine beaches of the archipelago's
four islands and varied fauna, which includes protected and endangered species such as
marine mammals (whales, dolphins and dugongs), marine turtles and corals.
Bazaruto National Park (Parque Nacional de Bazaruto) was created in 1971 by the Diploma
Legislativo n 46 of the Decreto n 40.040/71 and it comprises Magaruque, Benguerua and
Bangue islands. Bazaruto Island is not currently included in the park boundaries. However,
the Conservation Master Plan for Sustainable Development of the Bazaruto Archipelago
(Dutton & Zolho, 1990 submitted to the Government (Ministry of Agriculture) in 1991
recommended the inclusion of Bazaruto Island in the Park.
Inchope, the northern limit of the road, is located to the south-east of the Gorongosa National
Park, one of the most important conservation areas in Mozambique. The Park is renowned for
the protection of wildlife although wildlife populations may range over a wide area and large
mammals may occur outside the GNP particularly along the Rift Valley and surrounding
woodlands. Tinley (1977) lists 46 large mammal species (>5 kg mass) for the Gorongosa area
Appendix A (Table A8). However, several of these species are locally extinct/near extinction
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                            1-32



IMPAC'LO
and examples include tsessebe (extinct), the roan antelope (locally extinct) and the black and
white rhino (probably extinct).
Conservation measures in the Park are being undertaken under a five-year project (GERFFA
- Gestao dos Recursos Florestais e Faunisticos) financed by the African Development Bank
(1997-2002). Under the Project, rehabilitation and long-term development is intended. New
Park headquarters are being constructed, internal access is being improved and law
enforcement strengthened. Additionally, tourism with private sector involvement is being
promoted. The wildlife training school will be re-established in the Park and community
relations are being developed and it is intended that benefits from the Park will accrue to
local communities.
1.10.2    Coastal Barrier Lakes
Coastal barrier lakes extend along the southern coastline of Mozambique from Ponta do Oura
to Inhassoro. Typical freshwater/brackish water fish species occur in these lakes with the
exception of Lake Bilene (south of Xai-Xai), which has a mixture of marine and brackish
water species. During a brief survey (Hatton, 1995) recorded 29 species occurring in selected
coastal lakes of southern Mozambique (Appendix A, Table A8). The apparent species
paucity of the Chidenguele lakes probably reflects the sampling method (generally only one
day's catch per lake by local fishermen). A more intensive sampling program employing
other techniques will undoubtedly reveal the presence of other species in the Chidenguele
lakes.
In an earlier study (Tello, 1973) listed 30 different freshwater and estuarine species occurring
in the coastal lakes of Maputo Special Reserve, south of Maputo City of which three species,
Croilia mossambica, Serranochromis meridianu, and Nothobranchius orthonothus are listed
as rare (the former two) and endangered (the latter) by the IUCN (Skelton, 1993). C.
mossambica and N. Orthonothus are considered to be near endemic to the region. However,
it is not known whether these species occur in the Chidenguele barrier lakes.
Little is known about the vertebrate and invertebrate fauna associated with these water
bodies.  The consensus of opinion is that biodiversity is high, notably avifauna and
herpetofauna. Historically, the mammal fauna was also rich and diverse especially wetland
specialists such as reedbuck, duiker, mongoose, genet and otter.
The lake systems occur in the depressions of the undulating dunes. North of Xai-Xai
miombo elements enter the transition e.g. Brachystegia spp., Pterocarpus angolensis
("umbila") and Tamarindus indica (MU 28). Along this stretch population density is high,
and small-scale farms with coconut, cashew and mango trees have replaced much of the
natural woodlands to small-scale farms through slash and burn, only the more important
indigenous fruit-bearing trees are left untouched for example, Garcinia livingstonei
("bimbe"), Strychnos spp. And Sclerocarya birrea. Large areas have been planted with
cashew (Anacardium occidentale) and mango trees (Mangifera indica).
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              1-33



IMPAC'W
1.10.3    Threatened Plant Species
To date relatively little is known about the conservation status of plant species in
Mozambique. Reference is made to Bandeira et al. (1994). A study prepared by Arco (1998)
for the Sasol exploration drilling at Temane identified 23 species of plants occurring in the
ROI that may be of conservation concern. These species are listed in Appendix A (Table Al).
1.11 NOISE AND AIR QUALITY
Noise levels along the ENI project road, except in settlements, are low and predominantly
generated by natural conditions (i.e. from insects, winds and rivers flows). Noise levels
seldom exceed low background levels, however in settlements, noise levels can be higher
during the day in market areas.
Ambient air quality in all of the proposed road areas is good due to a lack of industry and
allied urban activities. Haze is often observed due to the frequently practiced slash and burn
agriculture and production of charcoal. Levels of airborne dust generated by winds can also
be high passing over agricultural fields, and from smoke from forest and grassland fires.

IMPAC' L
2 SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
2.1 INTRODUCTION
The purpose of this section is to describe the socio-economic environment for seven sections
of the National Road 1 (EN1) from Manhica in Maputo Province to Inchope in Sofala
Province. The road passes through Southern Region and the southern section of Central
Region. Southern Region consists of Maputo, Gaza and Inhambane Provinces. Central
Region consists of Sofala, Manica and Tete Provinces. Information has been derived from
existing reports and publications where information is disaggregated usually at the Provincial
and District levels. Field visits were also made to each of the seven sections of road during
the month of November 2000.
The objective of this section is to describe the overall socio-economic context in terms of the
living conditions for the populations living in proximity to the ENI road. Where possible
statistical information has been provided to enable the establishment of base line data from
which the impacts of road improvement may be made.
The seven road sections are:
ROAD SECTIONS                           PROVINCE
Maputo - Manica                         Maputo
Manica - Incoluane                      Maputo
Incoluane - Zandemela                   Gaza
Zandemela - Mazixe                      Inhambane
Maxixe - Cr.ER520 /ENI                  Inhambane
EN 1 CR. ENI / ER520 - Rio Save         Inhambane
Muxungue - Inchope                      Sofala/Manica
2.2 PROVINCIAL SETTINGS AND ADMINISTRATIONS
2.2.1   Introduction
The seven sections of the ENI pass through four Provinces: Maputo, Gaza, Inhambane, and
Sofala. The section of the ENI Muxungue to Inchope passes briefly through Manica
Province.  For socio-economic description purposes information for Maputo, Gaza,
Inhambane and Sofala Provinces was used predominantly supplemented with broad indices
of information from Gondola District in Manica Province.
2.2.2    Traditional Administration
Administrative structures accord with the rest of Mozambique whereby each Province is
divided into Districts.  Each District is sub-divided for administrative purposes into
Administrative Posts and further sub-divided into Localities. Provinces are administered
through a Governor selected by the Mozambican president. At the provincial level there
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              2-1



IMPA 'IO
exists the extensions of the departments of government such as Ministries of agriculture,
education, and health. Policy is made at the central government level to be implemented by
the various Provincial ministries. The Governor performs the function of co-ordinator of the
various ministries. The Governor does not have the authority to select or replace a Provincial
head of a ministry. This authority is vested in the respective Ministers of government.
Traditional administrative structures exist in Southern and Central Regions of Mozambique
based upon the concept of a Chief or headperson of a community or ethnic grouping. This
traditional structure exists, too lesser or greater extent, in most localities side-by-side with the
more modem administrative apparatus of the State and political parties. In general terms the
first government of an independent Mozambique from 1975 attempted to replace the
inherited colonial and traditional systems with a centralised system based upon the political
doctrines of socialism. With political and economic reform since the mid-1980's the
intention of the Government has been to dismantle a highly centralised system and replace it
by devolving increasing amounts of decision making authority to Provincial and District
levels.
2.2.3    Population
The combined population of the four Provinces the ENI passes through is 4,281,028 as of the
national census conducted in 1997 and represents approximately 25 per cent of
Mozambique's total population (17,300,000). Gaza and Inhambane Provinces are relatively
sparsely populated at 14 and 16.3 persons per square kilometre respectively, below the
national average of 18 persons per square kilometre. Sofala Province at 18.9 persons per
kilometre is marginally above the national average. Maputo Province has the highest
population density at 30.9 persons per square kilometre, but below Nampula Province, which
has the highest population density in Mozambique at 36.2 persons per square kilometre. The
population figures and population densities for the four Provinces the road passes through are
provided in Table 4.

Total               4,281,028     20.02 (average)
* Excludes Maputo City
Most of the population of the three Provinces (Maputo, Gaza, and Inhambane) in the
Southern Region is concentrated along the coast and the ENI. In general, this accords with
the more favourable agro-ecological conditions closer to the coast where rainfall and soil
fertility is higher and other opportunities for commercial activity are more possible. The
southern section of the ENI is the most heavily used section of road in Mozambique as it
provides the main north-south artery linking the capital Maputo City with the rest of the
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects   February 2001                             2-2



IMPA L'W
country. This in itself provides an attraction as the communication channel offered by the
ENI affords other income generating opportunities.
Population characteristics accord with general profile for Mozambique as a whole. The
population structure is young (approximately 35 percent of Mozambican's are under the age
of 30 years of age). Average age structures are 0-14 years constituting 46% of the population
(male 4,141,915 and female 4,115,191), 15-64 years constituting 51% of the population
(male 4,324,102 and 4,868,518 female, and 65 years and over 3% of the population (male
184,606 and 243,595 female). Household sizes are large at 5.5 members, and the ratio
between economically active and passive members (less than 15 years or more than 60 years)
of the population is high with a dependency ratio of 1:1. These figures are presented in Table
5.

2.2.4    Ethnicity
The main ethnic groups in the south are the Tsonga in the southern lowlands, and the Chopi
and Tonga along the coast and are the largest group in Inhambane Province. Mozambicans
have been mixing with different cultures for centuries and have absorbed many different
characteristics and customs. Arabs had been trading and living along the coast for hundreds
of years before the arrival of the Portuguese, and when the new colonisers came they quickly
settled and intermarried, creating a rich cultural mix. Mozambicans today reflect that
melding or races.
The diversity of population is reflected in the variety of languages spoken. The official
language is Portuguese although approximately 10 percent only of Mozambicans speak it as
their first fanguage. The main language along the ENI section in Inhambane Province is
Tsonga.  Further west and particularly in Gaza Province the Changane language
predominates.
2.2.5    Gender
The ethnic groups present in the Southern Region of Mozambique are patrilineal where
inheritance is from father to son. Although most ethnic groups are patrilineal, the prevalence
of male migration necessitates that in many rural areas particularly in the Southern Region of
Mozambique, women run their families and livings. FRELIMO as part of the liberation
struggle promoted concepts related to the equality of women. With independence in 1975
these progressive attitudes towards the role of women continued including the introduction of
new laws, which attempted to enshrine protection of rights for women.
Despite the advancement of the rights of women under independent administration,
Mozambican girls and women do less well than boys and men. Maternal mortality rate is
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                             2-3



IMPAL"1U
high at 1,100 per 100,000. Access to health services directed specifically at women is
generally very poor. Literacy rates for women are much lower than for men. Family law in
Mozambique still discriminates against women. A married woman has limited rights to make
independent economic decisions or enjoy full ownership of assets, and a widow's rights to
inheritance are secondary to males in the family. Girls in rural areas in general marry young
often as soon as they reach puberty.
2.2.6    Economy
Mozambique has undergone a period of significant economic recovery since the signing of
the peace accord in 1992. Annual growth rate as defined by gross domestic product (GDP) in
the past three years has been calculated at 8.8, 9.7 and 7.3 percent, respectively. Inflation has
fallen from a level of almost 50 percent in 1996 to a current level of less than 6% per annum.
Foreign investment into Mozambique has grown rapidly since the signing of the peace
accords in 1992. In the seven years 1993-1999, $4.2 billion in foreign investments were
approved, $1.2 billion in foreign direct investment and $3 billion in loans. Implementation
has been slower with only $284 million actually spent in 1998 ($213 million in investment
and $81 million in private sector credit).  The majority of foreign investment into
Mozambique has been largely orientated to the Southern Region and in particular Maputo
City and Maputo Province. For example, in Maputo City during 1999, work started on the
$1.3 billion Mozal aluminium smelter, by far Mozambique's largest investment project, and
was commissioned in September 2000. In large part due to Mozal, 1999 saw $348 million in
actual foreign direct investment and $495 million in foreign loans to the private sector.
Mozal alone is believed to account for more than 2 percent of the 7.3 percent per capita GDP
growth rate for 1999, leading to fears that growth is increasingly reliant on large projects.
Maputo, Gaza and to a lesser extent Inhambane Provinces benefit from their proximity to
South Africa, Mozambique's largest trading partner and biggest foreign investor. The
presence of a relatively large and strong economy affords several opportunities to
Mozambique. Maputo City provides port facilities for the exportation and importation of
goods for South Africa. The Southern Region and to a lesser extent Central Region of
Mozambique was historically a major holiday destination for South Africans. With the return
of peace and stability to Mozambique South African tourists have returned in increasing
numbers to Mozambique.
Despite the concentration of much of Mozambique's industrial capacity in the Southern
Region and in particular in and around Maputo City, the economy and households of the
Southern Region are still dependent upon subsistent agriculture. The floods of early 2000
badly affected agricultural production. The infrastructure of the Southern Region and in
particular roads and bridges were badly damaged as a result of the floods. The Government
of Mozambique managed to secure approximately $450 million of emergency funds from
international donors to address the repair and reconstruction of infrastructure. The damage to
sections of the EN I was severe, but emergency repairs enabled the reopening of the road.
2.2.7    Poverty
According to the National Poverty Assessment (1996-97) 68.1 % of the population are
categorized as poor corresponding to 2.1 million people. The poverty figures for Gaza and
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects  February 2001                            2-4



IMPAL 'i
Inhambane Provinces are 80% and 64.7% respectively. Life expectancy is similar in Gaza
and Inhambane as for the rest of Mozambique (37 years). More than 90% of the population
depend on agriculture, forestry and fisheries for their livelihood, mostly comprising the
family sector. There are few formal sector employment opportunities. The number of people
who have had any formal education is low and the situation is particularly acute with regards
to the numbers of persons who have attained any formn of higher-level training.
2.2.8    Political Environment
In the 1999 elections the ruling political party FRELIMO received the majority of the votes
cast in Maputo, Inhambane and Gaza Province. Gaza Province is the birthplace of Joaquim
Chissano the current president of Mozambique. The first president of Mozambique Samora
Machel was also born in Gaza Province. This historical lineage adds to Gaza Province strong
political allegiance to FRELIMO. In Sofala Province the 1999 elections the popular vote was
unanimously for RENAMO the main opposition political party. Sofala Province was the
political stronghold for Mozambique during the armed conflict of the 1980's. Political
tension exists to the extent that RENAMO believe they should be allowed to select a
candidate as Governor of the Province to reflect their electoral superiority. The issue of the
political allegiance of the Governor is currently vigorously debated in Mozambique.
2.2.9    Education
Demand on education facilities is extremely high in all four Provinces the road passes
through. This reflects the young age of the population (on average 46% of the population in
Mozambique is between 0 and 14 years old) and the interest families have to send their
children to school. Table 6 shows education provision for Maputo, Gaza, Inhambane and
Sofala Provinces.

2.2.10     Health
The illnesses occurring most frequently in the Southern Region and Central Region are
malaria, tuberculosis, bilharzia, intestinal parasites, acute respiratory infection, AIDS,
sexually transmitted diseases and non-specific diarrhoea. Current rates of HIV infection for
Southern Region are on average 23% and are projected to rise to 27% by the year 2010. It is
calculated that 50% children under five-years suffer from chronic malnutrition and 10% from
acute malnutrition.
2.2.11     Access To Services
Since 1992 the pace of redevelopment of services and infrastructure has been dramatic.
Despite the progress made, post-war reconstruction has still not been completed.
Infrastructure remains generally poor. Floods, such as those that occurred in the Southern
Region and to a lesser extent in Central Region of Mozambique in February and March 2000,
set farmers, fishermen and other small businesses back many years.  Many believe
Mozambique will continue to need high levels of international assistance to complete
reconstruction, and to build a more robust base for the kind of development that is essential
to ending acute poverty.

Access to services for the rural population is generally poor. Most villages on average do
have a primary school (EPI), but in general the condition of these schools could be
categorized as rudimentary and often availability of teachers is very low. Less the 2% of
villages in Southern Region has a secondary school. Most villages have access to a basic
health centre, but only access to a trained doctor is very low. Dependence on traditional
healers is the norm for the vast majority of the population and almost every village would
have at least one person categorised as a traditional healer. Table 8 gives the availability of
services by Province for Maputo, Gaza, Inhambane, and Sofala Provinces.
2.2.12    Livelihood Strategies
The principle economic activity in the four Provinces is agriculture. Most food production is
within the family sector upon which the majority of the population is dependent. The
principle food crops grown are cassava (total production for Maputo, Gaza, Inhambane and
Sofala Provinces 500,271 tons/metric for 1999/200) and maize (total production for Maputo,
Gaza, Inhambane and Sofala Provinces 241,653 tons/metric 1999/2000). The main crops
grown and production levels (tons/metric) for Maputo, Gaza, Inhambane and Sofala

2.3 DISTRICT CHARACTERISTICS
2.3.1    District Settings and Administration
This section describes socio-economic factors specific to the Maputo to Inchope ENI road.
The main sources of information relate to existing written reports concerning the Districts the
ENI passes through. This was supplemented with information collected during a brief field
visit along the ENI during November 2000.
The EN I passes through eleven Districts as indicated in Table 10.
The public administration structures for the eleven Districts include Administrative Posts,
District Government Directorates and a range of public institutions. The provision of public
administration services differs markedly between the Districts, depending on the District.

2.3.2    Traditional Administration
Traditional structures exist in all eleven Districts, and these complement the formal
administration. The chiefs and headman of tribes have authority at local and village levels but
the level of integration of this authority into District governance varies between Districts.
The major factor underpinning the problem of integration is the lack of a legal framework
linking administrative structures to traditional authorities.
The District Administrators and ward secretaries govern land allocation in the Districts. Land
may not be sold, but trees, buildings and other infrastructure may be traded. Apart from the
license fee, no payment is required to acquire land, and land rights are passed on to sons
through inheritance.
The administration of the eleven Districts occurs through both formal structures and
traditional structures. Traditional structures have authority only at local and village level. At
District level, the greater the perceived administrative needs of the District, the higher the
proportion of administration services provided. The major determinant of "perceived need"
appears to be population density.
2.3.3    Population
The combined population for the eleven Districts is 1,070,000. The most populated District
is Massinga (169,000 inhabitants).  The least populated District is Govuro (29,000
inhabitants). The average family size is 4.9 persons. The largest mean family size is found
in Zavala District (5.6) and the smallest mean family sizes are found in Massinga, Govuro
and Inhassoro Districts (4.2). The average population density for the eleven districts is 40.6
inhabitants per square kilometre. The highest population density is found in Zavala District
(89  inhabitants/Kmi2) and  the  lowest population  density  in  Govuro  District (6
inhabitants/kM2). A summary of the population statistics for the eleven Districts is provided
in Table 11.
2.3.4    Ethnicity
The main ethnic groups in the Southern Region of Mozambique are the Tsonga in the
southern lowlands, and the Chopi and Tonga along the coast.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects     February 2001                                2-8



IMPAC'iU
The main language along the ENI section in Inhambane Province is Tsonga. Further west
and particularly in Gaza Province the Changane language predominates.

2.3.5    Gender
The ethnic groups present in the Southern Region of Mozambique and within the eleven
Districts the sections of the EN1 passes through are patrilineal where inheritance is from
father to son. Although the ethnic groups are patrilineal, the prevalence of male migration
necessitates that in many rural areas particularly in the Southern Region of Mozambique,
women run their families and livings. FRELIMO as part of the liberation struggle promoted
concepts related to the equality of women. With independence in 1975 these progressive
attitudes towards the role of women continued including the introduction of new laws, which
attempted to enshrine protection of rights for women.
2.3.6    Economy
The economies of the Districts the ENI passes through are dependent upon small-scale
agriculture. Historically households in the Southern Region of Mozambique have been
dependent on remittances sent back from migrant labourers, mostly men working on the
mines in South Africa. Although the numbers of Mozambican migrant miners has during the
last decade reduced considerably, the culture of young men wanting to leave their home
village is strong. These migratory patterns have effects on household economies of families
living along or in proximity to the ENI. The most obvious one is the loss of young male
workers and their contribution to the subsistence agricultural activities of households. This
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects     February 2001                                2-9



IMPA 'LW
loss is balanced to a certain extent with the remittances men may send back to their families
and the fact that there is one less mouth to feed at home.
The economy of the Southern Region and in particular Maputo Province has benefited most
from the rapid economic growth that Mozambique has enjoyed over the last five years. The
impacts of this overall economic growth are more difficult to determine at the District level.
There have been tangible benefits to improvements in infrastructure such as schools, health
facilities, and roads. It is more difficult to determine if household incomes are increasing.
Overall gross domestic product for the Southern Region is going up and is currently at
approximately $220 a year per head. It is however probable that for the majority of
households in Southern Region there has been no significant increase in household incomes
during the last five years despite strong national and regional economic growth.
The floods of 2000 had a negative impact on rural household well being in particularly in the
Gaza and Inhambane Provinces. The ENI road was under water along several sections,
which also affected family agricultural output. Families most affected by the floods lost on
average between one-third and one-half of all crop production. This overall situation to an
extent disguises the fact that for some families, perhaps as much as 20% of all households in
Gaza Province, lost all their crops. There is obvious concern that a failure in crop production
for the growing season 2000 to 2001 could prove catastrophic for families still only just
recovering from the floods of 2000.

comparison of a number of economic and service indices. It is presented as an indication of
development in the eleven Districts.

2.3.7     Poverty
The main period of poor nutrition is the period between October and March when crop stores
are at their lowest and before the new season harvest. The principal deficiencies in dietary
terms for the Districts the ENI passes through are lack of vitamin A and iron leading to a
high incidence of anaemia. There is a problem particularly in the Districts situated in
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects         February 2001                                   2-10

Inhambane Province (e.g.. Inharrine, Zavala, Jangamo, Homoine, and Morrumbene) related
to intoxication due to the poor preparation of cassava mainly causing problems of vomiting.

over 50 to 60% of families are poor, 30 to 40% medium, and 0% to 10% rich.

2.3.8     Political Environment
The Southern Region of Mozambique supported the ruling political party FRELIMO at the
last general election in 2000. The policies of the current Government have included attempts
to devolve more responsibility and authority to the Provincial level. The general capacity of
Provincial Government departments is weak. The current problems relate to the capacity of
Provincial and District levels of Government to adequately deliver services to their
populations. These problems are recognised and projects, which concentrate on capacity
building, are encouraged. However, while capacity is in the process of being built there is
some political tensions between the Provincial and Central levels of Government as lines of
responsibility and authority are being clarified.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects        February 2001                                    2-11



IMPACJ'O
2.3.9    Education
In general the provision of primary (EPI) schools in Southern Region is better than in the
Central and Northern Regions of Mozambique. While most villages in the Districts along the
ENI road are well served, provision of upper (EP2) primary and secondary (ESG) schools is
still very poor. Table 14 summarises education provision in the eleven districts the ENI road

2.3.10    Health
Most villages in the Districts the ENI passes through have available basic health facilities in
the form of a health post. These health posts are able only to administer basic health
provision to their local populations. The level of training of staff at health posts is minimal
and often consists of having completed only a basic first aid course. Health Centres have
greater provision and more qualified staff usually to the level of nurse. Only one of the
Districts (Manhica) has available a rural hospital with a qualified doctor.  Table 15
summarises the availability of health facilities within the districts the EN I passes through.
2.3.11     Access to Services
The people living along the ENI and in close proximity to it generally have good access to a
variety of facilities. As Mozambique's main road, local populations can travel along the road
using the available public transport to access the services they require.

2.3.12    Livelihood Strategies
Rural production predominates throughout the study area. The main food crops include
maize, cassava, groundnuts, sorghum, beans, sweet potato and vegetables.  Fruit is an
important dietary supplement throughout the region. Wildlife is still present and game meat
is an important food source to the populations of these Districts. Use is also made of the
availability of wood for firewood, building materials and charcoal production and this has led
to deforestation and erosion.  Livestock farming is very limited.  The extent of the
agricultural activity is dependent upon several factors, notably:
o  fertility of the soil;
o  availability of water;
o  availability of good pasture;
o  availability of supportive transportation linkages to facilitate trade;
o  availability of communication linkages to facilitate trade;
o  population densities;
o  availability of social services and amenities to support the population; and
o  availability of supportive formal financial and credit institutions.
Table 16 lists the main crops produced, production in tons, and tons per hectare for the
growing season 1999 to 2000 for the eleven Districts the ENI passes through.
2.3.13    General Characteristics of Families within the District
A day for a typical family living along the Maputo to Inchope ENI road would involve
women working in the machambas. Men participate to some extent in the day-to-day
activities of the machambas, but this is normally confined to clearing of land and house
maintenance. In the house women will look after the children, cook, clean the house, and
would be involved in most daily household activities. Men are responsible for construction
and maintenance of the house.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects      February 2001                                 2-13



IMPA C'f()
The period of most work is between November and February, the main period of cultivation
and harvest. Women will control the household stocks of food while men will be responsible
for expenditure related to the children. The land cultivated will belong to the married couple.
In the case of the death of a spouse or divorce the belongings will be divided between the
wife and the family of the husband. Women of these areas will likely have more
responsibilities in terms of the day-to-day management of households due to the relatively
high levels of male migration.
