2.3.14    Archaeological and Cultural Sites
The Maputo to Inchope ENI already exists and in comparison to other roads in Mozambique
is in good condition. The road passes through no known archaeological or cultural sites.
Any planning for major extension to the road in terms of width or realignment should involve
consulting the appropriate Chefe de Poste (Chief of Post) to determine if any local cultural
sites, most probably cemeteries, are likely to be disturbed.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects       February 2001                                   2-14



IMPACT 0
3 POTENTIAL ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
3.1 IDENTIFICATION
This section provides an assessment of the potential environmental impacts associated with the
rehabilitation of the EN1 from Maputo to the Save River and from Muxungue to Inchope.
Environmental factors considered were:
o  land use change;
o  erosion and sedimentation;
o  water quality;
o  hydrology;
o  noise and vibration;
Li air quality;
o3 biological effects; and
o  socio-economic effects.
Each of these issues is addressed in sections covering the physical, biological and socio-
economic environments.
The majority of direct environmental impacts will be minor due to the limited extent of works
at any one site and the incorporation of impact mitigation measures into the improvement
works. The ENI currently has a sufficient and well defined road corridor which is identified
by ANE as 15m either side of the road centreline (Pieter Van Niekerk, pers. comm., ANE,
November 2000).
Indirect effects, may result from increased natural resource use due to increased local
enterprise activity to service increased traffic. These impacts are anticipated to be relatively
minor due to the fact that the road is already well established as the major north-south access
route in southern and central Mozambique.
Many of the potential environmental impacts associated with the construction of a new road do
not occur to the same degree when a road is improved within the existing road corridor. The
most likely impacts include:
oi altered land use patterns due to improved accessibility, such as a change from forestry to
agriculture, or from agriculture to industry (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997);
o  public health problems, particularly through the spread of communicable disease (Birley,
1997); and
oi increased illegal logging and poaching activities due to improved access into forest areas
(Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997).
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects   February 2001                             3-1



IMPA C'U
3.2 PHYSICAL EFFECTS
3.2.1    Land Use
i   Construction
The proposed road improvement works will not require a significant change to the land use
within the road corridor. Limited road improvement works will be undertaken and no road
realignments or clearances for improved visibility are anticipated. For the majority of its
length, there is no encroachment of cultivated land, woodland or settlements within the
existing corridor. However, it was observed that the road width and area cleared for the road
corridor is narrower along the Muxungue to Inchope section of the road than elsewhere along
the corridor.
The scope of road improvements is limited to the widening of the existing road formation and
the clearance of a range of land uses within the road corridor, including cultivated land, fallow
agricultural land, woodland and some grassland. In the towns and villages roadside stalls
('bancas'), and small bars ('barracas') are currently situated at distances of at least lOm from
the existing road centreline. There is generally no encroachment of village settlements along
the ENI with properties typically situated at distances in excess of 12m from the centre line.
Resettlement will not be a mitigation requirement.
Construction of additional roadside drains will be required particularly in areas where
gradients are steep, and also adjacent to villages.
The construction and installation of culverts are already underway along the ENI north of
Maxixe and south of the Vilanculos turnoff. Associated activities have been established as part
of these of current works such as the establishment of batching plants, work depots and
workforce camps, and the excavation of borrow pits.
Large borrow pits are frequently located close to the road corridor, generally within 1 5-20m of
the road edge. Where possible these existing borrow areas will be utilised during the road
works to avoid additional disturbance. The rehabilitation of these sites after completion of the
works will be undertaken through revegetation and levelling the landform to its original
condition. Where new borrow pits are established these will be located at least lOOm from the
road in accordance with current ANE guidelines.
A range of temporary ancillary activities will be required during the construction works but
will affect relatively small land areas. These sites will be rehabilitated upon completion of
activities.
ii   Land Use Change
Total land acquisition will be minimal relative to the project corridor length. Temporary areas
of land use change will occur at batching plant sites, borrow pits areas and workforce
campsites.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects     February 2001                                3-2



IMPAL1'U
The road improvement works will have a negligible indirect effect on adjacent land use
activities, but may result in the establishment of additional roadside services, such as Barracas
(roadside diners) and resting places, to service the additional traffic. The number of new
services is likely to be minor as the projected traffic increase for the road is within historic
growth rates.
The existing borrow areas are clearly visible scars on the landscape and reflect a number of
environmental problems. Steep grades and inappropriate land forming affects their future use
potential and creates a safety hazard to local people and wildlife. Specific problems include:
o soil instability;
o land degradation;
o inhibited re-establishment of native vegetation and limited suitability of land for
cultivation;
o inability to replace topsoil for revegetation; and
o ponding of water through lack of drainage which promotes insect vector diseases.
iii    Mitigation Measures
Rehabilitation and land forming of existing borrow pit sites will result in an environmental
improvement, as rehabilitation will be designed to mitigate these issues.
Mitigation measures for other temporary facilities include:
o measures identified under socio-economics for selection and negotiation of use; and
o rehabilitation of all sites to their original condition, or better, to allow resumption of prior
land use.
3.2.2    Erosion and Sedimentation
i   Erosion Hazard
The dynamic interplay of several key factors dictates the degree of susceptibility to water-
driven soil erosion processes. These are:
o seasonality and intensity of rainfall in relation to water run-off;
o landscape topography in relation slope gradients;
o soil profile parameters, notably depth and binding properties;
o degree of vegetation cover; and
o magnitude and frequency of seismic events.
For road works in general, where the capacity of existing drainage works to manage water run-
off is exceeded then the interaction of the above factors will determine the extent and
magnitude of erosion processes. These considerations provide the basis to determine the
overall erosion hazard. The erosion risk will generally be low along the majority of the ENI
because the terrain is predominantly flat to undulating. Localised erosion can occur where
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects     February 2001                                3-3



IMPA CL'O
sandy soils are left exposed. This is commonly observed on steep un-rehabilitated borrow pit
batters borrow pit batters where gully or rill erosion is evident. Similar problems can occur on
fill batters or other sloped areas.
The climate along the total stretch of the ENI is broadly comparable and typical of
Mozambique with rainfall patterns showing 80% of the rainfall recorded between November
and March. The erosion risk will be highest during this period.
The only section of the road that has a high frequency of seismic activity is between
Muxungue and Inchope. This section of the ENI falls within the zone of high seismic activity
which extends from the Rift Valley (Refer to Annexure C). The erosion risk along this section
is not exacerbated by the seismic potential as the road is located in rolling terrain and does not
cross valley sides or steep slopes which may be susceptible to seismic induced instability.
The ENI between Maputo and Inchope traverses a range of soil types. Main soil types include
alluvial deposits, sedimentary soils, volcanic rock and pre-Cambrian soils following
predominantly flat to undulating terrain gradually inclining as it approaches Inchope. The
greatest slopes traversed along the ENI road projects are up to 6%, occurring north of the
Limpopo River along the section between Xai Xai to Inhambane where the terrain is
characterised by rolling hills.
The overall erosion risk is low and is determined by a combination of deep to moderately deep
soils together with gentle slopes of less than 6%.
ii   Sedimentation Risk
Sedimentation is a secondary process of erosion and can be exacerbated by road works due to:
u deliberate and unanticipated modifications to surface and ground water hydrology;
o incomplete rehabilitation of roadside verge areas; and
o road rehabilitation through naturally hilly terrain.
Ground disturbance and the temporary stockpiling of materials during road works can trigger
uncontrolled and or accelerated sedimentation processes. The onset of this process will be
coincident with ground disturbance and will continue up until sites have been stabilised in the
road works zone. The extent of ground disturbance and clearing along the road corridor will
be marginal, with an average width of 16m disturbed during the construction stage.
Other areas where ground disturbance and clearing is required during the construction stage is
at stockpile areas, borrow pit sites, construction camps, and work compounds. The fill
required for the road works will be obtained from existing borrow sites or degraded areas
where possible to minimise the need for additional ground disturbance.
Borrow pits located along the side of the road are a common sight and are a source of scars on
the landscape. Some are located as close as 15m to the road edge and have not been
rehabilitated. Some are very large deep and have been left with steep slopes which are in most
cases almost vertical. The lack of levelling and topsoiling of these areas has prevented the
recovery of natural revegetation and the steep gradients and depth of the pits provides a safety
hazard to the local communities and fauna and are also at risk from erosion.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                               3-4



IMPAL 7U
The risk of uncontrolled sedimentation can also be exacerbated when the road traverses
wetlands and other surface water bodies. Sedimentation problems may occur during times of
heavy rain where the road becomes submerged due to back water flow from water courses and
drainage lines.
iii    Mitigation Measures
The provision of culverts along the road will improve cross-road drainage, reducing
sedimentation effects and scouring within drainage lines. This in turn will improve the
stability of road embankments and minimise sedimentation to the waterways.
Minor reconditioning of the existing culverts will be undertaken where their discharge is
causing downstream erosion. Specific measures for erosion and sediment control include:
o  design of drainage works to ensure these collect from and discharge to natural drainage
lines. This may not always be possible for mitre drains and in those situations frequent,
low volume drains are preferred;
Li provision of energy dissipation devices where discharge velocities and flows will erode
downstream drainage lines;
o  minimisation of disturbed areas;
o  design and construction of erosion and sedimentation controls such as diversion banks,
filter structures (filter fences or windrowed vegetation) and sediment traps;
o  diversion of significant upstream flows around working areas; and
o3 undertaking progressive rehabilitation and revegetation of all slopes and disturbed areas.
In addition, temporary erosion control drains will be installed prior to the wet season and no
new road excavation will be commenced during the wet season.
There are no significant slopes that the ENI intersects for the length of the project and no
significant erosion stabilisation measures, such as retaining walls will be required on road fill
or cut areas.
Establishment of vegetation will be undertaken to prevent localised rill erosion on slopes such
as batters and roadside embankments. Stone pitching may be required near culverts and
bridges where the embankments of the road approaches are steeper than a 1:2 grade.
The project provides an opportunity to undertake the planting of trees for soil conservation
purposes along the road corridor and within existing degraded areas, particularly gullies,
barren terrain, rivers and at borrow pit sites.
Vegetated embankments will minimise the effects of sedimentation during times of inundation.
The road rehabilitation works are likely to minimise erosion and sedimentation effects to the
rivers and streams with the implementation of roadside drainage and construction of culverts.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects      February 2001                                 3-5



IMPAL'iV
3.2.3    Water Quality
i   Construction
Sedimentation and resultant water quality effects were discussed in the previous section. This
section looks at other water quality issues.
Hydrocarbons from used lubricants sourced from improper disposal facilities and poor
operation and maintenance practices for construction vehicles and machinery can contaminate
surface waters. Improper disposal of oil filters and other materials from vehicle and machinery
maintenance can also cause pollution. This can occur during both construction and road
operation, but mostly during the former.
Spilled petroleum fuel, as well as bitumen and explosives residue, also have a potential to
contribute to the pollution of water bodies, but to a very minor extent.
Improper sanitation and waste disposal procedures in temporary construction camps can also
contaminate water resources.
ii    Operation
Borrow areas have the potential to result in adverse environmental impacts if they are not
rehabilitated correctly. If left in poor condition water can collect in the depressions created
and become weed infested as well as providing optimal breeding conditions for insect vectors
of parasitic diseases such as malaria.
iii    Mitigation Measures
All fuels, lubricants and bitumen will be stored in bunded areas. Vehicle maintenance and
refuelling will be confined to areas in construction camps designed to confine lubricants and
fuel spillages.
Adequate waste disposal and sanitation facilities will be provided at construction camps. Any
waste water or stormwater discharged from the site shall comply with the following objectives:
o  PH range                         6.0-9.0;
u  Total Suspended Solids (TSS)    <50mg/l;
o  Total Dissolved Solids (TDS)    <1500mg/l; and
o  Oils and surfactants            No visible film of oils or petrochemicals.
The Contractor shall ensure that all bitumen and fuel storage areas shall be bunded to contain
at least 120% of the maximum capacity of the largest storage tank. The floor of the bunded
area shall be impervious, and storage facilities and bunds shall be inspected weekly for spills.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects      February 2001                                 3-6



IMPACf'U
3.2.4    Hydrology
i   Construction
The road works will have a negligible impact to hydrology as the rehabilitation works will be
undertaken along an existing alignment. The main construction activity that has the potential
to impede flows will be the stockpiling of materials near drainage lines and wetland areas.
ii   Operation
The road crosses several major rivers and small stream crossings as discussed earlier. The
road is an existing feature, and culverts have already been installed along major watercourses
and have been reassessed as part of the emergency works after last year's major floods. The
provision of culverts along the road have been determined for engineering purposes as a
measure for protecting the road structure and they are therefore not within the scope of this
study.
The adequate provision of culverts along wetland areas is a concern for maintaining the
ecological regime, particularly near Macia and towards Vilanculos. This issue is discussed
later in this Chapter.
iii   Mitigation Measures
Particular attention shall be given to stockpiling materials away from existing drainage lines
and wetland areas to minimise sedimentation.
All designed drainage works shall be installed at the time of initial road construction to ensure
that flows along drainage lines are not impeded.
New or rehabilitated culverts shall be laid in a direction, which is close to the natural position
of the drainage flow. This will avoid altering the channel flow and prevent upstream and
downstream scouring of the watercourse.
3.2.5    Noise and Vibration
i   Construction
Some sensitive receptors, such as schools and hospitals, are located within 5-lOm of the ENI
road corridor. Additional noise will be generated along the ENI road corridors and at ancillary
sites during the construction period, particularly from the increased number of heavy vehicles.
Vibration effects will be negligible due to the generally sandy soil substrate. Peak noise levels
generated by construction activities are expected to be greater than the peak noise levels from
road operation.
Construction noise is generally intermittent and depends on the type of operation, location and
function of the equipment and the equipment use cycle. Noise attenuates quickly over
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects     February 2001                                3-7



IMPAC'TO
distance. Excavation noise levels, for example, assuming bulldozer and dump truck activity,
yield a level of approximately 85 dBA at 15m, but decrease by about 3-4 dBA with each
doubling of the setback distance, and would be reduced to approximately 70 dBA at about
240m.
Noise levels during the operation of the improved ENI road projects will increase due to an
increase in traffic and vehicle speeds.
ii   Operation
Accessibility will be improved following the road works but improvements are not expected to
generate additional traffic. Traffic growth is expected to be similar to historic growth. Noise
levels from traffic will not be increased as a result of the project.
The improved road design will allow an increase in vehicle speed and some increase in the
overall noise level is likely to result. When traffic speed doubles, this results in a 6 dB(A)
increase in noise level (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997). The majority of the ENI road projects
traverse open cultivation land and wetland areas, the impact from an increase in noise levels
from increased traffic speed will be minimal.
The road works may reduce existing noise levels in built-up areas as a result of improved road
surface. This will reduce noise levels through the more efficient operation of vehicles and less
noise from vehicle tyres.
As ambient noise levels are generally lowest at night, the impact from traffic noise is expected
to be greater during these,hours.
iii   Mitigation Measures
No national noise level standards exist for highway construction equipment, however the
World Bank recommends the use of construction equipment, which meets international
standards.
The following measures shall be implemented to minimise the impact of noise during the
construction phase:
Ei work compounds shall be located at least 0.5km from settlements;
Li construction shall not occur between 19:00 and 06:00 hours at sites within 150m of
residential areas; and
Li maintenance of machinery and vehicles shall be undertaken and repaired regularly to keep
noise at a minimum.
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects   February 2001                               3-8



IMPAC'WU
3.2.6    Air Quality
i      Construction
The deterioration in air quality from dust generation and vehicle emissions during road
construction will be a relatively minor environmental impact. Significant adverse effects are
not expected because of the limited duration of construction at any one site and the few
receptors. Most of the work will occur in open, cultivated areas.
A deterioration in air quality during road construction will primarily occur from dust generated
by road excavation, spoil transportation and disposal. In addition, most construction activities
will progressively move along the alignment, so prolonged deterioration in air quality will not
occur at any one site. The minor volumes of dust generated will cause a short-term localised
problem through settlements. Road construction workers will be exposed to minor dust
emissions over the construction period, with the quantity of dust partly dependent upon wind
conditions.
Exhaust emissions from traffic utilising the rehabilitated road will be minor due to a low
expected traffic growth (refer to Part B).
ii     Operation
Existing air quality is good and no significant air quality effects are expected during operation.
Traffic volumes are low and the road generally traverses rural areas.
iii    Mitigation
Mitigation measures will be implemented in areas where air quality problems would arise due
to construction activities. Areas of concern for dust control are in settlements and close to
sensitive sites such as schools and hospitals.
Minimising air emissions is particularly important during the construction stage, for activities
such as the operation of borrow pits and in-situ mixing of cement and sand, which have a
potential to generate high dust levels.
During the construction period, the following mitigation measures to suppress dust will be
implemented:
Li Water will be sprayed during the construction period at earth mixing sites, asphalt-mixing
sites, and on cleared surfaces. Water spraying will be undertaken during periods of high
wind, or when necessary, to suppress dust.
c  Operations shall be suspended during periods of dry windy weather, in areas where wind
blown dust is causing a nuisance in settlements or towns.
u  All equipment and plant machinery shall not emit visible smoke for a continuous period of
greater than 10 seconds.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects     February 2001                                3-9



IMPAL'i(
o All exhaust systems and engines on plant and equipment shall be in accordance with the
manufacturers specifications and kept well maintained to ensure that they have a high level
operating efficiency.
3.3 BIOLOGICAL EFFECTS
3.3.1    Direct Impacts
Vegetation will need to be cleared to undertake the road rehabilitation works and enable road
formation widening and construction of additional drainage. The conservation value of the
road corridor vegetation that will be cleared is not high. The woodland areas crossed by the
road corridor are highly utilised, particularly for slash and burn agriculture, which has been
undertaken adjacent to the ENI road projects. The amount of clearing that will need to be
undertaken will be minimal as the road corridor is well defined for the majority of its length.
The impact of any clearing required for the road projects will not be significant due to the
already highly degraded nature of the woodland habitat.
Vegetation may also be cleared in areas selected for ancillary sites, such as construction
camps, borrow pits and quarries, to enable the utilisation of these areas.
There are no known wildlife crossings along the length of the ENI road projects and the
habitat has been highly modified by anthropogenic uses. There is no potential for significant
adverse effects on wildlife as there is only one area along the road between Maputo and
Inchope that supports relatively intact woodland. This is within a hunting area (Coutada area
No. 5) which lies between the Rio Save and Muxungue. This section of the highway is not
included as part of the ENI road projects for Phase 1.
3.3.2   Indirect Impacts
There is a potential impact associated with the indirect loss and degradation of vegetation,
habitat and species in the ROI. These potential impacts are associated with:
u improved access;
La in-migration; and
o construction workforce behaviour.
Ecological degradation of the ROI may occur from vegetation harvesting and wildlife
poaching by the construction workforce, and increased harvesting during road operation due to
greater accessibility and additional local demands from road-induced activities.
The projects will provide improved access to the neighbouring areas and facilitate natural
resource use. The main potential for increased natural resource harvesting is likely to occur
within the area's woodlands.
The population increase along the road corridor would be derived either from people migrating
to the roadside from the immediate area (i.e. the 5km region of influence and adjacent to this),
or from other areas. This in-migration has a potential to create an increased local demand for
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              3-10



IMPA U'O
fuelwood and construction timber, as well as other woodland products to support secondary
enterprise activities, such as forage collection for grazing animals. Hunting may also increase.
The proposed road improvements will follow an existing alignment and almost all forest areas
neighbouring this road are currently accessible. The road will only increase potential access to
these forests, not open them up.
Importing a construction workforce into an area may increase demand on local natural
resources due to competition for firewood, woodland products and wildlife. These increased
demands will be focussed on the areas surrounding workforce camps and work compounds.
Worker camps may also encourage the temporary construction of markets and barracas to
service the workers. These temporary facilities may also increase demand on local natural
resources.
3.3.3    Mitigation Measures
i   Direct Impacts
Vegetation clearance along the road edge and at ancillary sites (such as borrow pits) will be
minimised. All construction areas will be clearly marked and construction activity prohibited
outside the defined areas. Tress within ancillary sites shall be retained wherever possible.
Retention of trees within areas such as worker camps will improve the camp amenity and
provide shade.
All temporarily disturbed areas will be rehabilitated following construction. The establishment
of ground cover is the initial priority of revegetation. Ground cover reduces raindrop impact,
binds the soil surface and creates a micro-climate favourable for the establishment of other
vegetation. Species should be selected for cover crops and permanent revegetation. A cover
crop consisting of a rapid growing species and several vigorous grass species should be used to
rapidly establish ground cover on disturbed areas. Permanent revegetation species should be
selected to be consistent with the local native species in the area.
Revegetation will aim to stabilise the road verges and assist the many desirable secondary uses
of the road including:
i production of forage, wood, flowers, fruit, medicinal items;
o shade and shelter;
o fencing and screens; and
o building supports.
Adequate and stable cross-road and road surface drainage is essential to ensure that the effect
of inundation caused by impeded flows is minimised. There are large areas of inundation and
wetland areas along the road, particularly near Macia and towards Vilanculos. These areas
have experienced a change in habitat regime due to prolonged inundation from last years
floods. In these areas, the road forms a barrier and divides the wetlands into two separate
hydrologic bodies and has resulted in alterations to the wetland ecology.
It is recommended that a wetland ecologist inspect the route to determine the need for
additional culverts or waterway structures to reconnect these wetland areas.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              3-11



IMPA CL'
ii   Indirect Impacts
The most potential for indirect effects was identified to be associated with workforce camps.
This is a temporary effect but may last several years depending on the duration of the works
project. To reduce the potential for increased exploitation of natural resources the following
migration measures will be implemented. The Contractor will:
o provide and maintain adequate drinking and bathing water supplies;
o provide kerosene or other fuel source and stoves for worker use;
Li educate workers about the responsible use of natural resources; and
u ensure workers act in a responsible manner and apply penalties for the harvesting of local
resources (wood, wildlife or wood products).
3.4 SOCIO-ECONOMIC EFFECTS
3.4.1    Direct Impacts
i   Loss of socio-economic assets
Improvements to the road will not involve the significant loss of cultivated land and no
displacement of population is required. Cultivation is not continuous along the road and there
is sufficient land for machambas if necessary to be realigned. No major building would be lost
to medium road improvement along the road.
Where an area of cultivated land has to be resumed for road construction, the farmer will be
compensated for loss of production. The following process will be applied:
u where possible the farmer should be notified of the loss of land one season in advance.
This is to avoid unnecessary loss of effort and seed; and
o the value of the lost production should be determined. Compensation will be paid to the
farmer for the value of the lost production.
Borrow pits, storage and equipment areas, and construction camps will be located on marginal
or disturbed land. The acquiring of land for such purposes must be subject to ANE and
Provincial approval. In selecting land, consultation must also be undertaken with local
communities, their traditional leaders as well as local authorities (at District and Ward level).
The EN1 road is Mozambique's most used road. It is also Mozambique's most important road
in terms of the north to south link it provides between the nations capital and the rest of the
country. The Maputo to Inchope section of the ENI has already undergone extensive
rehabilitation and development over the last five-years. It is unlikely that further minor
development or maintenance is likely to have significant impacts on loss of agricultural land,
buildings, sites of cultural value, population displacement. Any improvements are also
unlikely to effect natural resource degradation along or in close proximity to the road.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects   February 2001                              3-12



IMPACL'7
3.4.2   Indirect Impacts
i   Access to Services
Improvements to the Maputo to Inchope road are unlikely to have any more significant socio-
economic impacts in terms of access to services. Availability of services to the populations of
the area although relatively poor is better than most other areas in rural Mozambique.
Improvement of the road may facilitate the ability of for example sick patients to gain better
access to health services.
ii   Improved Access to Markets
Improvements to the Maputo to Inchope should enable goods and services to flow more easily
into the area particularly those associated with improving agricultural production techniques
and yields. A better quality of road would likely reduce transport costs both fuel consumption
and vehicle wear.
iii   In-migration of People
Improvement of roads in other rural areas within Mozambique leads often to increases in in-
migration as advantage is taken of the improved communication. It is probable that some in-
migration will take place particularly in areas in close proximity to the ENI road. The road
already exists and has been trafficable for several years (excluding floods). It is unlikely that
significant in-migration will occur as a direct result of the ENI road projects.
iv    Health Issues
It is likely that the road projects will necessitate the inflow of a number of construction
workers from other parts of Mozambique. The inflow of road gangs, particularly in remote
areas, has a potential for adverse effects. The most often cited problem is the one of HIV-AIDs
and other sexually transmitted diseases. Mitigation measures to prevent these impacts require
working closely with respective government departments; local NGOs, church based
organisations, local communities, and road gangs.  Educational programs for the local
populations are considered to be extremely important in trying to reduce the likelihood of HIV-
AIDs infection.
v    Construction Worker Camps
The health issues related to construction worker camps particularly in terms of the spread of
HIV-AIDs and other sexually transmitted disease have been mentioned above.  A
concentration of workers also attracts other inward migrants who attempt to exploit new
market opportunities. In other parts of Mozambique this have often resulted in the creation of
small-unplanned roadside shops. Such informal sites are often poorly managed in terms of
adherence to health regulations and waste disposal. Mitigation measures should include the
identification of suitable sites for the establishment of small shops and other facilities servicing
road-gangs.
58602 Annex E- EN1 Road Projects   February 2001                              3-13



IMPAL'i'O
vi   Recommendations
In conclusion road improvements to the Maputo to Inchope ENI would likely have some
positive impacts. Improved communication would improve the ability of local populations to
transport surplus agricultural production to market. It would also likely to stimulate more
production if farmers knew they had a means of transporting their goods. A better road is
likely to decrease maintenance costs on vehicles as potholes and other problems would be
improved. There are unlikely to be any major loss of land and therefore agricultural
production is unlikely to be effected.
The main negative socio-economic impacts relate to the problem of the spread of HIV-AIDs
infection. Campaigns in other parts of Mozambique have demonstrated that these problems can
be mitigated against particularly if resources and education are directed at the local population
and not just the influx of construction workers.
58602 Annex E- ENI Road Projects  February 2001                           3-14



IMPAC'fO
4 UPGRADING & MAINTENANCE MEASURES
The road works will include environmental impact mitigation measures within the Contract
Specifications. These measures will be incorporated as specific instructions and
recommendations to the Contractor and will be provided in a separate Environmental
Management Plan (EMP). The EMP will identify specific instructors to the Contractor and
will include responsibilities, outputs, costs and timing of mitigation measures.
Design, construction and maintenance mitigation recommendations for the proposed roads
have been formulated to avoid or mitigate potential biophysical impacts.  These
recommendations were primarily formulated from observations made during the inspection
of constructed roads.
The EMP describes the practical means to ensure effective implementation of the mitigation
measures recommended for the potential adverse environmental impacts identified in the
EIA.
The EMP will include but will not be limited to the following elements:
l practical means to implement the recommended mitigation measures;
a  implementation schedule and supervision programme;
Li monitoring and evaluation;
u  institutional strengthening;
o  management and training; and
u  further studies of environmental issues.
In addition, the program also specifies what action will be taken and by whom in the event
that the proposed mitigation measures fail, either partially or totally, to achieve the level of
environmental protection expected.
An outline of the EMP for the construction and operation stage is provided in Part E of this
report.

1 BIOLOGICAL & PHYSICAL ENVIRONMENTS
1.1 TOPOGRAPHY AND LANDFORMS
The road is located in the mid-altitude region of Nampula (the region defined as the area
between 2000 and I OOOm above sea level).
Throughout most of it's alignment the road passes over gently undulating ground at elevations
of between 420m above sea level (e.g., at Nampula City) and 650m above sea level).
A characteristic feature of the landscape is the spectacular granitic outcrops that rise up sharply
from plains. These granitic outcrops known as inselbergs may achieve heights of 1500m
(e.g. Mount Ribaue). The Project road usually lies several kilometres from the base of these
outcrops except approaching lapala, where the alignment passes over the base of Mt Malasa
(1300m asl).
The most spectacular inselbergs are:
o  Serra Chinga (maximum elevation 1,410m);
o Mt Mpalue (maximum elevation 1,777m);
o Mt. Ribaue (maximum elevation 1,554m); and
o Mt Malasa (maximum elevation at 1,314m).
The road is bisected by several perennial streams/river. These flow in a south-north direction.
The most important being:
o  Rio Namatara;
o  Rio Miava;
a  Rio Nhamaquete;
o  Rio Mutivaze;
o  Rio Mocubiri;
o  Rio Mequipui; and
o  Rio Natete.
Through the alignment the road passes through a mosaic of disturbed miombo woodland and
small-scale farms (mainly maize and cassava).
1.2 CLIMATE
The climate in the area of the Nampula to lapala road is classified as dry sub-humid with two
climatic seasons namely a hot and humid rainy season between December and March, and a
cooler and drier season from April to November. The mean annual monthly temperature
averages vary between a low of 21C in July, and a high of 27.5 C in November. Monthly
maximum and minimum average temperatures range between 29.5-32C and 15-19
respectively (Figure 1).

The climate within the area is distinctly drier in comparison to coastal regions with the mean
annual rainfall varying between 1000 and 1200mm. The dry season is relatively long,
extending from May to November. Relative humidity broadly correlates with rainfall; lower
during the dry season and increasing up to73% during the wet season (Figure 2).

The dominant prevailing winds within the area are easterlies (E and NE) and southerlies
(Figure 3). Over the period 1999-2000, no cyclones affected Nampula Province (Instituto
Nacional de Meteorologia).
1.3 GEOLOGY
Geological formations of the Pre-Cambrian period (more than 600 million years BP) cover
most of the area. The most important formations comprise granitoid migmatites (rich in
quartz) and in the Nampula super-group two major complexes can be distinguished, namely:
o Gneissic migmatites (P): which cover the major portion of Zambezia Province and include
biotites and amphibolites.
i Granitic migmatites (y): which are important mineralogically and include quartzites,
marble and graphite.
A great portion of the study area is comprised of acidic rocks due to their percentage quartz
content and the soils derived from these are typically sandy. The area is characterised by a
slightly undulating plain where low hills or inselbergs and dambos are found. The height of
these inselbergs varies between 600 and 1200m, while the height of the plain is normally
between 200 and 500m. The major geological features of the region are depicted in Figure 4.

1.4 SOILS
The Nampula-lapala road predominately passes through red sandy soils, red clayey soils, and
brown-grey sandy soils. The distribution of these soil types along the road is illustrated in
Figure 5 and a description of the soil types is given in Table 1.
The soils originate in their majority from acidic rocks and are characteristically red soils
(sometimes brown) of medium to fine texture. These soils are generally well to moderately
well drained and of varying thickness in the superficial sand layer. The clay percentage of
these soils varies between 35 and 50 %. At the bottom of the valleys or dambos are deep
hydromorphic soils that are poorly drained. The structure of the soils originating from acidic
rocks is generally weak to very weak in sandy soils and weak to moderate in soils of medium
to heavy texture (Semo, 1995).

1.5 LAND USE
Small settlements are located at varying intervals along the Nampula to lapala project road and
the larger settlements of Mutivaze, Namina, Ribaue and lapala are characterised by relatively
higher population densities.
The road passes through the following vegetation types - medium thickets, open woodland,
low altitude closed forests and other herbaceous communities all interspersed with cultivated
areas.
Approximately 15-30% of the District is cultivated by family sectors with an average plot size
of 0.5 to 1.5ha generally cleared by slash and burn techniques. The most commonly grown
crops are cassava, maize, groundnuts, sorghum, millet, cotton and sweet potato. These are
grown using a multi-cropping system with more than one yield (average four crops in Nampula
Province) grown in each field.
1.6 RIVERS AND WATER QUALITY
1.6.1    Rivers and Hydrology
The most important drainage basins near the project road are those of the Mecuburi and
Monapo Rivers. These rivers' flow rates vary considerably throughout the year and 90% of
the total volume of discharge occurs between December and April. Characteristically, most of
the rivers do not carry water during the dry season (Figure 6).
60 
MMecubuiri River
40      __i|*PtW                ~|mm31
30                    v                  EMonapo River
IZP  K l-               l N. ,  rn mm3
20 l iSt           
Oct  Dec   Feb   Apr  Jun   Aug
Figure 6:    Monthly average flow rate of Monapo and Mecuburi Rivers (Flow rate:
million cubic meters/month)
There is a marked difference between the levels of the water table in areas with mainly acidic
rocks (concave almost level ground) and the bottom of valleys with alkaline/base rocks. In the
former, the water table is at or near the surface during the dry season which means that the
population has access to water throughout the entire year, even if only in reduced amounts for
58602 Annex E- Nampula-lapala     February 2001                              1-7
Mozambique Road Sector EA



IMPAC IO
domestic purposes. The valley floors with alkaline rocks run dry soon after the rains stop and
unless deep wells are dug, the availability of water is a limiting factor to human settlement in
these areas. These circumstances are linked with the fact that the sandy nature of the soils of
the acidic rocks allows for the infiltration of water into the soils during the rainy season. In
contrast, the muddy soils of the alkaline rocks, with low infiltration capacity, allow for fast
run-off and little infiltration into the soils with the result that in the dry season the bottom of
the valleys no longer contain surface water (Semo, 1995).
1.6.2    Water Quality
Watercourses within the project area experience better water quality when river flows are
highest and dilution is greater. This occurs during the rainy season from November to March,
with flows generally reaching a peak in February and March. However, the rivers have the
highest sediment levels and are most turbid during the rainy season, when overland runoff and
erosion are at their greatest. River flows are lowest during the dry season months of June to
September, and the majority of the watercourses crossed by the road are perennial with no flow
during this time.
Rivers and ponds in the vicinity of the proposed roads are free of chemical pollutants due to
the absence of industry.  Despite this, these water sources may be microbiologically
contaminated near settlement areas due to poor sanitation practices.
1.7 VEGETATION COVER
The stretch of the project road between Nampula and Nova Chave is characterised by
Deciduous Woodland Miombo-Discontinuous Dry Forest-Savanna (Brachystegia spiciformis,
B. boehmii - Adansonia, Sterculia, Bombax). This vegetation type is confined to Mozambique
and covers the subplanaltic zone between 50 and 500m in soils, which are generally red to grey
and derived from granite or gneiss. Figure 7 shows the distribution of the major vegetation
types in areas adjoining the road.
The major vegetation communities include:
o  closed woodland, 12-18m high (including Brachystegia spiciformis, B. behmii);
o  scattered  patches of deciduous  forests (including  Adansonia  digitata, Bombax
rhododnaphalon, Sterculia appendiculata);
o  savanna woodland of medium  height (including Brachystegia boehmii, Julbernardia
globiflora, pterocarpus angolensis);
o  tree-shrub savanna in lower lying areas (including Acacia polyacantha, A. nigrescens,
kigelia pinnata); and
o  deciduous savanna or woodland (including Adansonia, Sterculia, Dalbergia).

Mozambique Road Sector EA

Additionally, on the rocky outcrops or inselbergs near Nampula and Ribaue succulent or
xerophytic plants such as Aloe sp. A. Mawii, A. chabaudii, A. corniculata, and Vellozia spp are
abundant (Wild and Barbosa, 1967).
West of the project road, towards Ribaue and lapala, the vegetation composition is deciduous
miombo savanna woodland (Brachystegia spiciformis - Julbernardia globiflora). In areas
bordering vleis (wetlands) and dambos, there is savanna woodland of Brachystegia boehmii,
Julbernardia globiflora, Burkea africana and Parinari curatellifolia amongst others, whilst
secondary areas are typically characterised by Entada abyssinica, Piliostigma thonningii,
Annona senegalensis and Antidesma venosum.
1.8 WILDLIFE
Five forest reserves were gazetted in Nampula Province in 1950, namely Mpalu&, Ribawe,
Matibane, Baixo Pinda and Mecuburi forest reserves. The first two are relatively close to the
project road. Currently the status of wildlife populations in these Reserves is uncertain. During
a recent fieldtrip bushpig, rock hyraxes, and klipspringers were observed (Costa, 1998). The
local communities using dogs, nets and traps commonly hunt the first two.   The latter
however, is a rare species and law in Mozambique prohibits hunting. The use of fire as a
hunting technique is also widespread.
Over the last few years, there has been a considerable reduction in wildlife numbers.
According to Costa (1998), eighteen mammal species were known to occur in these Reserves
(Table 2). At present, it is believed that most of these are locally extinct and even the small
solitary forest-dwelling antelope (suni and duiker) have been eradicated.
Table 2: Mammal species recorded for the forest Reserves of Mpalue and Ribawe
COMMON NAME                   SCIENTIFIC NAME                HABITAT PREFERENCE
Bushpig                       Potamochoerusporcus            Dense forest
Klipspringer                  Oreotragus oreotragus          Rocky outcrops
Suni                          Neotragus moschatsus           Dense forest
Red Duiker                    Cephalophus natalensis         Dense forest
Common duiker                 Silvicapra grimmia             Dense forest
Rock hyrax                    Procavia capensis              Rocky outcrops
Yellow-spotted rock hyrax     Heterohyrax brucei             Rocky outcrops
Tree hyrax                    Dendrohyrax arboreus           Dense forest
Yellow baboon                 Papio cynocephalus             Variety of habitats
Simango monkey                Cercopithecus mitis            Dense forest
Vervwet monkey                Cercopithecus aethiops         Variety of habitats
Slender bushbaby              Galago senegalensis            Woodland habitats
Thick-tailed bushbaby         Galago crassicaudatus          Dense forest
Cane rat                      Thryonomis swinderianus        Variety of habitats
Pygmy cane rat                Thryonomis gregorianus         Variety of habitats
Many of the species listed in Table 2 are restricted to the habitats associated with granite
inselbergs. These faunal assemblages are very vulnerable specifically because of the limited
extent and restricted distribution of their preferred habitat.

1.9 CONSERVATION AREAS
Two forest reserves, namely Reserva Florestal de Ribaue (5,100ha) and Reserva Florestal de
Mepalue (5200ha) are located West of Ribaue, in relative proximity to the project road (see
Figure 8). They were created as Integral Forest Reserves (with fully protected resources as
opposed to partial reserves where use of selected species is permitted) in 1950 with two main
purposes, i) to safeguard the rare evergreen mountain forest of the summits and higher slopes
and ii) to protect the catchment areas that feed the drainage basins of the Mpwipwe-Lalawa-
Lurio Rivers (Costa, 1998).
Source: Carta Topografia, 1:250,000
Figure 8:    Forest Reserves of Ribaue and Mepalue
The area is characteristically hilly and of granite-gneissic complex with altitudes varying
between 500m on valley floors and up to 1,553m in Ribaue and 1,777m in Mepalue. Miombo
vegetation in the valleys and in altitudes of up to 1,OOOm include species such as Brachystegia-
Julbernardia-Uapaca whilst in the open terrain, in addition to and associated with the above,
one finds the following: Millettia, Terminalia, Sclerocarya and Parinari amongst others.
Along rivers, vegetation typical of the central and northern part of Mozambique is common,
namely Breonadia, Erythrophloeum, Landolphia, Bombax, Treculia, etc. In areas with
altitudes above 1,100-1,200m rare evergreen mountain forests are present with species such as
Chlorophora, Khaya, Phoenix, Xylia, Treculia and Funtumia.
Due to the lack of institutional capacity and control, local populations have moved into the
Reserves over the last few years destroying a substantial part of the forest along the valleys for
cultivation at altitudes below lOOOm and sometimes even in the evergreen forest area (Costa,
1998). On a broader scale these reserves form part of the great Inselberg Archipelago of
Northern Mozambique, one of four recognised biological hotspots in Mozambique (Hatton,
1998).
This series of habitats occurs south of the Lurio River occupying a rectangular area of
approximately 500km by 160km wide lying in a SW to NE direction (approximately between
140 to 17S; 35040'E to 380 E thence NE-wards to 39040'E).
58602 Annex E- Nampula-lapala     February 2001                             1-11
Mozambique Road Sector EA



IMPACIW
This Inselberg archipelago presents a truly remarkable landscape of tall granite core remnants
in a savanna plains land.
The Inselberg Region has high scenic value as well as high biological value characterised by
unique flora and fauna (endemics and biogeographical outliers) contained in the isolated
rainforest, patches of which form aprons around the flanks and base of the inselbergs. Many of
the areas are unknown biologically and it is highly likely that undescribed species remain to be
discovered. Like Gorongosa Mountain these inselbergs are high enough to result in a
continuous belt of high rainfall - an orographic perennial aquifer.
1.10 NOISE AND AIR QUALITY
Noise levels along the Nampula to lapala Project Road, are very low due to the currently low
traffic volumes and sparse settlement. Noise levels are predominantly generated by natural
conditions (for example wind, insects and birds). Noise levels increase within the larger
settlements, particularly near the market areas.
Ambient air quality in all of the proposed road areas is very good due to a lack of air polluting
industries and urban activities. Haze may be observed due to the frequently practiced slash
and burn agriculture and production of charcoal. Levels of airborne dust generated by winds
can also be high over agricultural fields, as can smoke from woodland and grassland fires.
58602 Annex E- Nampula-lapala      February 2001                              1-12
Mozambique Road Sector EA



IMPAUI'(U
2 SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
2.1 PROVINCIAL CHARACTERISTICS
The purpose of this section is to describe the socio-economic environment for the EN8 road
between Nampula and lapala in Nampula Province. Information has been derived from
existing reports and publications where information is disaggregated usually at the Provincial
and District levels. A field visit was made to the EN8 and Nampula City during November
2000 to supplement information available at the Provincial and District level.
To describe the living conditions for the populations living along or in proximity to the EN8 a
variety of Provincial and District socio-economic indices have been used. Where possible
statistical information has been provided to enable the establishment of base line data from
which the impacts of road improvement may be made.
2.1.1    Context and Administration
i   Provincial setting and administration
Nampula Province is located in the Central Region of Mozambique. To the north of Nampula
Province are the Provinces of Niassa and Cabo Delgado. Bordering to the south is Zambezia
Province and the eastern boundary is formed along the Indian Ocean.
Administratively Nampula is divided into 19 Districts, 71 Administrative Posts, and 103
Localities. The Provincial Capital, Nampula, and the settlements of Angoche, and Nacala-
Porto are designated as cities. Nampula is the third largest Province in Mozambique with a
land surface of 81,606 km2 representing 10.2% of the land surface of Mozambique.
ii   Political environment
In the 1999 elections the RENAMO political party received the majority of the votes cast in
Nampula Province. Political tension exists to the extent that RENAMO believe they should be
allowed to select a candidate as Governor of the Province to reflect their electoral superiority.
The issue of the political allegiance of the Governor is currently vigorously debated in
Mozambique.  Demonstrations in Nampula City against the general elections of 1999
organised by RENAMO resulted in the deaths of two demonstrators.
iii   Traditional administration
Traditional administration authority exists in Nampula Province, although in some Districts the
links between traditional authorities and formal administrative authorities are weak. The
importance attached to the regulos varies from area to area. In those localities where the
regulos are strong and respected he will intervene in a variety of aspects of every-day life.
Such issues would include resolving social conflicts; allocating land for building housing,
managing water sources, supporting official health programmes such HIV/AIDS.
58602 Annex E- Nampula-lapala     February 2001                             2-1
Mozambique Road Sector EA



IMPACI 
Like most parts of Mozambique, tensions exist between the two types of administrative
systems. These tensions were exaggerated during the armed conflict. Areas controlled by
RENAMO largely reverted back to traditional leadership structures, while those under
Government control retained their post-independence organisational structures.
2.1.2    Population Characteristics
i   Population
The 1997 national survey estimated the population of Nampula to be 2,975,747 inhabitants
with slightly more women (1,495,822) than men (1,479,925). Nampula is Mozambique's most
populated Province. The annual population growth rate is estimated at 2.2% and therefore by
2009 it is calculated that the population will have grown by 34% to about 3,900,500.
The population of Nampula Province is young with more than 1.3 million people under the age
of 14 representing 43% the total population. The population density for Nampula Province
(36.4 inhabitants per kM2) is higher than for the average population density for the country (19
inhabitants per kM2). The total urban population is calculated at 743,638 and the rural
population at 2,232,109. In comparison to other Provinces Nampula has a relatively high
urban-rural ratio with approximately 1 in 3 people living in one of the three designated cities.
ii   Ethnicity
The main ethnic group of Nampula Province is the Macua. The languages spoken reflect the
dominant ethnic group and the three most common languages are Emakhuwa (91%), Coti
(1.9%), Elomwe (0.4%), and Portuguese (5.6%).
iii   Gender
The main ethnic group the Macua are patrilineal meaning that inheritance follows the male
linage. Despite the advancement of the rights of women under independent Mozambican
administration, girls and women in Nampula Province do less well than boys and men. Access
to health services directed specifically at women is generally very poor. Literacy rates for
women are much lower than for men. Family law in Mozambique still discriminates against
women. A married woman has limited rights to make independent economic decisions or
enjoy full ownership of assets, and a widow's rights to inheritance are secondary to males in
the family. Girls in rural areas in general marry young often as soon as they reach puberty.
2.1.3    Economic Characteristics
i   Economy
The economy of Nampula was estimated to have generated approximately US$460 million
between 1996 and 1998 representing approximately 13% of Mozambique's annual output.
Agriculture is the main activity contributing about 40% by value. The category of fishing
contributed 8.5%, commercial activity 13.1%; transport and communication contributed 9% by
58602 Annex E- Nampula-lapala     February 2001                            2-2
Mozambique Road Sector EA



IMPA LV
value and manufacturing 7% by value. Recent agreements for the mining of titanium will have
a dramatic impact on investment and production for Nampula Province. In 1998 gross
domestic product per head was calculated at $176 with an annual growth rate projected as
15%.
ii   Poverty
According to the National Poverty Assessment (1996-97) 68.9 % of the population of Nampula
Province are categorised as poor. This equates to approximately two million people. This
compares with a national average rate of poverty of 69.4 %.
Life expectancy in Nampula Province (37 years) is below the average levels registered for the
country (42 years). Despite the relative high urban-rural ratio, the vast majority of the
population of Nampula Province (more than 75%) depends on agriculture, forestry and
fisheries for their livelihood, mostly comprising the family sector. There are few formal sector
employment opportunities except in the city of Nampula and Nacala.
2.1.4    Services
i   Education
In 1998 Nampula Province has a total of 1,117 schools: 1,061 lower primary (EP1) schools, 48
upper primary (EP2) schools, seven lower secondary, and one upper secondary (ESG2) school.
The number of students recorded was 286,649 and the number of teachers was 5,746. The
average ratio of pupils to teachers in the Province is 52.
ii   Health
Nampula Province has a total of 21 health units, including three hospitals, 43 health centres,
107 health posts and twenty first-aid posts. There are 54 maternity units distributed throughout
the Province. The illnesses occurring most frequently in Zambezia Province are malaria,
tuberculosis, bilharzia, intestinal parasites, acute respiratory infection, AIDS, sexually
transmitted diseases and non-specific diarrhoea. Current rates of HIV infection are estimated at
approximately 21% and are projected to rise to 25% by the year 2010. It is calculated that
about half of all children under five-years suffer from chronic malnutrition and I in 10 from
acute malnutrition.
iii   Access to services
Access to services for the rural population is generally poor. Most villages do have a primary
school (EPI) (72%), but these schools could be categorised as rudimentary and often
availability of teachers is very low. Figures are not available for the provision of secondary
schools. Most villages do have access to a basic health centre but less than 1% of villages
have access to a doctor. Dependence on traditional healers is the norm for the vast majority of
the population and almost every village would have at least one person categorised as a
traditional healer. Table 3 gives the availability of services on a village basis for Nampula
Province.

2.1.5     Livelihood strategies
The principle economic activity in Nampula Province is agriculture. Most food production is
within the family sector upon which the majority of the population is dependent. The principle
food crop grown is cassava (total production 2,451,576 tonnes) followed by maize (total

2.2 DISTRICT CHARACTERISTICS
This section describes socio-economic factors more specific to the EN8 road. The main
sources of information relate to existing written reports concerning the Districts the EN8
passes through. In addition, a brief field visit to the EN8 was made during November 2000.
58602 Annex E- Nampula-lapala           February 2001                                    2-4
Mozambique Road Sector EA



IMPAC'C)
2.2.1    Context and Administration
i   District Setting
The Nampula to lapala EN8 road passes through the Districts of Nampula and Mecuburi
located in the central-west of Nampula Province. To the north Mecuburi District borders Cabo
Delgado Province. In both District headquarters Nampula and Mecuburi representatives of the
main sector ministries such as health, education, and agriculture.are found.
The EN8 begins in the peri-urban environment on the outskirts of Nampula, within the District
of Nampula passes through the southern part of Mecuburi District and continues into Ribaue
District.
ii   Traditional Administration
Traditional structures exist, to a lesser or greater extent, in most localities side-by-side with the
more modern administrative apparatus of the State and political parties. In general terms the
first government of an independent Mozambique from 1975 attempted to replace the inherited
colonial and traditional systems with a centralised system based upon the political doctrines of
socialism.
iii   Political Environment
No information is available for voting practices based on the Districts of Nampula Province.
Nampula Province voted strongly for the opposition party RENAMO during the general
elections of late 1999.
2.2.2    Population Characteristics
i   Population
The 1997 national census estimated the population of Nampula District as 105,000 inhabitants
with a density of population of 29 inhabitants per km2. Mecuburi District has 119,000
inhabitants with a density of population of 16 inhabitants per km2. The District has three
administrative posts at Milhana, Mecuburi and Namina. Ribaue District has a population of
131,000 inhabitants with a density of population of 21 inhabitants per km2. The combined
population of Nampula, Mecuburi and Ribaue Districts is 355,000.
ii   Ethnicity
Most people living along the road are from the Macua ethnic group. Succession and heritage
for the Macua are patrilineal, and the children are considered to belong to the father. Family
succession is granted to the eldest son.
58602 Annex E- Nampula-lapala      February 2001                               2-5
Mozambique Road Sector EA



IMPACLV
iii    Gender
Inheritance follows a patrilineal system where belongings are passed from father to the son.
On average women will be more illiterate than men, more boys will than girls attend school,
and higher rates of malnutrition are found for girls than boys. Some of the activities carried
out at household level broken down between men and women are described later.
2.2.3     Economic Characteristics
i    Economy
The economy of the three Districts the road passes through is almost entirely based on
subsistence agriculture.
ii    Poverty
The main period of poor nutrition is the period between October and March when crop stores
are at there lowest and before the new seasons harvest. The principal deficiency in dietary
terms for all three Districts the road passes through is lack of vitamin A and iron leading to a
high incidence of anaemia. Chronic malnutrition for children under 3 years is estimated at on
average 42%. Acute malnutrition for the same age group is estimated at approximately 10%.
There is a problem related to intoxication due to the poor preparation of cassava mainly
causing problems of vomiting.
Categorizing families in terms of poor, medium, and rich surveys indicate that on average
within Ribaue, Nampula, and Mecuburi Districts between 70% to 80% of families are poor,
15% to 25 % medium and 0% to 10% rich. Table 5 gives examples of the characteristics of
poverty for an average household in Ribaue, Nampula, and Mecuburi Districts.

2.2.4    Services
i   Education
Ribaue District has 64 EPI schools, 2 EP2 schools, I secondary school, and one agricultural
technical college. Total number of children in school for the year of 1997 was calculated as
16,573 of which 35% were girls. Approximately 50% of all children receive at least one year
of primary schooling. Similar statistics are not available for Mecuburi and Nampula Districts.
Most schools are built of locally available materials (mud and thatch) and only a small
proportion from bricks. Boy's account for the majority of the schools population. The
situation becomes more acute as pupil's progress from primary to secondary. On average in
Nampula 75% of pupils are boys and 25% girls. The main reasons identified for the imbalance
between boys and girls at schools related to the early age of marriage (often as young as
twelve), initiation rites, and the lack of jobs for women after schooling. On average 71.7% of
the population of Nampula Province are illiterate with a higher incidence of illiteracy amongst
women (85.9%) than for men (56.7%).
ii   Health
Nampula District has 3 health centres (Rapale, Anchilo, and Namaita) and 10 health posts.
Mecuburi District has 8 health units and 5 Health Posts (Muite, Napai, Momane, Popue and
Namine). A health unit is a category of health provision below a health post. Within the
District there are 17 health workers (a category of employee with a minimum of a one year
recognised qualification in health work), 17 traditional birth attendants (registered), and 5 first
aid workers (employees who have undertaken a basic course in first aid). Ribaue District has 1
Rural Hospital based at the District headquarters (Ribaue), 1 health centre at lapala, and 5
health posts. The District has 4 doctors, 3 of which are based at the Rural Hospital.
Within Nampula Province 38.4% of children suffer from chronic malnutrition and 6.8% suffer
from acute malnutrition. The most frequently treated health conditions in the three Districts
are malaria, diarrhoea, intestinal parasites, respiratory infections (particularly during the
months of October/November and March/May), tuberculosis, leprosy, sexually transmitted
diseases, conjunctivitis, and bilharzia. The HIV infection rate for Nampula Province is
currently estimated at 17%. Only 6.6% have access to potable water.
iii   Key Social Services and Facilitates
Table 6 summarises by average the distribution of services and median distances for Ribaue,
Nampula, and Mecubiuri Districts. While the three Districts are relatively well served by
primary (EPI) schools access to secondary schools is poor. Availability of qualified medical
staff is extremely low and most families are dependent upon traditional healers to meet their
medical requirements.

2.2.5     Livelihood
i    Livelihood strategies
Subsistence agriculture is the main activity of families living in Ribaue, Nampula, and
Mecuburi Districts. The main food crops grown are cassava, maize, and sorghum. Nampula
District has the largest cultivated area (70,093ha) and the highest total production. Proximity
to Nampula City could be a main factor for the higher concentration of agricultural production
in Nampula District in comparison to Ribaue and Mecuburi Districts. Table 7 shows total

ii    General characteristics of a family living along or in close proximity to the
Nampula to lapala EN232/104 road
A typical day for a family living along the Nampula to lapala EN232/104 road would involve
women working in the machambas. Men would and not generally involved in the day-to-day
activities of the machambas, but are responsible for clearing and preparation of land. The
main agricultural system is still based upon slash and burn agriculture. Once land is cleared it
will be cultivated for between 2 to 4 years before being left to fallow for a similar period.
In the house women will look after the children, cook, clean the house, and would be involved
in most daily household activities. Men are responsible for construction and maintenance of
the house. The period of most work is between November and February, the main period of
cultivation and harvest. Women will control the household stocks of food while men will be
responsible for expenditure related to the children. The land cultivated will belong to the
married couple. In the case of the death of a spouse or divorce the belongings will be divided
between the wife and the family of the husband.
2.2.6    Archaeological and cultural sites
The road does not pass through or affect any specific archaeological or cultural sites. Any
work outside the road corridor would necessitate consultation with the Chef do Posto to
identify the existence of any cultural sites such as sacred forests.
