4 POTENTIAL ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
4.1 IDENTIFICATION
This section provides an assessment of the potential environmental impacts associated with the
rehabilitation of the Meconta to Corrane road works. Environmental factors considered were:
o  Land use change;
o  Erosion and sedimentation;
o  Water quality;
oa Hydrology;
o  Noise and vibration;
o  Air quality;
o  Biological effects; and
o  Socio-economic effects.
Each of these issues is addressed in Sections covering the physical, biological and socio-
economic environments.
The majority of direct environmental impacts will be minor due to the limited extent of works
at any one site and the incorporation of impact mitigation measures into the improvement
works. The ENI currently has a sufficient and well defined road corridor which is identified
by ANE as 15m either side of the road centreline (Pieter Van Niekerk, pers. Comm., ANE,
November 2000).
Indirect effects, may result from increased natural resource use due to increased local
enterprise activity to service increased traffic. These impacts are anticipated to be relatively
minor due to the fact that the road is already well established as the major north-south access
route in southern and central Mozambique.
Many of the potential environmental impacts associated with the construction of a new road do
not occur to the same degree when a road is improved within the existing road corridor. The
most likely impacts include:
o  Altered land use patterns due to improved accessibility, such as a change from forestry to
agriculture, or from agriculture to industry (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997);
o  Public health problems, particularly through the spread of communicable disease (Birley,
1997); and
o  Increased illegal logging and poaching activities due to improved access into forest areas
(Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997).
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Corrane Road   January 2001                     4-1
lmo:ambique Road Sector EIA           DRAFT



I:IPA CTO L di (SNIEC
4.2 PHYSICAL EFFECTS
4.2.1    Land Use
i   Construction
Ancillary activities are temporary activities required during the construction period and will
affect a relatively small land area. These sites will be selected in consultation with the
landowners and rehabilitated upon completion of activities. Ancillary sites will be located in
areas close to existing settlements, where possible, to ensure that large areas of land will not
need to be cleared and the land use is consistent to the area.
Total land acquisition will be minimal relative to the project corridor length. Temporary areas
of land use change will occur as a result from establishment of ancillary sites such as batching
plant sites, borrow pits and workforce camp sites.
Where possible existing borrow areas will be utilised during the road works to avoid additional
disturbance. The rehabilitation of these sites after completion of the works will be undertaken
through revegetation and levelling the landform to its original condition. Where new borrow
pits are established these will be located at least 100m from the road in accordance with
current ANE guidelines.
ii   Operation
The proposed road works will not have a direct impact on land use change outside the road
corridor. The extent of the road to be rehabilitated includes a length of 68 km which will be
maintained within the road corridor and no realignments of the road are required. The
Meconta to Corrane road is currently in a highly degraded condition and rehabilitation works
will involve a road formation width of 6 m wide.
The road is also located within a highly populated area of Nampula Province and crosses an
important agricultural area which has links to the EN 8 leading to the provincial capital of
Nampula, the railway line at Namialo and the deep sea port of Nacala. Encroachment of
settlements and cultivated subsistence farming within the road corridor may occur along some
sections of the road in which case the land use will change due to the road works. The impact
is expected to be minor and compensatory measures will apply as discussed in Section 3.3.
The road works are not likely to have a significant effect on adjacent land use activities, as the
area is currently accessible however the road rehabilitation will improve the travelling time and
access of agricultural products to the major centres. This may result in an indirect effect of
increased agriculture and market areas.
iii   Mitigation Measures
Ancillary sites will be located in areas currently cleared or degraded, where possible, to ensure
that large areas of land will not need to be cleared and the land use is consistent to the area.
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Corrane Road    January 2001                       4-2
Alfozambique Road Sector EIA            DRAFT



IAIPACTO Ldra                                                             C0SMNIEC
All ancillary sites will be rehabilitated upon completion of activities to their previous land use,
unless the facilities are further required.
Mitigation measures for other temporary facilities include:
o  Measures identified under socio-economics for selection and negotiation of use; and
o  Rehabilitation of all sites to their original condition, or better, to allow resumption of prior
land use.
4.2.2    Erosion and Sedimentation
Erosion Hazard
The dynamic interplay of several key factors dictates the degree of susceptibility to water-
driven soil erosion processes. These are:
o  Seasonality and intensity of rainfall in relation to water run-off;
u  Landscape topography in relation slope gradients;
o  Soil profile parameters, notably depth and binding properties;
o  Degree of vegetation cover; and
o  Magnitude and frequency of seismic events.
For road works in general, where the capacity of existing drainage works to manage water run-
off is exceeded then the interaction of the above factors will determine the extent and
magnitude of erosion processes. These considerations provide the basis to determine the
overall erosion hazard.
The rating for erosion risk has been determined from the Mozambique Erosion Hazard Map,
1:2,000,000 produced by the Soil and Water Conservation and Land Utilization Co-ordination
Unit (1986).
The Meconta to Corrane road predominantly passes through red sandy clay loams, brown
sandy clay soils. These soils are characteristically fertile deep soils of medium texture giving
them a relatively low erosion risk. The erosion hazard along the project road is considered to
be low-medium according to the erosion hazard mapping and the road does not pass along
steep hilly terrain or areas of high seismic activity therefore landslides and mass wasting issues
are not of concern.
Erosion and sedimentation effects will be greater during the rainy period which occurs from
November to March. During this period 85 to 90 percent of the annual rains occur and erosion
potential will be highest. The impacts from construction will be minimal by limiting the area
of ground disturbance at any one site, and restricting works to the dry period.
Localised erosion can occur where soils are left exposed. This is commonly observed on steep
unrehabilitated borrow pit batters where gully erosion is evident. Similar problems can occur
on fill batters or other sloped areas.
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Corrane Road    January 2001                         4-3
Mfozambiquie Road Sector EI.A           DRAFT



LAIPACTO Ltli                                                           Nn'IWSMEC
ii   Sedimentation Risk
Sedimentation is a secondary process of erosion and can be exacerbated by road works due to:
i deliberate and unanticipated modifications to surface and ground water hydrology;
zi incomplete rehabilitation of roadside verge areas; and
o road rehabilitation through naturally hilly terrain.
Ground disturbance and the temporary stockpiling of materials during road works can trigger
uncontrolled and or accelerated sedimentation processes. The onset of this process will be
coincident with ground disturbance and will continue up until sites have been stabilised in the
road works zone. The extent of ground disturbance and clearing along the road corridor will
be marginal, with an average width of 16m disturbed during the construction stage. The
potential for sedimentation will be higher during times of heavy rain where the road becomes
submerged due to localised flooding from the drainage lines.
Other areas where ground disturbance and clearing is required during the construction stage is
at stockpile areas, borrow pit sites, construction camps, and work compounds. The fill
required for the road works will be obtained from existing borrow sites or degraded areas
where possible to minimise the need for additional ground disturbance.
The terrain along the project road is gently undulating and therefore the potential for erosion
from the improvement works is not significant. The soil types intersected by the road have a
moderate soil erodability rating with reference to sheet erosion potential (Wambeke, 1986).
There are no areas where significant cut and fill is required and retaining walls will not be
required. The need for road embankments will be minimal and will not create sedimentation
problems if vegetated properly and formed on 1:2 grades.
Ground disturbance and the temporary stockpiling of materials during road construction works
result in sedimentation adjacent to the drainage lines. Sedimentation can occur from the time
of ground disturbance up until sites have been stabilised along the length of construction
works. The potential for sedimentation will be higher during times of heavy rain where the
road becomes submerged due to localised flooding from the drainage lines. The provision of
culverts along the road will improve cross-road drainage and reduce scouring and erosion of
the road formation and adjacent land form.
The fill required for the road works will be obtained from existing borrows areas where
possible. These locations have high erosion potential due to large areas of ground disturbance
and steep slopes created.
iii   Mitigation Measures
The impact from sedimentation will be minimised by avoiding stockpiling of materials near
drainage lines and by not undertaking construction during times of heavy rain, particularly the
months of December to March.
Minor reconditioning of the existing culverts will be undertaken where their discharge is
causing downstream erosion. Specific measures for erosion and sediment control include:
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Conrane Road    January 2001                       4-4
tlozambiquie Road Sector El.4          DRAFT



IMPACTO L(lo                                                                QI  SNIEC
o  design of drainage works to ensure these collect from and discharge to natural drainage
lines. This may not always be possible for mitre drains and in those situations frequent,
low volume drains are preferred;
o provision of energy dissipation devices where discharge velocities and flows will erode
downstream drainage lines;
o  minimisation of disturbed areas;
o design and construction of erosion and sedimentation controls such as diversion banks,
filter structures (filter fences or windrowed vegetation) and sediment traps;
o diversion of significant upstream flows around working areas; and
Li undertaking progressive rehabilitation and revegetation of all slopes and disturbed areas.
In addition, temporary erosion control drains will be installed prior to the wet season and no
new road excavation will be commenced during the wet season.
The installation of side drains and culverts along the road will minimise sedimentation and
runoff effects during operation of the road.
4.2.3    Water Quality
i    Construction
The potential for sedimentation from construction activities is described in the previous section
and will have an impact on water quality. The rates of sedimentation will be higher during the
rainy season therefore construction impacts will be minimised by undertaking works during the
dry season because most of the streams intersected by the road are perennial. The occurrence
of sedimentation in permanent watercourses will have a greater effect during the dry season
when flows are low.
Hydrocarbons sourced from improper disposal facilities for used lubricants and poor operation
and maintenance practices for construction vehicles and machinery can contaminate surface
waters. Improper disposal of oil filters and other materials from vehicle and machinery
maintenance can also cause pollution. This can occur during both construction and road
operation, but mostly during the former.
Spilled petroleum fuel, as well as bitumen and explosives residue, also have a potential to
contribute to the pollution of waterbodies, but to a very minor extent.
Improper sanitation and waste disposal procedures in temporary construction camps can also
contaminate water resources.
ii    Operation
Borrow areas have the potential to result in adverse environmental impacts if they are not
rehabilitated correctly. If left in a poor condition water can collect in the depressions created
and become weed infested as well as providing optimal breeding conditions for insect vectors
of parasitic diseases such as malaria
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Corrane Road    January 2001                          4-5
Mozambique Road Sector EIA               DRAFT



LMIPACTO Lda                                                             VI,SMEc
iii   Mitigation Measures
Proper management of construction camps is required to prevent pollution to neighbouring
waterways. Any pollution resulting from these camps shall be remediated immediately.
All fuels, lubricants and bitumen will be stored in bunded areas. Vehicle maintenance and
refuelling will be confined to areas in construction camps designed to confine lubricants and
fuel spillages.
Adequate waste disposal and sanitation facilities will be provided at construction camps. Any
wastewater or stormwater discharged from the site shall comply with the following objectives:
:  pH range                        6.0-9.0
:  Total Suspended Solids (TSS)    <50mg/I
zi Total Dissolved Solids (TDS)    <1500mg/I
o  Oils and surfactants            No visible film of oils or petrochemicals
The Contractor shall ensure that all bitumen and fuel storage areas shall be bunded to contain
at least 120% of the maximum capacity of the largest storage tank. The floor of the bunded
area shall be impervious, and storage facilities and bunds shall be inspected weekly for spills.
4.2.4    Hydrology
i   Construction
The road works will have a negligible impact to hydrology, as the rehabilitation works will be
undertaken along an existing alignment. The main construction activity that has the potential
to impede flows will be the stockpiling of materials if placed near drainage lines.
ii   Operation
Although the road is an existing feature, it can cause an impedance to flow, alter the natural
channel position and result in increased velocities as a result of this change. The road crosses
five major watercourses, which will require the provision of adequate sized culverts.
Adequate and stable cross-road and road surface drainage is essential to ensure that storm
runoff across the road causes minimal damage to the road and minimises the effect of
inundation caused by impeded flows. Cross-road drainage shall be provided by causeways and
culverts to pass catchment flows past the road, either over or beneath it. Road surface drainage
shall be provided by side drains to direct runoff off the road surface.
iii   Mitigation Measures
Particular attention shall be given to stockpiling materials away from existing drainage lines
and avoid filling in wetland areas.
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Corrane Road    January 2001                       4-6
A!o:ambique Road Sector EIA             DR4FT



LTIPA CTO Lda                                                              ISMIEC
All designed drainage works shall be installed at the time of initial road construction to ensure
that flows along drainage lines are not impeded.
Culverts shall be laid in a direction, which is as close as possible to the natural position of the
drainage flow.  This will avoid altering the channel flow  and prevent upstream  and
downstream scouring of the watercourse.
4.2.5    Noise and Vibration
i   Construction
Some sensitive receptors, such as schools and hospitals, are located within 5-lOm of the ENI
road pavement. Additional noise will be generated along the ENI road corridors and at
ancillary sites during the construction period, particularly from the increased number of heavy
vehicles. Vibration effects will be negligible due to the generally sandy soil substrate. Peak
noise levels generated by construction activities are expected to be greater than the peak noise
levels from road operation.
Construction noise is generally intermittent and depends on the type of operation, location and
function of the equipment and the equipment use cycle. Noise attenuates quickly over
distance. Excavation noise levels, for example, assuming bulldozer and dump truck activity,
yield a level of approximately 85 dBA at 15m, but decrease by about 3-4 dBA with each
doubling of the setback distance, and would be reduced to approximately 70 dBA at about
240m.
ii    Operation
Accessibility will be improved following the road works but improvements are not expected to
generate additional traffic. Traffic growth is expected to be similar to historic growth. Noise
levels from traffic will not be increased as a result of the project.
Noise sources and factors which may contribute to the increase in the ambient noise level and
related adverse impacts during the operation of the improved roads will include vehicle noise,
vehicle maintenance and driver behaviour (such as the use of horns).
The improved road design will allow an increase in vehicle speed and some increase in the
overall noise level is likely to result. When traffic speed doubles, this results in a 6 dB(A)
increase in noise level (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997). The majority of the project roads
traverse open cultivated land and the impact from an increase in noise levels from increased
traffic speed will be minimal.
The road works may reduce existing noise levels in built-up areas as a result of improved road
surface. This will reduce noise levels through the more efficient operation of vehicles and less
noise from vehicle tyres.
As ambient noise levels are generally lowest at night, the impact from traffic noise is expected
to be greater during these hours.
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Cofrane Road    January 2001                       4-7
Mozambique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT



ItIIPACTO Lda                                                              'SNIEC
iii   Mitigation Measures
No national noise level standards currently exist for highway construction equipment in
Mozambique, however the World Bank recommends the use of construction equipment, which
meets international standards.
The following measures shall be implemented to minimise the impact of noise during the
construction stage:
Eo In construction sites within 150 m of residential areas, construction cannot occur from
6:00 am -7:00 pm.
z Maintenance of machinery and vehicles shall be undertaken regularly to keep noise at a
minimum.
4.2.6    Air Quality
i   Construction
The deterioration in air quality from dust generation and vehicle emissions during road
construction and operation will be a relatively minor environmental impact. Significant
adverse effects are not expected because of the limited duration of construction at any one site
and the few receptors. Most of the work will occur in open, cultivated areas.
Deterioration in air quality during road construction will be experienced primarily from dust
generated by road works, spoil transportation and disposal. Construction will be primarily
labour based, with excavation and material transportation undertaken by hand, therefore dust
generation should be minimal due to the small area and volume of soil disturbance at any one
time. In addition, most construction activities will progressively move along the alignment, so
prolonged deterioration in air quality will not occur at any one site.
ii   Operation
Existing air quality is good and no significant air quality effects are expected during operation.
Traffic volumes are low and the road generally traverses rural road areas. Exhaust emissions
from traffic utilising the rehabilitated road are likely to increase due to an expected increase in
traffic growth. The emissions are still expected to be low because existing traffic numbers
currently utilising the road are relatively low.
Improved road geometry will reduce exhaust emissions along the project road by reducing the
roughness of the road surface (particularly by removal of potholes) increasing the design speed
and thereby reducing the need for excessive acceleration and deceleration (Tsunokawa and
Hoban, 1997).
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Coffane Road    January 2001                       4-8
Mllozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



lIMPA CTO Lda dtM EC
iii   Mitigation Measures
Mitigation measures will be implemented in areas where air quality problems would arise due
to construction activities. Areas of concern for dust control are in major settlements and
sensitive sites such as schools and hospitals.
Minimising air emissions is particularly important during the construction stage, for activities
such as the operation of borrow pits and in-situ mixing of cement and sand, which have a
potential to generate high dust levels.
During the construction period, the following mitigation measures to suppress dust will be
implemented:
a  Water will be sprayed during the construction period at earth mixing sites, asphalt-mixing
sites, and on cleared surfaces. Water spraying will be undertaken during periods of high
wind, or when necessary, to suppress dust;
a  Operations shall be suspended during periods of dry windy weather, in areas where wind
blown dust is causing a nuisance in settlements or towns;
u  All equipment and plant machinery shall not emit visible smoke for a continuous period of
greater than 10 seconds, and
L  All exhaust systems and engines on plant and equipment shall be in accordance with the
manufacturers specifications and kept well maintained to ensure that they have a high level
operating efficiency.
4.3 BIOLOGICAL EFFECTS
4.3.1    Direct impacts
Vegetation will need to be cleared to undertake the road rehabilitation works and enable road
formation widening and construction of additional drainage. The conservation value of the
road corridor vegetation that will be cleared is not high. The woodland areas crossed by the
road corridor are highly utilised, particularly for slash and burn agriculture, which is frequently
practiced in the Province. The amount of clearing that will need to be undertaken will be
minimal, as the road corridor is well defined for the majority of its length. The impact of any
clearing required for the road projects will not be significant due to the already highly
degraded nature of the woodland habitat.
Vegetation may also be cleared in areas selected for ancillary sites, such as construction
camps, borrow pits and quarries, to enable the utilisation of these areas.
There are no known wildlife crossings along the length of the road project and the habitat has
been highly modified by anthropogenic uses. The road works have no potential for significant
adverse effects on wildlife.
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Corrane Road    Januaty 2001                       4-9
Mozaonbiqzue Road Sector EIA           DRAFT



JAIPACTO Lda                                                             wn(wSMEC
4.3.2    Indirect impacts
There is a potential impact associated with the indirect loss and degradation of vegetation,
habitat and species in the ROI. These potential impacts are associated with:
o Improved access;
i In-migration; and
Li Construction workforce behaviour.
Ecological degradation of the ROI may occur from vegetation harvesting and wildlife
poaching by the construction workforce, and increased harvesting during road operation due to
greater accessibility and additional local demands from road-induced activities.
The projects will provide improved access to the neighbouring areas and facilitate natural
resource use. The main potential for increased natural resource harvesting is likely to occur
within the area's woodlands.
The population increase along the road corridor would be derived either from people migrating
to the roadside from the immediate area (i.e. the 5km region of influence and adjacent to this),
or from other areas. This in-migration has a potential to create an increased local demand for
fuelwood and construction timber, as well as other woodland products to support secondary
enterprise activities, such as forage collection for grazing animals. Hunting may also increase.
The proposed road improvements will follow an existing alignment and almost all forest areas
neighbouring this road are currently accessible. The road will only increase potential access to
these forests, not open them up.
Importing a construction workforce into an area may increase demand on local natural
resources due to competition for firewood, woodland products and wildlife. These increased
demands will be focussed on the areas surrounding workforce camps and work compounds.
Worker camps may also encourage the temporary construction of markets and barracas to
service the workers. These temporary facilities may also increase demand on local natural
resources.
4.3.3    Mitigation measures
i   Direct Impacts
Vegetation clearance along the road edge and at ancillary sites (such as borrow pits) will be
minimised. All construction areas will be clearly marked and construction activity prohibited
outside the defined areas. Tress within ancillary sites shall be retained wherever possible.
Retention of trees within areas such as worker camps will improve the camp amenity and
provide shade.
All temporarily disturbed areas will be rehabilitated following construction. The establishment
of ground cover is the initial priority of revegetation. Ground cover reduces raindrop impact,
binds the soil surface and creates a micro-climate favourable for the establishment of other
vegetation. Species should be selected for cover crops and permanent revegetation. A cover
crop consisting of a rapid growing species and several vigorous grass species should be used to
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Corrane Road    January 2001                      4-10
Alozambique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT



IMPA CTO Ldta                                                          VWSEC
rapidly establish ground cover on disturbed areas. Permanent revegetation species should be
selected to be consistent with the local native species in the area.
Revegetation will aim to stabilise the road verges and assist the many desirable secondary uses
of the road including:
o production of forage, wood, flowers, fruit, medicinal items;
o shade and shelter;
o fencing and screens; and
o  building supports.
Revegetation along different sections of road will be designed to achieve site-specific aims,
such as maximising shade within villages and providing a species composition that is
compatible with surrounding vegetation cover.
ii   Indirect Impacts
The most potential for indirect effects was identified to be associated with workforce camps.
This is a temporary effect but may last several years depending on the duration of the works
project. To reduce the potential for increased exploitation of natural resources the following
migration measures will be implemented. The Contractor will:
o provide and maintain adequate drinking and bathing water supplies;
o provide kerosene or other fuel source and stoves for worker use;
o educate workers about the responsible use of natural resources; and
o ensure workers act in a responsible manner and apply penalties for the harvesting of local
resources (wood, wildlife or wood products).
4.4 SOCIO-ECONOMIC IMPACTS
4.4.1    Direct impacts
i   Loss of agricultural land, buildings, sites of cultural value, population displacement,
and natural resources degradation
Improvements to the road will not involve the significant loss of cultivated land, major
buildings or require the displacement of the local population. Cultivation is not continuous
along the road and there is sufficient land for machambas if necessary to be realigned.
Land may need to be acquired for the purposes of borrow pits as a source for road building
materials. Negotiation with local authorities and farmers would secure access for borrow pits
and appropriate compensation established.
Where an area of cultivated land has to be resumed for road construction, the farmer should be
compensated for loss of production. The following process will be applied:

MIo:ambique Road Sector EIA           DRAFT



LIIPACTO Ldt,                                                            AiJSNIEC
Li Where possible the farmer should be notified of the loss of land one season in advance.
This is to avoid unnecessary loss of effort and seed; and
io The value of the lost production should be determined in consultation with the District
Agricultural Officer and the local Chief of Post. Compensation will be paid to the farmer
for the value of the lost production.
Borrow pits, storage and equipment areas, and construction camps will be located on marginal
or disturbed land. The acquiring of land for such purposes must be subject to ANE and
Provincial approval.  In selecting land consultation must also be undertaken with local
communities and their traditional leaders as well as local authorities (at District and Ward
level).
4.4.2    Indirect impacts
The road has recently been rehabilitated and the works proposed are to finalise the
rehabilitation to a sealed road. Any impacts related to indirect effects would have commenced
under the previous roads program.
i    >.ccess to services
Improvement of the Meconta to Corrane road is likely to have significant socio-economic
impacts by enabling better access to services such as better health services located in the
Provincial capital of Nampula, as well as Nametil and Angoche.
ii   Improved access to markets
As the road is located within a rich agricultural area, the road rehabilitation is expected to
provide better opportunities for the local communities to transport surplus crop production to
Nampula City, Nametil and the port town of Angoche. These three major centres are within
easy distance of the road and offers a market for agricultural production. Continued
improvement and maintenance to the road will enable goods and services to flow more easily
into the area particularly those associated with improving agricultural production techniques
and yields.
iii   In-migration of people and concessions
Improvement of roads in other rural areas within Mozambique leads often to increases in in-
migration as advantage is taken of the improved communication. It is probable that some in-
migration will take place particularly in areas in close proximity to Nampula, which currently
has the greatest population pressure. Regulos have an influence in terms of the local allocation
of land. Mitigating measures should involve working with regutlos and other local authorities
to control any possible in-migration.
iv    Health issues
Further maintenance of the road is unlikely to require the inflow of significant numbers of
workers and therefore health issues normally associated with road gangs in other parts of
Mozambique would not have an impact.

Mozanmbique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT

v    Construction worker camps
As above in the health section, an influx of migrant workers is not envisaged and therefore no
impacts are predicted.
In conclusion road improvements and maintenance to the EN8 are unlikely to have any
significant socio-economic impacts.  Rehabilitation of the road has already taken place.
Maintaining the road in a good condition will encourage farmers to explore marketing options
for their surplus production and may encourage the production of cash crops such as cotton.
However, further studies are needed to better predict probable socio-economic impacts, which
would occur as a result of the road rehabilitation.
4.5 SOCIO-ECONOMIC IMPACTS
4.5.1    Direct impacts
i   Loss of agricultural land, buildings, sites of cultural value, population displacement,
and natural resources degradation
No displacement of population is likely and no major buildings are likely to be lost. However,
it was not possible to undertake an inspection of the road for the purposes of this report and all
information provided has been extrapolated from existing literature and knowledge of other
roads within the Nampula Province. It is not envisaged that there will be any displacement of
population of the local population due to road improvements.
Land acquisition may be required for the purpose of borrow pits to obtain road building
materials. Negotiation with local authorities and farmers would secure access for borrow pits
and appropriate compensation established.
Where an area of cultivated land has to be resumed for road construction, the farmer should be
compensated for loss of production. The following process will be applied:
o Where possible the farmer should be notified of the loss of land one season in advance.
This is to avoid unnecessary loss of effort and seed; and
3 The value of the lost production should be determined in consultation with the District
Agricultural Officer and the local Chief of Post. Compensation will be paid to the farmer
for the value of the lost production.
Borrow pits, storage and equipment areas, and construction camps will be located on marginal
or disturbed land. The acquiring of land for such purposes must be subject to ANE and
Provincial approval. In selecting land consultation must also be undertaken with local
communities and their traditional leaders as well as local authorities (at District and Ward
level).

4.5.2    Indirect impacts
The road has recently been rehabilitated and the works proposed are to finalise the
rehabilitation to a sealed road. Any impacts related to indirect effects would have commenced
under the previous roads program.
i   Access to services
Improvement of the Meconta to Corrane road is likely to have significant socio-economic
impacts. Availability of services to the populations of the District the road passes through is
generally poor. An improved road would enable for example sick patients to gain access to
better health services particularly in the Provincial capital Nampula.
ii   Improved access to markets
Continued improvement and maintenance to the road will enable goods and services to flow
more easily into the area particularly those associated with improving agricultural production
techniques and yields. Nampula is a major city within easy distance of the road and offers a
market for agricultural production. The road rehabilitation will improve the ability of local
communities to transport surplus crop production to Nampula City.
iii   In-migration of people and concessions
Improvement of roads in other rural areas within Mozambique leads often to increases in in-
migration as advantage is taken of the improved communication. It is probable that some in-
migration will take place particularly in areas in close proximity to Nampula, which currently
has the greatest population pressure. Regulos have an influence in terms of the local allocation
of land. Mitigating measures should involve working with regulos and other local authorities
to control any possible in-migration.
iv    Health issues
It is likely that the road project will necessitate the inflow of a number of construction workers
from other parts of Mozambique. The inflow of road gangs, particularly into remote areas has
a potential for adverse effects. The most often cited problem is the one of HIV-AIDs and
sexually transmitted diseases.  Mitigation measures against these likely impacts require
working closely with respective govemment departments; local NGOs and/or church based
organisations, local communities and road gangs. Educational programmes for the local
populations are considered to be extremely important in trying to reduce the likelihood of HIV-
AlDs infection.
v    Construction worker camps
The health issues related to construction worker camps particularly in terms of the spread of
HIV-AIDs and other sexually transmitted diseases have been mentioned above.  A
concentration of workers also attracts other inward migrants who attempt to exploit new
market opportunities. In other parts of Mozambique this has often resulted in the creation of
small unplanned roadside shops. Such informal sites are often poorly managed in terms of
58602 Annexure E - Meconta to Corrane Road    January 2001                      4-14
fo:zambique Road Sector EIA            DRAFT



MIPACTO Lda                                                              t4SMEC
adherence to health regulations and waste disposal. Mitigation measures should include the
identification of suitable sites for the establishment of small shops and other facilities servicing
road-gangs.
vi   Recommendations
In conclusion road improvements and maintenance to the Meconta to Corrane road section are
unlikely to have any significant socio-economic impacts. Maintaining the road in a good
condition will encourage farmers to explore marketing options for their surplus production and
may encourage the production of cash crops such as cotton.
The main negative socio-economic impacts relate to the problem of the spread of HIV-AIDs
infection. Campaigns in other parts of Mozambique have demonstrated that these problems
can be mitigated against, particularly if resources and education are directed at the local
population and not just the influx of construction workers.
Further studies are recommended to better predict probable socio-economic impacts, which
would occur as a result of the road rehabilitation.

5 ENVIRONMENTAL MITIGATION MEASURES
The road works will include environmental impact mitigation measures within the Contract
Specifications. These measures will be incorporated as specific instructions and
recommendations to the Contractor and will be provided in a separate Environmental
Management Plan (EMP). The EMP will identify specific instructions to the Contractor and
will include responsibilities, outputs, costs and timing of mitigation measures.
Design, construction and maintenance mitigation recommendations for the proposed roads
have been formulated to avoid or mitigate potential bio-physical impacts.  These
recommendations were primarily formulated from observations made during the inspection of
constructed roads.
The EMP describes the practical means to ensure effective implementation of the mitigation
measures recommended for the potential adverse environmental impacts identified in the EIA.
The EMP will include but will not be limited to the following elements:
Practical means to implement the recommended mitigation measures;
o Implementation Schedule and Supervision Programme;
o Monitoring and Evaluation;
oi Institutional Strengthening, Management and Training; and
o Further Studies of Environmental Issues.
In addition, the program also specifies what action will be taken and by whom in the event that
the proposed mitigation measures fail, either partially or totally, to achieve the level of
environmental protection expected.
Specific socio-economic impact mitigation measures will include further consultation with
potentially affected landholders and provision of compensatory measures in accordance with
the current Government of Mozambique policy. These measures have been formulated by the
Department of Agriculture and include guidelines for the displacement of people and
acquisition of land for the roads.
An outline of the EMP for the construction and operation stage is provided in Part E of this
report.

1 INTRODUCTION
This Annex contains an environmental assessment for one of four additional roads to be
included as part of the Phase I World Bank projects (giving a total of 15 project roads).
Due to time limitations, no field investigations have been undertaken for the purposes of this
environmental assessment.  The description of the environment, potential impacts and
recommendations have been prepared as a baseline study using information from existing
literature sources, available mapping sources, knowledge of Provincial characteristics and
personal field observations from ANE technical staff.
The primary references for this study include:
o  Mozambique Erosion Hazard Map (1986), 1:2,000,000.
o  Topographic map series (1997), 1:250,000.
o  Land use map - Carta de Uso e Cobertura da Terra (1999) 1: 250,000.
o Forestry Map - Mapa Florestal (1 994), 1:250,000.
Li Wild H. and Barbosa LA. (1967). Flora Zambesiaca, 1:2,500,000.
o Provincial Census Data, 1997.
o  Security and Nutrition, District Profiles, 1997.
ci  UNHCR & UNDP District Development Profiles, 1997.

2 BIO-PHYSICAL ENVIRONMENT
2.1 TOPOGRAPHY AND LANDFORMS
The Project road is located in the coastal region of Nampula Province with a topographic relief
ranging 0 - 200 m above sea level. The road traverses relatively flat terrain and intersects
several small rivers towards the EN 260, and as the road moves further from the coast towards
Nametil, the topography inclines and the terrain is gently undulating.
2.2 CLIMATE
The climate within the Nampula province is classified as dry sub-humid and has two climatic
seasons namely a hot and humid rainy season between December and March, and a cooler and
drier season from April to November. The mean annual monthly temperature averages vary
between a low of 210C in July, and a high of 27.5 C in November. Monthly maximum and
minimum average temperatures range between 29.5-32C and 15-19 respectively (Figure 1).

The climate within the ROI is distinctly drier in comparison to coastal regions with the mean

during the dry season and increasing up to73% during the wet season (Figure 2).

2.3 GEOLOGY
Geological formations of the Pre-Cambrian period (more than 600 million years BP) cover
most of the area. The most important formations comprise granitoid migmatites (rich in
quartz) and in the Nampula super-group two major complexes can be distinguished, namely:
o Gneissic migmatites (P): which cover the major portion of Zambezia Province and include
biotites and amphibolites.
o Granitic migmatites (y): which are important mineralogically and include quartzites,
marble and graphite.
A great portion of the study area is comprised of acidic rocks due to their percentage quartz
content and the soils derived from these are typically sandy. The area is characterised by a
slightly undulating plains. The major geological features of the region are depicted in Figure 4
below.
Sedimentary formations of the Quaternary period, comprising of alluviums and sandy
eluvium's are found as the road approaches nearer the coast just before Boila.

2.4 SOILS
The Nametil road predominately passes through red sandy clay loams, brown sandy clay soils
and sandy colluvium soils. A description of the soil types is given in Table I and the
distribution of these soil types along the road is illustrated in Figure 5.
The soils are characteristically fertile deep soils of medium texture and have generally poor to
moderate drainage. The structure of the soils originating from acidic rocks is generally weak to
very weak in sandy soils and weak to moderate in soils of medium to heavy texture.

2.5 LAND USE
The road -predominantly passes through woodland and thicket that has been disturbed for
anthropogenic purposes such as cultivation and settlement. The first 16 km of road from
Nametil passes medium thicket with approximately 45% vegetation cover then the next 35 km
predominantly passes an area of herbaceous species which has been mostly altered as a result
of agriculture. The road then passes through a relatively disturbed area of small shrubland for
8 km which has approximately 50% cleared for small fields and cashew groves before passing
12 km of medium thicket which has been highly disturbed. The last 10 km has mixed
regenerating vegetation of thicket and open forest which has approximately 5% of the area
cleared for cashew trees and small fields.
The project road traverses a highly populated area of the Nampula Province that has an average
population density of 66 people per km2 (compared to the National average of 22 people per
kM2). The road is connected to the provincial capital of Nampula, the major town of Nametil
and the coastal port town of Angoche. Angoche was once an important fishing port that is
planned for rehabilitation. The location of the road connecting these major centres has an
economic potential for the area as it traverses an important agricultural area where cashew,
cotton, tobacco and coffee are produced. No major industries are known to occur adjacent to
the road.
2.6 RIVERS AND WATER QUALITY
The road south of Nametil runs parallel to the MeMlie River, lying to the west, for
approximately 16 km south of Nametil. The road crosses several small rivers along its route,
with the major ones including Elepue, Luazi, Xilapane, Mupricomo and Chitalane Rivers. The
last three rivers mentioned are crossed by the southern section of the road near Boila and
drains into the Muchupe River and enters a large bay joining the Luazi River near Angoche
before flowing into the Indian Ocean.
2.7 NOISE AND AIR QUALITY
Noise levels along the Nametil Project Road, are expected to be very low due to the currently
low traffic noise and sparse settlement along the route.  The noise levels would be
predominantly generated by natural conditions (i.e. from insects and birds), however noise
would increase slightly as the road passes the larger settlements, particularly near the market
areas.
Ambient air quality along the project road is expected to be good due to a lack of air polluting
industries and urban activities, typical of other project roads inspected within the Nampula
Province. Haze may be observed due to practiced slash and burn agriculture and production of
charcoal, which are frequently practiced in the Province. Levels of airborne dust generated by
winds can also be higher over agricultural fields, as can smoke from forest and grassland fires.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road    January 2001                    2-1
Mozambique Road Sector EIA           DRAFT

2.8 VEGETATION COVER
The stretch of the project road between Nametil and EN260 traverses Moist Savanna to Mesic
Forest characterised by an annual rainfall of >1000 m. The broad vegetation type of the area is
tardily deciduous miombo, lowland type, and the project road traverses the subtype
characteristic of Nampula Province, which includes the Brachystegia spiciformis, B.
boehmii-Adansonia, Sterculia type. (Equivalent to Mapping Unit, 27 Wild and Barbosa,
1967)
This Vegetation type overs the subplanaltic zone between 50 and 500 m. especially in the
sublittoral areas from Angoche to Nampula and traverses soils which are generally red to
grey and derived from granite, gneiss or rocks of the primitive system.
This mosaic include the following communities:
(i)    Closed woodland 12-18 m. high of Brachystegia spiciformis, B. boehmii and
Julbernardia globiflora. In wetter woodland occur elements of the Pteleopsis
myrtifolia forest of sublittoral areas (see Type 2). Sometimes comnmunities with
abundant Brachystegia utilis occur (approaches Type 23).
(ii)   Scattered  patches of deciduous forest with Adansonia digitata, Bombax
rhodognaphalon, chlorophora excelsa, Sterculia appendiculata (very typical of
these communities), Dialium holtzii, Cordyla africana, Croton megalocarpus, etc.
(Type 49).
(iii)  Savanna woodland of medium height with Brachystegia boehmii (dominant),
Julbernardia globiflora, Erythrophleum africanum, Pterocarpus angolensis, etc.,
Brachystegia allenii is often represented in shallow or sloping soils (approaching
Type 29).
(iv)   Tree-shrub savanna in the lower lying areas with Acacia polyacantha subsp.
campylacantha, A. nigrescens, A. sieberana, Combretum spp., Kigela pinnata,
Ficus sp., Terminalia sericea, etc. Grass layer of Setaria, Pennisetum, etc. soils
grey.
(v)   Deciduous  savanna  or woodland  with Adansonia, Sterculia, Dalbergia,
Commiphora, etc. at the foot of 'inselbergs' with an abundance of Oxytenanthera
abyssinica bamboo thicket. Sometimes the dwarf Androstachys jhonsonii also
occurs in swallow soils near the 'inselbergs'. Soils red.
There are no designated production forest areas within the ROI of the Project Road.

2.9 WILDLIFE AND CONSERVATION AREAS
There are no designated conservation areas or Coutadas (hunting areas) located within the
project road ROI. The area has been highly disturbed for agricultural purposes and therefore
the diversity and species numbers in the area are expected to be low.
The mammal population within the ROI is expected to be sparse as there are only eighteen
mammal species known to occur within the reserves of Nampula Province and over the last
few years, there has been a considerable reduction in wildlife numbers due to hunting and
habitat pressures. It is believed that most of the species are locally extinct and even the small
solitary forest-dwelling antelope (suni and duiker) have been eradicated.

3 SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
3.1 PROVINCIAL CHARACTERISTICS
The purpose of this section is to describe the socio-economic environment for the road
between Nametil to the Corner of the EN 239 and EN 260 in Nampula Province. Information
has been derived from existing reports and publications where information is disaggregated
usually at the Provincial and District levels.
To describe the living conditions for the populations living along or in proximity to the
Nametil road a variety of Provincial and District socio-economic indices have been used.
Where possible statistical information has been provided to enable the establishment of base
line data from which the impacts of road improvement may be made.
3.1.1    Context and administration
i   Provincial setting and administration
Nampula Province is located in the Central Region of Mozambique. To the north of Nampula
Province are the Provinces of Niassa and Cabo Delgado. Bordering to the south is Zambezia
Province and the eastern boundary is formed along the Indian Ocean.
Administratively Nampula is divided into 19 Districts, 71 Administrative Posts, and 103
Localities. The Provincial Capital Nampula, Angoche, and Nacala-Porto are designated as
cities. In terms of area, Nampula is the third largest Province in Mozambique with a land
surface of 81,606 Km2 representing 10.2% of the land surface of Mozambique.
ii   Political environment
In the 1999 elections the RENAMO political party received the majority of the votes cast in
Nampula Province. Political tension exists to the extent that RENAMO believe they should be
allowed to select a candidate as Governor of the Province to reflect their electoral superiority.
The issue of the political allegiance of the Governor is currently vigorously debated in
Mozambique.  Demonstrations in Nampula City against the general elections of 1999
organised by RENAMO resulted in the deaths of two demonstrators.
iii   Traditional administration
Traditional administration authority exists in Nampula Province, although in some Districts the
links between traditional authorities and formal administrative authorities are weak. The
importance attached to the regulos varies from area to area. In those localities where the
regulos are strong and respected he will intervene in a variety of aspects of every-day life.
Such issues would include resolving social conflicts; allocating land for building housing,
managing water sources, supporting official health programmes such HIV/AIDS.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road    January 2001                   3-1
Mozambique Road Sector EIA           DRAFT



IMIPA CTO Lda                                                      NSA EG
Like most parts of Mozambique, tensions exist between the two types of administrative
systems. These tensions were exaggerated during the armed conflict. Areas controlled by
RENAMO largely reverted back to traditional leadership structures, while those under
Government control retained their post-independence organisational structures.
3.1.2    Population Characteristics
i   Population
The 1997 national survey estimated the population of Nampula to be 2,975,747 inhabitants
with slightly more women (1,495,822) than men (1,479,925). Nampula is Mozambique's most
populated Province. The annual population growth rate is estimated at 2.2% and therefore by
2009 it is calculated that the population will have grown by 34% to about 3,900,500.
The population of Nampula Province is young with more than 1.3 million peoples under the
age of 14 representing 43% of the total population. The population density for Nampula
Province (36.4 inhabitants per km2) is higher than for the average population density for the
country (19 inhabitants per kmi2). The total urban population is calculated at 743,638 and the
rural population at 2,232,109. In comparison to other Provinces Nampula has a relatively high
urban-rural ratio with approximately 1 in 3 people living in one of the three designated cities.
ii   Ethnicity
The main ethnic group of Nampula Province is the Mecua. The languages spoken reflect the
dominant ethnic group and the three most common languages are Emakhuwa (91%), Coti
(1.9%), Elomwe (0.4%), and Portuguese (5.6%).
iii   Gender
The main ethnic group the Mecua are patrilineal meaning that inheritance follows the male
linage. Despite the advancement of the rights of women under independent Mozambican
administration, girls and women in Nampula Province do less well than boys and men. Access
to health services directed specifically at women is generally very poor. Literacy rates for
women are much lower than for men. Family law in Mozambique still discriminates against
women. A married woman has limited rights to make independent economic decisions or
enjoy full ownership of assets, and a widow's rights to inheritance are secondary to males in
the family. Girls in rural areas in general marry young often as soon as they reach puberty.
3.1.3    Economic Characteristics
i   Economy
The economy of Nampula was estimated to have generated approximately US$460 million
between 1996 and 1998 representing approximately 13% of Mozambique's annual output.
Agriculture is the main activity contributing about 40% by value. The category of fishing
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road    January 2001                  3-2
Mozambique Road Sector EIA           DRAFT



d"""SINEC
J.4IPA CTO Lda                                                       V!W
contributed 8.5%, commercial activity 13.1 %; transport and communication contributed 9% by
value and manufacturing 7% by value. Recent agreements for the mining of titanium will have
a dramatic impact on investment and production for Nampula Province. In 1998 gross
domestic product per head was calculated at $176 with an annual growth rate projected as
15%.
ii   Poverty
According to the National Poverty Assessment (1996-97) 68.9 % of the population of Nampula
Province are categorised as poor. This equates to approximately 2 million people. This
compares with a national average rate of poverty of 69.4 %.
Life expectancy in Nampula Province (37 years) is below the average levels registered for the
country (42 years). Despite the relative high urban-rural ratio, the vast majority of the
population of Nampula Province (more than 75%) depends on agriculture, forestry and
fisheries for their livelihood, mostly comprising the family sector. There are few formal sector
employment opportunities except in the city of Nampula and Nacala.
3.1.4    Services
i   Education
In 1998 Nampula Province has a total of 1,117 schools: 1,061 lower primary (EPI) schools, 48
upper primary (EP2) schools, seven lower secondary, and one upper secondary (ESG2) school.
The number of students recorded was 286,649 and the number of teachers was 5,746. The
average ratio of pupils to teachers in the Province is 52.
ii   Health
Nampula Province has a total of 21 health units, including three hospitals, 43 health centres,
107 health posts and twenty first-aid posts. There are 54 maternity units distributed throughout
the Province.  The illnesses occurring most frequently in the Province are malaria,
tuberculosis, bilharzia, intestinal parasites, acute respiratory infection, AIDS, sexually
transmitted diseases and non-specific diarrhoea. Current rates of HIV infection are estimated at
approximately 21% and are projected to rise to 25% by the year 2010. It is calculated that
about half of all children under five-years suffer from chronic malnutrition and 1 in 10 from
acute malnutrition.
iii   Access to services
Access to services for the rural population is generally poor. Most villages do have a primary
school (EPI) (72%), but these schools could be categorised as rudimentary and often
availability of teachers is very low. Figures are not available for the provision of secondary
schools. Most villages do have access to a basic health centre but less than 1% of villages have
access to a doctor. Dependence on traditional healers is the norm for the vast majority of the
population and almost every village would have at least one person categorised as a traditional

villages within Nampula Province.

3.1.5     Livelihood Strategies
The principle economic activity in Nampula Province is agriculture. Most food production is
within the family sector upon which the majority of the population is dependent. The principle
food crop grown is cassava (total production 2,451,576 tonnes) followed by maize (total
production 106,995 tonnes), and rice (22,321 tonnes). Crop production for Nampula Province
is shown in Table 3 below.

3.1.6     Archaeological and Cultural Sites
The road does not pass through or affect any known archaeological or cultural sites. Any
major rehabilitation work would necessitate consultation with the Chief the existence of any
cultural sites such as sacred forests.

3.2 DISTRICT CHARACTERISTICS
3.2.1    Context and Administration
This section describes socio-economic factors more specific to the EN239 Nametil to Cnr. En
239/260 road. The main sources of information relate to existing written reports concerning
the District the road passes through. To better assess the possible and probable impacts of
improvements to the EN239 Nametil to Cnr. En 239/260 road, more detailed studies for socio-
economic purposes will be needed.
3.2.2    District setting and administration
The EN239 Nametil to Cnr. EN 239/260 road passes through the districts of Angoche and
Magovolas located in south-eastern Nampula Province. To the north, Angoche District
borders with the Districts of Meconta and Mogincual. Mogovolas District borders with the
Districts of Morrupula, Nampula and Meconta.
3.2.3    Traditional administration
Traditional structure exists to a lesser or greater extent, in most localities side-by-side with the
more modern administrative apparatus of the State and political parties. In general terms, the
first government of an independent Mozambique from 1975 attempted to replace the inherited
colonial and traditional systems with a centralised system based upon the political doctrines of
socialism. However, some efforts are currently being employed towards incorporating
traditional leadership into the formal administrative systems.
Political Environment
The information available relating to voting practices in the Districts of Angoche and
Mogovolas indicates that the majority of the voting population voted in favour of the
opposition RENAMO party during the general elections of late 1999.
ii   Population
The 1997 national census estimated the population of Angoche District as 228,526 inhabitants
totaling 65,358 families and with a population density of 77 inhabitants per km2.
Magovolas District was recorded to have 182,184 inhabitants divided into 54,499 families and
with a population density of 23 inhabitants per kM2.
iii   Ethnicity
Most people living along the road are from the Macua ethnic group. Succession and heritage
for the Macua are patrilineal, and the children are considered to belong to the father. Family
succession is granted to the eldest son.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road    January 2001                    3-5
Mozambique Road Sector EIA           DRAFT



ILPACTO Ldna                                                           (ve`SMTEC
iv    Gender
Angoche District
On average women will be more illiterate than men, more boys than girls will attend school,
and higher rates of malnutrition are found for girls than boys. Some of the activities carried
out at household level such as subsistence farming and herding are broken down between men
and women.
Principal activities carried out by men include the clearing of trees, planting, harvesting and
the selling of products from the machamba. Men are also responsible for building the house,
producing crafts and on the coast, approximately 95% of the men fish.
Apart from farming, typical economic activities carried out by women include the selling of
products from the machamba while at the coast some families collect marine products. They
also fish using small nets. Generally, the land and property belongs to the husband and it is
him who covers all family expenses.
Mogovolas District
Inheritance follows a patrilineal system where belongings are passed from father to the son.
On average women will be more illiterate than men, more boys than girls will attend school,
and higher rates of malnutrition are found for girls than boys. Some of the activities carried
out at household level such as subsistence farming and herding are broken down between men
and women.
Principal activities carried out by men include the clearing of trees, planting, harvesting and
the selling of products from the machamba. Men are also responsible for building the house,
producing crafts and selling firewood and coal.
Apart from farming, typical activities carried out by women include the selling of products
from the machamba and activities such as pottery. Women are also responsible for most of the
domestic work. Generally, the land and property belongs to the husband but in some families
they belong to both.
v    Economy
The economy of the Angoche District is almost entirely based on agriculture but fishing plays
a secondary role. Whereas in Magovolas District the economy is almost entirely based on
subsistence agriculture.
vi   Poverty
Angochie District
The main period of poor nutrition is the period between October and March when crop stores
are at their lowest and before the new season's harvest. The nutritional status of the District is
considered reasonable. Shortages of food often occur in the coastal areas as a result of low
production yields but sometimes it occurs due to the excessive seeling of harvested crops. The

3.2.4    Services
i   Education
Angoche District
According to the District Development Profiles, the district has 50 lower primary schools
(EPI) but the number of classrooms is not known. 261 teachers are allocated to the primary
schools with a ratio of 1 teacher to 46 students. Of the total number of students who attend
primary school, 70% are boys.
The next level of schooling is the upper primary school (EP2) teaching 6th and 7th grades.
Three such schools exist in the district at Angoche, Malatane and Ingure. Together the district
has 24 classrooms for this level of schooling. One secondary school is located in the district
capital. The school has 8 classrooms and provides education from the eight to the tenth grade.
In the District there are 296 teachers for 13,172 pupils. Teaching qualifications are varied: 185
teachers have sixth grade and an additional year of training, 47 teachers have grade six and
three years of training, four teachers have gth grade and two years of training, 11 teachers have
grade nine and three years of training, eight teachers have graduated from the Eduardo
Mondlane University as secondary school teachers, two teachers are expatriates and 85
teachers have received no training.
Mogovolas District
In the District of Mogovoals there are approximately 66 schools dsitributed in 12 localities.
They total 237 classrooms, a marked decrease form the 120 lower primary schools that existed
in 1982, with the consequence that no schools operate in the localities of Calipo, luluti and
Nanhupo-Rio according to available information.
No secondary school operates in the district. Upper level primary education (EP2) is taught in
just one school, in Nametil, which is also the district capital.
Approximately 231 teachers are posted at these schools. The majority of the teachers have
sixth grade (63 with one year of training, and 44 with three years), 25 teachers have undergone
no teacher training, 10 have ninth grade plus three years training, and two have higher level
courses. There are also 87 teachers who have had access to other, unspecified types of courses.
ii   Health
Angochie District
Angoche district has a total of 15 sanitary units including one rural hospital based in the
district capital. Three health centres are located in Namet6ria, Namaponda and Catamoio.
Further health posts can be found in Aube, Merrere, Natir, Napuma, Nacopa, M'papata,
Sangane, Angopesca, Maratane and Quiloa. On average, each health unit services 15,200
people who travel between I and 25 km to get to the nearest health unit.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road  January 2001                         3-8
Mozambique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                               w   NSIEC
According to the District Development Profiles compiled by UNDP and UJNHCR, only one
fixed vaccination post is known at Natir while three maternity units can be found at Aube,
Quilua Island and Natir. The district has the medical services of two doctors but no further
information was available on the staff in the remaining health units.
Mogovolas District
Information from the Ministry of Health indicates that there are four health centres in the
district. These are located in Nametil, Calipo, luluti and Muatua. Mogovolas possesses no
secondary level health unit. The distirct also has an additional three health posts, but only the
recently built one at Nanhupo-Rio has a maternity unit.
The administration of the Idsitrct says that community participation in health programmes is
significant, with the population assisting in the building of health posts using local materials,
as well as taking part in vaccination, nutrition and environmental health programmes.
However, the same source argues that the Mogovolas health network needs expansion to
ensure improvements in providing this kind of service. The needs of the district inlcude the
need to build two more health posts and three maternity wards as weel as the umprading and
expansion of the Nametil health centre.
iii    Key Social Services and Facilitates
While the District is relatively well served by primary (EPI) schools access to secondary
schools is poor. Availability of qualified medical staff is extremely low and most families are
dependent upon traditional healers to meet their medical requirements.
3.2.5    Livelihood
i   Livelihood strategies
Subsistence agriculture is one of the main activities of families living in Angoche and
Mogovolas Districts. The main food crops grown are cassava which occupies by far the
greatest crop area, followed by maize and sorghum.
Table 6 below shows total cultivated area by Crop (hectares) and total production
(tonnes/metric) for 1999/2000 for the district of Angoche. The District is not one with a
livestock farming tradition but many households keep ducks and chickens. The extent of the
agricultural activity is dependent upon several factors, notably:
o  The fertility of the soil
o  The availability of water
o  Availability of good pasture
o  The availability of supportive transportation linkages to facilitate trade
o  The availability of communication linkages to facilitate trade
o  Population densities
o  The availability of social services and amenities to support the population
o  The availability of supportive formal financial and credit institutions

ii   General characteristics of a family living along or in close proximity to EN239

Agricultural activities are carried out by the different members of the family who partake in
sowing, weeding and harvesting. Women are responsible for gathering fuel wood by chopping
down trees alongside men. Men also deal with crop storage. The agricultural produce grown
by the family is mainly used for own consumption but excess is sold in the markets of the
district capital or to traders who come from the provincial capital. It is common to find
families who use the same farmland every year and who do not leave the land fallow. The
main agricultural system is still based upon slash and bum techniques.
In the house women will look after the children, cook, clean the house, and would be involved
in most daily household activities. Men are responsible for construction and maintenance of
the house. The period of most work is between November and February, the main period of
cultivation and harvest. Women will control the household stocks of food while men will be
responsible for expenditure related to the children.
