4 POTENTIAL ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
4.1 IDENTIFICATION
This section provides an assessment of the potential environmental impacts associated with the
rehabilitation of the Nametil to EN 260 road works. Environmental factors considered were:
o  Land use change;
o  Erosion and sedimentation;
o  Water quality;.
o  Hydrology;
o  Noise and vibration;
o  Air quality;
o  Biological effects; and
o  Socio-economic effects.
Each of these issues is addressed in Sections covering the physical, biological and socio-
economic environments.
The majority of direct environmental impacts will be minor due to the limited extent of works
at any one site and the incorporation of impact mitigation measures into the improvement
works. The ENI currently has a sufficient and well defined road corridor which is identified
by ANE as 15m either side of the road centreline (Pieter Van Niekerk, pers. Comm., ANE,
November 2000).
Indirect effects, may result from increased natural resource use due to increased local
enterprise activity to service increased traffic. These impacts are anticipated to be relatively
minor due to the fact that the road is already well established as the major north-south access
route in southern and central Mozambique.
Many of the potential environmental impacts associated with the construction of a new road do
not occur to the same degree when a road is improved within the existing road corridor. The
most likely impacts include:
o Altered land use patterns due to improved accessibility, such as a change from forestry to
agriculture, or from agriculture to industry (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997);
o  Public health problems, particularly through the spread of communicable disease (Birley,
1997); and
o  Increased illegal logging and poaching activities due to improved access into forest areas
(Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997).
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road    January 2001                   4-1
Mozambique Road Sector ELJ           DRAFT



IMPA CTO Lda                                                           (k(iSwEC
4.2 PHYSICAL EFFECTS
4.2.1    Land Use
i   Construction
Ancillary activities are temporary activities required during the construction period and will
affect a relatively small land area. These sites will be selected in consultation with the
landowners and rehabilitated upon completion of activities. Ancillary sites will be located in
degraded areas, where possible, to ensure that large areas of land will not need to be cleared
and the change to land use is not significant.
Total land acquisition will be minimal relative to the project corridor length. Temporary areas
of land use change will occur as a result from establishment of ancillary sites such as batching
plant sites, borrow pits and workforce camp sites.
Where possible existing borrow areas will be utilised during the road works to avoid additional
disturbance. The rehabilitation of these sites after completion of the works will be undertaken
through revegetation and levelling the landform to its original condition. Where new borrow
pits are established these will be located at least lOOm from the road in accordance with
current ANE guidelines.
ii   Operation
The proposed road works will not have a direct impact on land use change outside the road
corridor. The extent of the road to be rehabilitated includes a length of 76km, which will be
maintained within the road corridor, and no realignments of the road are required. The
Nametil road is currently in a highly degraded condition and rehabilitation works will involve
a road formation width of 6 m wide.
The road is also located within a highly populated area of Nampula Province and crosses an
important agricultural area which has links to the provincial capital of Nampula, the major
town of Nametil and the coastal port town of Angoche. Encroachment of settlements and
cultivated subsistence farming within the road corridor may occur along some sections of the
road in which case the land use will change due to the road works. The impact is expected to
be minor and compensatory measures will apply as discussed in Section 3.3.
The road works will have a negligible effect on adjacent land use activities, as the area is
currently accessible however the road rehabilitation will improve the travelling time and access
of agricultural products to the major centres. This is may result in an indirect effect of
increased agriculture, market areas and additional roadside services such as Barracas (bars and
diners) and resting places, to service additional traffic. The number of new services is likely to
be minor as the existing traffic is low and the expected increase in the growth rate will not be
high enough to support a large number of new services.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road  January 2001                        4-2
Mozambique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT



IMPACTO lda                                                            VSW EC
iii   Mitigation Measures
Ancillary sites will be located in areas currently cleared or degraded, where possible, to ensure
that large areas of land will not need to be cleared and the land use is consistent to the area.
All ancillary sites will be rehabilitated upon completion of activities to their previous land use,
unless the facilities are further required. Rehabilitation and land forming of existing borrow pit
sites will result in an environmental improvement to the area.
Mitigation measures for other temporary facilities include:
o Measures identified under socio-economics for selection and negotiation of use; and
o Rehabilitation of all sites to their original condition, or better, to allow resumption of prior
land use.
4.2.2    Erosion and Sedimentation
i   Erosion Hazard
The dynamic interplay of several key factors dictates the degree of susceptibility to water-
driven soil erosion processes. These are:
Li Seasonality and intensity of rainfall in relation to water run-off;
o Landscape topography in relation slope gradients;
o Soil profile parameters, notably depth and binding properties;
o Degree of vegetation cover; and
o Magnitude and frequency of seismic events.
For road works in general, where the capacity of existing drainage works to manage water run-
off is exceeded then the interaction of the above factors will determine the extent and
magnitude of erosion processes. These considerations provide the basis to determine the
overall erosion hazard.
The rating for erosion risk has been determined from the Mozambique Erosion Hazard Map,
1:2,000,000 produced by the Soil and Water Conservation and Land Utilization Co-ordination
Unit (1986).
The Nametil road predominantly passes through red sandy clay loams, brown sandy clay soils.
These soils are characteristically fertile deep soils of medium texture giving them a relatively
low erosion risk. The erosion hazard along the project road is considered to be low-medium
according to the erosion hazard mapping and the road does not pass along steep hilly terrain or
areas of high seismic activity therefore landslides and mass wasting issues are not of concern.
Erosion and sedimentation effects will be greater during the rainy period that occurs from
November to March. During this period 85 to 90 percent of the annual rains occur and erosion
potential will be highest. The impacts from construction will be minimal by limiting the area
of ground disturbance at any one site, and restricting works to the dry period.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road  Januaty 2001                        4-3
Mozambique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT



IAIPACTO L (la                                                          VWSEC
Localised erosion can occur where soils are left exposed. This is commonly observed on steep
unrehabilitated borrow pit batters where gully erosion is evident. Similar problems can occur
on fill batters or other sloped areas.
ii   Sedimentation Risk
Sedimentation is a secondary process of erosion and can be exacerbated by road works due to:
o deliberate and unanticipated modifications to surface and ground water hydrology;
o incomplete rehabilitation of roadside verge areas; and
o road rehabilitation throuwh naturally hilly terrain.
Ground disturbance and the temporary stockpiling of materials during road works can trigger
uncontrolled and or accelerated sedimentation processes. The onset of this process will be
coincident with ground disturbance and will continue up until sites have been stabilised in the
road works zone. The extent of ground disturbance and clearing along the road corridor will
be marginal, with an average width of 16m disturbed during the construction stage. The
potential for sedimentation will be higher during times of heavy rain where the road becomes
submerged due to localised flooding from the drainage lines.
Other areas where ground disturbance and clearing is required during the construction stage is
at stockpile areas, borrow pit sites, construction camps, and work compounds. The fill
required for the road works will be obtained from existing borrow sites or degraded areas
where possible to minimise the need for additional ground disturbance.
The terrain along the project road is gently undulating and therefore the potential for erosion
from the improvement works is not significant. The soil types intersected by the road have a
moderate soil erodability rating with reference to sheet erosion potential (Wambeke, 1986).
There are no areas where significant cut and fill is required and retaining walls will not be
required. The need for road embankments will be minimal and will not create sedimentation
problems if vegetated properly and formed on 1:2 grades.
iii   Mitigation Measures
The impact from sedimentation will be minimised by avoiding stockpiling of materials near
drainage lines and by not undertaking construction during times of heavy rain, particularly the
months of December to March.
Minor reconditioning of the existing culverts will be undertaken where their discharge is
causing downstream erosion. Specific measures for erosion and seiiiment control include:
o  design of drainage works to ensure these collect from and discharge to natural drainage
lines. This may not always be possible for mitre drains and in those situations frequent,
low volume drains are preferred;
o  provision of energy dissipation devices where discharge velocities and flows will erode
downstream drainage lines;
o  minimisation of disturbed areas;
o  design and construction of erosion and sedimentation controls such as diversion banks,
filter structures (filter fences or windrowed vegetation) and sediment traps;
58602 Annexure E - Nametid to EN 260 Road  January 2001                         4-4
Mozambique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                             WIwSMEC
Li diversion of significant upstream flows around working areas; and
o  undertaking progressive rehabilitation and revegetation of all slopes and disturbed areas.
In addition, temporary erosion control drains will be installed prior to the wet season and no
new road excavation will be commenced during the wet season.
The installation of side drains and culverts along the road will minimise sedimentation and
runoff effects during operation of the road.
4.2.3    Water Quality
i   Construction
The potential for sedimentation from construction activities is described in the previous section
and will have an impact on water quality. The rates of sedimentation will be higher during the
rainy season therefore construction impacts will be minimised by undertaking works during the
dry season, particularly near perennial streams. The occurrence of sedimentation in permanent
watercourses will have a greater effect during the dry season when flows are low.
Hydrocarbons sourced from improper disposal facilities for used lubricants and poor operation
and maintenance practices for construction vehicles and machinery can contaminate surface
waters. Improper disposal of oil filters and other materials from vehicle and machinery
maintenance can also cause pollution. This can occur during both construction and road
operation, but mostly during the former.
Spilled petroleum fuel, as well as bitumen and explosives residue, also have a potential to
contribute to the pollution of waterbodies, but to a very minor extent.
Improper sanitation and waste disposal procedures in temporary construction camps can also
contaminate water resources.
ii   Operation
Borrow areas have the potential to result in adverse environmental impacts if they are not
rehabilitated correctly. If left in a poor condition water can collect in the depressions created
and become weed infested as well as providing optimal breeding conditions for insect vectors
of parasitic diseases such as malaria
iii   Mitigation Measures
Proper management of construction camps is required to prevent pollution to neighbouring
waterways. Any pollution resulting from these camps shall be remediated immediately.
All fuels, lubricants and bitumen will be stored in bunded areas. Vehicle maintenance and
refuelling will be confined to areas in construction camps designed to confine lubricants and
fuel spillages.
58602 Annexure E - Nametid to EN 260 Road  January 2001                         4-5
Mozambique Road Sector ElIA            DRAFT



IAIPACTO Ldta                                                             -'w^SMEC
Adequate waste disposal and sanitation facilities will be provided at construction camps. Any
wastewater or stormwater discharged from the site shall comply with the following objectives:
o  pH range                         6.0-9.0
L  Total Suspended Solids (TSS)    <50mg/I
o  Total Dissolved Solids (TDS)    <1500mg/I
o  Oils and surfactants             No visible film of oils or petrochemicals
The Contractor shall ensure that all bitumen and fuel storage areas shall be bunded to contain
at least 120% of the maximum capacity of the largest storage tank. The floor of the bunded
area shall be impervious, and storage facilities and bunds shall be inspected weekly for spills.
4.2.4    Hydrology
i    Construction
The road works will have a negligible impact to hydrology, as the rehabilitation works will be
undertaken along an existing alignment. The main construction activity that has the potential
to impede flows will be the stockpiling of materials if placed near drainage lines.
ii    Operation
Although the road is an existing feature, it can cause an impedance to flow, alter the natural
channel position and result in increased velocities as a result of this change. The road crosses
five major watercourses, which will require the provision of adequate sized culverts. The
provision of culverts along the road have been determined for engineering purposes as a
measure for protecting the road structure and they are therefore not within the scope of this
study.
Adequate and stable cross-road and road surface drainage is essential to ensure that storm
runoff across the road causes minimal damage to the road and minimises the effect of
inundation caused by impeded flows. Cross-road drainage shall be provided by causeways and
culverts to pass catchment flows past the road, either over or beneath it. Road surface drainage
shall be provided by side drains to direct runoff off the road surface.
iii    Mitigation Measures
Particular attention shall be given to stockpiling materials away from existing drainage lines
and avoid filling in wetland areas.
All designed drainage works shall be installed at the time of initial road construction to ensure
that flows along drainage lines are not impeded.
Culverts shall be laid in a direction, which is as close as possible to the natural position of the
drainage flow.  This will avoid altering the channel flow  and prevent upstream  and
downstream scouring of the watercourse.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road  January 2001                            4-6
Mozambique Road Sector EIA               DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                          'irSMEC
4.2.5    Noise and Vibration
i   Construction
Some sensitive receptors, such as schools and hospitals, are located within 5-lOm of the ENI
road pavement. Additional noise will be generated along the ENI road corridors and at
ancillary sites during the construction period, particularly from the increased number of heavy
vehicles. Vibration effects will be negligible due to the generally sandy soil substrate. Peak
noise levels generated by construction activities are expected to be greater than the peak noise
levels from road operation.
Construction noise is generally intermittent and depends on the type of operation, location and
function of the equipment and the equipment use cycle. Noise attenuates quickly over
distance. Excavation noise levels, for example, assuming bulldozer and dump truck activity,
yield a level of approximately 85 dBA at 1Sm, but decrease by about 3-4 dBA with each
doubling of the setback distance, and would be reduced to approximately 70 dBA at about
240m.
ii   Operation
Accessibility will be improved after completion of the road works, and vehicle noise levels
may increase slightly with an expected increase in traffic growth. After completion of the
works, the traffic on the Nametil Road is expected to experience a high growth rate due to
improved accessibility (See Part B).
Noise sources and factors which may contribute to the increase in the ambient noise level and
related adverse impacts during the operation of the improved roads will include vehicle noise,
vehicle maintenance and driver behaviour (such as the use of horns).
The improved road design will allow an increase in vehicle speed and some increase in the
overall noise level is likely to result. When traffic speed doubles, this results in a 6 dB(A)
increase in noise level (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997). The majority of the project roads
traverse open cultivated land and the impact from an increase in noise levels from increased
traffic speed will be minimal.
The road works may reduce existing noise levels in built-up areas as a result of improved road
surface. This will reduce noise levels through the more efficient operation of vehicles and less
noise from vehicle tyres.
As ambient noise levels are generally lowest at night, the impact from traffic noise is expected
to be greater during these hours.
iii   Mitigation Measures
No national noise level standards currently exist for highway construction equipment in
Mozambique, however the World Bank recommends the use of construction equipment, which
meets international standards.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road    January 2001                    4-7
Mozambique Road Sector EIA            DRAFT



I,IIPACTO Lda                                                             VWSMEC
The following measures shall be implemented to minimise the impact of noise during the
construction phase:
o Work compounds shall be located at least 0.5km from settlements;
o In construction sites within 150m of residential areas, construction shall occur between
19:00 and 06:00 hours; and
o Maintenance of machinery and vehicles shall be undertaken and repaired regularly to keep
noise at a minimum.
4.2.6    Air Quality
i Construction
The deterioration in air quality from dust generation and vehicle emissions during road
construction and operation will be a relatively minor environmental impact. Significant
adverse effects are not expected because of the limited duration of construction at any one site
and the few receptors. Most of the work will occur in open, cultivated areas.
Deterioration in air quality during road construction will be experienced primarily from dust
generated by road works, spoil transportation and disposal. Construction will be primarily
labour based, with excavation and material transportation undertaken by hand, therefore dust
generation should be minimal due to the small area and volume of soil disturbance at any one
time. In addition, most construction activities will progressively move along the alignment, so
prolonged deterioration in air quality will not occur at any one site.
ii   Operation
Existing air quality is good and no significant air quality effects are expected during operation.
Traffic volumes are low and the road generally traverses rural road areas. Exhaust emissions
from traffic utilising the rehabilitated road are likely to increase due to an expected increase in
traffic growth. The emissions are still expected to be low because existing traffic numbers
currently utilising the road are relatively low.
Improved road geometry will reduce exhaust emissions along the project road by reducing the
roughness of the road surface (particularly by removal of potholes) increasing the design speed
and thereby reducing the need for excessive acceleration and deceleration (Tsunokawa and
Hoban, 1997).
iii   Mitigation Measures
Mitigation measures will be implemented in areas where air quality problems would arise due
to construction activities. Areas of concern for dust control are in major settlements and
sensitive sites such as schools and hospitals.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road  January 2001                           4-8
Mozambique Road Sector EIA              DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                            'aSMEC
Minimising air emissions is particularly important during the construction stage, for activities
such as the operation of borrow pits and in-situ mixing of cement and sand, which have a
potential to generate high dust levels.
During the construction period, the following mitigation measures to suppress dust will be
implemented:
o  Water will be sprayed during the construction period at earth mixing sites, asphalt-mixing
sites, and on cleared surfaces. Water spraying will be undertaken during periods of high
wind, or when necessary, to suppress dust;
o  Operations shall be suspended during periods of dry windy weather, in areas where wind
blown dust is causing a nuisance in settlements or towns;
o  All equipment and plant machinery shall not emit visible smoke for a continuous period of
greater than 10 seconds, and
o  All exhaust systems and engines on plant and equipment shall be in accordance with the
manufacturers specifications and kept well maintained to ensure that they have a high level
operating efficiency.
4.3 BIOLOGICAL EFFECTS
4.3.1    Direct impacts
Vegetation will need to be cleared to undertake the road rehabilitation works and enable road
formation widening and construction of additional drainage. The conservation value of the
road corridor vegetation that will be cleared is not high. The woodland areas crossed by the
road corridor are highly utilised, particularly for slash and burn agriculture, which is frequently
practiced in the Province. The amount of clearing that will need to be undertaken will be
minimal, as the road corridor is well defined for the majority of its length. The impact of any
clearing required for the road projects will not be significant due to the already highly
degraded nature of the woodland habitat.
Vegetation may also be cleared in areas selected for ancillary sites, such as construction
camps, borrow pits and quarries, to enable the utilisation of these areas.
There are no known wildlife crossings along the length of the road project and the habitat has
been highly modified by anthropogenic uses. The road works have no potential for significant
adverse effects on wildlife.
4.3.2    Indirect impacts
There is a potential impact associated with the indirect loss and degradation of vegetation,
habitat and species in the ROI. These potential impacts are associated with:
o Improved access;

Mozambique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT

o In-migration; and
Li Construction workforce behaviour.
Ecological degradation of the ROI may occur from vegetation harvesting and wildlife
poaching by the construction workforce, and increased harvesting during road operation due to
greater accessibility and additional local demands from road-induced activities.
The projects will provide improved access to the neighbouring areas and facilitate natural
resource use. The main potential for increased natural resource harvesting is likely to occur
within the area's woodlands.
The population increase along the road corridor would be derived either from people migrating
to the roadside from the immediate area (i.e. the 5km region of influence and adjacent to this),
or from other areas. This in-migration has a potential to create an increased local demand for
fuelwood and construction timber, as well as other woodland products to support secondary
enterprise activities, such as forage collection for grazing animals. Hunting may also increase.
The proposed road improvements will follow an existing alignment and almost all forest areas
neighbouring this road are currently accessible. The road will only increase potential access to
these forests, not open them up.
Importing a construction workforce into an area may increase demand on local natural
resources due to competition for firewood, woodland products and wildlife. These increased
demands will be focussed on the areas surrounding workforce camps and work compounds.
Worker camps may also encourage the temporary construction of markets and barracas to
service the workers. These temporary facilities may also increase demand on local natural
resources.
4.3.3    Mitigation measures
i   Direct Impacts
Vegetation clearance along the road edge and at ancillary sites (such as borrow pits) will be
minimised. All construction areas will be clearly marked and construction activity prohibited
outside the defined areas. Tress within ancillary sites shall be retained wherever possible.
Retention of trees within areas such as worker camps will improve the camp amenity and
provide shade.
All temporarily disturbed areas will be rehabilitated following construction. The establishment
of ground cover is the initial priority of revegetation. Ground cover reduces raindrop impact,
binds the soil surface and creates a micro-climate favourable for the establishment of other
vegetation. Species should be selected for cover crops and permanent revegetation. A cover
crop consisting of a rapid growing species and several vigorous grass species should be used to
rapidly establish ground cover on disturbed areas. Permanent revegetation species should be
selected to be consistent with the local native species in the area.
Revegetation will aim to stabilise the road verges and assist the many desirable secondary uses
of the road including:
58602 Annexure E - Nametdl to EN 260 Road  January 2001                        4-10
Afozambique Road Sector EIA            DRAFT



IMVPACTO Ldl(i                                                         QINrSMEC
o production of forage, wood, flowers, fruit, medicinal items;
o shade and shelter;
o fencing and screens; and
o building supports.
Revegetation along different sections of road will be designed to achieve site-specific aims,
such as maximising shade within villages and providing a species composition that is
compatible with surrounding vegetation cover.
ii   Indirect Impacts
The most potential for indirect effects was identified to be associated with workforce camps.
This is a temporary effect but may last several years depending on the duration of the works
project. To reduce the potential for increased exploitation of natural resources the following
migration measures will be implemented. The Contractor will:
o provide and maintain adequate drinking and bathing water supplies;
o provide kerosene or other fuel source and stoves for worker use;
o educate workers about the responsible use of natural resources; and
o ensure workers act in a responsible manner and apply penalties for the harvesting of local
resources (wood, wildlife or wood products).
4.4 SOCIO-ECONOMIC IMPACTS
4.4.1    Direct impacts
i   Loss of agricultural land, buildings, sites of cultural value, population displacement,
and natural resources degradation
Improvements to the road will not involve the significant loss of cultivated land, major
buildings or require the displacement of the local population. Cultivation is not continuous
along the road and there is sufficient land for machambas if necessary to be realigned.
Land may need to be acquired for the purposes of borrow pits as a source for road building
materials. Negotiation with local authorities and farmers would secure access for borrow pits
and appropriate compensation established.
Where an area of cultivated land has to be resumed for road construction, the farmer should be
compensated for loss of production. The following process will be applied:
o where possible the farmer should be notified of the loss of land one season in advance.
This is to avoid unnecessary loss of effort and seed; and
o the value of the lost production should be determined in consultation with the District
Agricultural Officer and the local Chefe de Poste (Chief of the Post). Compensation will
be paid to the farmer for the value of the lost production.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road  January 2001                       4-11
Mozambique Road Sector EMI            DRAFT



IVPA CTO Ldu                                                           ( (iSMEC
Borrow pits, storage and equipment areas, and construction camps will be located on marginal
or disturbed land. The acquiring of land for such purposes must be subject to ANE and
Provincial appreval. In selecting land consultation must also be undertaken with local
communities and their traditional leaders as well as local authorities (at District and Ward
level).
4.4.2   Indirect impacts
i   Access to services
Improvement of the Nametil road is likely to have significant socio-economic impacts by
enabling better access to services such as better health services located in the Provincial capital
of Nampula.
ii   Improved access to markets
As the road is located within a rich agricultural area, the road rehabilitation is expected to
provide better opportunities for the local communities to transport surplus crop production to
Nampula City, Namialo and the port town of Nacala. These three major centres are within
easy distance of the road and offers a market for agricultural production.  Continued
improvement and maintenance to the road will enable goods and services to flow more easily
into the area particularly those associated with improving agricultural production techniques
and yields. A better quality of road would likely reduce transport costs both fuel consumption
and vehicle wear.
iii   In-migration of people and concessions
Improvement of roads in other rural areas within Mozambique leads often to increases in in-
migration as advantage is taken of the improved communication. It is probable that some in-
migration will take place particularly in areas in close proximity to Nampula, which currently
has the greatest population pressure. Regulos have an influence in terms of the local allocation
of land. Mitigating measures should involve working with regulos and other local authorities
to control any possible in-migration.
iv    Health issues
It is likely that the road project will necessitate the inflow of a number of construction workers
from other parts of Mozambique. The inflow of road gangs, particularly into remote areas has
a potential for adverse effects. The most often cited problem is the one of HIV-AIDs and
sexually transmitted diseases.  Mitigation measures against these likely impacts require
working closely with respective govemment departments; local NGOs and/or church based
organisations, local communities and road gangs. Educational programmes for the local
populations are considered to be extremely important in trying to reduce the likelihood of HIV-
AIDs infection.
58602 Annexure E - Nametil to EN 260 Road  January 2001                        4-12
Mozambique Road Sector EIA             DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                         'O3SME
v    Construction worker camps
The health issues related to construction worker camps particularly in -terms of the spread of
HIV-AIDs and other sexually transmitted diseases have been mentioned above.  A
concentration of workers also attracts other inward migrants who attempt to exploit new
market opportunities. In other parts of Mozambique this has often resulted in the creation of
small unplanned roadside shops. Such informal sites are often poorly managed in terms of
adherence to health regulations and waste disposal. Mitigation measures should include the
identification of suitable sites for the establishment of small shops and other facilities servicing
road-gangs.
vi   Recommendations
In conclusion road improvements and maintenance to the EN8 are unlikely to have any
significant socio-economic impacts. Maintaining the road in a good condition will encourage
farmers to explore marketing options for their surplus production and may encourage the
production of cash crops such as cotton.
The main negative socio-economic impacts relate to the problem of the spread of HIV-AIDs
infection. Campaigns in other parts of Mozambique have demonstrated that these problems
can be mitigated against, particularly if resources and education are directed at the local
population and not just the influx of construction workers.
Further studies are recommended to better predict probable socio-economic impacts, which
would occur as a result of the road rehabilitation.

5 ENVIRONMENTAL MITIGATION MEASURES
The road works will include environmental impact mitigation measures within the Contract
Specifications. These measures will be incorporated as specific instructions and
recommendations to the Contractor and will be provided in a separate Environmental
Management Plan (EMP). The EMP will identify specific instructions to the Contractor and
will include responsibilities, outputs, costs and timing of mitigation measures.
Design, construction and maintenance mitigation recommendations for the proposed roads
have been formulated to avoid or mitigate potential bio-physical impacts.  These
recommendations were primarily formulated from observations made during the inspection of
constructed roads.
The EMP describes the practical means to ensure effective implementation of the mitigation
measures recommended for the potential adverse environmental impacts identified in the EIA.
The EMP will include but will not be limited to the following elements:
o  Practical means to implement the recommended mitigation measures;
o  Implementation Schedule and Supervision Programme;
o  Monitoring and Evaluation;
o  Institutional Strengthening, Management and Training; and
o  Further Studies of Environmental Issues.
In addition, the program also specifies what action will be taken and by whom in the event that
the proposed mitigation measures fail, either partially or totally, to achieve the level of
environmental protection expected.
Specific socio-economic impact mitigation measures will include further consultation with
potentially affected landholders and provision of compensatory measures in accordance with
the current Government of Mozambique policy. These measures have been formulated by the
Department of Agriculture and include guidelines for the displacement of people and
acquisition of land for the roads.
An outline of the EMP for the construction and operation stage is provided in Part E of this
report.

Annex G
MOZAMBIQUE ROAD SECTOR PROGRAM
ENVIRONMENTAL STUDY BRIEFING NOTE
The Administraccao Nacional de Estradas (ANE) has commissioned SMEC International, in
association with Impacto, to prepare an Environmental Study for the next Road Sector Program
for Mozambique (ROADS3).   This briefing note provides some details about the
environmental study to be undertaken.
The ROADS3 program aims to rehabilitate and upgrade portions of the Mozambique road
network over ten years (2000 to 2010). It is a further development of the Government's
Transport Policy. The program is divided into three phases:
L Phase I - 2001 to 2004 for confirmed and defined projects and interventions;
L Phase II - 2005 to 2006 for currently estimated projects and interventions; and
o Phase III - 2008 to 2010 for currently anticipated projects and interventions.
An attached figure identifies the roads to be examined.
The type of work to be undertaken falls into two broad categories, Periodic Maintenance and
Road Upgrading. Periodic Maintenance involves the following activities:
l where necessary widening the road to provide a minimum lane width in each direction of
3.2 metres;
constructing or improving the road shoulder (the formed part of the road to the side of the
travelling lane); and
o improving drainage including the table drains (the drains that run adjacent to the road and
remove water from the travelling surface) and culverts (the drains that pass beneath the
road).
Rehabilitation involves the same works as periodic maintenance but also includes
improvement or reconstruction of the road structure. A road consists of several layers of
material that form the road structure. These are usually referred to as (from bottom to top) the
sub-base, base and wearing courses. The wearing course can be either a select gravel or a
bituminous seal.
Funding is being sought from the World Bank for parts of the ROADS 3 Program. That part of
the program is subject to Environmental Assessment (EA) in compliance with requirements of
the World Bank and the Government of Mozambique. The EA will build on ANE's existing
environmental policy and legislation and the institutional framework developed during earlier
roads programs. The EA will contain two types of assessment, a Sectoral Environmental
Assessment (SEA) and an Initial Environmental Assessment (IEE), both conducted to World
Bank standards.
The SEA will examine the overall roads program proposed for the next 10 years. It will
include:
Mozambique Road Sector EA          October 2000                               1






L  an overview of the environmental characteristics of Mozambique concentrating on those
areas potentially affected by the roads program;
L  a categorization of the road projects according to the potential significance of their
environmental effects. The environmental studies required for projects with a potential for
significant effects would be broadly scoped;
L  an overview of the environmental effects associated with the different categories of road
projects;
L  an assessment of the overall effects of the roads program; and
o an outline environmental management plan to eliminate or mitigate identified adverse
environmental effects.
The IEE will examine the specific projects to be undertaken as part of Phase 1 of the roads
program. An IEE is a limited type of environmental analysis focussing on specific impacts and
their mitigation. Their function is also to determine if further, more detailed assessment is
required.
SMEC will also produce two other documents for ANE:
L  Environmental Management Guidelines. These will be prepared from the:
- findings of the environmental assessment studies;
- analysis of the existing guideline documents used by ANE; and
-  SMEC's experience in preparing similar documents.
The guidelines will be directed at facilitating practical implementation of environmental
mitigation measures.
o  A Training Needs Manual. This will be developed from a monitoring and enforcement
analysis completed as part of the EA. It will specifically focus on the training needs of
ANE staff (including provincial offices) to assess, control and monitor environmental
impacts.
If you would like to find further information about the project please contact Jan Parsons of
SMEC on telephone 499 636/7 or facsimile 493 019.

1 INTRODUCTION
This Annex contains an environmental assessment for one of four additional roads to be
included as part of the Phase I World Bank projects (giving a total of 15 project roads).
Due to time limitations, no field investigations were undertaken for the this environmental
assessment. The description of the environment, potential impacts and recommendations were
prepared as a baseline study using information from existing literature sources, available
mapping sources, knowledge of Provincial characteristics and personal field observations from
ANE technical staff.
The primary references for this study include:
o  Mozambique Erosion Hazard Map (1986), 1:2,000,000.
u  Topographic Map Series (1997), 1:250,000.
c  Land Use Maps - Carta de Uso e Cobertiura da Terra (1999) 1: 250,000.
O  Forestry Maps - Mapa Florestal (1994), 1:250,000.
O  Wild H. and Barbosa LA. (1967). Flora Zambesiaca, 1:2,500,000.
O  Laymeyer International et al (1999). Mepanda Uncuia and Cahora Bassa North Project

2 BIOLOGICAL AND PHYSICAL ENVIRONMENTS
2.1 TOPOGRAPHY AND LANDFORMS
The project road runs parallel and approximately 5km to the south of the southern shoreline of
lake Cahora Bassa over uniformly flat terrain at altitudes typically falling between 400 and
500m above sea level. It traverses an enormous number of seasonal watercourses draining in
to Cahora Bassa, mostly flowing from south-east to north-west.
2.2 CLIMATE
Lying in the inter-tropical zone, the area has a tropical and subtropical climate with two
seasons, wet and hot (November to March), and dry and cool (April to October). Drought
conditions are a frequent occurrence during the dry season.
The average annual temperature is 270 C, the coldest months June and July, and the hottest
months October and November (see Figure 1).

The average annual rainfall is 642mm, and is concentrated mainly in the wet season.
Winds blow mainly North-South for most of the year.
58602 Estima - Magoe                January 2001                                2-1
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



IMPACTO Ldlai                                                           ((:!SMIEC
The probability of extreme events (cyclones, floods) is minimal; over the past five years there
have been none. Overtopping the full supply level of Cahora Bassa can occur periodically
resulting in localised flooding along the lake shoreline. It is likely that sections of the project
road fall within this region of influence.
2.3 GEOLOGY
The road runs parallel and to the south of Lake Cahora Bassa in the Zambezi basin. The main
geological formations of the Zambezi River basin belong to the following units:
o Pre-Cambrian formations
o Overlying formations: Karroo and Post-Karroo
o Quaternary sedimentary formations
Pre-Cambrian formations occur extensively in the Tete Province especially north of the
Zambezi River where outcrops generally correspond to the large plateau, 800 to 1,500m high
best shown in the Zambue and Ang6nia regions in northern Tete Province. South of the
Zambezi, these same plateaux, 600 to 1,500m high, are located on the Catandica-Guro-
Gorongosa ridge area. The formations considered to be pre-Cambrian are composed mainly of
highly metamorphosed and folded sediments with which ancient-eruptive rocks are associated.
They are quite displaced, either due to major intrusions of basic and ultrabasic rocks or to
granitic intrusions of several ages although most of them have been emplaced during the upper
pre-Cambrian and some during the Palaeozoic.
The Karroo and Post-Karroo deposits occur north of the Zambezi river e.g., north of Cahora
Bassa but are better developed south of the Zambezi River. Karroo deposits outcrop in a huge
east - west band that stretches from Zumbo to the Sanangoe River. They also cover
considerable areas in the Moatize and Z6bue regions, north of the Zambezi River. The
outcrops correspond generally to major tectonic depressions inside the pre-Cambrian
formations and occur at heights ranging from 200 to 600m (See Figure 1). Post-karroo
deposits (lower to mid-Cretaceous) include the Magoe sandstones south of Lake Cahora Bassa.
The formations of the Quatemary - Recent zone (c. 3 million years to Recent) occur mainly in
the lower Zambezi, from Quelimane to Beira (most predominantly in the Zambezi Delta) and
penetrate inland far as Sena. Quaternary Deposits in the study area (Tete - Cahora Bassa) are
small and limited.
The Precambrian basement rocks of the Zambezi basin are overlain by sediments and lavas of
the Karoo Supergroup (between 160 - 225 m.y.) and by post-Karroo sediments. Associated
with them are large outcrops of volcanic and plutonic eruptive rocks revealing the intense
eruptive activity that took place during the Mesozoic.
Karroo Super Group
The sediments of the Karroo are of continental origin with dominance of tillites,
conglomerates, sandstones, shales and carbonaceous shales. They make an uninterrupted
outcrop from Chicoa village south-east of Lake Cahora Bassa to Zumbo at the headwater of
Cahora Bassa and northwards, along the Luangua River, as far as the Meze River (Ri and Rs).
58602 Estima - Magoe               January 2001                              2-2
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IAMPA CTO L,l                                                            ((I(SMEC
Karroo sediments again appear to the west and south the Tete Complex, the latter between
Boroma and Tete. Ri (Ecca Group) comprise conglomerates, sandstones and schists and may
have coal bearing deposits. Rs formations are of the upper Karroo comprising conglomerates
and arcositic sandstone.
Post-Karroo Mesozoic Sedimentary Formations
The post-Karroo sedimentary deposits (lower to mid-Cretaceous of the overlying formations
are mainly sandstones. and conglomerates of the Magoe Formation (JK) inter-bedded with
mesozoic lava rocks.
Economically viable deposits of titanium, iron, copper, and zinc occur in the region serviced
by the project road.
The major geological formations in the region are depicted in Figure 2.

2.4 SOILS
The Magoe-Estima road passes through red clayey soils and shallow soils. The distribution of
these soil types along the road is illustrated in Figure 3 and a description of the soil types is
given in Table 1.
Both soil types originate from calcareous rocks and are characteristically reddish brown and
clayey. These soils are generally well to moderately well drained and of moderate depth.

2.5 LAND USE
2.5.1     Description
Along the Magoe-Estima road many medium sized settlements are found. These are generally
non-urbanized settlements. Magoe and Estima are the larger settlements found along the road.
Scattered homesteads mark the area away from the road. Small cultivated fields are scattered
throughout most of the area amidst herbaceous formations, which in some portions are
characterised by trees.
The vegetation is characterised mainly by open mopane and mixed baobab woodlands. There
is no known large-scale commercial exploitation of hardwoods in areas surrounding the Lake
(Direc,co Provincial de Agricultura e Pescas, Tete). Higher local population densities occur at
Estima, Nova Chicoa, Daque and Bawa on the south bank and at Zumbo on the north bank of
the lake. Local communities residing adjacent to the Lake use tree species listed in Table 2 for
subsistence purposes. Lakeshore fishermen smoke-roast a variety of fish species for sale in
Estima and Tete using mainly mopane as firewood.
There is little commercial sale of charcoal and firewood as access is poor for much of the area.

2.5.2    The Fisheries of Lake Cahora Bassa
There are two distinct fisheries in Lake Cahora Bassa. The first is a capital-intensive semi-
industrial fishery that takes the exotic kapenta, Limnothrissa nmiodon, which were introduced
into Lake Kariba in 1967-68 and later invaded Cahora Bassa. The second is the inshore fishery
operated by the local people that take a variety of native species.
i   The Kapenta Fishery
This fishery has grown rapidly in the last few years and the annual catch is now around 10,000
tonnes. There is some concern about its sustainability and potential yield.
The kapenta in Lake Cahora Bassa, like those in Kariba, are small and probably have a 6-9
month life cycle. Because few fish survive to the age of 9 months, mortality rates are
extremely high although there is some debate over their exact magnitude.
The short life cycle of these fish, and their consequent high mortality, has important
implications for the fishery. In Kariba, and probably also in Cahora Bassa, kapenta become
sexually mature when they are about 3 months old (about 35 mm long). These mature fish will
then live, on average, for about another 3 months. The fishery is therefore taking fish, which
will shortly die in any case, and fishing mortality simply substitutes for natural mortality. This
contrasts to the situation in longer-lived fish species where fishing mortality is additional to
natural mortality. This means that it is theoretically possible for the fishery to take the entire
annual production of kapenta without affecting the parent stock.
ii   The inshore fishery
This fishery is based on the native Zambezi River fish, which live in the relatively shallow
inshore waters . Relatively little is known about the biology or abundance of these fish in Lake
Cahora Bassa.
Most of the fishermen living around the lake are involved in the artisanal fishery; most of them
are poor and the fishery is unsophisticated. Dugout canoes are used almost exclusively and
gillnets are the only fishing method employed in this fishery. It is most unlikely that any other
fishing gear will ever be used. The drowned trees and very rocky shoreline prevent the
employment of methods such as beach seines or trawls. The use of longlines has been
suggested and they have been tried previously with some success but are not now used to any
significant extent. The loss of hooks on the drowned trees would probably discourage most
fishermen from attempting this method.
About 39 species of fish have been recorded in the area of the lake but few of them will be of
much economic importance. Only four species are of importance in the fishery: Bottlenose,
Mormyrus longirostris (local name "n'bzio"); Tigerfish, Hydrocynts vittatus ("inchene");
Chessa, Distichoduts schenga ("tchenga"); Kariba tilapia, Oreochromis mortimeri ("pende")
and the African catfish Clarias gariepinuis ("mulamba"). Additional species noted in the
catches include Hippopotamyrus discorhynchus ("nhakaboi"), Brycinus imberi ("mbele") and
Tilapia rendalli ("pende"). Some other species, notably Labeo altivelis ("tsimbo"), would be
expected in the more riverine regions in the western part of the lake.
58602 Estima - Mago6                 January 2001                                2-8
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                             jI.wSEC
The artisanal fishery is centred on numerous fishing villages scattered around the lake; some of
them are relatively large villages with permanent populations but others are simple
encampments.
Attempts to develop community-based management of natural resources have begun through
the Tchuma Tchato program (discussed in a later section) , which has been most successful so
far in the western part of the lake among the Bawa community. So far, this program has been
largely concerned with wildlife management and has not evolved into a fisheries management
project. Nevertheless, it is hoped that fisheries will become part of this programme in time and
that a linkage between the kapenta industry, the inshore fishermen and the local community
will develop to the benefit of all parties.
2.6 RIVERS AND WATER QUALITY
The project road traverses many seasonal watercourses draining in to Cahora Bassa, mostly
flowing from south-east to north-west . The major watercourses from east to west are the
Nhacapiriri, Muze, and Daque Rivers. All these watercourses are seasonal, and only flow
during the summer rainy season.
The lake covers an area of approximately 266,500ha (top storage level). The dam itself was
constructed between 1966-1975. The maximum length of the lake is 246km, and maximum
width is 39.8km.
The Zambezi enters Cahora Bassa at Zumbo (1536'S, 3030'E). The bulk of the water
entering the dam comes from the Zambezi, but in addition, there are 20 north shore, and 36
south shore affluent streams. The north shore catchment is approximately 16,000 km2 in
extent and none of the streams are perennial. The south shore catchment is substantially larger
(40,966 kM2) and two rivers are perennial (Hunyani and Messenguezi Rivers). The road does
not traverse these rivers.
2.7 VEGETATION COVER AND USE
Between Estima and Magoe the road traverses three broad vegetation types:
1. Mopane Woodland (Colophospermum)
2. Mixed Baobab Woodland (Adansonia, Sterculia, Kirkia, Cordyla, Acacia)
3. Dry Deciduous Miombo (Julbernardia)
Mopane Woodland is the co-dominant vegetation type. (Mapping Unit 35, Wild & Barbosa
1967).
Mixed Baobab Woodland is the other co-dominant vegetation type along the project
road (Equivalent to Mapping Units 49 & 56, Wild and Barbosa, 1967).
Dry Deciduous Miombo (Jutlbernardia globiflora) restricted to the vicinity of Estima
(Mapping Unit 30, Wild & Barbosa 1967).
58602 Estima - Mago6               January 2001                              2-9
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



I,PACTO Lda                                                              w(iwsMEC
i   Mopane Woodland
(Mapping Unit 35, Wild & Barbosa 1967).
Mopane woodland, after miombo woodland, is the second most important woodland type in
Mozambique. Mopane covers large areas in the Zambezi Valley (Tete Province) and the
valleys of its tributaries from Lupata to Zumbo below 400 m. The annual rainfall is 500-
700mm.
In the Zambezi valley the Colophospermum mopane woodland is usually medium or tall (10-
15 m.), almost pure but with some Adansonia digitata. The soils are brownish, clayey,
compact, with some calcareous material, relatively deep and mainly derived from Karroo
formations. In pans and depressions with calcareous soils, grassland with Setaria sp. is
dominant.
In shallow, stony and also calcareous soils Acacia nigrescens (subdominant), Dalbergia
melanoxylon, Acacia nilotica, Combretum ghasalense, Dalbergia boehmii, Adansonia digitata,
Albizia harveyi, Terminalia prunioides, T. stiihlmannii, Ziziphus mllcronata, Combretum
apiculatum, Afzelia quanzensis, etc. accompany the still dominant mopane. Associated shrubs
or perennial include Euphorbiaceae (Etuphorbia spp., Erythrococca sp.); Capparidaceae
(Capparis, Thylacium, Cadaba, Maerua); Acanthaceae (Barleria, Disperma); Apocynaceae
(Adenium); Liliaceae (Aloe, Sansivieria) and Salvadoraceae (Salvadora, Azima). The grass
layer is of tufted species such as Ischaemum glaucostachyum, Eragrostis spp., Aristida spp.,
Setaria sp., etc.
Mopane savanna woodland often grades into Julbernardia globiflora savanna woodland in drier
areas with poorer well-drained soils and into the Adansonia, Sterculia savanna domain.
On the banks of the Zambezi River the mopane is often replaced by Ziziphus mauritiana,
Diospyros mespiliformis and sometimes Berchemia discolor.
In small depressions there are savannas with shrubby Combretum ghasalense (generally
dominant) and Diospyros kirkii, Terminalia sericea, Acacia nilotica, A. spp., Lonchocarpus
capassa, Ziziphuis mucronata, etc.
ii   Mixed Baobab Woodland
(Mapping Units 49 & 56, Wild and Barbosa, 1967)
A formation of this bushland/woodland type comprising Adansonia digtata, Sterculia africana,
Kirkia acuminata, Cordyla africana, Sterculia apendiculata, Milletia stuhlmanni,
Lonchocarpus capassa, Otryoderris stuhlmanni,Sclerocarya birrea etc. occurs along a zone of
medium altitude (about 500 - 600m.) between the southern bank of the Zambezi River and the
Barue plateau north of Mungari on formations derived from granites and gneisses or from post-
Karroo lava (Equivalent to Mapping Unit 49, Wild and Barbosa, 1967).
This formation grades into (eastwards) a dry deciduous tree or shrub savanna (sub-planaltic)
comprising Adansonia, Sterculia-Acacia nigrescens, Pterocarpus brenanii-Diplorhynchus
(Equivalent to Mapping Unit 56, Wild and Barbosa, 1967).
58602 Estima - Mjgo6                January 2001                             2-10
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



IMPA CTO Lda                                                            C!I*SMIEC
This type is confined to Mozambique in an area between 200 and 400m. which is situated
between the Zambezi on the north-east, the Chiramba valley to the north and the Urema valley
to the soutlh-east.
Annual rainfall is about 600 to 800mm. The vegetation is a mosaic type with a tree savanna of
Adansonia digitata, Sterculia apendiculata, Afzelia quanzensis and Kirkia acumninata
associated with a scattered shrub savanna of Diplorhynchus condylocarpon, Pterocarpus
brenanii, Acacia nigrescens, Albizia harveyi, Terminalia sericia, etc. occurring on slopes and
gravelly patches. The grass layer consists of Hyparrhenia, Andropogon, Panicum, Urochloa,
etc.
In sheltered areas vestiges of dry dense forest including Millettia stuhlmannii, Cordyla
africana, Albizia versicolor, Ostryoderris stuhlmannii, Sclerocarya birrea, Kigelia pinnata,
Lonchocarpus, Dalbergia melonoxylon, etc. This type grades into a savanna type with Acacia
xanthophloea or A. polyacantha subsp. compylacantha, A. tortilis subsc. spirocarpa,
Combretum imberbe, C. microphyllum, etc. The grasslands in good seasons develop in low
lying areas bordering the ?tandos? with Acacia spp. and Piliostigma thonningii.
iii   Dry Deciduous Miombo
(Julbernardia globiflora) (Mapping Unit 30, Wild & Barbosa 1967).
A .Julbernardia globiflora type occurs in Tete Province and is widespread in belts between the
Brachystegia boehmii communities and the drier Colophospermum mopane savanna
woodlands. It is abundant on the Zambezi valley slopes from 400-700m. and in the lowlands
where the topography is irregular. Annual rainfall is about 800-900 mm and the soils are
orange to greyish. The following species in a decreasing order of frequency occur where
Julbernardia globiflora is dominant: Diplorhynchus condylocarpus, Terminalia sericea,
Brachystegia boehmii, Diospyros kirkii, Oxytenanthera abyssica, Colophospermum mopane,
Pterocarpus brenanii, Combretum ghasalense, Burkea africana, Piliostigma thonningii,
Pseudolachnostylis maprouneifolia, Acacia nigrescensis, Kirkia acuminata, Dalbergia
melanoxylon, etc. (Barbosa, 1958).
The distribution of vegetation types is depicted in Figure 4.

2.8 WILDLIFE AND CONSERVATION AREAS
The wilderness area from the Daque River (35km east of Magoe) west to the border with
Zimbabwe is under the control of Tchuma Tchato, a community-based sustainable natural
resource management initiative. A pilot project was started in Chintopo ward and due to its
success, rapidly expanded to include both Daque and Saiaris wards within the Districts of
Magoe and Cahora Bassa. Currently, Tchuma Tchato controls an area of approximately
9,250km2 mostly along the south-western shoreline of Cahora Bassa. The programme is
currently receiving funding from the Ford Foundation and the IDRC, and technical assistance
from the IUCN.
There are two permanent camps, one at Bawa and one at Daque. The Bawa camp is the more
established and has for the past three years hosted safari hunters and sport fishermen. There
are currently sixteen game guards working at Bawa and ten at Daque. Although no actual
censuses of wildlife numbers have been conducted the game wardens confidently stated that
poaching is decreasing and that populations of many important big game species are increasing
(based on more frequent sightings and new sightings in previously unoccupied habitats). The
only negative impact reported was an increase in habitat destruction by elephant.
Some of the large mammals believed to be increasing in number, and others of conservation
concern are listed in Table 3.

Over the last three years, approximately 33% of the revenue generated from safari hunting
(US$112,000) and sport fishing (US$38,000) activities have been redistributed among
participating communities. Tchuma Tchato's vision for the future is to be actively involved in
the development of ecotourism along the southern shoreline of Cahora Bassa and to promote
rural development (e.g. new schools, clinics and agricultural land) through their partnerships
with local communities.
The Ford Foundation has already invested US$500,000 in the project and will provide a further
US$280,000 for the setting up of an animal sanctuary over the next three years. The reserve
covers an area of approximately 2,500km2 and is home to numerous species of large mammals
(including the "big five", lion, leopard, rhino, elephant, and buffalo). Tchuma Tchato is of
critical importance if wildlife is to be effectively conserved in this area. The Province suffered
severe poaching during the war years. Community involvement in the Tchuma Tchato
programme has largely facilitated the re-establishment of game in this area.
2.9 NOISE AND AIR QUALITY
Noise levels along the Estima to Magoe Project Road, are very low due to the absence of
traffic noise and sparse settlement along the route. The noise levels are predominantly
generated by natural conditions (wind, insects, frogs, birds, etc).
Ambient air quality in all of the proposed road areas is very good due to a lack of industry and
urban activity.  Haze may be observed due to the frequently practiced slash and burn
agriculture and production of charcoal. Levels of airborne dust generated by winds or smoke
from forest and grassland fires can be high on occasion.

3 SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
3.1 PROVINCIAL CHARACTERISTICS
The purpose of this section is to describe the socio-economic environment in Tete Province.
Information was derived from existing reports and publications where information is
disaggregated usually at the Provincial and District levels.
To describe the living conditions for the populations living along or in proximity to the road a
variety of Provincial and District socio-economic indices was used. Where possible statistical
information was provided to enable the establishment of base line data from which the impacts
of road improvement may be made.
3.1.1    Context and Administration
i   Provincial setting and administration
Tete Province is located in the north western region of Mozambique and borders three
countries, Malawi to the east, Zambia in the north and Zimbabwe to the west. To the south of
Tete Province lies the Province of Manica. The shape of Tete Province as a peninsula
surrounded by foreign countries has influenced the economic, cultural and social links of Tete
with surrounding countries. The province also acts as a main corridor between the surrounding
countries.
Administratively Tete is divided into 12 Districts, with a Provincial Capital also called Tete.
Tete is the third largest Province in Mozambique with a land surface of 100,724km2
representing 12.8% of the land surface of Mozambique. Conversely it has the second lowest
population density at 11 people per km2.
ii   Traditional administration
Traditional administration authority exists in Tete Province. The importance attached to the
regulos varies from area to area. In those localities where the regulos are strong and respected
they will intervene in a variety of aspects of every-day life. Such issues would include
resolving social conflicts; allocating land for building housing, managing water sources and
supporting official health program such HIV/AIDS management.
Like most parts of Mozambique, tensions exist between the two types of administrative
systems. These tensions were exaggerated during the armed conflict. Areas controlled by
RENAMO largely reverted back to traditional leadership structures, while those under
Government control retained their post-independence organisational structures.
58602 Estima - Magoe                January 2001                              3-1
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



IMPA CTO Lda                                                            Q(qS NEC
3.1.2    Population Characteristics
i   Population
The 1997 national survey estimated the population of Tete to be 1,144,604 inhabitants with
more women (595,674) than men (548,930). The country's annual population growth rate is
estimated at 2.2% and if that rate continues to 2009 it is calculated that the population will
have grown by 34% to about 1,533,769.
The population of Tete Province is young with more than 50% aged 14 or younger. The
population density for Tete Province (11 inhabitants per kmi2) is lower than for the average
population density for the country (19 inhabitants per kmi2). The total urban population is
calculated at 15% of the Provincial population In comparison to other Provinces Tete has a
relatively low urban percentage.
The population of Tete Province has experienced significant mobility over the last 40 years.
This has been associated with both the wars in Mozambique and also the Zimbabwe liberation
war. While most of Mozambique has experienced similar problems the people of Tete
probably experienced greater dislocation.
ii   Ethnicity
There are three main ethnic groups in Tete Province, the Nyanja (48%), Nyungwe (28%) and
Sena (12%). These percentages were based on the mother tongue of the people. Less than 3%
of the population speak Portuguese as their mother tongue.
iii   Gender
Despite the advancement of the rights of women under independent Mozambican
administration, girls and women in Tete Province do less well than boys and men. Access to
health services directed specifically at women is generally very poor. Literacy rates for
women are lower than for men. Family law in Mozambique still discriminates against women.
A married woman has limited rights to make independent economic decisions or enjoy full
ownership of assets, and a widow's rights to inheritance are secondary to males in the family.
Girls in rural areas in general marry young often as soon as they reach puberty.
3.1.3    Economic Characteristics
i   Economy
The economy of Tete is predominantly agricultural. Based on the percentage of people in
occupations the most important activities at the 1997 census were agriculture (90%), service
industries (5.5%), manufacturing (1.5%) and construction (1.4%).
58602 Estima - Mf, Joe             January 2001                              3-2
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IVPA CTO Lda IM"'OS IEC
There is a low urban-rural ratio, with a majority of people (90%) dependant on subsistence
agriculture for their livelihood. There are few formal sector employment opportunities except
in the city of-Tete.
ii    Poverty
Tete is one of the poorest provinces in the country with 82% classed as poor and 54% as very
poor. This compares with national averages of 69% (poor) and 38% (very poor).
Life expectancy in Tete Province is expected to be below the average level of 42 years
registered for the country.
3.1.4    Services
i    Education and health facilities
Information about the specific education and health facilities in Tete Province were not
compiled. Based on other roads studied for this project it is expected that:
o There will be few schools and the availability of education will decrease as a child ages.
The average ratio of pupils:teacher is expected to be in excess of 50:1.
Li Common illnesses in the Province are malaria, tuberculosis, bilharzia, intestinal parasites,
acute respiratory infection, AIDS, sexually transmitted diseases and non-specific diarrhoea.
o Current rates of HIV infection are in excess of 23% and are projected to rise. The central
region containing the provinces of Tete, Manica, Sofala and Zambezia has the highest rate
of infection in the country due to the presence of major transport corridors and proximity
to borders.
c Probably half of all children under five-will suffer from chronic malnutrition and I in 10
from acute malnutrition.
ii    Access to services
Access to services for the rural population is generally poor. Most villages will have a primary
school but these schools could be categorised as rudimentary and often the availability of
teachers is very low. Figures are not available for the provision of secondary schools. Most
villages will have access to a basic health centre but few have access to a doctor. Dependence
on traditional healers is the norm for the vast majority of the population and almost every
village would have at least one person categorised as a traditional healer.
3.1.5    Livelihood Strategies
The principle economic activity in Tete Province is agriculture and 90% of the population
listed agriculture, forestry or fishing as their main area of activity at the 1997 census. Forestry
and fishing activities are generally for personal consumption. Most food production is within
the family sector upon which the majority of the population is dependent. The principle food
crops grown are maize, sorghum and millet.
58602 Estima - Magoe                  January 2001                                  3-3
Mozambique Road Sector EA                DRAFT



BIPACTO Lda                                                               4 ,SMEC
Livestock production is important to the well being of people in Tete. Most families will have
goats and a lesser number cattle. These animals are kept for reproduction and used as an
income source (money or barter).
3.1.6    Archaeological and Cultural Sites
The road does not pass through or affect any known archaeological or cultural sites. Any
major rehabilitation work would necessitate consultation with local administrative or
traditional leaders to identify any local cultural sites.
3.2 DISTRICT CHARACTERISTICS
3.2.1    Context and Administration
i   Introduction
This section describes socio-economic factors more specific to the ER555 Estima to Magoe
road. The main sources of information relate to existing written reports concerning the
Districts the ER555 Estima to Magoe road passes through.
ii   District setting and administration
The ER555 Estima to Magoe road road passes through the Districts of Cahora Bassa and
Magoe located in the central-south of Tete Province. To the north, Lake Cahora Bassa borders
both districts.
The ER555 Estima to Magoe road project road begins on the outskirts of Estima, within the
District of Cahora Bassa and continues into Magoe District where it ends at Magoe.
iii   Political Environment
No information is available for voting practices based in the two Districts. Tete Province
voted for both the ruling party FRELIMO and for the opposition party RENAMO during the
general elections of late 1999.
3.2.2    Population Characteristics
i   Population
The 1997 national census estimated that the population of Cahora Bassa District was 57,675
inhabitants, totaling 12,842 families. These are distributed in the three administrative posts at
Songo, Chitholo and Chitima.
58602 Estima - Magoe                January 2001                               3-4
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT

The 1997 national census estimated that the population of Magoe District was 39,304
inhabitants, totaling 8,624 families. These are distributed in the three administrative posts at
Mpheende, Chinthopo and Mukumbura.
ii    Ethnicity
People living along the road are from the Nyungwe ethnic group (Cahora Bassa) and the yao
and Nyungwe ethnic groups (Magoe).
3.2.3     Economic Characteristics
i    Economy
The economy of the both Districts is almost entirely based on subsistence agriculture. Some
fishing also occurs on the southern shores of Lake Cahora Bassa.
ii    Poverty
The available data suggests that nutritional conditions in the Districts are improving. Cases of
malnutrition are not frequent although some cases do appear. Records of conditions such as
vitamin A deficiency and hyperthyroidism are often related to malaria cases. Many cases are
treated locally.
Categorizing families in terms of poor, medium and rich, surveys indicates some
differentiation between the Distrcts. On average within Cahora Bassa District between 85% to
90% of families are poor, 5% to 10 % medium and 1% to 5% rich. On average within Magoe
District, between 80% to 85% of families are poor, 10% to 15 % medium and 5% to 10% rich.

3.2.4    Services
i   Education
a    Cahora Bassa District
The secondary school in Songo teaches grade 8 to 10. the school has 12 classrooms catering
for approximately 420 students who are taught by 10 teachers. An upper primary school (EP2)
is based in Songo and it also has 12 classrooms. There are 20 teachers for 810 pupils with a
teacher pupil ration of I to 41. Cahora Bassa district also has twenty lower primary schools
wehre two-hundred teachers provide education from I st to 5th grades to 6,900 pupils.
The majority of the teachers, approximately 63% of the total number of teachers, have 6'h
grade plus one or three years of training. Only 2.8% of the teachers attended University in
Maputo.
b    Mdgo& District
Magoe District has five lower primary schools which total 25 classrooms. The schools are
located in Mphende, Dack, Mukumbura, Chinthopo and Mussenguezi. Forty-seven teachers
provide education from the I st to the 5th grade. No secondary school exists in the district but
an EP2 school with three classsrooms was built in Mphende.
The majority of the 32 teachers working in the district have 6th grade andthree years of
training.
ii   Health
Cahora Bassa District has one rural hospital situated in the district capital that has 75 beds. An
additional three health centres are located on the plateau, in Estima and another in Chipira.
The latter has a maternity unit but no vaccination post. Two health posts are also located in
Chipanda and Nharapirini. An average of 11,000 people are serviced by each health unit and
the maximum distance that people must travel is 115km. The district posses a number of
medical staff, namely I middle level medical assistant, 24 basic medical assistants, 12
elementary medical assistants and 39 orderlies.
In Magoe District there are one health centre and six health posts. The district has 19 medical
technicians, nine of whom are elementary level technicians while the remainder have basic
level. On average, each health unit services 3,000 people and the maximum distance that
patients need to walk to get to such a unit is of 60km.
The most frequent diseases include diarrhoeas, malaria, dysentery and parasite-related
diseases. The latter are most frequent between the months of November through to March.
Other diseases diagnosed during the year are tuberculosis and sexually transmitted diseases.

iii    Key Social Services and Facilitates
While the Districts are relatively well served by primary (EPI) schools access to secondary
schools is poor. Availability of qualified medical staff is extremely low and most families are
dependent upon traditional healers to meet their medical requirements.
3.2.5    Livelihood
i   Livelihood strategies
Subsistence agriculture is the main activity of families living in the District. The main food
crops grown are maize and sorghum. Table 5 shows the total cultivated area by Crop
(hectares) and total production (tonnes/metric) for 1999/2000. While livestock production is
not very significant, pigs, chickens, cattle and goats are kept for household consumption, as
well as for the market. The extent of the agricultural activity is dependent upon several
factors, notably the:
a  fertility of the soil;
L  availability of water;
E  availability of good pasture;
a  availability of supportive transportation linkages to facilitate trade;
o  availability of communication linkages to facilitate trade;
L  population density;
o  availability of social services and amenities to support the population; and
o  availability of supportive formal financial and credit institutions
