ii    General characteristics of a family living along or in close proximity to the ER555
Estima to Migoe road
Agricultural activities are carried out by the different members of the family who partake in
sowing, weeding and harvesting. Women are responsible for gathering fuel wood by chopping
down trees alongside men. Men also deal with crop storage. The agricultural produce grown
by the family is mainly used for own consumption but excess is sold in the markets of the
58602 Estima - Magoe                  January 2001                                 3-7
Mozambique Road Sector EA               DRAFT



lAlPA CTO L,l,,                                                               ISEC
district capital or to traders who come from the provincial capital. It is common to find
families who use the same farmland every year and who do not leave the land fallow. The
main agricultural system is still based upon slash and burn techniques.
In the house women will look after the children, cook, clean the house, and would be involved
in most daily household activities. Men are responsible for construction and maintenance of
the house. The period of most work is between November and February, the main period of
cultivation and harvest. Women will control the household stocks of food while men will be
responsible for expenditure related to the children.
3.2.6    Archaeological and cultural sites
The road does not pass through or affect any known archaeological or cultural sites. Any
major rehabilitation work would necessitate consultation with the local communities to ensure
that possible sites are as little adversely affected as possible.
58602 Estima - Magoe                 January 2001                                3-8
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



1IIPACTO tltd                                                                   NIsEC
The project road traverses sandy, gravel, and red soils of medium to fine texture. The road
does not pass along steep hilly terrain therefore land slides and mass wasting issues are not of
concern.
Although the soils have a medium erosion potential, the erosion hazard along the project road
is considered to be low-medium and is a function of the gentle undulating topography
traversed. Erosion and sedimentation effects will be greater during the rain period, however
the impacts from construction will be minimised by limiting the area of ground disturbance at
any one site, and restricting works to the dry period.
ii   Sedimentation Risk
Sedimentation is a secondary process of erosion and can be exacerbated by road works due to:
o deliberate and unanticipated modifications to surface and ground water hydrology;
o incomplete rehabilitation of roadside verge areas; and
o road rehabilitation through naturally hilly terrain.
Ground disturbance and the temporary stockpiling of materials during road works can trigger
accelerated sedimentation processes. The onset of this process will be coincident with ground
disturbance and will continue up until sites have been stabilised. The extent of ground
disturbance and clearing along the road corridor will be small, with an average width of less
than 15m disturbed during construction.
Other areas where ground disturbance and clearing is required during construction include
stockpile areas, borrow pit sites, construction camps, and work compounds.
iii    Mitigation Measures
Specific measures for erosion and sediment control during construction include:
o design of drainage works to ensure these collect from and discharge to natural drainage
lines. This may not always be possible for mitre drains and in those situations frequent,
low volume drains are preferred;
o provision of energy dissipation devices where discharge velocities and flows will erode
downstream drainage lines;
o  minimisation of disturbed areas;
o design and construction of erosion and sedimentation controls such as diversion banks,
filter structures (filter fences or windrowed vegetation) and sediment traps;
o diversion of significant upstream flows around working areas;
o undertaking progressive rehabilitation and revegetation of all slopes and disturbed areas;
and
o installation of temporary erosion control drains prior to the wet season. No new road
excavation will be commenced during the wet season.
Vegetation will be established on slopes, such as batters and roadside embankments, to prevent
localised rill erosion. Stone pitching may be required near culverts and bridges where the
embankments of the road approaches are steeper.
58602 Estima - Magoe                 January 2001                                4-3
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



I.IPACTO Lda tihSMEC
The project provides an opportunity to undertake the planting of trees for soil conservation
purposes along the road corridor and within existing degraded areas, particularly gullies,
barren terrain, rivers and at borrow pit sites.
4.2.3    Water Quality
i    Construction
Sedimentation and resultant water quality effects were discussed in the previous section. This
section looks at other water quality issues.
Hydrocarbons sourced from improper disposal facilities for used lubricants and poor operation
and maintenance practices for construction vehicles and machinery can contaminate surface
waters. Improper disposal of oil filters and other materials from vehicle and machinery
maintenance can also cause pollution.
Spilled petroleum fuel, as well as bitumen and explosives residue, also have a potential to
contribute to the pollution of waterbodies, but to a very minor extent.
Improper sanitation and waste disposal procedures in temporary construction camps can also
contaminate water resources.
ii    Operation
Vehicle movements and the roadside maintenance of vehicles can result in the contamination
of water resources along a road corridor by fuel and lubricants. Accidents may result in the
spillage of fuels or other products.
These potential effects already exist. Improved road conditions will improve vehicle operating
conditions. Road accidents involving accidental spills of fuel, lubricants, chemicals or other
toxic materials, which could reach waterbodies, are likely to be reduced. The effect of the road
works is therefore likely to reduce water pollution potential from these factors.
iii    Mitigation Measures
All fuels, lubricants and bitumen will be stored in bunded areas. Vehicle maintenance and
refuelling will be confined to areas in construction camps designed to confine lubricants and
fuel spillages.
The Contractor shall ensure that all bitumen and fuel storage areas are bunded to contain at
least 120% of the maximum capacity of the largest storage tank. The floor of the bunded area
shall be impervious, and storage facilities and bunds shall be inspected weekly for spills.
Adequate waste disposal and sanitation facilities will be provided at construction camps.
Any waste water or stormwater discharged from the site shall comply with the following
objectives:
58602 Estima - Magoe                   January 2001                                 4-4
Mozambique Road Sector EA                DRAFT



IIPA CTO La t'SMiEC
o  pH range                        6.0-9.0
o  Total Suspended Solids (TSS)    <50mg/I
o  Total Dissolved Solids (TDS)    <1500mg/I
o  Oils and surfactants            No visible film of oils or petrochemicals
4.2.4    Hydrology
i   Construction
The main construction activity that has the potential to impede flows will be the stockpiling of
materials if placed near drainage lines.
ii   Operation
Although the road is an existing feature, it can cause an impedance to flow, alter the natural
channel position and result in increased velocities as a result of this change. The road crosses
numerous seasonal watercourses. Adequate provision of culverts along the road will improve
cross-road drainage.
Adequate and stable cross-road and road surface drainage is essential to ensure that storm
runoff across the road causes minimal damage to the road and minimises the effect of
inundation caused by impeded flows. Cross-road drainage shall be provided by causeways and
culverts to pass catchment flows past the road, either over or beneath it. Road surface drainage
shall be provided by side drains to direct run-off off the road surface.
The potential exists for unpredictable rises in the water level of lake Cahora Bassa. This may
result in flooding along sections of the road, and ponding where the road passes over or
through seasonal watercourses. The flat terrain of the littoral zone on the southern lakeshore
precludes the occurrence of significant scouring and sedimentation impacts.
iii   Mitigation Measures
All road rehabilitation should take place during the dry season to decrease the probability of
inducing impacts associated with flowing water and surface water bodies.
Particular attention shall be given to stockpiling materials away from existing drainage lines
and avoid filling in wetland areas.
All designed drainage works shall be installed at the time of initial road construction to ensure
that flows along drainage lines are not impeded.
Culverts shall be laid in a direction, which is as close to the natural position of the drainage
flow. This will avoid altering the channel flow and prevent upstream and downstream
scouring of the watercourse.
58602 Estima - Mago6                 January 2001                                4-5
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



LIIPA CTO L da                                                           N",'SIEC
Routine monitoring of road condition should be more intensive in the wet season when
watercourses are in flow . Dialogue between ANE and HCB (Hidroelectricidade de Cahora
Bassa) should be established regarding any anticipated rise in lake levels that may flood the
road. This will allow for the immediate implementation of post flood monitoring and an
assessment of flood impacts on the road.
4.2.5    Noise and Vibration
i   Construction
Additional noise will be generated along the project road corridors and at ancillary sites during
the construction period, particularly from increased numbers of heavy vehicles.
Peak noise levels generated by construction activities are expected to be greater than the peak
noise levels from road operation. Construction noise is generally intermittent and depends on
the type of operation, location and function of the equipment and the equipment use cycle. It
attenuates quickly over distance. For example excavation noise levels, assuming bulldozer and
dump truck activity, are approximately 85dBA at 15m. This decreases by about 3-4dBA with
each doubling of distance and would be reduced to approximately 7OdBA at about 240m.
ii   Operation
As accessibility is improved after completion of the road works, vehicle noise levels may
increase slightly with an expected increase in traffic volumes. After completion of the works,
the traffic on the project road is expected to experience a high growth rate due to improved
accessibility (See Part B). Overall traffic volumes will be low probably numbering a few
hundred vehicles a day.
Noise sources and factors which may contribute to the increase in the ambient noise level and
related adverse impacts during the operation of the improved roads will include vehicle noise,
vehicle maintenance and driver behaviour (such as the use of horns).
The improved road design will allow an increase in vehicle speed and some increase in the
overall noise level is likely to result. When traffic speed doubles, this results in a 6 dB(A)
increase in noise level (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997). However, the project corridors are not
densely populated, and few sensitive sites, such as schools and hospitals, are expected to be
located close to the road. The majority of the road traverse cultivation land and woodland
areas. There is no potential for significant adverse noise effects.
The road works may reduce existing noise levels in built-up areas. The improvement of roads
will result in an improved road surface, which will consequently reduce noise levels through
the efficient operation of vehicles.
58602 Estima - Magoe                January 2001                               4-6
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



IIIPA CTO Ldut                                                              VW*SEC
iii   Mitigation Measures
No national noise level standards currently exist for highway construction equipment in
Mozambique, however the World Bank recommends the use of construction equipment, which
meets international standards.
The following measures shall be implemented to minimise the impact of noise during the
construction phase:
o  work compounds shall be located at least 0.5km from settlements;
o construction activities within 150m of residential areas shall cease between 19:00 and
06:00 hours; and
o machinery and vehicles shall be maintained and repaired regularly.
4.2.6    Air Quality
i   Construction
The deterioration in air quality from dust generation and vehicle emissions during road
construction will be a relatively minor environmental impact. Significant adverse effects are
not expected because of the limited duration of construction at any one site and the few
receptors. Most of the work will occur in cultivated or woodland areas.
A deterioration in air quality during road construction will primarily occur from dust generated
by road excavation, spoil transportation and disposal. In addition, most construction activities
will progressively move along the alignment, so prolonged deterioration in air quality will not
occur at any one site. The minor volumes of dust generated will cause short-term localised
problems through settlements.
Road construction workers will be exposed to minor dust emissions over the construction
period, with the quantity of dust partly dependent upon wind conditions.
ii    Operation
Exhaust emissions from traffic utilising the rehabilitated road are likely to increase due to the
high percentage increase in traffic growth expected (See Part B). Total emissions will be low
because the increase will be from a low existing traffic volume.
The level of dust is expected to reduce with rehabilitation as a result of stabilising the road
surface.
Improved road geometry will also reduce exhaust emissions along the project roads by
reducing the roughness of the road surface (particularly by removal of potholes), increasing the
design speed and thereby reducing the need for excessive acceleration and deceleration.
58602 Estima - Magoe                 January 2001                                4-7
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



,IIPACTO L d,                                                             WMSMEC
iii   Mitigation Measures
Mitigation measures will be implemented in areas where significant air quality problems
would arise due to construction activities and during road operation in major settlements and
near sensitive sites such as schools and hospitals.
Minimising air emissions is particularly important during the construction stage, for activities
such as the operation of borrow pits and in-situ mixing of cement and sand, which have a
potential to generate high dust levels.
During the construction period, the following mitigation measures to suppress dust will be
implemented:
o Water will be sprayed during the construction period at earth mixing sites, asphalt-mixing
sites, and on cleared surfaces. Water spraying will be undertaken during periods of high
wind, or when necessary, to suppress dust.
o Operations shall be suspended during periods of dry windy weather, in areas where wind
blown dust is causing a nuisance in settlements or towns.
a All equipment and plant machinery shall not emit visible smoke for a continuous period of
greater than 10 seconds.
O All exhaust systems and engines on plant and equipment shall be in accordance with the
manufacturers specifications and kept well maintained to ensure that they have a high level
operating efficiency.
4.3 BIOLOGICAL ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
4.3.1    Identification
i   Direct impacts
Vegetation will need to be cleared to undertake the road rehabilitation works and enable road
formation widening and construction of additional drainage. The conservation value of the
road corridor vegetation that will be cleared is not high and woodland areas crossed by the
road are utilised for slash and burn agriculture. The impact of vegetation clearing for the
construction works will not be significant due to the already degraded nature of the woodland
habitat and low conservation value.
Vegetation may also be cleared in areas selected for ancillary sites, such as construction
camps, borrow pits and quarries, to enable the utilisation of these areas.
ii   Indirect impacts
There is a potential impact associated with the indirect loss and degradation of vegetation,
habitat and species in the ROI. These potential impacts are associated with:
o improved access;
58602 Estima - Mago6                January 2001                               4-8
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



MIPA CTO La tiI'*SMEC
Li in-migration; and
o construction workforce behaviour.
Ecological degradation of the ROI may occur from vegetation harvesting and wildlife
poaching by the construction workforce, and increased harvesting during road operation due to
greater accessibility and additional local demands from road-induced activities.
The projects will provide improved access to the neighbouring areas and facilitate natural
resource use. The main potential for increased natural resource harvesting is likely to occur
within the area's woodlands.
The population increase along the road corridor would be derived either from people migrating
to the roadside from the immediate area (i.e. the 5km region of influence and adjacent to this),
or from other areas. This in-migration has a potential to create an increased local demand for
fuelwood and construction timber, as well as other woodland products to support secondary
enterprise activities, such as forage collection for grazing animals. Hunting may also increase.
The proposed road improvements will follow an existing alignment and almost all woodland
areas neighbouring this road are currently accessible. The road will only increase potential
access to these areas, not open them up.
Importing a construction workforce into an area may increase demand on local natural
resources due to competition for firewood, woodland products and wildlife. These increased
demands will be focussed on the areas surrounding workforce camps and work compounds.
Worker camps may also encourage the temporary construction of markets and barracas to
service the workers. These temporary facilities may also increase demand on local natural
resources.
West of the Daque River, the road traverses Tchumo Tchato controlled land where large
mammal populations are increasing as a consequence of active conservation management.
One consequence of increased traffic volume will be an increase in accident risk from large
mammals crossing the road both during the day and at night.
4.3.2    Mitigation measures
i   Direct Impacts
Vegetation clearance along the road edge and at ancillary sites (such as borrow pits) will be
minimised. All construction areas will be clearly marked and construction activity prohibited
outside the defined areas. Trees within ancillary sites shall be retained wherever possible.
Retention of trees within areas such as worker camps will improve the camp amenity and
provide shade.
All temporarily disturbed areas will be rehabilitated following construction. The establishment
of ground cover is the initial priority of revegetation. Ground cover reduces raindrop impact,
binds the soil surface and creates a micro-climate favourable for the establishment of other
vegetation. Species should be selected for cover crops and permanent revegetation. A cover
crop consisting of a rapid growing species and several vigorous grass species should be used to
58602 Estima - Mago4                 January 2001                                4-9
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



IIPA CTO L d(1(1                                                             SMEC
rapidly establish ground cover on disturbed areas. Permanent revegetation species should be
selected to be consistent with species native to an area.
Revegetation will aim to stabilise the disturbed areas and assist the desirable secondary uses of
natural resources including:
o  production of forage, wood, flowers, fruit, medicinal items;
o shade and shelter;
o fencing and screens; and
o building supports.
Revegetation along different sections of road will be designed to achieve site-specific aims,
such as maximising shade within villages and providing a species composition that is
compatible with sLirrounding vegetation cover.
ii   Indirect Impacts
The most potential for indirect effects was identified to be associated with workforce camps.
This is a temporary effect but may last several years depending on the duration of the works
project. To reduce the potential for increased exploitation of natural resources the following
migration measures will be implemented. The Contractor will:
o provide and maintain adequate drinking and bathing water supplies;
o provide kerosene or other fuel source and stoves for worker use;
o educate workers about the responsible use of natural resources; and
o ensure workers act in a responsible manner and apply penalties for the harvesting of local
resources (wood, wildlife or wood products).
Management measures will ensure that construction workers are educated about illegal
poaching and forest clearing and strict penalties will be enforced. Signage will be required
warning motorists of the presence of large mammals (notably elephant and antelopes)
especially to the west of the Daque River. Local communities involved in conservation-based
initiatives must be consulted and informed of road rehabilitation activities taking place within
conservation areas. The workforce must be informed concerning the dangers associated with
working in wildlife areas.
4.4 SOCIO-ECONOMIC IMPACTS
4.4.1    Direct Impacts
i   Loss of agricultural land, buildings, sites of cultural value, population displacement,
and natural resources degradation
It is unlikely that any improvements to the road will require significant areas of cultivated land.
The main loss will occur at borrow pits and minor realigning of the road. It is likely that small
areas of land currently used for cultivation will be lost. Where an area of cultivated land has to
58602 Estima - Magoe                 January 2001                               4-10
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



IJVIPA CTO L da                                                              ':SEC
be resumed for road construction, the farmer will be compensated for loss of production. The
following process will be applied:
o where possible the farmer should be notified of the loss of land one season in advance.
This is to avoid unnecessary loss of effort and seed; and
o the value of the lost production should be determined in consultation with the District
Agricultural Officer and the local Chefe de Poste. Compensation will be paid to the farmer
for the value of the lost production.
Borrow pits, storage and equipment areas, and construction camps will be located on marginal
or disturbed land. The acquiring of land for such purposes must be subject to ANE and
Provincial approval.  In selecting land consultation must also be undertaken with local
communities and their traditional leaders as well as local authorities (at District and Ward
level).
No displacement of population is likely. Cultivation is not continuous along the road and there
is sufficient land for machambas if necessary to be realigned. It is not envisaged that there
would any displacement of population of the local population due to road improvement.
4.4.2   Indirect Impacts
i   Access to services
Improvement of the road is likely to have significant socio-economic impacts. Availability of
services to the populations of Districts is poor. An improved road would enable, for example,
sick patients to gain access to better health services. Improved road communication would
also improve access for children to higher education facilities.
ii   Improved access to markets
Poor communication has been cited as a major problem which, perhaps most importantly,
limits the ability of the local population to transport surplus agricultural production to market.
Similarly improvements to the road would enable goods and services to flow more easily into
the area particularly those associated with improving agricultural production techniques and
yields.
MAgoe town is likely to benefit significantly from road improvement. The town is remote and
the road is its principle access to other parts of Mozambique. Improvements to the road would
reduce this travelling time and expense for people accessing the main road to Tete and other
parts of the Province.
An improved access road may also facilitate access to the Tchuma Tchato project and assist in
the development of ecotourism.
58602 Estima - Mago6                 January 2001                               4-11
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



BIPACTO Lda                                                                 Nx!SMEC
iii   In-migration of people and concessions
The road adjoins areas identified to have a high potential for forestry. Road improvements will
enable improved access for people wanting to exploit the natural resources of the District.
Mitigation measures are necessary if these natural resources are not over exploited.
Improvement of roads in other rural areas within Mozambique leads often to increases in in-
migration as advantage is taken of the improved communication. It is probable that some in-
migration will take place particularly in areas in close proximity to the road. Regullos have an
influence in terms of the local allocation of land. Mitigating measures should involve working
with regulos and other local authorities to control any possible in-migration.
Under existing Mozambican legislation all land belongs to the State. However, private sector
farmers may acquire long-term rights over land by obtaining a Title of Use and Exploitation.
Individuals may also seek concession rights over tracts of land to harvest timber.
Requests for "concessions" are lodged with an area's Provincial Agricultural Services. Once
access to hitherto isolated areas is improved following rehabilitation of a road it could be
expected that concession seekers will attempt to access these lands.
Although, at present, all land in Mozambique belongs to the State the defacto situation is that
land is being managed and allocated according to traditional customary land rights. The
potential for conflict over land between local communities and "concession seekers" is high.
Very few traditional farmers have formalized their rights to the land under existing legislation.
Where it is expected that demand for concessions increase as a result of improved access the
existing traditional land "ownership" should be mapped within the roads region of influence.
This should be undertaken at the road design phase to reduce the later potential for conflict
between traditional landowners and concession seekers.
iv    Health issues
The improvement of the road will necessitate the inflow of a number of construction workers
from other parts of Mozambique. The inflow of road gangs particularly into remote areas has a
potential for adverse effects. The most often cited problem is the one of HIV-AIDs and
sexually transmitted diseases.  Mitigation measures against these likely impacts require
working closely with respective govemment departments, local NGOs and/or church based
organisations, local communities, and road gangs.  Educational programs for the local
populations are considered to be extremely important in trying to reduce the likelihood of HIV-
AIDs infection.
v    Construction worker camps
The health issues related to construction worker camps particularly in terms of the spread of
HIV-AIDs and other sexually transmitted disease have been mentioned above.   A
concentration of workers also attracts other inward migrants who attempt to exploit new
market opportunities. Such informal sites are often poorly managed in terms of adherence to
health regulations and waste disposal. Mitigation measures should include the identification of
suitable sites for the establishment of small shops and other facilities servicing road-gangs.
58602 Estima - MJgoe                 January 2001                               4-12
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



JAIPA CTO Lda                                                             ((i*sSEC
In conclusion road improvements to the road will have significant positive impacts. Improved
communication would improve the ability of local populations to transport surplus agricultural
production to market. It would also likely to stimulate more production if farmers knew they
had a means of transporting their goods. Magoe town and the Tchuma Tchato project would
be beneficiaries of road improvements. The main negative socio-economic impacts relate top
the problem of the spread of HIV-AIDs infection. Campaigns in other parts of Mozambique
have demonstrated that these problems can mitigate against particularly if resources and
education are directed at the local population and not just the influx of construction workers.

5 UPGRADING AND MAINTENANCE MITIGATION
MEASURES
Design, construction and maintenance mitigation recommendations for the proposed roads
were formulated to avoid or mitigate potential biophysical impacts.
The road works will include environmental impact mitigation measures within the Contract
Specifications. These measures will be incorporated as specific instructions to the Contractor
and will be provided in a separate Environmental Management Guideline (EMG) identifying
specific instructions for the Contractor including responsibilities, outputs, costs and timing of
mitigation measures.
The EMG describes the practical means to ensure effective implementation of the mitigation
measures recommended for the potential adverse environmental impacts identified in the EIA.
The EMG will include but will not be limited to the following elements:
oi practical means to implement the recommended mitigation measures;
o implementation schedule and supervision program; and
o monitoring and evaluation.
In addition, the program also specifies what action will be taken and by whom in the event that
the proposed mitigation measures fail, either partially or totally, to achieve the level of
environmental protection expected.
An outline of the EMG for the construction and operation stage is provided in the main volume
of this report.
Specific socio-economic impact mitigation measures will include further consultation with
potentially affected land holders and provision of compensatory measures in accordance with
the current Government of Mozambique policy. These measures have been formulated by the
Department of Agriculture and include guidelines for the displacement of people and
acquisition of land for the roads.

1 INTRODUCTION
This Annex contains an environmental assessment for one of four additional roads to be
included as part of the Phase I World Bank projects (giving a total of 15 project roads).
Due to time limitations, no field investigations were undertaken for the this environmental
assessment. The description of the environment, potential impacts and recommendations were
prepared as a baseline study using information from existing literature sources, available
mapping sources, knowledge of Provincial characteristics and personal field observations from
ANE technical staff.
The primary references for this study include:
o  Mozambique Erosion Hazard Map (1986), 1:2,000,000.
o Topographic Map Series (1997), 1:250,000.
O Land Use Maps - Carta de Uso e Cobertura da Terra (1999) 1: 250,000.
o  Forestry Maps - Mapa Florestal (1994), 1:250,000.
o Wild H. and Barbosa LA. (1967). Flora Zambesiaca, 1:2,500,000.
o Laymeyer International et al (1999). Mepanda Uncua and Cahora Bassa North Project
Preliminary Environmental and Social Impact Assessment - Technical Annexes

2 BIOLOGICAL AND PHYSICAL ENVIRONMENTS
2.1  TOPOGRAPHY AND LANDFORMS
The road is located in a plateau zone generally at altitudes between 400 and l,000m. The area
traversed is a mixture of undulating terrain with steeper areas at either end of the road. There
are some elevations at 1,000 to 1,500 m above sea level to the north of Fingoe where the
landscape becomes more rugged and mountainous.
2.2 CLIMATE
Lying in the inter-tropical zone, the area has a tropical and subtropical climate with two
seasons, wet and hot (November to March), and dry and cool (April to October). The average
annual rainfall is 642mm, concentrated mainly in the wet season. Drought conditions are a
frequent occurrence during the dry season.
The average annual temperature is 270 C, the coldest months June and July, and the hottest
months October and November (see Figure 1).

Winds blow mainly North-South for most of the year.
The probability of extreme events (cyclones, floods) is minimal. Over the past five years there
have been no such events.
2.3 GEOLOGY
The geological formations traversed by the road are of Karroo origin and derive from the
oldest geological period, the Pre-Cambrian. Granite outcrops belonging to the Gabbro-
Anorthositic Complex are a feature of the geology of the region.
Pre-Cambrian formations occur extensively in the Tete Province especially north of the
Zambezi River where outcrops are generally found in the large plateau at elevations between
800 to 1,500m. These are best shown in the in northern Tete Province.
The Karroo geological formation covers a sizeable area to the north of the Zambeze River. In
general the outcrop corresponds to the large tectonic depressions in the Pre-Cambrian
formation and occurs at an average height of 200 to 600m.
Regionally the main type of rock in the area belongs to the Gabbro-Anorthositic Complex that
extends as far as Estima in the west, crossing the Zambezi to the north of Tete to the Revubue
river in the east.
The predominant rocks in the Gabbro-Anorthositic Complex in Tete are gabbros and norites.
The other lithotypes are: diorites, Anorthosites, rock with ferrous oxide, titanium and
vanadium, pyroxenite, basic dikes, and granulites with pyroxene.

The Fingo~-Bene road passes throuigh mainly red and brown clayey soils originating from acid
rocks such as granite and gneiss. These are characteristically of medium texture and are

The soil erosion potential along the road is generally medium to high. The high classification
is associated with the more rugged terrain around either end of the road and rainfall intensity.
2.5 LAND USE
Dispersed small settlements and homesteads are found along the road while larger settlements
are found in Fingoe and Bene. Herbaceous formations interspersed with small cultivated fields
make up the greater portion of the vegetation of the area. Thickets of shrubs averaging
between 0.5 and 3m but with heights to 7m are also found along the road alignment.
2.6 RIVERS AND WATER QUALITY
The road crosses the Capoche River approximately 20km west of Bene and the Nhimbe River
(a minor tributary of the Capoche) approximately 20km east of Fingoe. The area falls within
the hydrographic basin of the Capoche River, which is a sub-basin of the Zambezi River. Most
of the rivers in the area flow from north-west to south-east.
2.7 VEGETATION COVER AND USE
The area has open woodland vegetation, with grasses and some small deciduous trees that lose
their leaves in the dry season, giving the area a semi-arid character. The composition of the
vegetation is predominantly Miombo woodland common to a vast geographic area and does
not include species of biological value or with special conservation status. To the west of Bene
there is an initial area of Mopane woodland extending approximately 10 km to the east and
west of the Capoche River.
Adjacent to the road are extensive tracts of relatively undisturbed vegetation. Disturbance for
small farms or cultivated areas is concentrated along the road corridor, particularly in the lower
undulating areas.
The following vegetation types occur along the road:
i   Tardily Deciduous Miombo Woodland (Northern Plateau)
Brachystegiafloribunda, B. longifolia and often Julbernadia globiflora (Mapping Unit 18,
Wild and Barbosa 1967)
In Tete Province this corresponds approximately to a woodland where Brachystegia boehmii,
Prinari curatellifolia, and combretum molle occurs interspersed with tree or shrub savannas of
parinari, Uapaca, Faurea, etc. or shrub savannas of protea, Vitex, securidaca and several
species of Dombeya, i.e. D. nyaasica, D. johnstonii, D.lasiostylis and D. rotundifolia. These
communities appear at altitudes between 700 and 1,500m in red leached ferralitic soils.
Brachystegia manga is very common at altidues between 700 and 1,OOOm associated with B.
boehmii, J. globiflora, Pericopsis angolensis, Pterocarpus angolensis, Burkea africana,
Swartzia madagascariensis, Diplorhynchus condylocarpon, and Combretum spp. At lower
altitudes, Brachystegia bussei becomes common.
The grass flora includes Hyparrhenia spp. Andropong sp. and Chloridion cameronii.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                 January 2001                             2-6
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                            MMiSEC
ii   Deciduous Miombo Savanna Woodland (North-Eastern Lowland & Escarpment)
Brachystegia boehmii, B. allenni, Julbernardia globiflora (Mapping Unit 29, Wild & Barbosa
1967)
In Tete Province Brachystegia boehmii is dominant and abundant. The trees are 8 to Im tall in
orange, ferralitic more or less shallow sloping soils. The principal species are B. manga,
Julbernadia globiflora, Brachystegia utilis (relatively rare), Pericopsis angolensis,
Pterocarpus angolensis, Burkea africana, and Swartzia madagascariensis.
Another typical community of this type includes Brachystegia boehmii, Julbernadia
globiflora, Burkea africana, Pterocarpus angolensis, P. tinctorius, Hymenocardia acida,
Terminalia sericea, and Acacia macrothyrsa.
The grass layer is predominantly Hyparrhenia and Themeda triandra.
iii   Dry Deciduous Miombo
(Julbernardia globiflora) (Mapping Unit 30, Wild & Barbosa 1967).
A Julbernardia globiflora type occurs in Tete Province and is widespread in belts between the
Brachystegia boehmii communities and the drier Colophospermum mopane savanna
woodlands. It is also abundant on the Zambezi valley slopes from 400-700 m. and in the
lowlands where the topography is irregular. The following species in a decreasing order of
frequency occur where Julbernardia globiflora is dominant: Diplorhynchus condylocarpus,
Terminalia sericea, Brachystegia boehmii, Diospyros kirkii, Oxytenanthera abyssica,
Colophospermum mopane, Pterocarpus brenanii, Combretum ghasalense, Burkea africana,
Piliostigma thonningii, Pseudolachnostylis maprouneifolia, Acacia nigrescensis, Kirkia
acuminata, Dalbergia melanoxylon, etc. (Barbosa, 1958).
iv    Mopane Woodland
(Mapping Unit 35, Wild & Barbosa 1967).
Mopane woodland, after miombo woodland, is the second most important woodland type in
Mozambique. Mopane covers large areas in the Zambezi Valley (Tete Province) and the
valleys of its tributaries from Lupata to Zumbo below 400 m. The annual rainfall is 500-700
mm.
In the Zambezi valley the Colophospermum mopane woodland is usually medium or tall (10-
15 m.), almost pure but with some Adansonia digitata. The soils are brownish, clayey,
compact, with some calcareous material, relatively deep and mainly derived from Karroo
formations. In pans and depressions with calcareous soils, grassland with Setaria sp. is
dominant.
In shallow, stony and also calcareous soils Acacia nigrescens (subdominant), Dalbergia
melanoxylon, Acacia nilotica, Combretum ghasalense, Dalbergia boehmii, Adansonia digitata,
Albizia harveyi, Terminalia prunioides, T. stuhlmannii, Ziziphus mucronata, Combretum
apiculatum, Afzelia quanzensis, etc. accompany the still dominant mopane. Associated shrubs
or perennial include Euphorbiaceae (Euphorbia spp., Erythrococca sp.); Capparidaceae
58602 Bene - Fingoe               January 2001                              2-7
Mozambique Road Sector EA           DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                                4iftSI1EC
(Capparis, Thylacium, Cadaba, Maerua); Acanthaceae (Barleria, Disperma); Apocynaceae
(Adenium); Liliaceae (Aloe, Sansivieria) and Salvadoraceae (Salvadora, Azima). The grass
layer is of tufted species such as Ischaemum glaucostachyum, Eragrostis spp., Aristida spp.,
Setaria sp., etc.
Mopane savanna woodland often grades into Julbernardia globiflora savanna woodland in drier
areas with poorer well-drained soils and into the Adansonia, Sterculia savanna domain.
On the banks of the Zambezi River the mopane is often replaced by Ziziphus mauritiana,
Diospyros mespiliformis and sometimes Berchemia discolor.
In small depressions there are savannas with shrubby Combretum ghasalense (generally
dominant) and Diospyros kirkii, Terminalia sericea, Acacia nilotica, A. spp., Lonchocarpus
capassa, Ziziphus mucronata, etc.
The distribution of these vegetation types is depicted in Figure 4.

2.8 WILDLIFE AND CONSERVATION AREAS
Faunal assemblages are relatively depauperate in the area. Mammals are scarce with
herbivores such as Impala only occasionally sighted. A list of reported species is presented in
Table 2.
Table 2. List of mammals reported to occur in the area
Scientific name                      Common name
Aepyceros melampus                   Impala
Cercopithecus aethiops               Vervet monkey
Dasymys incomtus                     Cane rat
Potamochoerus porcus                 Wild pig
Rattus rattus                        Common rat
Sylvicarpra grimmia                  Grey duiker
There are no conservation areas in the immediate vicinity of the road.
2.9 NOISE AND AIR QUALITY
Noise levels along the Bene to Fingoe Project Road, are very low due to the absence of traffic
noise and sparse settlement along the route. Noise would be predominantly generated by
natural conditions (wind, insects, frogs, birds etc.).
Ambient air quality in all of the proposed road areas is good due to a lack of industry and
urban activity. Haze may be observed due to the frequently practiced slash and bum
agriculture and production of charcoal. Levels of airborne dust generated by wind passing
over agricultural field and smoke from forest and grassland fires can also be high.

3 SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
3.1 PROVINCIAL CHARACTERISTICS
The purpose of this section is to describe the socio-economic environment in Tete Province.
Information was derived from existing reports and publications where information is
disaggregated usually at the Provincial and District levels.
To describe the living conditions for the populations living along or in proximity to the road a
variety of Provincial and District socio-economic indices was used. Where possible statistical
information was provided to enable the establishment of base line data from which the impacts
of road improvement may be made.
3.1.1    Context and Administration
i   Provincial setting and administration
Tete Province is located in the north westem region of Mozambique and borders three
countries, Malawi to the east, Zambia in the north and Zimbabwe to the west. To the south of
Tete Province lies the Province of Manica. The shape of Tete Province as a peninsula
surrounded by foreign countries has influenced the economic, cultural and social links of Tete
with surrounding countries. The province also acts as a main corridor between the surrounding
countries.
Administratively Tete is divided into 12 Districts, with a Provincial Capital also called Tete.
Tete is the third largest Province in Mozambique with a land surface of 100,724km2
representing 12.8% of the land surface of Mozambique. Conversely it has the second lowest
population density at 11 people per km2.
ii   Traditional administration
Traditional administration authority exists in Tete Province. The importance attached to the
regulos varies from area to area. In those localities where the regulos are strong and respected
they will intervene in a variety of aspects of every-day life. Such issues would include
resolving social conflicts; allocating land for building housing, managing water sources and
supporting official health program such HIV/AIDS management.
Like most parts of Mozambique, tensions exist between the two types of administrative
systems. These tensions were exaggerated during the armed conflict. Areas controlled by
RENAMO largely reverted back to traditional leadership structures, while those under
Govemment control retained their post-independence organisational structures.
58602 Bene - Fingoe               January 2001                             3-1
Mozambique Road Sector EA           DRAFT



IMIPACTO Lda                                                          4iNSMEC
3.1.2    Population Characteristics
i Population
The 1997 national survey estimated the population of Tete to be 1,144,604 inhabitants with
more women (595,674) than men (548,930). The country's annual population growth rate is
estimated at 2.2% and if that rate continues to 2009 it is calculated that the population will
have grown by 34% to about 1,533,769.
The population of Tete Province is young with more than 50% aged 14 or younger. The
population density for Tete Province (11 inhabitants per kmi2) is lower than for the average
population density for the country (19 inhabitants per kM2). The total urban population is
calculated at 15% of the Provincial population In comparison to other Provinces Tete has a
relatively low urban percentage.
The population of Tete Province has experienced significant mobility over the last 40 years.
This has been associated with both the wars in Mozambique and also the Zimbabwe liberation
war. While most of Mozambique has experienced similar problems the people of Tete
probably experienced greater dislocation.
ii   Ethnicity
There are three main ethnic groups in Tete Province, the Nyanja (48%), Nyungwe (28%) and
Sena (12%). These percentages were based on the mother tongue of the people. Less than 3%
of the population speak Portuguese as their mother tongue.
iii   Gender
Despite the advancement of the rights of women under independent Mozambican
administration, girls and women in Tete Province do less well than boys and men. Access to
health services directed specifically at women is generally very poor. Literacy rates for
women are lower than for men. Family law in Mozambique still discriminates against women.
A married woman has limited rights to make independent economic decisions or enjoy full
ownership of assets, and a widow's rights to inheritance are secondary to males in the family.
Girls in rural areas in general marry young often as soon as they reach puberty.
3.1.3    Economic Characteristics
i   Economy
The economy of Tete is predominantly agricultural. Based on the percentage of people in
occupations the most important activities at the 1997 census were agriculture (90%/O), service
industries (5.5%), manufacturing (1.5%) and construction (1.4%).
58602 Bene - Fingoe               January 2001                             3-2
Mozambique Road Sector EA           DRAFT



IMUPA CTO L da                                                           "VSMEC
There is a low urban-rural ratio, with a majority of people (90%) dependant on subsistence
agriculture for their livelihood. There are few formal sector employment opportunities except
in the city of Tete.
ii   Poverty
Tete is one of the poorest provinces in the country with 82% classed as poor and 54% as very
poor. This compares with national averages of 69% (poor) and 38% (very poor).
Life expectancy in Tete Province is expected to be below the average level of 42 years
registered for the country.
3.1.4    Services
i   Education and health facilities
Information about the specific education and health facilities in Tete Province were not
compiled. Based on other roads studied for this project it is expected that:
o  There will be few schools and the availability of education will decrease as a child ages.
The average ratio of pupils:teacher is expected to be in excess of 50:1.
cl Common illnesses in the Province are malaria, tuberculosis, bilharzia, intestinal parasites,
acute respiratory infection, AIDS, sexually transmitted diseases and non-specific diarrhoea.
o  Current rates of HIV infection are in excess of 23% and are projected to rise. The central
region containing the provinces of Tete, Manica, Sofala and Zambezia has the highest rate
of infection in the country due to the presence of major transport corridors and proximity
to borders.
o  Probably half of all children under five-will suffer from chronic malnutrition and 1 in 10
from acute malnutrition.
ii   Access to services
Access to services for the rural population is generally poor. Most villages will have a primary
school but these schools could be categorised as rudimentary and often the availability of
teachers is very low. Figures are not available for the provision of secondary schools. Most
villages will have access to a basic health centre but few have access to a doctor. Dependence
on traditional healers is the norm for the vast majority of the population and almost every
village would have at least one person categorised as a traditional healer.
3.1.5    Livelihood Strategies
The principle economic activity in Tete Province is agriculture and 90% of the population
listed agriculture, forestry or fishing as their main area of activity at the 1997 census. Forestry
and fishing activities are generally for personal consumption. Most food production is within
the family sector upon which the majority of the population is dependent. The principle food
crops grown are maize, sorghum and millet.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                 Januaty 2001                               3-3
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



IMPA CTO Lda                                                           Q'fwSMEC
Livestock production is important to the well being of people in Tete. Most families will have
goats and a lesser number cattle. These animals are kept for reproduction and used as an
income source (money or barter).
3.1.6    Archaeological and Cultural Sites
The road does not pass through or affect any known archaeological or cultural sites. Any
major rehabilitation work would necessitate consultation with local administrative or
traditional leaders to identify any local cultural sites.
3.2 DISTRICT CHARACTERISTICS
3.2.1    Context and Administration
i   Introduction
This section describes socio-economic factors more specific to the EN221 Bene-Fingoe road.
The main sources of information relate to existing written reports concerning the Districts the
EN221 passes through. To better assess the possible and probable impacts of improvements to
the EN221 more detailed studies for socio-economic purposes will be needed.
ii   District setting and administration
Th  Xene to Fingoe EN221 road passes through the Districts of Chifunde and Maravia located
in t . central-north of Tete Province. To the north, Chifunde and Maravia District border with
Zambia. In the District headquarters representatives of the main sector ministries such as the
Health Ministry are found.
The EN221 project road begins on the outskirts of Bene, within the District of Chifunde and
continues into Maravia District where it ends at Fingoe.
iii   Political Environment
No inforrnation is available for voting practices based in the two Districts. Tete Province
voted for both the ruling FRELIMO party and RENAMO during the general elections of late
1999.
3.2.2    Population Characteristics
i   Population
The 1997 national census estimated the population of Chifunde District as 48,498 inhabitants
with a density of population of 5 inhabitants per km2. The sex ration is close to parity with
23,719 males and 24,779 females. The District has three administrative posts at Chifunde,
58602 Bene - Fingoe                January 2001                              3-4
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFr



IMIPA CTO Lda                                                                    ((I'SMEC
Mualadzi and N'Sadzo. The administrative post of Mualadzi is the most populous with
approximately 31,550 inhabitants.
The 1997 national census estimated that the population of Maravia District was 53,031
inhabitants, totaling 12,405 families, with a population density of 3 inhabitants per km2. These
are distributed in the four administrative posts of Maravia District, namely the posts at
Chibuto, Fingoe, Molowera and Chipera.
ii    Ethnicity
Most people living along the road are from the Nyanja ethnic group.
3.2.3     Economic Characteristics
i    Economy
The economy of the Maravia and Chifunde Districts is almost entirely based on agriculture.
ii    Poverty
The nutritional situation in the District is reasonable although the first semester of the year is
one where high hunger incidence is recorded. .Acute malnutrition , anaemia, hyperthyroid
disease and a lack of micro-nutrients are the major problems experienced in the district.
Although many cases are dealt with locally, the more serious cases are transferred to the Tete
Provincial Hospital.
Categorizing families in terms of poor, medium and rich indicates some differentiation
between the two Districts. Surveys indicate that on average within Chifunde District, between
75% to 80% of families are poor, 10% to 15 % medium and 5% to 10% rich. In Maravia
District approximately 85% of families are poor, 10 % medium and 5% rich Table 4 gives
examples of the characteristics of poverty for an average household.

3.2.4    Services
i   Education
According to information available from the District Development Profiles, of the 23 primary
schools in Chifunde District in 1982, only four schools with a total of 39 classrooms were
operating at the time of publishing the reports. No secondary schools exist in the District. The
schools cater from the I st to 4th/5th grade and each teacher has an average of 53 pupils in each
class. Of the 47 teachers in the District, 22 have no training, 16 have 6th grade and an
additional three years of training and nine have 6th grade and one year of training.
Maravia district has six lower primary schools. These are located in Fingoe town, Malowera,
Uncanha, Chiputo, Chipera and Nhenda. Forty-six teachers provide education from 1st to 5th
grades. The schools total 42 classrooms of which 18 are in the school at Fingoe. In the other
schools, the numbers of classrooms range from two to eight. No secondary school or upper
primary exists in Maravia district. Consequently, students must travel long distances to attend
upper primary schools.
ii   Health
Although no district hospital exists in Chifunde District there are three health posts, two of
which have a total of ten beds. The third post has still to be rehabilitated. Additionally, a
maternity unit with six beds was built in Nsadzo locality. A health unit is a category of health
provision below a health post. Within the District there are four basic medical assistants, three
elementary level assistants, four orderlies an 11 traditional midwives.
The District of Maravia possesses a rehabilitated health centre with six beds in the
administrative post of Maravia, one health post in Malowera with a vaccination post but no
maternity unit, and one at Chipera, with four beds, a maternity unit and a vaccination post.
The district posses a number of medical staff, namely seven basic medical assistants, seven
elementary medical assistants, one community health worker, 23 traditional midwives and
three orderlies.
3.2.5    Livelihood
i   Livelihood strategies
Subsistence agriculture is the main activity of families living in the Districts. The main food
crops grown are maize sorghum, beans and millet. Some sweet potato and groundnuts are also
grown.
Table 5 shows total cultivated area by Crop (hectres) and total production (tonnes/metric) for
1999/2000 for the Districts. While livestock production is not very significant, pigs, chickens,
cattle and goats are kept for household consumption, as well as for the market. The extent of
the agricultural activity is dependent upon several factors, notably the:

o  availability of supportive transportation linkages to facilitate trade;

o  population density;
o  availability of social services and amenities to support the population; and
o  availability of supportive formal financial and credit institutions.

ii   General characteristics of a family living along or in close proximity to the Fingoe
to Bene EN221 road
Agricultural activities are carried out by the different members of the family who partake in
sowing, weeding and harvesting. Women are responsible for gathering fuel wood by chopping
down trees alongside men. Men also deal with crop storage. The agricultural produce grown
by the family is mainly used for own consumption but excess is sold in the markets of the
district capital or to traders who come from the provincial capital. It is common to find
families who use the same farmland every year and who do not leave the land fallow. The
main agricultural system is still based upon slash and bum techniques.
In the house women will look after the children, cook, clean the house, and would be involved
in most daily household activities. Men are responsible for construction and maintenance of
the house. The period of most work is between November and February, the main period of
cultivation and harvest. Women will control the household stocks of food while men will be
responsible for expenditure related to the children.
3.2.6    Archaeological and cultural sites
The road does not pass through or affect any known archaeological or cultural sites. However,
any major rehabilitation work would necessitate consultation with the local communities to
ensure that possible sites are as little adversely affected as possible.
