4 POTENTIAL ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
4.1 INTRODUCTION
This section provides an assessment of the potential environmental impacts that are likely to
occur as a result of the Bene to Fingoe road works. Identified impacts will be mitigated
through the implementation of appropriate mitigation measures, which are discussed in detail
in Part D of this report. Environmental factors considered were:
o  land use change;
o  erosion and sedimentation;
o  water quality;
o  hydrology;
O  noise and vibration;
o  air quality;
o  biological effects; and
o  socio-economic effects.
Each of these issues is addressed in Sections covering the physical, biological and socio-
economic environments.
The majority of direct environmental impacts will be minor due to the limited extent of works
at any one site and the incorporation of impact mitigation measures into the improvement
works.
Indirect biophysical impacts may result from increased natural resource use due to increased
local enterprise activity to service increased traffic. These impacts are also anticipated to be
relatively minor due to the fact that the road is already well established as the major access
route from the northem interior of the Province to the Provincial capital of Tete.
Many of the potential environmental impacts associated with the construction of a new road do
not occur to the same degree when a road is improved within the existing road corridor. The
most likely impacts include:
o altered land use patterns due to improved accessibility, such as a change from forestry to
agriculture, or from agriculture to industry (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997);
o  public health problems, particularly through the spread of communicable disease (Birley,
1997); and
o  increased illegal logging and poaching activities due to improved access into forest areas
(Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997).
58602 Bene - Fingoe                January 2001                             4-1
Mozambique Road Sector EA            DRAFT



IMPACTO Lda                                                               VWSMEC
4.2 PHYSICAL EFFECTS
4.2.1    Land Use
i   Construction and Operation
The proposed road improvement works will not require a significant change to the land use
within the road corridor. Limited road improvement works will be undertaken and no road
realignments are anticipated. Resettlement will not be required.
A range of temporary ancillary activities (such as batching plant sites, borrow pits and
workforce camp sites) will be required during the construction works but will affect relatively
small land areas. These sites will be selected in consultation with local communities and
rehabilitated upon completion of activities.
There are no known existing borrow areas located nearby the project road but suitable
materials are available within the region to use as fill. The nearest active stone quarry is
located approximately 75km south of Bene along the main road to Tete.
The road works are not likely to have a significant effect on adjacent land use activities, as the
access currently exists.
ii   Mitigation Measures
Mitigation measures include:
o  measures identified under socio-economics for selection and negotiation of use;
L  preferably locating ancillary sites in areas currently cleared or degraded to avoid the need
for additional land clearing; and
o  rehabilitation of all sites to their original condition, or better, to allow resumption of prior
land use.
4.2.2    Erosion and Sedimentation
i   Erosion hazard
The dynamic interplay of several key factors dictates the degree of susceptibility to water-
driven soil erosion processes. These are:
o  seasonality and intensity of rainfall in relation to water run-off;
o  landscape topography and slope gradient;
o  soil profile parameters, notably depth and binding properties;
o  degree of vegetation cover; and
o  magnitude and frequency of seismic events.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                 January 2001                               4-2
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFr



IJlPA CTO L(la                                                               NiSIEC
The project road traverses red and brown clayey soils of medium texture.
The road traverses mostly gently undulating terrain but steeper gradients are encountered close
to Fingoe and Bene.
The erosion hazard along the project road is medium to high and is a function of the
topography landscape, soils and rainfall intensity. Erosion and sedimentation potential will be
greater during the wet season.
ii   Sedimentation Risk
Sedimentation is a secondary process of erosion and can be exacerbated by road works due to:
o deliberate and unanticipated modifications to surface and ground water hydrology;
o incomplete rehabilitation of roadside verge areas; and
o road rehabilitation through naturally hilly terrain.
Ground disturbance and the temporary stockpiling of materials during road works can trigger
accelerated sedimentation processes. The onset of this process will be coincident with ground
disturbance and will continue up until sites have been stabilised. The extent of ground
disturbance and clearing along the road corridor will be small, with an average width of less
than 1 5m disturbed during construction.
Other areas where ground disturbance and clearing is required during construction include
stockpile areas, borrow pit sites, construction camps, and work compounds.
There is the potential for erosion from the improvement works. No road designs are available
and it is not possible to say whether significant cut and fill is required or whether retaining
walls will be required. There may be a need for earthworks and specific stabilisation works.
These will need to be identified during the project design phase.
iii   Mitigation Measures
Specific stabilisation measures that may be incorporated into the project design include:
o  identification of geotechnically unstable areas.  Engineering designs would need to
consider road stability in these areas;
o  design of cut and fill batter slopes for long-term stability;
o  incorporation of retaining walls (dry rock and/or gabions) into the project;
o  hydrologic designs to specifically consider channel stability and identify the need for
linings such as rip-rap rock.
Specific measures for erosion and sediment control during construction include:
o  design of drainage works to ensure these collect from and discharge to natural drainage
lines. This may not always be possible for mitre drains and in those situations frequent,
low volume drains are preferred;
o  provision of energy dissipation devices where discharge velocities and flows will erode
downstream drainage lines;
58602 Bene - Fingoe                  January 2001                                4-3
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



IJIPACTO Ldal                                                                 N'tISEC
o  minimisation of disturbed areas;
z  design and construction of erosion and sedimentation controls such as diversion banks,
filter structures (filter fences or windrowed vegetation) and sediment traps;
3  diversion of significant upstream flows around working areas;
o  undertaking progressive rehabilitation and revegetation of all slopes and disturbed areas;
and
i installation of temporary erosion control drains prior to the wet season. No new road
excavation will be commenced during the wet season.
Vegetation will be established on slopes, such as batters and roadside embankments, to prevent
localised rill erosion. Stone pitching may be required near culverts and bridges where the
embankments of the road approaches are steeper.
The project provides an opportunity to undertake the planting of trees for soil conservation
purposes along the road corridor and within existing degraded areas, particularly gullies,
barren terrain, rivers and at borrow pit sites.
4.2.3    Water Quality
i    Construction
Sedimentation and resultant water quality effects were discussed in the previous section. This
section looks at other water quality issues.
Hydrocarbons sourced from improper disposal facilities for used lubricants and poor operation
and maintenance practices for construction vehicles and machinery can contaminate surface
waters. Improper disposal of oil filters and other materials from vehicle and machinery
maintenance can also cause pollution.
Spilled petroleum fuel, as well as bitumen and explosives residue, also have a potential to
contribute to the pollution of waterbodies, but to a very minor extent.
Improper sanitation and waste disposal procedures in temporary construction camps can also
contaminate water resources.
ii    Operation
Vehicle movements and the roadside maintenance of vehicles can result in the contamination
of water resources along a road corridor by fuel and lubricants. Accidents may result in the
spillage of fuels or other products.
These potential effects already exist. Improved road conditions will improve vehicle operating
conditions. Road accidents involving accidental spills of fuel, lubricants, chemicals or other
toxic materials, which could reach waterbodies, are likely to be reduced. The effect of the road
works is therefore likely to reduce water pollution potential from these factors.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                    January 2001                                 4-4
Mozambique Road Sector EA                DRAFT



IMIPA CTO Lda -l3,Q',,S1IEC
iii    Mitigation Measures
All fuels, lubricants and bitumen will be stored in bunded areas. Vehicle maintenance and
refuelling will be confined to areas in construction camps designed to confine lubricants and
fuel spillages.
The Contractor shall ensure that all bitumen and fuel storage areas are bunded to contain at
least 120% of the maximum capacity of the largest storage tank. The floor of the bunded area
shall be impervious, and storage facilities and bunds shall be inspected weekly for spills.
Adequate waste disposal and sanitation facilities will be provided at construction camps.
Any waste water or stormwater discharged from the site shall comply with the following
objectives:
o  pH range                        6.0-9.0
o  Total Suspended Solids (TSS)    <50mg/I
o  Total Dissolved Solids (TDS)    <1500mg/I
o  Oils and surfactants            No visible film of oils or petrochemicals
4.2.4    Hydrology
i   Construction and Operation
The main construction activity that has the potential to impede flows will be the stockpiling of
materials if placed near drainage lines.
Although the road is an existing feature, it can cause an impedance to flow, alter the natural
channel position and result in increased velocities as a result of this change. The road crosses
several seasonal watercourses and two perennial rivers. Adequate provision and appropriate
placement of culverts along the road will improve cross-road drainage.
Adequate and stable cross-road and road surface drainage is essential to ensure that stormwater
run-off does not damage the road and minimises the effect of inundation caused by impeded
flows. Cross-road drainage shall be provided by causeways and culverts to pass catchment
flows past the road, either over or beneath it. Road surface drainage shall be provided by side
drains to direct run-off off the road surface.
ii   Mitigation Measures
Particular attention shall be given to stockpiling materials away from existing drainage lines
and avoid filling in wetland areas.
All designed drainage works shall be installed at the time of initial road construction to ensure
that flows along drainage lines are not impeded.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                  January 2001                                4-5
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



MIPACTO Lda                                                                 N'S! EC
Culverts shall be laid in a direction, which is as close to the natural position of the drainage
flow.  This will avoid altering the channel flow and prevent upstream  and downstream
scouring of the watercourse.
4.2.5    Noise and Vibration
i   Construction
Additional noise will be generated along the project road corridors and at ancillary sites during
the construction period, particularly from increased numbers of heavy vehicles.
Peak noise levels generated by construction activities are expected to be greater than the peak
noise levels from, road operation. Construction noise is generally intermittent and depends on
the type of operation, location and function of the equipment and the equipment use cycle. It
attenuates quickly over distance. For example excavation noise levels, assuming bulldozer and
dump truck activity, are approximately 85dBA at 15m. This decreases by about 3-4dBA with
each doubling of distance and would be reduced to approximately 7OdBA at about 240m.
ii   Operation
As accessibility is improved after completion of the road works, vehicle noise levels may
increase slightly with an expected increase in traffic volumes. After completion of the works,
the traffic on the project road is expected to experience a high growth rate due to improved
accessibility (See Part B). Overall traffic volumes will be low probably numbering a few
hundred vehicles a day.
Noise sources and factors which may contribute to the increase in the ambient noise level and
related adverse impacts during the operation of the improved roads will include vehicle noise,
vehicle maintenance and driver behaviour (such as the use of horns).
The improved road design will allow an increase in vehicle speed and some increase in the
overall noise level is likely to result. When traffic speed doubles, this results in a 6 dB(A)
increase in noise level (Tsunokawa and Hoban, 1997). However, the project corridors are not
densely populated, and few sensitive sites, such as schools and hospitals, are expected to be
located close to the road. The majority of the road traverse cultivation land and woodland
areas. There is no potential for significant adverse noise effects.
The road works may reduce existing noise levels in built-up areas. The improvement of roads
will result in an improved road surface, which will consequently reduce noise levels through
the efficient operation of vehicles.
iii   Mitigation Measures
No national noise level standards currently exist for highway construction equipment in
Mozambique, however the World Bank recommends the use of construction equipment, which
meets international standards.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                  January 2001                                4-6
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



I,lPA CTO Lda                                                              -"'i*Q.SIEC
The following measures shall be implemented to minimise the impact of noise during the
construction phase:
o work compounds shall be located at least 0.5km from settlements;
o  construction activities within 150m of residential areas shall cease between 19:00 and
06:00 hours; and
Li machinery and vehicles shall be maintained and repaired regularly.
4.2.6    Air Quality
Construction
The deterioration in air quality from dust generation and vehicle emissions during road
construction will be a relatively minor environmental impact. Significant adverse effects are
not expected because of the limited duration of construction at any one site and the few
receptors. Most of the work will occur in cultivated or woodland areas.
A deterioration in air quality during road construction will primarily occur from dust generated
by road excavation, spoil transportation and disposal. In addition, most construction activities
will progressively move along the alignment, so prolonged deterioration in air quality will not
occur at any one site. The minor volumes of dust generated will cause short-term localised
problems through settlements.
Road construction workers will be exposed to minor dust emissions over the construction
period, with the quantity of dust partly dependent upon wind conditions.
ii   Operation
Exhaust emissions from traffic utilising the rehabilitated road are likely to increase due to the
high percentage increase in traffic growth expected (See Part B). Total emissions will be low
because the increase will be from a low existing traffic volume.
The level of dust is expected to reduce with rehabilitation as a result of stabilising the road
surface.
Improved road geometry will also reduce exhaust emissions along the project roads by
reducing the roughness of the road surface (particularly by removal of potholes), increasing the
design speed and reducing the need for excessive acceleration and deceleration.
iii   Mitigation Measures
Mitigation measures will be implemented in areas where air quality problems would arise due
to construction activities. Areas of concern for dust control are in settlements and sensitive
sites such as schools and hospitals.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                  January 2001                                4-7
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



LTIPACTO Lda (iS EC
Minimising air emissions is particularly important during the construction stage, for activities
such as the operation of borrow pits and in-sitL mixing of cement and sand, which have a
potential to generate high dust levels.
During the construction period, the following mitigation measures to suppress dust will be
implemented:
o  Water will be sprayed during the construction period at earth mixing sites, asphalt-mixing
sites, and on cleared surfaces. Water spraying will be undertaken during periods of high
wind, or when necessary, to suppress dust.
c  Operations shall be suspended during periods of dry windy weather, in areas where wind
blown dust is causing a nuisance in settlements or towns.
L  All equipment and plant machinery shall not emit visible smoke for a continuous period of
greater than 10 seconds.
L  All exhaust systems and engines on plant and equipment shall be in accordance with the
manufacturers specifications and kept well maintained to ensure that they have a high level
operating efficiency.
4.3 BIOLOGICAL ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
4.3.1    Identification
i   Direct impacts
Vegetation will need to be cleared to undertake the road rehabilitation works and enable road
formation widening and construction of additional drainage. The conservation value of the
road corridor vegetation that will be cleared is not high and woodland areas crossed by the
road are utilised for slash and burn agriculture. The impact of vegetation clearing for the
construction works will not be significant due to the already degraded nature of the woodland
habitat and low conservation value.
Vegetation may also be cleared in areas selected for ancillary sites, such as construction
camps, borrow pits and quarries, to enable the utilisation of these areas.
ii   Indirect impacts
There is a potential impact associated with the indirect loss and degradation of vegetation,
habitat and species in the ROI. These potential impacts are associated with:
o improved access;
o in-migration; and
o construction workforce behaviour.
Ecological degradation of the ROI may occur from vegetation harvesting and wildlife
poaching by the construction workforce, and increased harvesting during road operation due to
greater accessibility and additional local demands from road-induced activities.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                 January 2001                               4-8
Mozambique Road Sector E,              DRAFT



IMPA CTO LdIla                                                                vI.*SWNIEC
The projects will provide improved access to the neighbouring areas and facilitate natural
resource use. The main potential for increased natural resource harvesting is likely to occur
within the area's woodlands.
The population increase along the road corridor would be derived either from people migrating
to the roadside from the immediate area (i.e. the 5km region of influence and adjacent to this),
or from other areas. This in-migration has a potential to create an increased local demand for
fuelwood and construction timber, as well as other woodland products to support secondary
enterprise activities, such as forage collection for grazing animals. Hunting may also increase.
The proposed road improvements will follow an existing alignment and almost all woodland
areas neighbouring this road are currently accessible. The road will only increase potential
access to these areas, not open them up.
Importing a construction workforce into an area may increase demand on local natural
resources due to competition for firewood, woodland products and wildlife. These increased
demands will be focussed on the areas surrounding workforce camps and work compounds.
Worker camps may also encourage the temporary construction of markets and barracas to
service the workers. These temporary facilities may also increase demand on local natural
resources.
4.3.2    Mitigation Measures
i   Direct Impacts
Vegetation clearance along the road edge and at ancillary sites (such as borrow pits) will be
minimised. All construction areas will be clearly marked and construction activity prohibited
outside the defined areas. Trees within ancillary sites shall be retained wherever possible.
Retention of trees within areas such as worker camps will improve the camp amenity and
provide shade.
All temporarily disturbed areas will be rehabilitated following construction. The establishment
of ground cover is the initial priority of revegetation. Ground cover reduces raindrop impact,
binds the soil surface and creates a micro-climate favourable for the establishment of other
vegetation. Species should be selected for cover crops and permanent revegetation. A cover
crop consisting of a rapid growing species and several vigorous grass species should be used to
rapidly establish ground cover on disturbed areas. Permanent revegetation species should be
selected to be consistent with species native to an area.
Revegetation will aim to stabilise the disturbed areas and assist the desirable secondary uses of
natural resources including:
o production of forage, wood, flowers, fruit, medicinal items;
o shade and shelter;
o fencing and screens; and
o building supports.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                  January 2001                                  4-9
Mozambique Road Sector EA               DRAFT



IlIPACTO Ldta                                                             """,SNIEC
Revegetation along different sections of road will be designed to achieve site-specific aims,
such as maximising shade within villages and providing a species composition that is
compatible with surrounding vegetation cover.
ii   Indirect Impacts
The most potential for indirect effects was identified to be associated with workforce camps.
This is a temporary effect but may last several years depending on the duration of the works
project. To reduce the potential for increased exploitation of natural resources the following
migration measures will be implemented. The Contractor will:
o provide and maintain adequate drinking and bathing water supplies;
o provide kerosene or other fuel source and stoves for worker use;
oi educate workers about the responsible use of natural resources; and
oi ensure workers act in a responsible manner and apply penalties for the harvesting of local
resources (wood, wildlife or wood products).
4.4 SOCIO-ECONOMIC IMPACTS
4.4.1    Direct Impacts
i   Loss of agricultural land, buildings, sites of cultural value, population displacement,
and natural resources degradation
It is unlikely that any improvements to the road will require significant areas of cultivated land.
The main loss will occur at borrow pits and minor realigning of the road. It is likely that small
areas of land currently used for cultivation will be lost. Where an area of cultivated land has to
be resumed for road construction, the farmer will be compensated for loss of production. The
following process will be applied:
o where possible the farmer should be notified of the loss of land one season in advance.
This is to avoid unnecessary loss of effort and seed; and
o the value of the lost production should be determined in consultation with the District
Agricultural Officer and the local Chefe de Poste. Compensation will be paid to the farmer
for the value of the lost production.
Borrow pits, storage and equipment areas, and construction camps will be located on marginal
or disturbed land. The acquiring of land for such purposes must be subject to ANE and
Provincial approval.  In selecting land consultation must also be undertaken with local
communities and their traditional leaders as well as local authorities (at District and Ward
level).
No displacement of population is likely. Cultivation is not continuous along the road and there
is sufficient land for machambas if necessary to be realigned. It is not envisaged that there
would any displacement of population of the local population due to road improvement.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                 January 2001                              4-10
Mozambique Road Sector EA             DRAFT



I,MIPA CTO L da                                                             ((,S-MEC
4.4.2    Indirect Impacts
i   Access to services
Improvement of the road is likely to have significant socio-economic impacts. Availability of
services to the populations of Districts is poor. An improved road would enable, for example,
sick patients to gain access to better health services. Improved road communication would
also improve access for children to higher education facilities.
ii   Improved access to markets
Poor communication has been cited as a major problem which, perhaps most importantly,
limits the ability of the local population to transport surplus agricultural production to market.
Similarly improvements to the road would enable goods and services to flow more easily into
the area particularly those associated with improving agricultural production techniques and
yields.
Fingoe town is likely to benefit significantly from road improvement. The town is remote and
the EN 221 is its principle access. Improvements to the road would reduce this travelling time
and expense for people accessing the main road to Tete and other parts of the Province.
iii   In-migration of people and concessions
The road adjoins areas identified to have a high potential for forestry. Road improvements will
enable improved access for people wanting to exploit the natural resources of the District.
Mitigation measures are necessary if these natural resources are not over exploited.
Improvement of roads in other rural areas within Mozambique leads often to increases in in-
migration as advantage is taken of the improved communication. It is probable that some in-
migration will take place particularly in areas in close proximity to the road. Regulos have an
influence in terms of the local allocation of land. Mitigating measures should involve working
with regulos and other local authorities to control any possible in-migration.
Under existing Mozambican legislation all land belongs to the State. However, private sector
farmers may acquire long-term rights over land by obtaining a Title of Use and Exploitation.
Individuals may also seek concession rights over tracts of land to harvest timber.
Requests for "concessions" are lodged with an area's Provincial Agricultural Services. Once
access to hitherto isolated areas is improved following rehabilitation of a road it could be
expected that concession seekers will attempt to access these lands.
Although, at present, all land in Mozambique belongs to the State the defacto situation is that
land is being managed and allocated according to traditional customary land rights. The
potential for conflict over land between local communities and "concession seekers" is high.
Very few traditional farmers have formalized their rights to the land under existing legislation.
58602 Bene - Fingoe                  January 2001                               4-11
Mozambique Road Sector EA              DRAFT



IJIPACTO Lla                                                               (IAWSMEC
Where it is expected that demand for concessions increase as a result of improved access the
existing traditional land "ownership" should be mapped within the roads region of influence.
This should be undertaken at the road design phase to reduce the later potential for conflict
between traditional landowners and concession seekers.
iv    Health issues
The improvement of the road will necessitate the inflow of a number of construction workers
from other parts of Mozambique. The inflow of road gangs particularly into remote areas has a
potential for adverse effects. The most often cited problem is the one of HIV-AIDs and
sexually transmitted diseases.  Mitigation measures against these likely impacts require
working closely with respective government departments, local NGOs and/or church based
organisations, local communities, and road gangs.  Educational programs for the local
populations are considered to be extremely important in trying to reduce the likelihood of HIV-
AlDs infection.
v    Construction worker camps
The health issues related to construction worker camps particularly in terms of the spread of
HIV-AIDs and other sexually transmitted disease have been mentioned above.   A
concentration of workers also attracts other inward migrants who attempt to exploit new
market opportunities. Such informal sites are often poorly managed in terms of adherence to
health regulations and waste disposal. Mitigation measures should include the identification of
suitable sites for the establishment of small shops and other facilities servicing road-gangs.
In conclusion road improvements to the EN221 will have significant positive impacts.
Improved communication would improve the ability of local populations to transport surplus
agricultural production to market. It would also likely to stimulate more production if farmers
knew they had a means of transporting their goods. Fingoe town would be a significant
beneficiary of road improvements as the town is relatively isolated because of the poor
condition of the existing road. The main negative socio-economic impacts relate top the
problem of the spread of HIV-AIDs infection. Campaigns in other parts of Mozambique have
demonstrated that these problems can mitigate against particularly if resources and education
are directed at the local population and not just the influx of construction workers.

5 UPGRADING AND MAINTENANCE MITIGATION
MEASURES
Design, construction and maintenance mitigation recommendations for the proposed roads
were formulated to avoid or mitigate potential biophysical impacts.
The road works will include environmental impact mitigation measures within the Contract
Specifications. These measures will be incorporated as specific instructions to the Contractor
and will be provided in a separate Environmental Management Guideline (EMG) identifying
specific instructions for the Contractor including responsibilities, outputs, costs and timing of
mitigation measures.
The EMG describes the practical means to ensure effective implementation of the mitigation
measures recommended for the potential adverse environmental impacts identified in the EIA.
The EMG will include but will not be limited to the following elements:
o  practical means to implement the recommended mitigation measures;
U implementation schedule and supervision program; and
o monitoring and evaluation.
In addition, the program also specifies what action will be taken and by whom in the event that
the proposed mitigation measures fail, either partially or totally, to achieve the level of
environmental protection expected.
An outline of the EMG for the construction and operation stage is provided in the main volume
of this report.
Specific socio-economic impact mitigation measures will include further consultation with
potentially affected land holders and provision of compensatory measures in accordance with
the current Government of Mozambique policy. These measures have been formulated by the
Department of Agriculture and include guidelines for the displacement of people and
acquisition of land for the roads.
